              1.1.1.4.4.7.6.1. Con:
              The cosmological argument does not require a thing to begin to exist, but to exist at all.

              1.1.1.4.4.7.6.2. Con:
              Lack of knowledge of the cause of quantum particles appearing does not count as evidence that they are non-caused.

            1.1.1.4.4.7.7. Pro:
            If causality did not exist, the universe would never have been caused to exist.

              1.1.1.4.4.7.7.1. Con:
              Causality is not something that logically could fail to exist because it is an abstract concept like evil.  Evil is essentially that which opposes the all powerful being whether there is any manifestation of it or not.  The concept exists.  Whether it applies to anything is another matter altogether.

              1.1.1.4.4.7.7.2. Con:
              The form of the parent argument contradicts itself. By explaining that the universe would not need a reason to exist, it gives a reason why the universe would not need a reason to exist.

            1.1.1.4.4.7.8. Con:
            The axiom of logic that states that effects have causes that precede them in a chain of causation is independent of physical laws, and as such remains in effect regardless of the status of the universe.

              1.1.1.4.4.7.8.1. Con:
              We assume effects have causes because this is what we see. It is not an axiom of logic which allows us to assume a cause without providing a reason to do so.

                1.1.1.4.4.7.8.1.1. Con:
                We do not in fact observe all causes for all effects.  We do in fact assume causes for all effects because effects would be unintelligible/inexplicable without them (i.e. no reason, cause, or ground for them to be).  Accordingly, the law of cause and effect is necessary for intelligible science.  Therefore, it is appropriately an axiom of logic because without it the universe is unintelligible.

                  1.1.1.4.4.7.8.1.1.1. Con:
                  The universe does not need to be intelligible, and science does not need to be true. The evidence suggests that in general they are, and they have proven useful, but that science is convenient is not a logical argument that it exists.

                  1.1.1.4.4.7.8.1.1.2. Con:
                  We have observed random events in the form of quantum mechanics. As we know random events exists, we know that there are events without a cause.

                    1.1.1.4.4.7.8.1.1.2.1. Con:
                    An event being random doesn't mean that it lacks a cause.

                      1.1.1.4.4.7.8.1.1.2.1.1. Con:
                      If events 1a and 1b are equally likely random events assuming event 1, event 1 may be considered a cause, but if a happens, that a happened, and b did not was not caused.

                    1.1.1.4.4.7.8.1.1.2.2. Con:
                    Quantum events do have a cause in the form of fluctuating fields and consistent, though imprecise, laws.

                      1.1.1.4.4.7.8.1.1.2.2.1. Con:
                      The causes of quantum events are partial, and insufficient to explain, or predict the event.

          1.1.1.4.4.8. Con:
          According to the b-theory [82] of time the universe has always existed.

            1.1.1.4.4.8.1. Pro:
            -> See 1.1.1.4.2.2.1.3.15.2.1.1.1.1.2.2.2.1.1.

          1.1.1.4.4.9. Pro:
          -> See 1.1.1.4.2.2.1.3.15.2.1.1.1.1.2.

          1.1.1.4.4.10. Pro:
          A universe (or temporarily ordered series of universes) that did not begin to exist is philosophically incoherent because it implies the formation of a concrete actual infinity by successive addition of events in time

            1.1.1.4.4.10.1. Con:
            That describes a universe which began to exist an infinite amount of time in the past, an idea which is indeed incoherent but is not identical with a universe that did not begin to exist.

            1.1.1.4.4.10.2. Pro:
            It is impossible to traverse an infinite number of past events of finite size. Consider:
            
            A. If one started today to count an infinite number of days, they will never reach the end of that sequence. Every day counted would leave them an infinite number of days away.
            
            B. In an infinitely old universe, a day must exist that is an infinite number of days prior to now. 
            
            These two statements are mutually-exclusive and cannot both be true, yet both must be true in an infinitely old universe.

              1.1.1.4.4.10.2.1. Con:
              B is false. Infinity is not a real number [83], and the number of days ago some particular day was can only be an integer.

                1.1.1.4.4.10.2.1.1. Con:
                Infinity and transfinite arithmetic are well defined in set theory [84]. A universe without beginning means the number of days is actually infinite (i.e. the cardinality is the same as the natural numbers). Therefore, starting from today (day 0) and counting backwards (day 1,2,3...) there must exist some day that will be labeled with the lowest infinite ordinal omega. That day or any day beyond it (omega+1, omega+2, ...) motivates saying there would be "an infinite number of days prior" to today.

                  1.1.1.4.4.10.2.1.1.1. Con:
                  That is assuming that a 'day' is a consistent form of measurement, which we know is incorrect as as you approach plank time the standard definitions of time and space cease to have meaning.

                    1.1.1.4.4.10.2.1.1.1.1. Con:
                    The planck time [85] is an extremely small amount of time, but the grandparent argument is talking about counting a series of 24-hour days, not shortening existing days. Thus, it is not clear how the days in the grandparent argument "approach planck time."

            1.1.1.4.4.10.3. Con:
            According to the b-theory [86] of time, all time exists at once and therefore there is no need to traverse it.

              1.1.1.4.4.10.3.1. Pro:
              -> See 1.1.1.4.2.2.1.3.15.2.1.1.1.1.2.2.2.1.1.

          1.1.1.4.4.11. Con:
          The law of causality works within the universe, where time exists.

            1.1.1.4.4.11.1. Pro:
            Causes and effects are events in the universe. They occur at a given time, in a given place. There is no event where and when 'The universe began to exist'.

            1.1.1.4.4.11.2. Con:
            Cosmologists who consider multiverse theory assume that laws of causality still hold outside of our universe in the larger multiverse. In other words, they see no reason to offer a defense of the laws of causality when moving to a contexts where the laws of physics might be entirely different. This is suggestive that we all intuitively think of the law of causality as a metaphysical principle that applies to any physical reality and not just to our particular universe.

              1.1.1.4.4.11.2.1. Con:
              "Multiverse theory" is ill-specified. The many-worlds interpretation of quantum mechanics - the most prominent theory of a multiverse - implies universes with identical physical laws, so causality would obviously be present but not in a sense for which this argument would be relevant.

              1.1.1.4.4.11.2.2. Con:
              Any 'physical reality' would have matter and hence time. This confirms the claim that causality works within the universe, where time exists.

          1.1.1.4.4.12. Pro:
          The evidence in cosmology is best explained by Big Bang theory [87], which implies that our observable universe began to exist

            1.1.1.4.4.12.1. Pro:
            If our universe was infinitely old it would have undergone heat death long ago, and it hasn't.

              1.1.1.4.4.12.1.1. Con:
              Our universe would not be heat dead if it undergoes a cycle that restores it to a state of low entropy.

                1.1.1.4.4.12.1.1.1. Con:
                Without an external source of energy, such a cycle would lose energy after every iteration, like a basketball losing energy after every bounce unless it is being dribbled.
                
                After a long, but finite number of cycles, the universe would indeed suffer heat death. In an infinitely old universe, this would have happened an infinitely long time ago.

              1.1.1.4.4.12.1.2. Con:
              Our universe would not be heat dead if it didn't have activity for an infinitely long period of time (ie. by being quantum mechanically infinitesimally probable to suffer any change during it's infinitely long initial stage).

                1.1.1.4.4.12.1.2.1. Con:
                "Infinitely long" implies that time was existent during this time, which is philosophically impossible.

                1.1.1.4.4.12.1.2.2. Con:
                This ignores Brownian motion [88]. If the assertion is that even Brownian motion did not happen, then it appears that the universe was already in heat death.

            1.1.1.4.4.12.2. Pro:
            You say that there needed to be space, time, and matter for the big bang. Well, in Genesis 1:1 it says "In the beginning (time) God created the heavens (space) and the earth (matter)." You say our belief takes faith, well, so does yours. You weren't there when the big bang happened. And, to be honest, it takes more faith to believe that then it does to believe in an all powerful God.

            1.1.1.4.4.12.3. Pro:
            The evidence that God exists is overwhelming, not only on the basis of scripture but in the world around us even from the Big Bang, which is where God spoke the universe into existence through the Big Bang.

            1.1.1.4.4.12.4. Con:
            The second law [89] of thermodynamics has no known exceptions. The claim that the universe can bring the order we see around us from the disorder of the big bang is incompatible with this law.

              1.1.1.4.4.12.4.1. Con:
              Some versions of the Big Bang theory hypothesize that the Universe was compressed into a single particle just 13.8B years ago. The Second Law of Thermodynamics is limited to systems of many particles, so it would not apply in such a scenario. Thus, this law does not tell us what preceded such an event.

              1.1.1.4.4.12.4.2. Con:
              The second law is about the entropy of a closed system, however, the Earth is not a closed system as both mass and energy can enter or leave it (e.g. we continually receive radiation energy from the sun and lose helium to space.)

              1.1.1.4.4.12.4.3. Con:
              The above claim is comparable to saying that water cannot freeze because entropy would decrease. The fact is that it is possible to decrease entropy locally, it just has to be offset by a larger entropy increase in the whole system.

              1.1.1.4.4.12.4.4. Con:
              The entropy of the universe at the Big Bang was far lower than it is today. Thus, the universe today has more entropy than it did at the Big Bang and so the second law is not violated.

              1.1.1.4.4.12.4.5. Pro:
              The second law of thermodynamics indicates that the universe is running out of usable energy. If the universe had existed for an infinite number of past days, then all usable energy should have disappeared long ago.

                1.1.1.4.4.12.4.5.1. Con:
                The total amount of energy in the universe can be shown [90] to be 0. It simply not evenly distributed.

                  1.1.1.4.4.12.4.5.1.1. Con:
                  This is entirely consistent with a changing distribution of energy that makes less of it usable over time (i.e. entropy), so does not oppose the parent.

                1.1.1.4.4.12.4.5.2. Con:
                Scientists do not claim that the universe has existed for an infinite number of past days. The current estimate is about 13.8 billion years [91].

              1.1.1.4.4.12.4.6. Con:
              Entropy is not exactly a measure of disorder. Instead, it is a measure of the possible microstates of the system. The entropy of the early universe was lower than today, perfectly in agreement [92] with the second law of thermodynamics.

              1.1.1.4.4.12.4.7. Con:
              This claim is against the Big Bang, not in support of the existence of God.

            1.1.1.4.4.12.5. Con:
            Cosmologists propose a beginning for the current timeline of the universe, not for the universe as a whole.

              1.1.1.4.4.12.5.1. Con:
              It is unclear that there is a meaningful distinction between "current timeline of the universe" and "the universe as a whole."

                1.1.1.4.4.12.5.1.1. Con:
                The current timeline of the universe is defined as events which occurred since the rapid expansion. It is not known if the universe as a whole extend beyond this point. The current timeline is the universe as we know it today, whereas the universe as a whole may possibly extend past that point.

                  1.1.1.4.4.12.5.1.1.1. Con:
                  The "compact universe" before its expansion still needs a cause for its existance.

                  1.1.1.4.4.12.5.1.1.2. Con:
                  The universe's expansion requires a cause. Otherwise it would have stayed in its state.

              1.1.1.4.4.12.5.2. Pro:
              -> See 1.1.1.4.4.12.5.1.1.

            1.1.1.4.4.12.6. Con:
            Even if the age of the universe is finite (which we do not know), that would not suggest or support in any way the existence of a god.

            1.1.1.4.4.12.7. Con:
            The argument uses misleading language, as 'began to exist' does not mean what one would think it does.

            1.1.1.4.4.12.8. Con:
            There is at least one cosmological model that does not hold that the universe began to exist and is better supported by current evidence.

              1.1.1.4.4.12.8.1. Pro:
              Steady state models [93] do not hold that the universe began to exist.

              1.1.1.4.4.12.8.2. Pro:
              The Gott-Li model (Closed Timelike Curves) [94] does not hold that the universe began to exist.

              1.1.1.4.4.12.8.3. Con:
              If there is a cosmological model of a universe with an infinite past, that is better supported by current evidence, it would replace the Big Bang as the mainstream theory.

              1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4. Con:
              Multiverse theories fail by every philosophical measure.

                1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.1. Pro:
                Multiverse theories have low explanatory scope, since they don't explain any data other than the fine-tuning of the universe. In contrast, theism explains the existence of the universe and of objective morality, as well as many miracle claims and subjective experiences.

                  1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.1.1. Con:
                  The multiverse theory can be treated as part of a larger philosophy of naturalism that also aims to explain all things theism attempts to explain. Then they would have the same explanatory scope.

                1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.2. Pro:
                Multiverse theories are very unparsimonious, since they posit a bafflingly large number of entire universes that cannot be observed. Theism only proposes a single extra entity.

                  1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.2.1. Con:
                  This assumes parsimony concerns limiting the number of entities as opposed to the number of types of entities. God would represent a significantly distinct class of entities not present in naturalism. Many people's intuitions may consider this less parsimonious than adding more universes like our own.

                1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.3. Pro:
                Multiverse theories are ad hoc, since the existence of causally disconnected universes isn't very plausibly supported outside of its use as an answer to fine-tuning. Theism is supported by other arguments, as well as by the subjective experiences of many.

                  1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.3.1. Con:
                  Max Tegmark's mathematical universe hypothesis [95] gives a philosophical framework for the nature of existence that implies a form of multiverse.

                    1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.3.1.1. Con:
                    It's not clear that consciousness can be simulated by purely mathematical processes.

                      1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.3.1.1.1. Pro:
                      The Chinese room argument [96] holds that simulating consciousness within non-conscious systems is impossible.

                        1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.3.1.1.1.1. Con:
                        The Chinese room argument is a hypothetical scenario, and merely purports to show how the illusion of consciousness may not be actual consciousness, not that creating, or even simulating consciousness is impossible

                          1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.3.1.1.1.1.1. Con:
                          By Occam's Razor [97] (parsimony) there would be no need for the additional entity of conscious experience to explain the behavior of the machine or Room.

                            1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.3.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con:
                            There is no explanation for the behavior in the scenario, simple or otherwise.

                        1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.3.1.1.1.2. Con:
                        The Chinese room thought experiment demonstrates an entity that is incapable of performing a task, speaking Chinese, can have components added that allow it to appear to perform this task. It extends this argument to say the same is true of computers that appear conscious. However, this ignores that the thing between the first entity, and the audience, is itself an entity. And this entity as a whole can perform this task, thus could be considered conscious.

                      1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.3.1.1.2. Pro:
                      Quantum mechanics has shown [98] that consciousness isn't describable by purely physical processes.

                  1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.3.2. Con:
                  Multiverse theories build on principles of cosmology developed and verified while explaining various phenomena within our universe. Therefore, extending these ideas to explain the phenomena of fine-tuning and the origin of our universe is motivated hypothesis building as opposed to pure ad hoc stipulation.

                  1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.3.3. Con:
                  This assumes the existence of God is supported by other arguments (see Cons for the head claim of this debate (i.e. God exists) as well as those underneath the Pros for the head claim)

                  1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.3.4. Pro:
                  See the Pros for the head claim of this debate (i.e. God exists)

                1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.4. Pro:
                Multiverse theories have low explanatory power: P1. A perfectly random system is more likely to produce simpler results than more complex ones P2. There are simpler possible universes than ours which contain conscious beings P3. We should expect to find ourselves in the most common situation that produces observers (Copernican principle) C. Under multiverse theories, our existence in this universe is unlikely

                  1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.4.1. Pro:
                  By contrast to the multiverse theory, a conscious creator could have many valid reasons to create a more complex universe like our own.

                  1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.4.2. Con:
                  The universe looks complex to us because we are a part of it, and one can't accurately, and completely model something they are a part of

                    1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.4.2.1. Con:
                    This doesn't show that the universe is not complex or that simpler, consciousness-producing universes cannot exist.

                    1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.4.2.2. Con:
                    One doesn't need a complete or fully accurate model of something in order to judge its complexity.

                      1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.4.2.2.1. Con:
                      If our brains were so simple that we could understand them, we would be so simple that we could not understand them, because this is an example of something trying to model itself. Any model that we could make would need to necessarily be quite crude, because an accurate one would need to be all encompassing, and anything we want to model that can model itself needs to be able to do other things.

                        1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.4.2.2.1.1. Con:
                        This statement isn't true in all possible worlds. In a universe with different laws, much simpler intelligent systems are conceivably possible.

                          1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.4.2.2.1.1.1. Con:
                          Suppose a computer has a total of N possible states. Suppose there is something outside of the computer in the universe, so the universe has at least N+1 possible states. With zero overhead, each state of the computer can correspond to a state of the universe, but since the universe has more states than the computer, some states of the universe will map to the same state of the computer, in which case the simulation will not be able to distinguish between them.

                  1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.4.3. Con:
                  Just because there are simpler "possible" universes than ours does not mean that the universe is all that complicated. The rules guiding this universe are relatively simple.

                  1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.4.4. Con:
                  A simpler model that allows for sentient beings has not been presented.

                    1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.4.4.1. Con:
                    A Boltzmann Brain [99] type universe allows for sentient beings and is simpler than the multiverse.

                  1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.4.5. Con:
                  Multiverse models are not required to be completely random in the universes they generate. They can, in principle, have motivated constraints on the types of universes generated within the multiverse which would lead to universes like ours being among the most probable.

                1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.5. Pro:
                Multiverse theories are unfalsifiable, since it is impossible by definition for a causally disconnected universe to interact with our own. Theism is at least possibly falsifiable, since God would be able to interact with the universe.

                  1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.5.1. Con:
                  The multiverse theory predicts [100] that the vacuum energy is probably nearly as far from zero as possible for a universe capable of supporting sentient life, whereas a single finely-tuned universe ought to have it at exactly zero; this constitutes a testable prediction.

                    1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.5.1.1. Con:
                    There is no reason to assume that a creator would be compelled to make the vacuum energy zero.

                  1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.5.2. Con:
                  Most multiverse models allow for causal interaction between universes. At the very least there is interaction between our universe and the universe within/from which our universe arose. Depending on this process, various marks might be left on our universe to hint at what came before. No such evidence has been observed allowing us to falsify various multiverse models.

            1.1.1.4.4.12.9. Pro:
            Naive extrapolation of the Big Bang theory back in time implies a singularity in the past for our universe

              1.1.1.4.4.12.9.1. Con:
              Big Bang theory is based on theories of physics that do not apply at the energy scales attained during the earliest moments of the universe (e.g. Planck time). Therefore the retrodictions of Big Bang theory all the way to a singularity are not reliable.

                1.1.1.4.4.12.9.1.1. Con:
                If the retrodictions of Big Bang theory are considered unreliable because time breaks down completely, then one still has a universe which begins to exist

                  1.1.1.4.4.12.9.1.1.1. Pro:
                  There is no sense in which the Plank era can be said to 'precede' the classical universe and so our universe still has a beginning

                  1.1.1.4.4.12.9.1.1.2. Pro:
                  Currently, no physical model exists for explaining how our classical universe, which has a well-defined notion of time, could emerge from or be causally connected to a Planck era in which time has completely broken down (Deltete & Guy 1996 [101]). So this Planck era is indistinguishable from an independent universe, leaving our universe with a beginning

                1.1.1.4.4.12.9.1.2. Con:
                If the retrodictions of Big Bang theory are considered unreliable because time is modified, we have no plausible reason to believe physics changes so dramatically as to avoid a beginning

                  1.1.1.4.4.12.9.1.2.1. Pro:
                  -> See 1.1.1.4.2.2.1.3.15.1.2.4.1.1.1.

            1.1.1.4.4.12.10. Con:
            The Big Bang Theory [102] postulates the earliest know periods of our observable universe [103] however "Physicists are undecided whether this means the universe began from a singularity, or that current knowledge is insufficient to describe the universe at that time." As such arguing that our universe began, when prevailing science does not claim it, is unsound.

          1.1.1.4.4.13. Con:
          The phrase "begins to exist" cannot be adequately defined such that it applies to the universe but not to God (see Bobier 2013 [104] or this blog [105] summarizing the paper)

            1.1.1.4.4.13.1. Con:
            A theist could allow God to be temporally prior to the universe (i.e. time exists before the universe), which would cause the article's [106] objection to BTE2 ("X begins to exist at T1 iff (i) X exists at T1 and (ii) there is no time prior to T1 at which X exists"), to fail.

            1.1.1.4.4.13.2. Pro:
            X's being is a tensed fact defined in terms of x becoming in a temporal sense. This is circular as 'temporal becoming' is the same as 'beginning'.

          1.1.1.4.4.14. Con:
          Using the above linked definition of 'begins to exist', the universe did not begin to exist. Time exists within the universe (as a direction in spacetime), and not the other way around. Therefore it doesn't make sense to say 'The universe exists at time t.'

            1.1.1.4.4.14.1. Con:
            -> See 1.1.1.4.2.2.1.3.15.2.1.1.1.1.2.2.2.1.

          1.1.1.4.4.15. Con:
          A5 [107] is not the natural definition of 'began to exist'. (A1 is the natural definition). Definition A5 was deliberately created so that the Kalam cosmological argument could retain its appealing-to-common-sense form [108].

            1.1.1.4.4.15.1. Con:
            A5 may be philosophically technical but it has to be in order to capture all the various usages of phrases like "begins to exist", "the creation of", or "came into existence" that appear in various popular sources discussing cosmology as well as our mundane usage in everyday life. One can find this phrase being used in a non-A1 sense by cosmologists [109] (skip to 5:40) or generic science blogs [110] with no interest in the Kalam argument

          1.1.1.4.4.16. Con:
          There is no support for this argument beyond human intuition, and human intuition is known to be unreliable in many fields of study.

            1.1.1.4.4.16.1. Con:
            The support for all beliefs, philosophical and scientific, eventually work their way down to fundamental intuitions for which no external evidence can be offered.

            1.1.1.4.4.16.2. Con:
            While one can safely question/disregard the philosophical intuitions of a single person or small group of people, to give no credence to the shared philosophical intuitions of large groups of sincere thinkers, if not the majority of thinkers in this case, undermines rational inquiry.

              1.1.1.4.4.16.2.1. Pro:
              "All things that begin to exist require a cause" is assumed true in all other other notable contexts. In other words, there is no independent case in either philosophy or science where this principle is not assumed true by the majority

                1.1.1.4.4.16.2.1.1. Con:
                We have no other examples besides the universe of anything that "began to exist" in the same sense - that is, to come into being as opposed to being rearranged from existing matter. Thus this statement about "all other notable contexts" is false or vacuously true [111].

                  1.1.1.4.4.16.2.1.1.1. Con:
                  "X begins to exist" vs "X is the product of a rearrangement of matter" is a false dichotomy. Plenty of things begin to exist (using A5). For example, the computer I am using right now came into being when it was made.

                    1.1.1.4.4.16.2.1.1.1.1. Con:
                    The fact that A5 does not draw a distinction between things which begin to exist as a rearrangement of existing atoms and things which begin to exist without preexisting matter is true, but irrelevant, and in no way indicates that to draw such a distinction is a false dichotomy.

                1.1.1.4.4.16.2.1.2. Pro:
                The existence of something that has a beginning or is contingent, but yet does not have a cause has never been observed.

                1.1.1.4.4.16.2.1.3. Con:
                'Begins to exist' is an arbitrary definition. A cup does not begin to exist, it comes from something else. A cup is made from glass, which is made from silicon, which is mined, which is.... everything we have observed has a precursor.

                  1.1.1.4.4.16.2.1.3.1. Con:
                  Before the glass was formed into a cup, the cup didn't exist, all there was was glass. It seems counterintuitive to refer to a chunk of glass as a "cup" simply because it will be made into a cup in the future. Since there was no cup when it was still glass, and there was a cup after the glass was shaped into a cup, the cup began to exist at a specific, well-defined point.

                1.1.1.4.4.16.2.1.4. Con:
                Quantum theory postulates [112] that things pop in and out of existence, no cause known. To say that everything that begins must have a cause when it cannot be identified that some things have a cause is an extrapolation without evidence. Claiming that 'everything that begins to exist has a cause' is therefore unsound.

                  1.1.1.4.4.16.2.1.4.1. Con:
                  This is a common misconception [113] about virtual particles in quantum theory. They have a known cause, namely fluctuations in the underlying quantum fields. In the case of vacuum fluctuations, they are caused by the vacuum state not being just a 0-particle eigenstate of the various quantum fields in space (wikipedia [114])

                  1.1.1.4.4.16.2.1.4.2. Con:
                  To say "All things that begin to exist must have a cause", does not require all things have a deterministic cause (e.g. identical initial conditions always cause the same effect). It is consistent with things having indeterministic causes such as those postulated in some interpretations of quantum mechanics

            1.1.1.4.4.16.3. Con:
            This fails to distinguish gut reactions (e.g. this person seems untrustworthy) and intuitive speculation (e.g. the stock market is going to go up) from philosophical intuitions (e.g. the people I experience have minds like my own). Most evidence of "human intuition" being unreliable covers only the first two cases.

          1.1.1.4.4.17. Pro:
          -> See 1.1.1.4.4.16.2.1.

          1.1.1.4.4.18. Pro:
          The rejection of this principle undermines science as it becomes acceptable to hypothesize that a given phenomena (e.g. unexpected galaxy rotation curves, photons beginning to exist during radioactive decays, species distributions across continents, changes in atmospheric temperatures) lacks a cause

            1.1.1.4.4.18.1. Con:
            Something can be unacceptable to hypothesise in science without being impossible in principle; this is the situation of the supernatural under methodological naturalism.

            1.1.1.4.4.18.2. Con:
            This is not strictly correct. You can have causes without beginnings. One can cause a rock to move. This does not presuppose the rock 'beginning' simply that it exists at the time of intervention.

              1.1.1.4.4.18.2.1. Con:
              This is not an example of a cause without a beginning because when one causes a rock to move, the motion of the rock begins.

              1.1.1.4.4.18.2.2. Pro:
              Assuming the stone was obsidian, you can then trace the steps of it's existence back. Obsidian comes about from molten silica, and you can continue the step-wise path all the way back to Planck time [115]. At which point our methods of observing the universe crease to have any meaning.

            1.1.1.4.4.18.3. Con:
            Anything within the universe obeys cause and effect, but that doesn't mean the universe as an entire entity obeys cause and effect.

              1.1.1.4.4.18.3.1. Pro:
              Outside the universe the law of cause and effect could very well not apply

                1.1.1.4.4.18.3.1.1. Pro:
                The differentiation of (i)outside and (ii)inside suggests different laws, otherwise there would be no difference between (i) and (ii).

                1.1.1.4.4.18.3.1.2. Con:
                By using Occhams Razor the simplest explanation suggests only one set of natural laws, and there being no ‘outside’ of the universe.

                1.1.1.4.4.18.3.1.3. Pro:
                The theory of multiple universes supports the claim of multiple sets of different physical laws

              1.1.1.4.4.18.3.2. Con:
              The universe as a physical entity needs to obey the laws of logic and physics like the law of cause and effect, to be considered a physical entity

                1.1.1.4.4.18.3.2.1. Con:
                Virtual particles [116] does not obey the law of cause and effects and is considered a physical entity.

                1.1.1.4.4.18.3.2.2. Pro:
                Just like a particle or a wave needs a cause [117] for it's existence, so does the universe.

                  1.1.1.4.4.18.3.2.2.1. Con:
                  The universe could be working under different laws than the ones whe know

                  1.1.1.4.4.18.3.2.2.2. Con:
                  -> See 1.1.1.4.2.2.1.3.15.2.1.1.1.4.

              1.1.1.4.4.18.3.3. Pro:
              -> See 1.1.1.4.2.2.1.3.15.2.1.1.1.4.

          1.1.1.4.4.19. Con:
          'Beginning' is a human definition, there is no objective nature to this term. As the definition is subjective in nature saying the universe 'began' is not specific enough to be useful.

            1.1.1.4.4.19.1. Pro:
            For example it could be defined that the universe began at the big bang, or at the point that time is measurable (post plank time).

            1.1.1.4.4.19.2. Pro:
            A person could define them-self as beginning at the time of birth, conception, or the development of the ovum within their maternal parent.

        1.1.1.4.5. Pro:
        If a universe that begins to exist is not preceded by some prior universe that can serve as its cause, the cause of that universe must be timeless, spaceless, and immaterial

          1.1.1.4.5.1. Pro:
          If the cause of the universe is timeless, it must have a will in order to be able to exist in a timeless state and act as the cause of a single universe (or finite number of universes).

            1.1.1.4.5.1.1. Con:
            Having a will does not enable a timeless cause to act a finite, nonzero number of times.

            1.1.1.4.5.1.2. Pro:
            -> See 1.1.1.4.2.2.1.3.15.2.1.1.1.1.2.5.

            1.1.1.4.5.1.3. Pro:
            A non-personal cause of the Universe (e.g. quantum vacuum fluctuation) can only create an infinite number of universes or no universe. This is because a non-personal cause is defined such that it always acts when a set of sufficient antecedent conditions is met. In a timeless state, if those conditions are ever met, then they are always met and such a non-personal cause would generate an infinite number of universes.

              1.1.1.4.5.1.3.1. Con:
              As we cannot see beyond the one universe we live in, we have no reason to think we do not live in one among an infinite number of universes.

            1.1.1.4.5.1.4. Pro:
            Incompatibilist free will exists (i.e. willed actions that are not determined by antecedent causes)

            1.1.1.4.5.1.5. Con:
            Will [118] is an aspect of the mind. The mind [119] is "roughly identical with the brain or reducible to physical phenomena such as neuronal activity".

              1.1.1.4.5.1.5.1. Pro:
              The claim that a 'will' existed, sans matter, or pre the cause of the universe and the matter present in our observable space time is inconsistent with general observations and science.

              1.1.1.4.5.1.5.2. Con:
              -> See discussion #26452: The mind [120] and the brain [121] are distinct.

            1.1.1.4.5.1.6. Pro:
            -> See 1.1.1.4.5.1.5.2.

          1.1.1.4.5.2. Con:
          The cause of the universe (our observable space-time) must precede time, as time is subject to matter and matter, in our space-time, came into being after and during the Big Bang [122]. This does not suggest that the cause is timeless or immaterial, simple not dependent on our space-time.

            1.1.1.4.5.2.1. Pro:
            There are naturalistic explanations [123]for the origin of the universe which do not require the assumption of the supernatural.

          1.1.1.4.5.3. Pro:
          The cause of the universe must be eternal. It is as reasonable to believe that that eternal thing is God than it is to believe it is a multiverse.

            1.1.1.4.5.3.1. Con:
            "It is no more reasonable to believe that eternal thing is God than it is to believe it is a Multiverse," is an unwarranted conclusion. It assumes there is no warrant for belief in God outside of the cosmological argument. One would have to do a full survey of evidence for God and the multiverse and make a comparison between these before this conclusion is warranted.

            1.1.1.4.5.3.2. Con:
            The multi-verse also requires a cause of its own. The problem of a cause has only been pushed back further by invoking a multiverse.

              1.1.1.4.5.3.2.1. Con:
              It is not logical to argue that only God is permitted to be un-caused. What justification is there to attach a requirement of cause to the Multiverse but not to God? To do so merely favors one prime mover over the other and states the purely speculative as fact.

        1.1.1.4.6. Con:
        There is no reason to believe a single being existed over a number of beings. The existence of multiple such creators would run counter to the definition of a classical God.

          1.1.1.4.6.1. Con:
          Ockham's razor would support favoring a single being.

          1.1.1.4.6.2. Con:
          The definition of God as "that than which nothing greater can be conceived" describes an essentially unique, non-contingent being. Thus, there can be only one God.

            1.1.1.4.6.2.1. Con:
            This is clearly incomplete. The definition ensures there cannot be more than one. But says nothing about whether such a one exists.

            1.1.1.4.6.2.2. Con:
            There could be more than one, if we could conceive of 2 supreme beings who, while they differ in some respect, we cannot conceive of a higher being.

              1.1.1.4.6.2.2.1. Pro:
              Consider regular and dark matter. We thought that regular matter was the whole of the universe, now we know that is not true.

          1.1.1.4.6.3. Con:
          If there is not One Supreme Being to have existed before time, there would be nowhere for "gods" to have come from.

        1.1.1.4.7. Con:
        God is not the best candidate for the cause of the universe. Instead, the cause is better delineated by naturalistic explanations.

          1.1.1.4.7.1. Pro:
          If the Universe has a cause ('prime mover'), there is no justification to attribute that cause with 'unmoved', 'uncaused' etc., since that something may have pertained to another, predating Universe or 'realm', where it was in turned caused by something, etc. Infinite causation is possible in infinite time-space.

          1.1.1.4.7.2. Pro:
          Ekpyrotic cosmologies [124], string cosmology [125], quantum gravity models [126], chaotic inflationary models [127], vacuum fluctuation models [128], and oscillating models [129] provide a cause for our universe.

          1.1.1.4.7.3. Pro:
          Hawking, in his collection of essays, "Black Holes and Baby Universes," suggests that matter collapsing into a singularity in one Universe could poke a hole in space-time and explode into a new Universe.

            1.1.1.4.7.3.1. Con:
            From Hawking [130]: “There is no baby universe branching off, as I once thought. The information remains firmly in our universe. I’m sorry to disappoint science fiction fans, but if information is preserved, there is no possibility of using black holes to travel to other universes.”

      1.1.1.5. Con:
      Cosmological arguments commit the God of the gaps [131] fallacy.

        1.1.1.5.1. Con:
        Science may one day explain things that we currently have no explanation for other than "God did it," but science is equally likely to reveal new concepts and topics that seem inexplicable without God.

          1.1.1.5.1.1. Pro:
          Past scientific discoveries have uncovered facts about the universe that are more difficult to explain without God.

        1.1.1.5.2. Pro:
        Cosmological argumentation is speculation in the entire lack of data. Any explanation may be proffered, but until there is actual data about the singularity preceding the appearance of our Universe, no explanation will always be better than any.

        1.1.1.5.3. Pro:
        Cosmological arguments arrive at a God that is not shown to be necessary. One could posit any sufficient entity and it would be just as valid. Universe creating pixies that disappear after creating the cosmos are just as sufficient.

          1.1.1.5.3.1. Con:
          -> See 1.1.1.4.3.8.1.

          1.1.1.5.3.2. Pro:
          Consider two identical universes, one with God and the other without. If no distinction can be made between the two universes, then the existence of a deity does not hold any explanatory power.

            1.1.1.5.3.2.1. Pro:
            As adding God into our worldview adds nothing in terms of making sense of the universe/nature, it is an unnecessary hypothesis and would be removed by Occam's Razor.

            1.1.1.5.3.2.2. Con:
            Positing the existence of an otherwise identical Godless reality to show that God is not necessary in our reality is a circular argument. It requires the assumption that God is not necessary for all realities to prove that assumption.

              1.1.1.5.3.2.2.1. Con:
              The argument involving two identical realities exemplifies that the claimed necessity of God, as well as claims that God is unnecessary, are bald and are presumed. To say God is or is not necessary requires more than lipservice. Since we can discard mere lipservice, we're left with the dilemma that each hypothetical universe is identical in all other respects. That means there's no experience or feature of reality that can stand to tell us the kind of universe we actually live in.

              1.1.1.5.3.2.2.2. Con:
              This argument itself is a circular argument. It assumes that to show that God exists, one has to exlude from consideration all realities where God doesn't exist.

            1.1.1.5.3.2.3. Con:
            The posited consideration requires the assumption that we collectively understand enough about the universe to model its behavior in entirety.

            1.1.1.5.3.2.4. Con:
            This argument is incoherent, because it suggests one to think of two identical universes that are different from each other. It is impossible to be identical and different at the same time.

          1.1.1.5.3.3. Con:
          The Aristotelian argument from the Act/Potency divide does in fact show that a god meeting the formal definition of God, is necessary.

        1.1.1.5.4. Pro:
        Throughout history, human beings have attributed events they did not understand to God. For example, natural events like thunder were interpreted as supernatural phenomena.

          1.1.1.5.4.1. Pro:
          God is an attempt to answer questions that our brain is not capable of answering at the moment, rather than a real thing that has existed since the beginning of time.

          1.1.1.5.4.2. Con:
          That some religious ideas were later explained by science does not ensure that all religious ideas will be explained by science in the future.

          1.1.1.5.4.3. Pro:
          We have lots of proofs about the no necessity of a god, and they increase during the years. In the same way, we have no proofs of its existence or necessity for life.

          1.1.1.5.4.4. Pro:
          Natural events have been regarded as a sign from God and, consequently, natural calamities are regarded as punishment from God.

            1.1.1.5.4.4.1. Pro:
            God sent incessant rains to flood the earth [132] as punishment for the sins of humanity. Then, he created a rainbow as a sign that he will keep his promises to Noah.

            1.1.1.5.4.4.2. Pro:
            Rain is often considered as a sign of blessing from God [133], as demonstrated by phrases such as "showers of blessing."

            1.1.1.5.4.4.3. Con:
            In Matthew 5:45 [134], Jesus says, "He causes his sun to rise on the evil and the good, and sends rain on the righteous and the unrighteous."

          1.1.1.5.4.5. Pro:
          God was the catch-all hypothesis that allowed rationality to take hold in primitive eras, when no alternative model was available. Over time, religion was replaced by philosophy, then by science. Thus, God is a necessary cognitive step in the genealogy of knowledge.

          1.1.1.5.4.6. Pro:
          Human beings fear what they do not understand, as this makes it difficult to predict and avoid danger. Therefore, human beings have evolved to not accept claims based on ignorance.

        1.1.1.5.5. Con:
        The alternative is a homunculus fallacy, in which nature is explained in purely naturalistic terms, resulting in an infinite regress of causes.

          1.1.1.5.5.1. Con:
          One must demonstrate the absolute impossibility of an infinite regress of causality before dismissing it.

          1.1.1.5.5.2. Con:
          It is reasonable to search for naturalistic explanations until a phenomenon is demonstrated to actually be caused by supernatural event. We have never observed anything to be caused supernaturally, we don't have the means to determine so, nor do we know if it is possible in the first place.

            1.1.1.5.5.2.1. Con:
            The premise "we have never observed anything to be caused supernaturally" may not be true. According to some religions, supernatural occurrences such as Jesus walking on water and Mohammad splitting the moon were supernatural events that were observed.

            1.1.1.5.5.2.2. Con:
            It is not reasonable to search for a naturalistic explanation for nature itself. Entities can be uncaused (or eternal), but not self-caused. A supernatural explanation for nature would be more reasonable.

            1.1.1.5.5.2.3. Con:
            Saying we can’t know a supernatural event occured dismisses revelation a priori as a source of knowledge. It cannot be empirically verified (like all personal hypotheses), but it can be known through revelation. Infinite regresses however cannot be empirically verified either, so it would be inconsistent to claim on these grounds that it is more reasonable.

          1.1.1.5.5.3. Pro:
          An infinite regress of causes would result in a past-eternal universe and that concept runs contrary to observation. If the universe is dying, which is not controversial, it already should have died if it is the result of an infinite regress of causes.

        1.1.1.5.6. Con:
        To say that science has explained some things previously attributed to God and therefore God is unnecessary for existence is a hasty generalization. Science would have to prove that God is not needed for the universe to exist before this argument is valid.

        1.1.1.5.7. Con:
        The claim that science will one day be able to explain the gaps is a post dated cheque. It's an empty promise.

          1.1.1.5.7.1. Pro:
          You wouldn't sell your house to someone and hand over the deed of ownership if they had given you a post dated cheque, so why should we believe the scientists when they say they will one day be able to explain how consciousness is produced as an epiphenomenon of matter, how life came to be, or how the universe originated?

          1.1.1.5.7.2. Pro:
          The progress science has made in explaining the gaps can be compared to the progress Olympic athletes have made in extending human abilities. I could claim I'm going to jump over the moon one day, and I can give as evidence the fact that yesterday I was able to jump 2ft, and today I can jump 4ft therefore tomorrow I will jump over the moon. There is a limit to how high humans can jump, in the same way there is a limit to how much science can explain within a materialistic framework.

        1.1.1.5.8. Con:
        Even if true this claim does not disprove Gods existence because God by definition eludes the scope of science. Science could come up with a complete description of how the world is functioning, and the possibility still remains that God could have made it that way.

          1.1.1.5.8.1. Con:
          However, if true this would still undermine cosmological arguments as positive evidence for God, thus weakening the case for theism.

        1.1.1.5.9. Pro:
        Everything that science cannot explain [135] might be considered as evidence for the existence of God. This is called the God-of-the-gaps reasoning.

          1.1.1.5.9.1. Con:
          The First Cause argument [136] is an example of this fallacy.

          1.1.1.5.9.2. Pro:
          If God does not exist, there is no way to know for sure because we can never know everything, and of all the things we don't know at any given time, God can always be one of them.

            1.1.1.5.9.2.1. Con:
            The following reasoning is invalid: "we cannot know x because we do not know everything, and x could be one of those things we do not know". Indeed, if you inspect an empty glass, you can know that there is no gnome in it, despite there being other things you do not know.

            1.1.1.5.9.2.2. Con:
            We can never "know for sure" anything about the universe, because senses and memory are fallible. A correct understanding of what it means for a human to know something requires a degree of uncertainty - and it is possible in principle to "know" with that degree of uncertainty that God does no exist.

            1.1.1.5.9.2.3. Pro:
            This being true makes positive atheism (the belief that God does not exist) totally irrational, leaving the only rational position for someone who has not seen good evidence for God's existence a neutral one.

              1.1.1.5.9.2.3.1. Pro:
              To be consistent with this logical fact attitudes on religion must be restricted to those for which the burden of proof can be met. This means that a lack of respect for theologies and religious practices which enrich people's lives cannot be justified, and the only reasonable position is to be open to the possibility that the teachings are true.

              1.1.1.5.9.2.3.2. Pro:
              A neutral stance would involve acceptance of the possibility that some of the various religions on offer may contain some truth. This would be similar to how many scientists believe it's possible aliens exist, while believing it's possible they don't.

            1.1.1.5.9.2.4. Pro:
            However if in fact God does exist, then he can reveal him self to us, and his existence could thus be known to us as a self-evident [137] truth.

              1.1.1.5.9.2.4.1. Pro:
              Numerous people claim to have had experiences which indicate God's existence.

              1.1.1.5.9.2.4.2. Con:
              The linked source states that claims of a self-evident nature are the foundation upon which you make arguments. To use the Existence of God as a self-evident proof of the existence of God is disingenuous as you cannot build upon this claim. In other words the linked source says you cannot use a claim of self-evidence to prove said self-evident claim.

                1.1.1.5.9.2.4.2.1. Con:
                The evidence functions as proof to the one who has had the experience in the same way that I can experience the benefits of eating food in a self-evident manner.

                  1.1.1.5.9.2.4.2.1.1. Pro:
                  If I am seeing the sun rise nobody is going to be able to convince that it's still night because the experience of the sunrise is self evident. God can reveal Himself to a specific individual in a similar way.

            1.1.1.5.9.2.5. Con:
            Just because something cannot be disproved does not validate the claim. This would be an argument from ignorance [138].

          1.1.1.5.9.3. Pro:
          A God which does not manifest in reality and is not evidenced by reality is indistinguishable from a God which does not exist.

            1.1.1.5.9.3.1. Con:
            It is question begging to equate "reality" with empirical reality. To assume that physical reality covers the whole of reality is to presume that non-physical beings (such as God) do not exist.

              1.1.1.5.9.3.1.1. Con:
              This question-begging assumption only exists on a misreading: "Is indistinguishable from" is not the same thing as "is".

                1.1.1.5.9.3.1.1.1. Con:
                To assume that a pronouncement of "indistinguishable from" is valid, after only considering the empirical, is to assume either an epistomolical or an ontological materialism. That is, it commits the same basic error.

              1.1.1.5.9.3.1.2. Pro:
              Many believers claim that God is really manifest to them on a spritual level. Until these claims have been shown to be universally false, it cannot be said that God "does not manifest in reality".

            1.1.1.5.9.3.2. Con:
            This argument, while logical, is not commensurate with reality because there is in fact evidence of God's hand in the world in several phenomenona.

              1.1.1.5.9.3.2.1. Con:
              A lack of an explanation is exactly that. It isn't logical to attribute everything unexplainable to God.

                1.1.1.5.9.3.2.1.1. Con:
                Intelligent design meets all the criteria required of a scientific explanation. Any argument which is capable of defeating intelligent design has unintended consequences if applied across the board. For example, it is claimed that Intelligent design is not a scientific theory because it invokes an unobserved force. Darwinian evolution also invokes an observed force.

                1.1.1.5.9.3.2.1.2. Pro:
                This is just the "God of the gaps [139]" fallacy. It invents an agentic explanation for an unexplained observation.

              1.1.1.5.9.3.2.2. Pro:
              Mathematical improbabilities associated with the fine tuning of the laws of physics, the fine tuning of our planet and eco systems, and the immense unliklihood of mutations in genes leading to beneficial changes all provide data for which God is the best possible explanation.

                1.1.1.5.9.3.2.2.1. Con:
                While 50% of Americans still do not accept the theory of evolution, it is now also considered a fact [140] by numerous national and international scientific bodies.

                1.1.1.5.9.3.2.2.2. Con:
                Thinking in probabilities, it is quite easy to understand how Earth ended up with life.

                  1.1.1.5.9.3.2.2.2.1. Pro:
                  Routinely, people win state lotteries; it is in fact a common occurrence. While the odds of any one ticket-holder winning are astronomically against, as more tickets are sold, the chance that someone will win increases. There are trillions of planets in the universe, and as it happens, the right events happened to support life on Earth. Some planet(s) was bound to win, and it was this one (at least).

                1.1.1.5.9.3.2.2.3. Con:
                It is true that most mutations are not beneficial; some are harmful, many have no apparent effect, but occasionally some are good. It happens with sets of genes that have been mutating in parallel as well.

                  1.1.1.5.9.3.2.2.3.1. Pro:
                  From a sheer volume of evidence standpoint, there is no other theory in science that even comes remotely close to having the the amount and variety of supporting evidence that evolution has.

                    1.1.1.5.9.3.2.2.3.1.1. Con:
                    Perhaps a more valid statement would be to say there is no theory which has had more of its so called evidence turn out to be either false or an out right hoax. Furthermore no other theory has had its false evidence [141] and hoax evidence remain in textbooks after being exposed to the extent that the theory of Evolution has.

                      1.1.1.5.9.3.2.2.3.1.1.1. Con:
                      Critique of Wells at talkorigins [142].

                    1.1.1.5.9.3.2.2.3.1.2. Con:
                    The above statement can be considered true only with a very sketchy use of the word evidence. That is to say if you lump hoaxs, evidence that later turned out to be false, and evidence which is used to argue for evolution but which is in actualoty is a non-sequitur as evidence then you can amass a large volume of "evidence".

                    1.1.1.5.9.3.2.2.3.1.3. Pro:
                    There are hundreds [143] of fossils of transitional species of hominids that preceded humans.

                  1.1.1.5.9.3.2.2.3.2. Pro:
                  This has been observed in the laboratory [144] at Michigan State University.

                  1.1.1.5.9.3.2.2.3.3. Pro:
                  This has been observed in bacteria that becomes resistant to antibiotics.

                  1.1.1.5.9.3.2.2.3.4. Pro:
                  This has been observed in the wing color of the British peppered moth [145].

                  1.1.1.5.9.3.2.2.3.5. Pro:
                  This notion aligns with the fact that our DNA shows how all life on Earth is linked to together. While mutations are random, their selection is not.

                  1.1.1.5.9.3.2.2.3.6. Pro:
                  The evolution [146] of wolves to dogs is beyond question.

                  1.1.1.5.9.3.2.2.3.7. Pro:
                  The route of the laryngeal nerve in giraffes [147] is powerful evidence of evolution.

          1.1.1.5.9.4. Pro:
          Any gap in understanding that is filled by God only creates a higher level gap - namely "How did God come to exist?" Using God to fill the gap is the equivalent of using "magic" to explain a process we don't understand.

            1.1.1.5.9.4.1. Con:
            God does not possess the qualities of something which was created because He doesn't have a beginning, He doesn't have moving parts and He is capable of manifesting His own existence.

            1.1.1.5.9.4.2. Con:
            In any explanation there is going to be something inconceivable, God is the better candidate for this position because when we accept God's powers then everything else becomes explainable but on naturalism we find inconceivable things all over the place.

              1.1.1.5.9.4.2.1. Pro:
              God is also a better explanation than matter because God, being a supernatural being, has qualities which justify his inconceivable power to self-manifest and to be eternal. Matter on the other hand being inert and mindlessly subject to the laws of physics provides us with no reason to believe it is capable of generating its own existence.

                1.1.1.5.9.4.2.1.1. Con:
                The simplest matter, particles & the hydrogen atom didn't create themselves. They're organized energy. The force of gravity made the heavier elements inside stars. Some are inert, and some are very reactive.

          1.1.1.5.9.5. Con:
          This principle is too restrictive. It requires one not infer that God exists even if God does, in fact, exist and were putting forth every display of miracle to evidence that God exists.

            1.1.1.5.9.5.1. Con:
            The idea that scientific explanations eliminates God as the source implies that all God's work is inexplicable.

          1.1.1.5.9.6. Con:
          To accept this principle is to admit that one's lack of belief in God does not need to be based on a consideration of evidence. Instead, it implies one does not need to look at the evidence because, in principle, there can be no observation for which God is the best explanation.

            1.1.1.5.9.6.1. Con:
            Lack of belief derives directly from lack of sufficient evidence to make it appear likely to be true. Many atheists are former believers who came to the conclusion that the evidence does not justify belief.

              1.1.1.5.9.6.1.1. Con:
              To hold there is a lack of sufficient evidence for God implies one believes it is possible for there to be such evidence (i.e. observations for which inferring God as an explanation is not god-of-the-gaps reasoning). Therefore, one cannot hold to this and the initial principle that all inferences to God from observation are god-of-the-gaps reasoning.

            1.1.1.5.9.6.2. Con:
            Accepting Gödel's theorem is to accept the limitations of our knowledge, not our belief. Our belief can be founded on facts, mathematics, and beauty, as well as on provable evidence. Any proof that it is wrong, would invalidate our belief, but some beliefs, according to Gödel, are just that unverifiable.

          1.1.1.5.9.7. Con:
          Scientists a priori rejecti supernatural causes. Thus, scientists can be said to commit the naturalistic-theory-of-the-gaps fallacy, as they assume that there will be a future natural explanation for perplexing phenomena.

            1.1.1.5.9.7.1. Con:
            Scientific advancements continue to replace supernatural explanations with natural ones, while not even one supernatural explanation has unseated a scientific cause. Thus, belief in yet-to-be-discovered natural explanations is justified.

          1.1.1.5.9.8. Con:
          It's possible for God to be the best possible explanation for observational data and if such data were present then to deduce God as a hypothesis would not be pasting a hole in science with God but rather arriving at God via a scientific method.

            1.1.1.5.9.8.1. Con:
            The ancient Greeks and Romans had Gods to explain many natural events they observed. Those Gods have been replaced by science.

              1.1.1.5.9.8.1.1. Con:
              Science has made an egregious philosophical error in claiming to have proven these Gods don't exist. There is nothing about understanding the physics of how the sky is blue that proves It's not an artistic choice on the part of God. Similarly there is nothing about understanding meteorology that proves there isn't an entity controlling these mechanisms.

            1.1.1.5.9.8.2. Pro:
            -> See 1.1.1.5.9.3.2.2.

    1.1.2. Pro:
    Those best equipped to answer the question of whether a God exists, namely, scientists, do not believe a God exists.

      1.1.2.1. Pro:
      Only a minority of surveyed scientists [148] (33%) believe in a God.

        1.1.2.1.1. Con:
        The study only included members of the American Association for the Advancement of Science (AAAS), meaning that the results likely do not represent the population of scientists outside of that organization.

          1.1.2.1.1.1. Con:
          While this sample may not represent scientists generally, there is no a priori reason to think so. Lacking any good reason to think otherwise, the results may be taken as (provisionally) representative, or nearly so.

        1.1.2.1.2. Con:
        Saying a lot of smart people believe something doesn't make it true. There are smart people on both sides of the debate. Galileo, Issac Newton, Johannes Kepler, and Francis Collins are examples of smart people who believe in God. Saying all these smart people believe in God is not evidence of God (there are other better pieces of evidence). Likewise, saying 33% of scientists believe in God is not a valid argument against God.

          1.1.2.1.2.1. Con:
          That only a minority of scientists believe in God is not a valid deductive argument that God does not exist, but it's not being presented as one; it's evidence in an inductive argument.

          1.1.2.1.2.2. Con:
          Early scientists like Galileo, Newton or Kepler are bad examples of believing scientists, because the state of available evidence was different then; one could just as easily say they didn't believe in evolution and therefore there is real controversy [149] among scientists.

      1.1.2.2. Con:
      There is no associated set criteria for determining those best equipped to answer the question.

      1.1.2.3. Con:
      Since God is metaphysical, it is erroneous to consider scientists as “best equipped” to investigate the matter.

        1.1.2.3.1. Pro:
        Since god is considered absolutely metaphysical by definition, that is something non-material so non-perceivable and only in our minds, there is no kind of person or knowledge that is "best equipped" to dis/prove god's existence in the world.

          1.1.2.3.1.1. Con:
          This is a non sequitur; even if God not being material meant that material evidence was irrelevant to the question of whether God exists, that doesn't change whether there is an ordering of who is best or worst equipped to know whether God exists, only the position of those who deal in material evidence in the ordering.

          1.1.2.3.1.2. Con:
          If God is absolutely metaphysical, non-material, non-perceivable, and only in our minds, then He cannot be considered as "existing", and has no influence on the real world.

            1.1.2.3.1.2.1. Pro:
            These characteristics can also describe a fictional being in a story, a dream character, an invention to make children behave, an "ideal being" used as an example by a dictatorship to control the population.

        1.1.2.3.2. Pro:
        Theologians (scholars of religion) are the best equipped to investigate the existence of God, leading other specialties on the subject.

          1.1.2.3.2.1. Pro:
          Leaving out the relevantly best equipped specialists equates to committing the fallacy of omission.

          1.1.2.3.2.2. Con:
          This is analogous to expecting cryptozoologists [150] to be best equipped to answer the question of the existence of Bigfoot. Those who dedicate their lives to the study of beings whose very existence is debated, will tend to be committed partisans of the camp that says they exist.

        1.1.2.3.3. Pro:
        Since there aren't any experiments following the scientific method on the methaphysical level, there's never to be evidence about God's existance, thus making it impossible to prove.

        1.1.2.3.4. Con:
        A metaphysical God that has no interaction with existence is akin to no God existing. If God interacts with existence then science should be able to examine that interaction.

      1.1.2.4. Con:
      To say that scientists are the best equipped to answer the question is to make a non-sequitur.

        1.1.2.4.1. Pro:
        Religious folk espousing religious claims often refer to science and philosophy as peripheral concerns, and focus on miracles, scripture, and textual analysis instead.

      1.1.2.5. Con:
      Some of the earliest and greatest scientists [151] pursued their work because they believed that the universe was created by a rational mind, and could be interpreted by a rational mind.

        1.1.2.5.1. Con:
        Due to the cumulative nature of scientific progress, the earliest scientists are almost by definition the least knowledgeable. Thus, we have a connection between scientists coming to better understand the world, and becoming atheists.

        1.1.2.5.2. Con:
        Einstein [152], the first referenced scientist in the above source, repeatedly stated that he was agnostic and did not believe in the Abrahamic God [153].

        1.1.2.5.3. Con:
        The scientists didn't believe for scientific reasons

          1.1.2.5.3.1. Con:
          Many, such as Antony Flew, were convinced by scientific evidence such as the fine-tuning of the universe for life.

            1.1.2.5.3.1.1. Con:
            To believe that universe is fine-tuned for life is a fallacy we commit as beings which exist in this universe.

              1.1.2.5.3.1.1.1. Pro:
              What we see as fine-tuned is the only possible state for the universe to exist in a way that would lead to this very moment. It is not that Earth has miraculously been given atmosphere containing oxygen that we, conveniently, use to survive. We use oxygen because that's what we evolved to breathe because of how the universe turned out to be.

          1.1.2.5.3.2. Pro:
          The scientists did not provide a valid scientific work proving the existence of God. I.e. a work with, at least, hypotheses, methods and results that respect scientific criteria of reproducibility and refutability.

          1.1.2.5.3.3. Con:
          Many scientists became convinced of God's existence due to the the complexity, sophistication and intracacy they learned of from science.

        1.1.2.5.4. Con:
        Appealling to intelligent individuals as an argument is a logical fallacy known as an appeal to authority [154].

          1.1.2.5.4.1. Con:
          Saying that an argument is valid because an intelligent individual makes it is a logical fallacy. Citing an argument made by an intelligent individual is not.

        1.1.2.5.5. Con:
        There are also quite a few great minds [155] that are or were atheists.

    1.1.3. Con:
    There is no way to prove the absence of one thing from the list of all things [156]. Instead, the best science can provide in the way of evidence is an absence of positive evidence for God.

      1.1.3.1. Con:
      There is no way to prove existence of the list of all things. Therefore trying to define something as absence from such list doesn't make sense.

      1.1.3.2. Con:
      Reasoning using evidence, in general, is a matter of inductive reasoning [157], not deductive. It is confused to attribute a syllogistic fallacy to an inductive argument.

      1.1.3.3. Con:
      Absence of a thing is presumed unless sufficient evidence is presented to confirm its existence. Until such evidence is provided a claim is only presumed to be possible, at best, not true.

        1.1.3.3.1. Pro:
        If one presumes things exist without good evidence, a polytheistic set of gods, no gods or a monotheistic god that does not meet the constraints of classical theism are all equally as likely, thereby drastically reducing the likelihood of the specific god exists.

        1.1.3.3.2. Con:
        Existence or Non-Existence of some artifact is largely based on presuppositions. As such the default position should be agnosticism, not absence.

    1.1.4. Pro:
    If God was real, the earth and sun would be the only objects in the whole universe. All the other stars and planets wouldn't matter, since he would only care about the humans in earth. Since we observe other planets, we can infer that God is not real.

      1.1.4.1. Con:
      This is blatantly false; in the Bible, we read clearly that God created the stars and other celestial bodies beyond the earth in multiple places such as where it says He counts the stars and knows them all by name. The Bible describes in great detail the creation of the universe in its entirety.

    1.1.5. Con:
    There are scientific hypotheses for the existence of a creator of our world: simulated reality [158] theories and universe-in-a-lab [159] theories.

      1.1.5.1. Con:
      Such potential creators fail to meet the requirements of the god of classical theism, as the potential agents of construction of such simulations and labs cannot be qualified as omnipotent, omniscient or omnibenevolent.

        1.1.5.1.1. Pro:
        Religions and religious texts such as the Bible have not described their God using explanations with anything resembling a simulated reality (or similar), which appear to be concepts produced by science and philosophy.

          1.1.5.1.1.1. Pro:
          If such gods are clearly described as such, then they do not qualify as a god of classical theism [160] which has been defined at the root of this discussion.

            1.1.5.1.1.1.1. Con:
            To some or many, the distinction is not clear or blurry. Often, people use the term God outside of more narrow and accurate definitions. While the term is unfit, some use it regardless or use broader descriptions such as "religious notion of a creator [161]" or "godlike being [162]" with the context of [163] such hypotheses, including in [164] fiction [165]. The lack of specificity as to what exactly religions refer or don't refer to with the term God could be a major issue.

          1.1.5.1.1.2. Pro:
          Several bible verses [166] describe God this way and could easily be interpreted as such.

      1.1.5.2. Con:
      While possible, it is a dead end in terms of debate and rationality as if true, there would be no conceivable means to perceive yet alone escape such a simulation and thus its "subjects" would need to operate based on their perceived sensory input anyways and would not be justified in belief unless backed by evidence.

        1.1.5.2.1. Pro:
        There are no hints that point to these hypotheses and hence they should not be assumed to be true. Any potential hints may just as well point to a multitude of multiverse hypotheses, mathematical universe hypotheses, holographic universe hypotheses, and so on.

        1.1.5.2.2. Con:
        Some scientists and philosophers consider ideas of simulated reality, the holographic principle and so on as not unlikely [167] and there are ideas for experimentally [168] investigating [169] associated hypotheses. In late 2022 [170], a list of potential tests [171] may inspire further thought on potential tests.

          1.1.5.2.2.1. Pro:
          There are also hypothetical observational hints [172] but these are unlikely to not also have other explanations.

            1.1.5.2.2.1.1. Con:
            The phenomena of "spooky" action at a distance [173] may be one hint because [174] it seems as if effects can't travel faster than light but still things are connected in ways (quantum nonlocality [175]) that would then suggest nonrealism [176] that points toward a simulation or possibly simulation-related superdeterminism [177].

              1.1.5.2.2.1.1.1. Con:
              This is just an unresolved problem of current physics and an open question that confounds scientists, but is not hinting at this world being a simulation or similar in specific.

          1.1.5.2.2.2. Con:
          There are remarks these experiments would be likely boring (and a waste of effort) and without any valuable results, or extremely dangerous [178]. The experiments proposed are not necessarily likely to find evidence even if a 'simulation' or something similar is an sufficiently accurate description of the universe.

      1.1.5.3. Pro:
      What could also be considered to be part of such hypotheses are a) creative entities that live/d in a parallel universe of a multiverse created our universe to 'grow' parallel to theirs or b) created another 'dimension' or similar which could be considered to be our world; c) creative entities that lived in previous universe set up conditions for the subsequent ones in Big Bounce [179]-type scenarios; and d) similar science-based hypotheses.

        1.1.5.3.1. Con:
        While such could be part of such hypotheses, this is not what the God(s) of religions refers to or could refer to. Religions and religious people do not debate, think about, write, or hypothesize such things. These are not religious ideas but philosophical and/or scientific ones. Due to these and other reasons, they can't be what the term "God" refers to.

        1.1.5.3.2. Pro:
        There are no hints that point to any of these things. Big Bounce scenarios, for example, would not require beings to interfere to have impacts on the future and if they are able and do have a fingerprint on the subsequent universe(s), it's unlikely to be in a way that could be accurately described as act(s) of "creation".

        1.1.5.3.3. Pro:
        None of the artificial involvements appear to be necessary or constructive in terms of explaining physics or cosmology (or open questions and issues thereof).

      1.1.5.4. Con:
      Such creators are not gods, as they are not supernatural beings, which is part of the definition of a god [180].

        1.1.5.4.1. Pro:
        Insofar as a hypothesis about a simulator or other creator in "the next universe up" is scientific, it is describing a natural being without supernatural qualities.

        1.1.5.4.2. Con:
        If they do live in a parallel or superordinate world, that may fit the term or concept of a god for some believers, including a minority of theists if prompted since they would live in a nature that is beyond [181] our nature, where for example laws of physics may differ (e.g. different values of physical constants or a world that is more real instead of simulated).

          1.1.5.4.2.1. Con:
          That is still not what is meant with the term God, which is clear by a) descriptions of religious scriptures and doctrines b) the positions of the majorities and respective religious authorities of theists.

    1.1.6. Con:
    There are many accomplished, respected, knowledgeable scientists and philosophers at the top of their fields who are theists and/or Christians.

      1.1.6.1. Con:
      Most academic philosophers [182] (69.7%) don't believe a God exists.

        1.1.6.1.1. Con:
        The study sample was too small to be representative. There are ~4.5M people in the US with a Philosophy [183] Doctorate and fewer than 1M full-time postsecondary education faculty [184].

        1.1.6.1.2. Pro:
        For a sample size of around 4.5 million, with a confidence level of 95% and a margin of error of 2%, the sample size needs to be at least 2400 [185]. The sample size for the poll was over 3000. Thus, the results are significant.

        1.1.6.1.3. Con:
        As Quentin Smith argues [186], the fact that a majority of philosophers are non-theists is not to say that this majority holds good reasons for their view.

          1.1.6.1.3.1. Con:
