          It is unlikely that the majority of philosophers, who have dedicated their careers to answering this question, have come to an erroneous conclusion.

        1.1.6.1.4. Con:
        This could be due to social conformity.

          1.1.6.1.4.1. Con:
          Religious people make up the majority [187] of the global population, and of the population of most countries. Social conformity alone could not drive the majority of philosophers to believe the opposite of what the majority of their neighbours believe.

          1.1.6.1.4.2. Pro:
          Most academics in general tend [188] to be socially progressive, and socially progressive people are less likely [189] to believe in the existence of God. This environment likely encourages academic philosophers to outwardly express atheistic views.

      1.1.6.2. Con:
      -> See 1.1.2.1.

      1.1.6.3. Con:
      This is basically an argument from collective authority [190] and does not refute that available scientific evidence strongly supports the non-existence of God as that term is defined throughout history and in the present. Scientists are not aware of all the existing evidence and often do not seek or consider it. If anything, more meaningful would be survey results about views or conclusions of such scientists only after they are made aware of and considered in-depth this scientific evidence.

    1.1.7. Pro:
    Scientific and historical observation appears to indicate that religion is a human construction, and all claims to the existence of God are religious in nature.

      1.1.7.1. Pro:
      Observation shows religions evolving and changing based on social norms, and borrowing rules, stories and customs from other sects, indicating a lack of divine origin.

        1.1.7.1.1. Pro:
        Christianity is derived from Judaism, given the textual source for the Old Testament is the Tanakh.

        1.1.7.1.2. Pro:
        Islam acknowledges [191] that Jesus was a prophet of god, but not the messiah or the son of god.

        1.1.7.1.3. Pro:
        A large number of myths from the Bible directly parallel other religions. [192]

        1.1.7.1.4. Pro:
        The birth of Jesus [193] is likely to have occurred between September and October. However, Christianity adopted the celebration of Christmas [194] from various [195] Roman [196] and Pagan [197] celebrations.

      1.1.7.2. Pro:
      Archeological evidence shows religions have independent origins [198] from each other, separated by vast geographic distances. The fact they don't align with each other is indicative that religion is a result of social behavior and supernatural entities (such as a god) aren't actually involved in the world.

        1.1.7.2.1. Pro:
        A majority of god claims assert that god (or a set of gods) desire worship, possess great supernatural power and seek the conversion of others. It does not follow that such a god (or gods) would restrict themselves to predominantly one geographic locale of origin and not be uniform on a global scale, particularly in areas inaccessible by early civilizations.

        1.1.7.2.2. Pro:
        There is evidence of religion having evolutionary origins [199] based upon anthropology [200] and evolutionary psychology [201].

      1.1.7.3. Pro:
      Various forms of mental illness have a tangible effect of the religiosity of people, implying the practice of religion is a form of human behavior.

        1.1.7.3.1. Pro:
        It is well documented that abused children who develop Dissociative Identity Disorder [202] (formerly known by the outdated term Multiple Personality Disorder) develop alters [203] that are non-human [204] based on childhood influences.

        1.1.7.3.2. Pro:
        Several mental conditions such as Schizophrenia [205], Bipolar Disorder [206], Geschwind Syndrome [207] and FTLD [208] often carry with them religious delusions [209] and hypereligiousity [210] as symptoms.

        1.1.7.3.3. Pro:
        People who suffer from Autism [211] demonstrate a reduced belief in god and religious belief [212]

      1.1.7.4. Pro:
      A large number of religious claims have been scientifically debunked as having natural causes or as being historically inaccurate. This reduces the credibility of the claims of the religion as a whole, and thus its specific god claim as well.

        1.1.7.4.1. Pro:
        Earthquakes [213] and lightning [214] are natural phenomena. They occur without any apparent form of divine intervention.

        1.1.7.4.2. Pro:
        The story of Noah's Ark is both physically impossible [215] and did not occur, given we have evidence of floods, much smaller then a global flood, and there is no evidence for a global flood [216] as one would expect to find if the story was accurate.

        1.1.7.4.3. Pro:
        Several separate written languages [217] existed before the founding of Babylon [218], some with separate independent origins, thus disproving the story of the Tower of Babel [219].

        1.1.7.4.4. Pro:
        Several religions have varying creation myths [220], of which none adequately align to current scientific consensus concerning the creation of the universe, or even the earth itself and seldom align with each other.

        1.1.7.4.5. Pro:
        -> See discussion #14445: There is no historical evidence of Exodus happening.

        1.1.7.4.6. Pro:
        Archeological evidence indicates that the Walls of Jericho were destroyed during a well documented Egyptian Campaign between 1700-1600BCE [221] and consensus amongst amongst scholars is that the Book of Joshua holds little historical value. [222]

        1.1.7.4.7. Pro:
        The Genesis creation myth [223] of Judaism, Christianity [224] and Islam [225] suffers from several scientific impossibilities and inaccuracies.

        1.1.7.4.8. Pro:
        Young Earth Creationism [226] which several Christian scholars over centuries have espoused as, states the earth having an age of 4-10 thousand years. This has been proven as incorrect [227] by multiple scientific disciplines.

          1.1.7.4.8.1. Pro:
          -> See discussion #10278: The earth cannot be under 10,000 years old.

      1.1.7.5. Con:
      Aristotelian and Thomistic philosophy use reasoning and the study of the natural world to make a claim of a “Prime Mover” also called “First Cause”. This is not a claim based on religious principles, or theological interpretation of texts said to be “sacred”.

        1.1.7.5.1. Con:
        Neither a first cause, nor a prime mover say anything of the god of classical theism as there is no commentary as to such a claim's omnipotence, omniscience, morality or even the involvement of potential agency required.

          1.1.7.5.1.1. Pro:
          The "First Cause" could simply be that a big bang is inherent to broader cosmology.

          1.1.7.5.1.2. Pro:
          The creation of the universe does not require any of the three above attributes. Thus a sufficiently advanced being could substitute for a god in this scenario.

      1.1.7.6. Pro:
      The evolutionary psychology of religion [228] appears to indicate the practice of religion is a form of social behavior.

      1.1.7.7. Con:
      Religious thinking is not just a human construction but an outcome of neuroevolution or ingrained in Homo sapiens neurology. Evolutionary psychology of religion [229], along with the prevalence of religious thinking or religions, indicate religion is a natural common outcome of human biology more broadly and group-level evolution [230].

        1.1.7.7.1. Pro:
        That humans evolved to commonly believe in God around the world indicates this it's not just a human construction or that this construction has underlying, i.e. psychobiological, origins.

        1.1.7.7.2. Con:
        If so this is an admission that religion is simply a consequence of biology, and a god is not involved in its creation.

        1.1.7.7.3. Con:
        It makes no sense for a god capable of ingraining religious behavior to not ingrain specific religious behavior.

      1.1.7.8. Con:
      This commits the genetic fallacy [231], trying to discredit something because of how someone came to believe it.

        1.1.7.8.1. Con:
        Whether a hypothesis was brought into consideration without good reason is relevant to how much consideration the hypothesis should be afforded; this is only fallacious if viewing the inductive argument through the lens of deductive logic.

        1.1.7.8.2. Con:
        When a core justification for belief is that it is of divine origin (as is the case for god), observation and evidence that said origin is mundane, detracts from it's believably.

      1.1.7.9. Con:
      This assumes that proof of, or belief in God, cannot be arrived at through non-religious means such as philosophy.

    1.1.8. Pro:
    Scientific evidence shows that on Earth, humanity has multiple [232], often quite different or contradicting, religions and views about God as well as a substantial population of atheists. If there was an omnipotent God, one would expect there to be only one large religion. This implies that either a) God is not omnipotent or interested in what humans do and believe in; or b) there are multiple gods (polytheism [233]); or c) no such God exists. Islam and Christianity have nearly the same share of believers.

      1.1.8.1. Con:
      God purportedly values free will, so mankind’s devotion or lack thereof does not imply anything about God’s existence or lack thereof.

        1.1.8.1.1. Con:
        In practice though people technically have free will, organised religion operates under the premise and MO that your eternal future or lack thereof after you die is subject to doing as you’re told while you live. This makes a case that, once one believes, creates a choice that in practice is so one sided, eternity vs your finite lifetime, that free will is only for the extremely stupid.

        1.1.8.1.2. Con:
        This may be reasonable only if all humans had the same religion but then diverged, due to free will, to believe in other 'false' religions or to become irreligious. This is not the case and one's geographical location (e.g. host country) or one's parents' faith is the largest determinant of which religion is followed.

      1.1.8.2. Con:
      The old theory that the infidels from any one particular perspective that are worshiping another god or gods are mistaken is consistent with the existence of one real God.

        1.1.8.2.1. Con:
        In the face of the rise of secular government and the decline in religious power, religions have in some ways tried to stand together, sometimes claiming that in some manner their separate gods are different views of the same god.

          1.1.8.2.1.1. Con:
          Generally the religions are so separate and set in their ways that when pushed or asked directly [234] they will fall back upon division.

          1.1.8.2.1.2. Con:
          Such claims are not only rare and not part of the religious doctrines themselves, but also not compatible with their religious doctrines which often claim that their God is the only true one and/or that their religious scriptures are completely accurate and/or the only true ones. Such claims would be invalid or inconsistent because these texts directly and indirectly contradict other religions, especially polytheistic ones.

        1.1.8.2.2. Con:
        The issue is not that people are mistaken; it's that so many people would then be mistaken and that the factors for which religion or God is believed in seem to be rather of geographical and cultural/familial upbringing nature. There is not one large religion and a few minor small ones but multiple large ones quite comparable in their share of believers.

      1.1.8.3. Con:
      The 2 statements "Multiple religions exist" and "God is omnipotent" are not contradictory; there must be hidden assumptions to come to that conclusion, not to mention many religions say their gods allow you to be free moral agents, such as Christianity. And many religions don't believe that their god will force you.

    1.1.9. Pro:
    The existence of God is based on metaphysics, which cannot be proved, and there is no logical need for it to exist. Therefore, God's existence is illogical.

    1.1.10. Con:
    Empirical evidence cannot prove the existence or non-existence of God.

      1.1.10.1. Con:
      Representationalism [235] is false. It is not possible for words to faithfully "mirror" the real world; thus, it's not necessary for the belief in God to mirror reality in order for those beliefs to be true.

      1.1.10.2. Con:
      A valid hypothesis must accurately describe and predict all available data. The hypothesis "there is no god" accurately fits all of the available data, whereas "God exists" does not fit all of the available data.

        1.1.10.2.1. Con:
        The conclusion "God exists" does not obviously conflict with any available data. The arguments to the contrary are primarily arguments of why we should not accept it, rather than claims that it could not be true.

          1.1.10.2.1.1. Con:
          "God exists" is not a falsifiable hypothesis, and therefore not viable to proper research. For a hypothesis to be falsifiable, there has to be a "if A were to be discovered, that would disprove the hypothesis". For the hypothesis that God doesn't exist, A could be for example an unadulterated video recording of God in his true (distinguishable from human) form. For the inverse hypothesis, there is no possible A where "if A is found, the hypothesis is disproven".

        1.1.10.2.2. Pro:
        In investigating a hypothesis, there is always a margin of error. In scientific research, this margin of error (most often 5%) is preset to determine when a result, until falsified, can be considered evidence. This means that even if you perform research in the most stringent of settings, you will have 5% false positives (i.e. falsehoods retained as evidence). So by that factor alone some evidence could virtually be found to support any  hypothesis, regardless of truth.

        1.1.10.2.3. Con:
        Relational claims contingent on the character of being are not subject to strict prediction testing. E.g. I might say "If John loves Mary, John will buy her flowers," but that is only a statement of probablility, and not subject to the same predictable requirements of natural science. The same is true with the predictions we have about a personal God.

          1.1.10.2.3.1. Con:
          The primary thesis of this discussion does not claim that the god in question is a "personal God".

        1.1.10.2.4. Con:
        The statement God doesn't exist gits the available data with a lot of mechanisms left unexplained and with major mathematical improbabilities.

          1.1.10.2.4.1. Con:
          Accepting God on these premises is a God of the gaps [236] fallacy.

        1.1.10.2.5. Con:
        The statement God exists has more explaining power than any other possible explanation. As it accounts for all the anomalies encountered and it explains consciousness and the metaphysical world of values and morals.

          1.1.10.2.5.1. Con:
          "The metaphysical world of values and morals" does not exist, and a theory that explains things that do not exist is correspondingly less likely to be true.

          1.1.10.2.5.2. Con:
          Just like "this is all a dream" or "I am insane", "God did it" can explain any possibility and consequently has zero explanatory power.

          1.1.10.2.5.3. Con:
          God explains no more than any other MacGuffin [237] answer and is not valuable in seeking truth.

      1.1.10.3. Con:
      Empirical evidence could trivially prove that God exists through the direct observation of God's actions such as miracles.

        1.1.10.3.1. Con:
        Even the direct observation of miracles would be inconclusive as proof of God's existence.

          1.1.10.3.1.1. Pro:
          Even the empirical observation of a genuine miracle could be naturalistically rationalized.

            1.1.10.3.1.1.1. Pro:
            Documented, medically inexplicable healing miracles could yet be rationalized as false diagnoses or intentional fabrications.

            1.1.10.3.1.1.2. Pro:
            Personal encounters of God in bodily form could be rationalized as hallucinations.

          1.1.10.3.1.2. Con:
          While truly conclusive proof for anything beyond "cogito ergo sum" is not really possible, sufficient miraculous evidence could make the existence of God as empirically-supported as, say, the theory of gravity, or the idea that the Earth is round.

        1.1.10.3.2. Pro:
        There is an abundance of documented miracle claims [238] that resist naturalistic explanation.

          1.1.10.3.2.1. Pro:
          Supernatural explanations have unseated scientific ones in the cases of Eucharistic miracles [239].

            1.1.10.3.2.1.1. Con:
            There were no theories about miracles to overthrow.

            1.1.10.3.2.1.2. Pro:
            The Miracle of Lanciano [240] has scientific support. The physician who examined the Eucharist confirmed that the contents were cardiac tissue and fresh blood.

          1.1.10.3.2.2. Con:
          The article states, "the vast majority of these miracles occur in the third world and lack medical and scientific documentation." This is a natural explanation [241].

            1.1.10.3.2.2.1. Con:
            Since the medically documented reports from western countries were treated not as a statistical sample but rather as case studies, survivorship bias does not apply.

          1.1.10.3.2.3. Con:
          As demonstrated by the placebo effect [242], behavior change can occur simply because a person has faith in the process. Therefore, a change in behavior is not evidence of the legitimacy of an intervention, divine or otherwise.

            1.1.10.3.2.3.1. Pro:
            The placebo effect [243] is so powerful that it can even cure diseases. Thus, much of what are regarded as miracles may merely be attributable to the placebo effect.

              1.1.10.3.2.3.1.1. Con:
              Our bodies could have been purposefully designed by God to have complex neurological reactions to cure diseases and heal the body.

              1.1.10.3.2.3.1.2. Pro:
              If our body is constructed in such way that enables it to heal in response to some placebo treatment, then remarkable recovery is not necessarily caused by a miracle; it might just be how our bodies work.

            1.1.10.3.2.3.2. Pro:
            Placebos are so powerful that they can alleviate the symptoms of conditions like irritable bowel syndrome [244], even if the affected person is aware that the pill is a placebo.

          1.1.10.3.2.4. Con:
          When a miracle is by our current understanding of the world impossible and illogical, there needs to be a crushing evidence to accept that miracle as real.

          1.1.10.3.2.5. Pro:
          A miracle [245] is, by definition, inexplicable by any naturalistic means; otherwise, we would simply accept it as a natural event.

            1.1.10.3.2.5.1. Pro:
            A miracle that does defy the rules of the natural world (for example feeding 5000 people with 5 loaves and 2 fish) is impossible in this universe.

              1.1.10.3.2.5.1.1. Con:
              The natural order and laws of physics are based on experience and observations, and as such any observation that appears to violate them should be investigated and our understanding of the world adjusted to match, rather than simply denying unexpected results as impossible.

                1.1.10.3.2.5.1.1.1. Con:
                The current observation is that such a miracle is impossible and, while any miracle claims are being investigated, the matter should be seen in this way.

                1.1.10.3.2.5.1.1.2. Con:
                The claim is true, in the sense that this is exactly what happens when we: i) observe something happening which is supposed not to happen, and ii) independent sources duplicate and re-affirm the result through experiment or scientifically controlled, i.e. precisely measured, observation. This unfortunately means that all biblical mentions of miracles are, by default, non-scientific, and basically a multi-corroborated hearsay, coming from an non-scientific age with minimal deciphering abilities.

              1.1.10.3.2.5.1.2. Con:
              Interference by an external omnipotent force could account for such miracles by inserting extra matter/energy, in the same way that the energy of someone removing a billiards ball wasn't accounted for originally. Such an act of interference is certainly logically possible.

            1.1.10.3.2.5.2. Con:
            -> See 1.1.10.3.2.5.1.1.

        1.1.10.3.3. Pro:
        Miraculous healing serves as evidence for the existence of God.

          1.1.10.3.3.1. Pro:
          Barbara Snyder's [246] rapid and mysterious full recovery from multiple sclerosis following a Christian radio show's appeal for prayer on her behalf is a striking example of miraculous healing.

          1.1.10.3.3.2. Pro:
          Sharyn Mackay [247] of Newcastle's recovery from cancer suggests miraculous intervention.

          1.1.10.3.3.3. Con:
          Lourdes is regarded as a sanctified site for many Catholics where miraculous healing would appear, but in fact, there are no more cancer healings [248] in this site than there are spontaneous unexplained cancer healing in any other place.

      1.1.10.4. Pro:
      Science is the study of the natural world, but God is a supernatural entity. Therefore, science cannot prove or disprove the existence of God.

        1.1.10.4.1. Con:
        If God has any influence on the real world, then these manifestations should be observable (in a broad term, not restricted to vision), and therefore be the subject of a scientific process.

          1.1.10.4.1.1. Pro:
          If God does not have any influence on the real world, then it 'corresponds to deist conceptions of God [249]. It is impossible to prove that they do not exist.

          1.1.10.4.1.2. Con:
          There are occurrences in the real world that are God's influence. However, using scientific process on God's influence would always result in a scientific outcome (an explanation without God) or no outcome at all.

        1.1.10.4.2. Con:
        There aren't good reasons to presume the supernatural exists.

          1.1.10.4.2.1. Pro:
          Empirical studies of the complex processes of nature indicate that the natural world can be understood without appealing to divine creation.

            1.1.10.4.2.1.1. Con:
            Stephen Meyer [250] - an American advocate of the principle of intelligent design - has presented [251] evidence for intelligent design.

              1.1.10.4.2.1.1.1. Pro:
              He showed in "Darwin's Doubt [252]" how the fossil record poses great problems for the theory of evolution.

              1.1.10.4.2.1.1.2. Pro:
              He formulated an argument for Intelligent Design based on the digital information contained inside DNA which follows the same method of argument as Darwin used to establish evolution and which is immune to the God of the Gaps accusation.

                1.1.10.4.2.1.1.2.1. Pro:
                There are no known causes to explain 'the complex specified digital information found in DNA'. There are only hypothetical explanations, and more than one of those.

                  1.1.10.4.2.1.1.2.1.1. Con:
                  A mind is the only explanation backed up by observation. Minds have been observed creating information, natural processes have not.

                    1.1.10.4.2.1.1.2.1.1.1. Pro:
                    Genetic mutations occur by breaking information not by creating it.

                      1.1.10.4.2.1.1.2.1.1.1.1. Pro:
                      Genetic mutations come at a cost [253] because they break information.

                1.1.10.4.2.1.1.2.2. Con:
                DNA is a way for cells to replicate their attributes for the next generation. It occurs by natural mechanisms (observed and studied), does not requires any God, and is not proof of intelligent design.

            1.1.10.4.2.1.2. Pro:
            Phylogenetics [254] indicates [255] that evolution, not creation, is responsible for the diversity of life.

              1.1.10.4.2.1.2.1. Con:
              The phylogenetic tree is a product of circular reasoning. The theory of descent with common ancestry is used to structure the data and the structure produced is given as evidence for the theory.

                1.1.10.4.2.1.2.1.1. Pro:
                The fossil record shows discrete organisms with a stark absence of intermediaries. The story this tells does not indicate common ancestry as the links the theory invokes are not present.

                  1.1.10.4.2.1.2.1.1.1. Pro:
                  Punctuated equilibrium was proposed as an attempt to rationalise this lack of intermediaries, but it fails as a solution because sudden dramatic changes are mathematically implausibe with the current understanding of the mutation selection mechanism.

            1.1.10.4.2.1.3. Pro:
            That organisms carry evidence [256] of common ancestry indicates that evolution, not creation, is responsible for the diversity of life.

            1.1.10.4.2.1.4. Pro:
            Evolutionary opportunism [257] indicates that evolution, not creation, is responsible for the diversity of life.

            1.1.10.4.2.1.5. Pro:
            Molecular sequence evidence [258] indicates that evolution, not creation, is responsible for the diversity of life.

              1.1.10.4.2.1.5.1. Con:
              Genetic similarities between species are also consistent with design as designers are known to use the same blue print and make variations on it.

                1.1.10.4.2.1.5.1.1. Pro:
                A variety of cars from the same manufacturer may share the same engine and chassis.

                1.1.10.4.2.1.5.1.2. Con:
                A single designer may use similar blueprints, or may experiment with wildly different designs each time. As such, design as a hypothesis makes no predictions in this area, while evolution specifically predicts the genetic similarity that we in fact see, so by Bayes' rule the genetic similarity of life better supports common descent than a common designer.

                  1.1.10.4.2.1.5.1.2.1. Con:
                  The liklihood of these similaraities is no greater in the case of evolution than it is if intelligent design is the case. In fact similaraties in design are practically uniquiteous.

            1.1.10.4.2.1.6. Pro:
            Observed changes [259] in living things indicate that evolution, not creation, is responsible for the diversity of life.

              1.1.10.4.2.1.6.1. Con:
              The observed changes show minor variations within existing species and not major innovations in body plans and functions.

                1.1.10.4.2.1.6.1.1. Con:
                Speciation has been observed [260]; we are not just talking about changes within species.

                  1.1.10.4.2.1.6.1.1.1. Con:
                  While this demonstrates a variation resulting in an inability of the new variant to bred with its relatives, it does not demonstrate major innovation in body plan and function. It merely satisfies a technical definition of species.

                    1.1.10.4.2.1.6.1.1.1.1. Pro:
                    The definition of species as 1 unable to breed with its relatives may serve a purpose, but it does not serve to define genetic variations significant enough to demonstrate that the mutation selection mechanism is capable of producing novel body plans and major innovations in function.

                    1.1.10.4.2.1.6.1.1.1.2. Pro:
                    To generate new body plans it must be done at the level of dGRNs, which are gene regulatory networks responsible for the embryological development of orgisms. Mutations to these genes are always catastrophic [261] making major innovation impossible.

                      1.1.10.4.2.1.6.1.1.1.2.1. Pro:
                      Mutations to genes that don't effect embryological development may produce organisms which can survive and reproduce, but they are unable to generate novel body plans.

                        1.1.10.4.2.1.6.1.1.1.2.1.1. Pro:
                        "There is always an observable consequence if a dGRN subcircuit is interrupted. Since these consequences are always catastrophically bad, flexibility is minimal, and since the subcircuits are all interconnected, the whole network partakes of the quality that there is only one way for things to work. And indeed the embryos of each species develop in only one way." - Eric Davidson

                1.1.10.4.2.1.6.1.2. Con:
                Seeing "major innovations in body plans and functions" on human timescales would contradict the theory of evolution as we understand it. That we do not see evidence that would refute the theory of evolution is not much of an argument against the theory of evolution.

                  1.1.10.4.2.1.6.1.2.1. Con:
                  The fossil record doesn't show evidence gradual changes in form and function leading to big changes as outlined in "Darwin's Doubt". The theory punctuated equilibrium was advanced in an attempt to rationalise this fact.

          1.1.10.4.2.2. Pro:
          The supernatural has no explanatory power and inhibits the investigation of natural causes. We could ascribe anything unknown to supernatural causes which is what people did in the earlier days when the scientific method was not as popular.

            1.1.10.4.2.2.1. Con:
            Not all hypotheses that can explain anything unknown (and thus have no explanatory power) are supernatural, and not all supernatural hypotheses have this property.

              1.1.10.4.2.2.1.1. Pro:
              "We live in a computer simulation" is a naturalistic hypothesis which can nevertheless be used to explain any otherwise inexplicable event, and thus used has no explanatory power.

              1.1.10.4.2.2.1.2. Pro:
              If you see a swarm of bats coalesce into an unnaturally pale man with elongated canines, "that guy's a vampire" is a supernatural hypothesis with explanatory power.

          1.1.10.4.2.3. Pro:
          -> See 1.1.7.4.

          1.1.10.4.2.4. Pro:
          If one is to accept supernatural claims, then one must equally accept the supernatural claims of other non-monotheistic faiths, thereby weakening the case for a monotheistic god, specific or otherwise.

        1.1.10.4.3. Pro:
        Science merely describes natural phenomena, but it cannot explain why they occur. As such, science can never prove or disprove the existence of God.

          1.1.10.4.3.1. Con:
          Science suggests that how an event occurs is by way of thermodynamical processes, which is precisely the same as the reason why they occur i.e. because of thermodynamical processes

          1.1.10.4.3.2. Pro:
          Issac Newton [262] claims that while "gravity explains the motions of the planets, it cannot explain who sets the planets in motion.”

            1.1.10.4.3.2.1. Con:
            According to the Einstein's theory of relativity, [263] planets are in free fall in the gravitational field created by the other nearby objects when they move around the stars. So there is no need to have any entity that would set the planets in motion.

            1.1.10.4.3.2.2. Con:
            Asking "Who set the planets in motion?", or "Who caused the big bang?" begs the question and assumes without justification that there must be a prime mover.

              1.1.10.4.3.2.2.1. Pro:
              If the motion of planets can indeed be loosely attributed to a "person" as the cause, this does not provide an argument that the person responsibile is omniscient and omnipotent as they would have to be, to be described as a God.

        1.1.10.4.4. Con:
        Arguments for the existence of God are designed to show that the existence of God is more compatible with the evidence we have than the non-existence of God.

        1.1.10.4.5. Pro:
        Just as God's existence cannot be proven, he cannot be proven to not exist. Thus, the concept of God is a belief system which people can take or leave based on personal preference.

          1.1.10.4.5.1. Pro:
          If empiricism is not relevant to beliefs which accord with reality, one can believe anything and that belief should be held to the same regard as any other faith based belief.

          1.1.10.4.5.2. Pro:
          According to Protagoras, "Man is the measure of all things" and, therefore, "For those that believe god exists, he exists; for those that do not believe god exists, he does not exist." Subjectivity entails uncertainty and, therefore, no support can be given.

            1.1.10.4.5.2.1. Con:
            If subjectivity entails uncertainty, then no special support can be given to the mere human Protagoras, and the argument that we cannot support a notion from philosophy defeats itself.

          1.1.10.4.5.3. Con:
          One cannot choose what to believe [264]. You believe or you do not, you cannot fake this mindset.

        1.1.10.4.6. Pro:
        The scientific community dismisses evidence supporting supernatural phenomena, such as the existence of God, due to rigid adherence to methodological naturalism.

          1.1.10.4.6.1. Con:
          -> See 1.1.10.4.2.2.

          1.1.10.4.6.2. Con:
          While true, the claim in no way shows that it is improper to use the conclusions of scientific research as evidence for non-scientific conclusions. Nor is it improper to use certain non-scientific conclusions as scientific evidence.

            1.1.10.4.6.2.1. Con:
            Science can only test for natural causes; therefore, it is always assumed that there is a natural cause. Thus it would be a mistake to attempt to use science as evidence for the supernatural or non-scientific. This sort of thinking will result in circular reasoning, in which one cannot prove nor disprove the existence of God. And because belief in God is an ancient belief, the burden of proof is on those who claim there is no God.

            1.1.10.4.6.2.2. Pro:
            The "scientific method" is not derived from science. Indeed, it could not be, for that would mean that it existed before it was derived. Therefore, science itself is based on a philosophical, not a scientific foundation.

              1.1.10.4.6.2.2.1. Con:
              This statement is a fallacy of equivocation between science, a category of knowledge, and the scientific method, which is a means of testing explainatuons and predictions. Kialo is an example of the scientific method, but it deals in subjects not limited to science.

          1.1.10.4.6.3. Con:
          If God intervenes in the world then there should be empirical evidence of His interventions.

            1.1.10.4.6.3.1. Pro:
            If the story of the Exodus was accurate, we would expect to find signs of a substantial population decline as a result of the plagues of Egypt, but no such evidence has been found and all evidence points to consistent population growth over time. [265]

            1.1.10.4.6.3.2. Pro:
            If the story of Noah was accurate, we would expect to find a massive population bottleneck [266] as a result of somehow repopulating all species on earth with only a single pair of each animal, say nothing of the massive complications from inbreeding [267] as a result.

              1.1.10.4.6.3.2.1. Pro:
              We would not only find a massive population bottleneck but it would (likely) not be possible for these species to survive (without biotechnology) for long due [268] to [269] inbreeding [270] or [271] genetic diversity issues; they'd be dead species walking [272] (living for a while after the events but it being physically impossible for them to survive). There is a "minimum viable population [273]" and even if it is small, it is larger than two. This also refutes the Adam and Eve story.

            1.1.10.4.6.3.3. Con:
            The premise is faulty because it assumes that God is interested in proving His existence.

              1.1.10.4.6.3.3.1. Pro:
              If Athiests were interested in discovering God they might follow a process purported to reveal God to the practitioner similar to how people interested in acquiring wealth follow a process chalked out by others who have achieved wealth.

                1.1.10.4.6.3.3.1.1. Con:
                Many atheists [274]/agnostics were once Christians who believed that something is good when it helps people rather than something being good because God likes it, so when they read the bible they realised God is evil [275] and therefore not deserving of worship, or have found holes in the story.

            1.1.10.4.6.3.4. Con:
            Traditions from cultures all over the world have stories of God intervening and sending prophets.

              1.1.10.4.6.3.4.1. Con:
              -> See 1.1.7.4.

              1.1.10.4.6.3.4.2. Pro:
              If scriptures, writings, and oral histories and stories from world cultures are given the benefit of the doubt then there is strong reason to believe God had more gross interaction with ancient societies than He does today.

                1.1.10.4.6.3.4.2.1. Con:
                Such literature cannot be given the benefit of the doubt, as such literature is fundamentally at odds with historical and scientific fact.

                  1.1.10.4.6.3.4.2.1.1. Pro:
                  -> See 1.1.7.

              1.1.10.4.6.3.4.3. Con:
              Older cultures had a more primitive understanding of the natural world. Lack of knowledge, mental diseases, and corruption of oral histories could lead to misrepresentation of purely natural behaviour.

          1.1.10.4.6.4. Pro:
          The author of the book Forbidden Archeology [276] argues that fossil evidence is filtered out by the scientific community [277] when it challenges strongly held beliefs about materialism and evolution.

          1.1.10.4.6.5. Pro:
          Near-death experiences are evidence for consciousness existing separate from the body, but are dismissed without empirical consideration.

            1.1.10.4.6.5.1. Con:
            People having unusual conscious experiences when their bodies are in extreme situations follows naturally from consciousness being embodied.

            1.1.10.4.6.5.2. Con:
            Empirical studies have been performed [278] regarding near-death experiences [279] and out of body experiences near-death [280], and not a single case has yet to produce a result to indicate an actual out of body perception and do not appear to contradict known physiological models [281], most supported being such experiences are symptoms of Hypoxia [282].

            1.1.10.4.6.5.3. Con:
            What you see in a near-death experience, is influenced by your cultural background, including your religion [283].

              1.1.10.4.6.5.3.1. Con:
              Cultural background definitely shapes up the way the experience is presented, however, universal elements such as seeing deceased relatives, a supreme being, a white light tunnel do exist.

                1.1.10.4.6.5.3.1.1. Con:
                Seeing white light during near death experiences could be a biological phenomenon.

                  1.1.10.4.6.5.3.1.1.1. Pro:
                  The brain enters a state of high activity [284] before death. This may be a reason for why people see bright lights.

          1.1.10.4.6.6. Pro:
          Richard Dawkins [285] attempts to debunk metaphysical and religious claims without even looking at the evidence.

          1.1.10.4.6.7. Pro:
          Rupert Sheldrake has done research demonstrating telephone telepathy [286] using close friends predicting who's calling them.

          1.1.10.4.6.8. Pro:
          Ian Stevenson [287] has done comprehensive research pointing towards reincarnation.

      1.1.10.5. Pro:
      We are physically unable to search everywhere for definite evidence of the non-existence of God due to our finite nature.

      1.1.10.6. Pro:
      The world is a very complicated place, and we perceive so little of it. We see only 3 dimensions, see only the 1 trillionth part of the light spectrum and we cant see past the observable universe. We will never know how immense the reality actually is! We know and perceive so little. Its impossible to know if a God exists, but would that be the case if we perceived the world as it is?

      1.1.10.7. Con:
      In science, you don't need to prove the non-existence of something; for something to be considered existent, empirical evidence must be given.

      1.1.10.8. Pro:
      Empirical evidence presupposes purely material phenomena, but God is immaterial. Therefore, there could never, even in principle, be a purely empirical proof of the existence of God.

        1.1.10.8.1. Pro:
        If God is pure actuality, then he cannot be material (physical), for matter always and necessarily contains an admixture of potentiality.

          1.1.10.8.1.1. Pro:
          In fact, traditional Aristotelian understanding of matter is that it is pure potentiality, but agreement with this idea is not necessary for the statement about God to be true.

        1.1.10.8.2. Con:
        Empirical proofs can be used if the immaterial entity interacts with a material entity. If a god is interacting with this world, we should be able to measure it with empirical methods.

          1.1.10.8.2.1. Con:
          The concept of divine intervention assumes that the world runs or could run by itself, but classical theism denies this, instead holding that God not only created everything, but sustains everything in being from moment to moment.

        1.1.10.8.3. Pro:
        The God claim and any possible proof of it is metaphysical therefore it may never be proven by experimentation, fixing it outside the knowable. This means that to believe in God one will always have to make a very large series of assumptions, which leaves the believer open to a wealth of inaccuracies. To not believe must be the default.

          1.1.10.8.3.1. Con:
          It is an error to presume that only that which is measurable can be shown to exist.

            1.1.10.8.3.1.1. Pro:
            The abstract number "1" exists. Yet, we cannot measure the number 1 itself. Thus, although we may not have a way to measure a god, it does not necessarily follow that there is no reason to believe a god exists.

              1.1.10.8.3.1.1.1. Con:
              1 is a measurement. It does not require measurement. God is not a measurement and therefore should be measurable.

                1.1.10.8.3.1.1.1.1. Pro:
                The concept of "1" is used to comprehend measurements. The concept exists. But the concept itself cannot be measured.

                1.1.10.8.3.1.1.1.2. Pro:
                Measurements are standardized. A god is not.

            1.1.10.8.3.1.2. Pro:
            Qualia [288] are another existing feature of reality that cannot be measured.

          1.1.10.8.3.2. Con:
          Metaphysical means neither "beyond experimentation" nor "outside the knowable". It means beyond sensual. Senses are the common platform on which science is founded. This only means that objectivity of metaphysical cannot be verified by senses. But in religion there are also experiments, knowledge and the sharing of knowledge. Difference is in spiritual knowledge being subjective and beyond senses. Not a series,but a single assumption on "where all this came from" or "what is my purpose" will do.

        1.1.10.8.4. Con:
        We do see the effects of God interacting with this world. For example, research shows that religion helps people better cope with life stresses [289]. If God exists, then interaction with Him would have an expected positive impact, like these [290] studies [291] show [292]. The problem is not "do we see evidence of God interacting", but "do we attribute what evidence we're seeing to God, or to some other causal mechanism."

      1.1.10.9. Pro:
      The existence or non-existence of God cannot be established through the scientific process.

        1.1.10.9.1. Con:
        Science is not the only source of human knowledge.

          1.1.10.9.1.1. Pro:
          Science is itself based on philosophical and mathematical reasoning.

          1.1.10.9.1.2. Con:
          Science is one way to structure and interpret information acquired through the senses. Knowledge can be accumulated from data that we collect, and we can use science to analyze the data. There are non-scientific methods of data analysis, but those methods have not been proven effective.

            1.1.10.9.1.2.1. Con:
            Presumably, the philosophical foundations of science have proven effective. It seems incorrect, therefore, to assume that philosophy can simply be dismissed as ineffective.

            1.1.10.9.1.2.2. Con:
            If this argument is to avoid circularity, it must first justify its means of proving a method effective. This seems fraught with difficulty over what should be considered proof, as well as what should be considered effective.

            1.1.10.9.1.2.3. Con:
            This scientific method can be applied to analysis of metaphysical phenomenon and subjective experiences felt by practitioners of a spiritual or religious processes. Such a pursuit would provide evidence for God.

              1.1.10.9.1.2.3.1. Pro:
              The claim nothing exists which cannot be empirically verified contradicts itself as it cannot be empirically verified its self. It is therefore self refuting.

                1.1.10.9.1.2.3.1.1. Pro:
                This is self refuting claim is used, generally unconsciously, to claim there is no metaphysical reality, no God or at minimum we can never know. It's essentially the unstated premise by which the philosophically erroneous claim that science proves God doesn't exist is based.

            1.1.10.9.1.2.4. Con:
            The claim science is the gatekeeper of knowledge is almost always tied in with the assumption that nothing exists which cannot be empirically verified.

              1.1.10.9.1.2.4.1. Pro:
              The statement nothing exists which cannot be empirically verified cannot be empirically verified and is therefore self refuting

          1.1.10.9.1.3. Pro:
          God can be known only by faith, which is a personal experience. Therefore, the existence of God cannot be proven to those without faith.

            1.1.10.9.1.3.1. Con:
            Proof of personal experiences are demonstrated in courts of justice to our peers using physical evidence that removes reasonable doubt.

              1.1.10.9.1.3.1.1. Pro:
              In events where an allegation of rape has been made, the parties involved may provide conflicting statements, and the alleged assailant may deny involvement. Physical evidence collected from the victim and the scene of the crime can prove/disprove the testimony of either party.

            1.1.10.9.1.3.2. Con:
            Faith based on personal experiences is not a reliable means of deriving truth.

              1.1.10.9.1.3.2.1. Pro:
              Using personal faith to support the existence of God is logically fallacious.

                1.1.10.9.1.3.2.1.1. Pro:
                This is also known as confirmation bias [293].

                1.1.10.9.1.3.2.1.2. Pro:
                There is a cart before the horse problem here. "One must believe that God exists," is being used to support the above claim that "supports the existence of God."

                  1.1.10.9.1.3.2.1.2.1. Con:
                  One must have sufficient faith to conduct an experiment before he can see the outcome, therefore faith does not invalidate the conclusions that required it. Formal proof uses this very pattern: "Assume XYZ. Then it would follow that..."

                    1.1.10.9.1.3.2.1.2.1.1. Con:
                    I could conduct an experiment because I believe you are 100% wrong and want to prove it, and then see the outcome.

              1.1.10.9.1.3.2.2. Pro:
              Faith is indistinguishable from a claim of faith. In other words, true faith and false faith both manifest as claims of faith. Therefore, faith claims cannot be used to justify belief over disbelief.

                1.1.10.9.1.3.2.2.1. Con:
                There is a correlation between true beliefs and survival-producing behaviors. Therefore, true beliefs are more likely to be selected for than false beliefs.

                  1.1.10.9.1.3.2.2.1.1. Pro:
                  Stephen Law [294] argues that "conceptual constraints" link survival-producing behaviors with true beliefs more strongly than with false beliefs.

                  1.1.10.9.1.3.2.2.1.2. Con:
                  This argument assumes a logical connection in the brain between beliefs and behaviors. Evolution has no reason to favor brains whose beliefs are logically connected to their behaviors. It only cares about the behaviors.

                    1.1.10.9.1.3.2.2.1.2.1. Pro:
                    A brain structure where seeing food when hungry triggers the true belief that eating is required to avoid starvation and the behavior of eating the food is no more likely to be selected for than a brain structure where seeing food when hungry triggers the false belief that trees are lizard people in disguise and the behavior of eating the food.

                      1.1.10.9.1.3.2.2.1.2.1.1. Con:
                      There is a true sense in which behaviors are caused by beliefs. My belief that my child is endangered (e.g. I hear it's frightened cry) causes me to try to protect it (I don't yet know what I need to do to protect it (maybe defend it, maybe heal it, maybe carry or lead it away: whatever is needed). As this is clearly highly selected for, it follows that we can expect beliefs and behaviors to be related, and not arbitrary.

                      1.1.10.9.1.3.2.2.1.2.1.2. Con:
                      Language (e.g. English) is a stimulus-belief-behavior relationship and process that cannot be implanted by evolution. As most people learn a language and communicate with others, it follows that beliefs and behaviors must be related in at least this case. As we have no reason to consider this a special or anomalous case, it follows that stimulus, belief, and behavior must be related.

                    1.1.10.9.1.3.2.2.1.2.2. Con:
                    Beliefs supervene on neurochemical brain states [295]. Neurochemical brain states cause behaviors. Thus, beliefs can be said to cause behaviors.

                  1.1.10.9.1.3.2.2.1.3. Pro:
                  A hunter-gatherer picking berries in the woods who ate a berry, falsely believing it was not poisonous, could not make the same mistake twice.

                1.1.10.9.1.3.2.2.2. Pro:
                A child may truly and completely believe a monster lives under the bed, but that doesn't make it so.

                1.1.10.9.1.3.2.2.3. Pro:
                Belief is subjective, and is not relevant to a discussion regarding the objective ontology of an entity (e.g. God).

                1.1.10.9.1.3.2.2.4. Pro:
                Belief in the existence of God can be, and often is, held with no justification in regards to the truth of that idea.

                1.1.10.9.1.3.2.2.5. Con:
                Belief does not require justification, as it is inherently subjective.

              1.1.10.9.1.3.2.3. Pro:
              Faith and belief are cognitive functions the holder possess because of the inability to explain super complex ideas and sensations beyond the mental maturity of the holder.

              1.1.10.9.1.3.2.4. Pro:
              Faith can be used to come to mutually exclusive conclusions.

                1.1.10.9.1.3.2.4.1. Pro:
                Incorrect things can be believed by faith. As such, it is not a form of empirical proof [296].

                  1.1.10.9.1.3.2.4.1.1. Con:
                  Incorrect things can be believed by empirical proof. This is the basis for almost all illusions, such as optical illusions.

            1.1.10.9.1.3.3. Con:
            It is possible to know that God exists without faith. Aristotle reasoned his way into an abstract understanding of the existence of God, despite having no reported personal experience of God.

            1.1.10.9.1.3.4. Pro:
            God communicates with those who wish to know him. Through this, one can know that God exists.

              1.1.10.9.1.3.4.1. Con:
              Subjective experiences are not reliable. When a person affirms that God is communicating with them, there is no way to verify if what they are saying is true.

              1.1.10.9.1.3.4.2. Pro:
              Jesus said [297] "my sheep hear my voice," so believers who hear Jesus' voice can confirm that Jesus exists.

                1.1.10.9.1.3.4.2.1. Con:
                Hearers may not be able to accurately discern if the speaker is, in fact, Jesus Christ.

            1.1.10.9.1.3.5. Con:
            This is not an argument for the existence of God as much as it is an argument against reason and rational inquiry.

            1.1.10.9.1.3.6. Pro:
            God could have created world in such way that finding proof for his existence is impossible, to give us possibility of believing - not knowing for sure.

              1.1.10.9.1.3.6.1. Con:
              "Faith" originates from the Greek word "pistis [298]," which Aristotle defined as "forensic proof" when used as a noun and "trust based on evidence" when used as a verb. The public acts that Jesus performed serve as evidence for "pistis" in Christ.

              1.1.10.9.1.3.6.2. Pro:
              It is not possible to strictly prove existence of God on a rational level, but only on spiritual level of being. Spiritual being is in many ways contrary to the rational approach. The more we try to rationally prove God, the more we move away from spirituality and from God itself.

        1.1.10.9.2. Pro:
        -> See 1.1.10.4.

        1.1.10.9.3. Con:
        Testable predictions have been developed to investigate the existence of God, but they have not yielded supporting evidence.

          1.1.10.9.3.1. Pro:
          God's existence could be tested through prayer. If one were to pray for something to happen, and what was prayed for happened, the observation of a prayer being answered would support the existence of God.

            1.1.10.9.3.1.1. Con:
            The classical definition of God does not require that God answer prayers.

            1.1.10.9.3.1.2. Pro:
            Prayer studies [299] are evidence that a God who intervenes to answer prayers [300] does not exist.

              1.1.10.9.3.1.2.1. Con:
              The participants may have prayed to a God who does not intervene, so the findings are not proof that God does not exist.

              1.1.10.9.3.1.2.2. Con:
              The study did not control control for prayers received from family and friends, so it is possible that such prayers received by the control participants influenced the findings.

              1.1.10.9.3.1.2.3. Con:
              There are well documented cases of scientifically inexplicable recoveries after prayer [301].

            1.1.10.9.3.1.3. Pro:
            One may pray for months and get nothing that (s)he awards to God, whereas one may never pray and award something to God. Then, this God's existence theory is not reliable.

            1.1.10.9.3.1.4. Con:
            God may not answer prayers offered as part of a prayer study. Therefore, such findings don't prove that God doesn't exist.

              1.1.10.9.3.1.4.1. Pro:
              Biblically, prayers are answered only when one truly believes and has faith that their prayers will be answered. A prayer study, by nature, entails skepticism and doubt. Therefore, prayer studies are inappropriate to test their effectiveness.

          1.1.10.9.3.2. Pro:
          According to 1 Kings 18:24 [302], God would answer in very specific ways when you call on his name.

          1.1.10.9.3.3. Pro:
          According to the Mark 16:18 [303], if God existed people who believed in Him would be able to drink deadly poison and not be hurt at all. That has been demonstrated false [304].

            1.1.10.9.3.3.1. Con:
            Not everything included in Bible is literal in eyes of every Christian. Therefore proving that some fragment is not literaly true is not enough to show that main thoughts of Bible are false.

            1.1.10.9.3.3.2. Con:
            Even if it would prove the non-existence of Christian God it is not a proof for the non-existence of a classical God, which is the topic of this discussion.

            1.1.10.9.3.3.3. Con:
            The linked incident does not meet the conditions that are described in Mark. Notably, they violate scriptures prohibiting tempting the Lord [305], or attempting to make Him perform just to see a miracle (Matthew 4:7).

            1.1.10.9.3.3.4. Con:
            Acording to Luke 17:6 [306] if we would have faith as a mustard seed we could move mountains; even greatest saints haven't done that, therefore it is hard to determine whether people included have enough faith.

          1.1.10.9.3.4. Con:
          There are mathematical theories which prove that some theories can't be proven nor disproven. Same could apply to some establishing existence of some being.

            1.1.10.9.3.4.1. Pro:
            Undecidable [307] problems cannot be solved.

              1.1.10.9.3.4.1.1. Pro:
              Semidecidable problems that are not decidable contain a subproblem that is impossible to solve.

              1.1.10.9.3.4.1.2. Con:
              This is not true of all undecidable problems. Some undecidable problems are semi-decidable [308], meaning that either a true positive or true negative result can be attained in finite time, while an attempt to prove the opposite case will never terminate. Semi-decidability provides an exact analog for falsifiability.

        1.1.10.9.4. Pro:
        Claims about God have to be falsifiable [309] before they can be experimentally tested. (Popper, 1959 [310])

          1.1.10.9.4.1. Con:
          Christianity does make falsifiable claims. For example, if God created the universe, we would expect it to have begun at some point. Therefore the universe not having ever begun would be evidence contrary the Christian narrative. Also examples: the resurrection of Jesus and surrounding events, human nature, and even the existence of anything at all bzw. Theism.

          1.1.10.9.4.2. Pro:
          When making the null hypothesis, "God does not exist," one must define "God" before accepting or rejecting the hypothesis. However, there is no universal definition of God, so his existence cannot be empirically tested or proven.

            1.1.10.9.4.2.1. Con:
            A poor or weak definition is not evidence for the existence or non-existence of the entity being defined.

          1.1.10.9.4.3. Pro:
          Falsifiability [311] is required to prove that something is or is not true and cannot be achieved without a measurable focus. As God is not measurable, the scientific method cannot be used to test the validity of god.

            1.1.10.9.4.3.1. Con:
            There are numerous examples of nonfalsifiable ideas that we hold as true. This claim makes the assumption that only measurable things are things that exist. Examples of nonfalsifiable things that’s everyone accepts as valid thoughts: the existence of other minds, love, the laws of logic and reason, various unprovable numerical/mathematical axioms, etc. clearly falsifiability is not the only test for truth.

            1.1.10.9.4.3.2. Con:
            To the extent that you can not use the scientific method to test the validity of God this is true. However this suggests that, as God is not a scientific theory, the scientific approach is wrong. It would be the same to try to scientifically demonstrate the existence of love or other abstract concepts.

              1.1.10.9.4.3.2.1. Con:
              There is no problem to demonstrate existence of love scientifically. It exists as a social phenomenon and behaviour of people.

              1.1.10.9.4.3.2.2. Con:
              Abstract concepts can be and have been modelled scientifically for example in computer science. Software Design patterns [312] Abstraction in computer science [313] Category theory [314]

                1.1.10.9.4.3.2.2.1. Con:
                A software design pattern is an abstraction... until it is implemented. Then it is a concrete implementation of an abstract pattern.

            1.1.10.9.4.3.3. Con:
            Falsifiability serves as a test for a scientific theory, but positive evidence can maintain a theory as scientific despite failing the falsifiability test.

              1.1.10.9.4.3.3.1. Pro:
              For example, Darwin said that the fossil record is incomplete and serves as the biggest objection to his theory. The fossil record still poses a problem for evolution, but in light of other positive evidence the theory continues to be accepted by the majority of scientists.

              1.1.10.9.4.3.3.2. Con:
              Falsifiability or other scientific principles would not require completeness of the fossil record. It does require that when new measurements are made, the theories adapt to include the new evidence to the extent it's valid. Usually accepted theories are very resistant to new evidence (they include it already).

                1.1.10.9.4.3.3.2.1. Con:
                The existence of God is an accepted theory. It is resistant to new evidence. The theory does adapt to include the new evidence.

          1.1.10.9.4.4. Pro:
          The concept of God is unfalsifiable.

            1.1.10.9.4.4.1. Pro:
            God, or Gods, have evolved as human understanding [315] of the world around them has increased until we have reached a position where the current depiction is unknowable and unseeable. This is an example of shifting the goal posts of falsifiability.

              1.1.10.9.4.4.1.1. Pro:
              Instead of treating the claim of the existence of a God or Gods, as falsifiable, mankind continued to shift the cultural relationship of Gods until the current depiction because unknowable and unseeable.

            1.1.10.9.4.4.2. Con:
            Many concepts of God have been falsified.

              1.1.10.9.4.4.2.1. Con:
              Unfalsifiablity of a claim about concept of God does not imply that the claim is false. And it does not imply that the claim is true.

              1.1.10.9.4.4.2.2. Con:
              Even if many arguments for the existence of God have been falsified, it does not follow that all such arguments will be falsified.

                1.1.10.9.4.4.2.2.1. Pro:
                2+2 has exactly one correct answer, namely 4. It has an infinite number of incorrect answers.

                  1.1.10.9.4.4.2.2.1.1. Con:
                  2+2 only has one correct answer in our numeral system, currently 4. Another numeral system [316] has an infinite number of correct and incorrect answers.

                1.1.10.9.4.4.2.2.2. Con:
                There would be no material way to differentiate a false description of God from a correct one.

                1.1.10.9.4.4.2.2.3. Con:
                While this is theoretically correct, in context, there no reason to assume that a new formulation of God could not be falsified in the same way other formulations have.

            1.1.10.9.4.4.3. Pro:
            If God cannot be disproven, then it is unfalsifiable and hence not a meaningful idea.

              1.1.10.9.4.4.3.1. Con:
              An idea that cannot be disproven can also be true and can be proven. If proven it becomes extremely meaningful. While unproven it sensible to look for proof. It is prudent to restrict the search for truth to ideas which can possibly be true. Ideas that cannot be proven false remain possible truths.

              1.1.10.9.4.4.3.2. Con:
              The above claim implies a false equivalency. Namely it conflates Russell's Tea pot (a meaningless claim by any measure) with ideas of great consequence. Whether there is a tea pot orbiting the sun or not has no consequence, but if there is a metaphysical reality and God there are profound implications.

              1.1.10.9.4.4.3.3. Con:
              A bomb threat cannot be proven a hoax or bluff yet authorities take bomb threats very seriously. Authorities take bomb threats seriously for the same reasons we should take the inquiry into God's existence seriously. It's possible that a bomb threat is real, and the consequences of such are serious.

              1.1.10.9.4.4.3.4. Con:
              The assertion that everything will one day have natural explanations cannot be disproven and is therefore not scientific.

          1.1.10.9.4.5. Con:
          Assuming that infalsifiable claims are false, by default, makes better pragmatic sense.

            1.1.10.9.4.5.1. Pro:
            There is nothing which can be demonstrably completely true or completely false. All Science does is tend to confirm or tend to deny. Pragmatically assuming that the unfalsifiable is false is as close to actually finding something to be false as is possible.

              1.1.10.9.4.5.1.1. Con:
              It is demonstrably completely true that that which is defined as violet light has the following properties, A wavelength of 380-450nm, a frequency of 670-790 THz and a phontonic energy of 2.75-3.26 eV. Conversely it is demonstrably completely false that violet light has a wavelength of 1100-1450nm.

                1.1.10.9.4.5.1.1.1. Con:
                If one defines violet light by these physical properties, it is a statement of a definition and not of a fact.

                1.1.10.9.4.5.1.1.2. Con:
                It is impossible to demonstrate these physical properties to a Boltzmann brain, or a butterfly dreaming it is Zhuang Zhou, which is sufficient to fail "demonstrably completely true".

                  1.1.10.9.4.5.1.1.2.1. Con:
                  Not being able to demonstrate something to an entity which cannot be shown to exist does not discount that a thing can be demonstrated. This is akin to saying that if something cannot be demonstrated to a round square it cannot be completely true. Ignoring the fact that a round square, or Boltzmann's brain, are thought experiments rather than actual things.

                    1.1.10.9.4.5.1.1.2.1.1. Con:
                    A Boltzmann brain is a solipsistic entity that's hallucinating a whole universe around it, a universe that doesn't really exist. The point is that the universe you observe might be a hallucination, asking whether there are actual observable Boltzmann brains in the real cosmos outside your head is an irrelevant, silly question.

            1.1.10.9.4.5.2. Con:
            The Higgs Bosun particle was first theorized [317] in 1964. At this time, the theory was not falsifiable. Technology had not progressed to a point that the theory could be experimentally tested. Assuming this theory is false, just because it could not be falsified, would be incorrect.

            1.1.10.9.4.5.3. Con:
            Assuming the unfalsifiable claims as false gives the claims more credibility than they actually deserve. To be scientifically rigorous, unfalsifiable claims must be clearly distinguished from claims experimentally provable as true and from claims experimentally provable as false.

          1.1.10.9.4.6. Pro:
          Unfalsifiable claims are neither true nor false: "It is not even wrong [318]."

          1.1.10.9.4.7. Con:
          Unfalsifiable claims that are true can be verified, only unfalsifiable claims that are false cannot be verified.

          1.1.10.9.4.8. Pro:
          Reproducibility is a core component of the scientific method. Experiments may often require more resources to reproduce than an amateur has access to, but if someone has the resources to actually replicate an experiment, and the attempted replication fails, this is a serious problem for a theory and no scientist should simply assert "you must have done something wrong, or missed an interfering variable".

            1.1.10.9.4.8.1. Con:
            If a hypothesis has been reproduced sufficiently to the point of being accepted as a theory then if a student fails to reproduce it they will be told they did something wrong.

              1.1.10.9.4.8.1.1. Pro:
              The benefits of spiritual practices have been reproduced by a significant number of people throughout recorded history and is to this day experienced by a large number.

    1.1.11. Con:
    If God created this reality as a test to judge our faith, then scientific evidence of his existence would be deliberately made hard if not impossible.

      1.1.11.1. Pro:
      The Bible records that miraculous events by Jesus Christ rightly credited to Him were not to be credited to him openly. (Matt. 8:4)studylight.org [319]

      1.1.11.2. Con:
      While conceivably possible, this no more makes God's existence likely despite a lack of evidence than the hiddenness of the wizarding world of Harry Potter makes it likely that said wizarding world exists in real life under our noses despite a lack of evidence.

      1.1.11.3. Con:
      An all knowing, all powerful God would know the results of the test before commencing the test. Therefore, there is no reason to perform the test.

      1.1.11.4. Con:
      To exist is to "have objective reality or being". To have objective reality or being, there must be evidence that proves it. How difficult proof is to find has no bearing on whether said proof is necessary to have existence. Without evidence, God cannot by definition exist.

      1.1.11.5. Pro:
      The evidence might originate from the enemy of the God, or might be placed as a temptation.

        1.1.11.5.1. Pro:
        One of the crucial concepts in Bible is concept of snake which deceives Eve, and Satan as the tempter. It is possible he would provide false evidence to trick people

          1.1.11.5.1.1. Con:
          Genesis 2-3 [320] outlines that god told Adam and Eve that if they ate from the tree of knowledge of good and evil, they would die which was not the case. The serpent rightly advises Eve that eating from it will cause her to know good and evil which was in fact, the case. God is the one who is deceiving in this story.

          1.1.11.5.1.2. Con:
          According to this narrative, until Adam & Eve ate from the tree of knowledge of good and evil, they would not have had the knowledge that disobedience to god was evil in the first place, and thus we innocents placed in an impossible position by god.

          1.1.11.5.1.3. Con:
          The story illustrates that God fails to meet the definition of the god of classic theism. It shows that either god was incapable of stopping the serpent (showing a lack of omnipotence), did not foresee the serpent's tempting, (showing a lack of omniscience) or opted to setup the scenario intentionally (showing a lack of omnibenevolence).

        1.1.11.5.2. Con:
        Any evidence that does not suit the believer in God might be interpreted as false evidence provided by enemy of the God.

        1.1.11.5.3. Con:
        Most definitions of a god see it as the creator of everything. The "enemy of god" would be created by god itself. Therefore, the god would hide itself and not actually be hidden by someone else.

        1.1.11.5.4. Con:
        It is difficult to believe in an all knowing and almighty God who has an enemy and that this loving God allows us to be tricked by him. There are simpler and less convoluted explanations for things being as they are.

        1.1.11.5.5. Con:
        Neither the existence of the enemy of God nor God himself have been established let alone a talking snake.

      1.1.11.6. Pro:
      The lack of verifiable empirical support for God's existence is consistent with a religious worldview that depends on faith, suggesting that it is possible for God to specifically avoid non-faith-based forms of verification.

        1.1.11.6.1. Con:
        If God cannot be verified, that he does not exist remains a simpler explanation than that he is hiding.

        1.1.11.6.2. Con:
        Faith is not a form of verification, and a benevolent God would not reward or depend on human insanity.

        1.1.11.6.3. Pro:
        God wants humanity to believe in Him, so He will never be proven by scientific evidence; only real faith can prove the existence of God.

          1.1.11.6.3.1. Con:
          God can be proven through inductive reasoning, and often with the help of science.

          1.1.11.6.3.2. Con:
          Faith is incapable of proving anything. Literally any position could be taken up and justified as faith. As such, it can't demonstrate what is actually true.

        1.1.11.6.4. Con:
        -> See 1.1.11.6.3.2.

      1.1.11.7. Con:
      A just God could not possibly condemn us for not believing in him as he is doing such a good job in concealing the evidence for his existence. And yet we are told he does indeed condemn us for infidelity.

      1.1.11.8. Con:
      An arbitrary, eccentric and quixotic "hiding" God is neither just nor compassionate and so not worthy of worship.

        1.1.11.8.1. Pro:
        Such as god would fail the requirements of the god of classical theism as they would not be omnibenevolent.

  1.2. Pro:
  Testimonial evidence of divine revelations and religious experiences supports the existence of God.

    1.2.1. Pro:
    The holy scriptures of major world religions support the existence of God.

      1.2.1.1. Pro:
      The Book of Mormon provides reasonable evidence of the existence of God.

        1.2.1.1.1. Pro:
        The Book of Mormon [321] chronicles the lives of the people and prophets of God who lived in the Americas from approximately 600 BC to AD 400, all of it chiseled by the hands of those who lived it.

          1.2.1.1.1.1. Con:
          It's a story about the beliefs of several people. Even if the story itself is historically true, that does not make the beliefs true.

            1.2.1.1.1.1.1. Pro:
            People have believed that some women were witches. [322] That does not means that witches existed.

          1.2.1.1.1.2. Con:
          The Book of Mormon makes claims regarding early inhabitants of the Americas, many of which are demonstrably false [323].

        1.2.1.1.2. Con:
        The Book of Mormon provides claims of the existence of a god. The claims themselves cannot be the evidence as that is circular reasoning.

          1.2.1.1.2.1. Con:
          The Book of Mormon, Another Testament of Jesus Christ, substantiates the claims of the existence of God from the Old Testament and New Testament of the Bible and thus it is not circular reasoning.

        1.2.1.1.3. Con:
        Central to the Book of Mormon is the claim of direct origin from Hebrews of indigenous Americans. This is floridly inaccurate [324] and ahistorical, rendering the Book of Mormon at best an incomplete source of truth.

        1.2.1.1.4. Con:
        Joseph Smith [325] was a well known charlatan for his time, and ended up being murdered by his own followers after it was revealed he was deceiving them. There's no evidence that the Book of Mormon [326] is anything other than fiction.

      1.2.1.2. Pro:
      The Quran offers proof and evidence for the existence of God.

        1.2.1.2.1. Con:
        There are reasons to doubt the veracity of the claims made in the Quran.

          1.2.1.2.1.1. Pro:
          The Quran makes reference to Jinni, but no such entities have been demonstrated to exist.

            1.2.1.2.1.1.1. Con:
            Since the Jinn are portrayed in Islam to be metaphysical, physical evidence cannot exist.

          1.2.1.2.1.2. Pro:
          The Quran claims the moon was split, but there is no historical or scientific evidence this ever happened, and evidence that it did not happen.

            1.2.1.2.1.2.1. Con:
            King Chakrawati Farmas of Malabar, who was a Chera king of the state of Kerala in India, recorded [327] the happening of that event. He lived in the same time period as prophet Mohammed.

              1.2.1.2.1.2.1.1. Con:
              This account is considered by historians and scholars to be legendary [328], rather than factual.

                1.2.1.2.1.2.1.1.1. Pro:
                The king was a known contemporary of a figure known to have lived during the late 7th and early 8th centuries, whereas Mohammed lived during the late 6th and early 7th. Thus, the king could not possibly have witnessed an event that took place in Muhammad's adulthood.

            1.2.1.2.1.2.2. Pro:
            Geological studies of the moon offer no evidence the moon was ever split and rejoined. NASA Lunar Science Institute scientist Brad Bailey stated "No current scientific evidence reports that the Moon was split into two (or more) parts and then reassembled at any point in the past." [329]

            1.2.1.2.1.2.3. Pro:
            The splitting of the moon would have violated the laws of conservation of energy/momentum.

            1.2.1.2.1.2.4. Con:
            The Mayans recorded [330] this happening. This counts as historical evidence.

              1.2.1.2.1.2.4.1. Con:
              This claim that the Mayans saw a cleft moon is based on a dubious and unsupported interpretation of a symbol [331] in a Mayan manuscript [332] dating back to around 1300 CE [333], 700 years after the alleged event [334]. This evidence is wholly insufficient to justify the claim that Mayans witnessed the alleged miracle of the splitting of the moon.

          1.2.1.2.1.3. Pro:
          Islam teaches that Allah created the world in six days [335]. Yet, there is a body of research demonstrating that it was created over billions of years [336]. It follows that either Allah is not as taught by Islam, or he does not exist.

          1.2.1.2.1.4. Con:
          An example is the Quran’s unveiling of many secrets of Ancient Egyptian Civilization, more than a 1000 years before the world uncovered these secrets empirically. [337]

        1.2.1.2.2. Pro:
        The Quranic challenge as a whole (inimitability, preservation, non-contradiction, and unlikely predictions) is not disputed in any rigorous knowledge discipline. This shows that the Quran is authored by Allah.

          1.2.1.2.2.1. Con:
          The Quran is not inimitable. Any text can be imitated, even if only by random chance [338].

          1.2.1.2.2.2. Con:
          Literary quality is subjective, so the inimitability of the Quran is only a matter of opinion and thus cannot prove God's existence.

          1.2.1.2.2.3. Con:
          Arguing that the Quranic teachings are true because they have yet to be falsified commits the fallacy of making an argument from ignorance [339], and thus should be rejected.

          1.2.1.2.2.4. Con:
