          Inimitability is more likely evidence of factual inaccuracy than accuracy; assuming we occupy the same objective reality, one would expect multiple texts describing the fabric of existence to converge.

          1.2.1.2.2.5. Pro:
          The Quranic text is the only ancient text of the Abrahamic texts that is pristinely preserved [340] from a historical and testimonial sense.

            1.2.1.2.2.5.1. Con:

            1.2.1.2.2.5.2. Con:
            The standardization and apparent uniformity of the Quranic text can be largely attributed to Uthman burning all the textual variants after establishing a single canonical copy [342]. As destruction is the opposite of preservation, it cannot be said that the Quran has been "pristinely preserved" over its history.

          1.2.1.2.2.6. Con:
          The Quran was supposedly revealed only in Arabic. [343] This contradicts Allah's status as a non-human overseeing all the world's people. If Allah truly ruled all humans eternally, he'd lack any special interest in Arabic, which is a major language in only one region.

            1.2.1.2.2.6.1. Pro:
            Allah could have revealed his law through all languages directly without the aid of human translators. The exclusivity of this revelation to Arabic speakers (and only one at that, Muhammad) shows that the Quran has its origins in Arabs.

          The quality of Qur'an, both in content and in form is so great, that it must have been the work of God. It is not possible for a human to write anything like it (inimitability [344])

        1.2.1.2.3. Pro:
        The Holy Qur’an predicted [346] and foretold several events with total confidence and categorical details. The only justified answer to the cause of the precise accuracy of the predictions is that it was revealed by Allah.

          1.2.1.2.3.1. Pro:

            1.2.1.2.3.1.1. Con:
            The Persians defeated the Byzantines and captured Jerusalem at about 614-615 CE. The Byzantine counter-offensive did not begin until 622 CE and the victory was not complete until 628 CE, making it a period of around 13-14 years, not 3-9, as claimed in the Quran [348].

            1.2.1.2.3.1.2. Con:

              1.2.1.2.3.1.2.1. Con:
              In the context of warfare and unlikely victories, a 6 or even 20 year range of a prediction of such outcome is so improvable that it is perhaps inhuman; as it unfound in other history.

              1.2.1.2.3.1.2.2. Pro:
              A precise claim would specify the day, or at least the year. If the claim is being given by the creator and governor of the universe, the claim should be arbitrarily precise, not a range of 6 years.

            1.2.1.2.3.1.3. Con:
            Given the Persian capture of Jerusalem in 614, the imminent retaliation of the more powerful Byzantines was not unlikely, as claimed in the grandparent claim.

              1.2.1.2.3.1.3.1. Con:

                1.2.1.2.3.1.3.1.1. Pro:
                In his book, The History of the Decline and Fall of the Roman Empire, Edward Gibbon says, “At the time when this prediction is said to have been delivered, no prophecy could be more distant from its accomplishment, since the first twelve years of Heraclius announced the approaching dissolution of the empire.”

                  1.2.1.2.3.1.3.1.1.1. Pro:

                  1.2.1.2.3.1.3.1.2.1. Con:
                  The sections “recent loss made a future victory more likely” goes against reason because a loss of a battle entails losing influence, land, resources, men, reputation, and patience. A retrospective look may look simpler and clearer, but predicting the future is much more difficult.

                  1.2.1.2.3.1.3.1.2.2. Con:
                  The use of “undoubtedly” here is perhaps due to the attribution error fallacy. Looking at history, one might think everything makes sense and every betrayal makes sense. However, evidence posted on this discussion suggests that that period was named the decline and fall of the Byzantine empire, even by european writers of the time.

          1.2.1.2.3.2. Pro:

(Yunus, 10.92)) speaking of Ramses ||, an ancient Pharoh of egypt. (13:00-14:06) [349]. In the contemporary era, a Pharoh’s body was retrieved intact, and was identified to be Ramses || [350].

            1.2.1.2.3.2.1. Pro:

                  1.2.1.2.3.2.3.1.1.4. Pro:
                  The Quranic prophecies are either interpreted after the fact to correspond to past events, or the prophecies are open ended and can then await fulfillment indefinitely. Thus, there can never be an unfulfilled prophecy: if a prophecy hasn't been fulfilled, it will be sometime in the future, or perhaps it has been fulfilled, and we just don't know it yet. As this precludes unfulfilled prophecies, it is unsurprising that there are no unfulfilled prophecies.

As this reduction in support didn't happen, the new evidence cannot count as an increase in support.

                1.2.1.2.3.2.3.3.1. Pro:
                If the conditional probability of A given B is higher than the unconditional probability of A, it necessarily follows that the conditional probability of A given not-B is lower than the unconditional probability of A. In other words, if presence of evidence is evidence of presence, absence of evidence is evidence of absence.

          The four gospels - Matthew, Mark, Luke, and John - were written by eyewitnesses.

            1.2.1.7.1.3.1. Pro:
            It is typically accepted by historians that the Gospel narratives were preserved by oral tradition [427] in the time between Jesus's death and the writing of the four Gospels.

              1.2.1.7.1.3.1.1. Con:
              Oral transmission of information leads to the loss and or distortion of that information. The more generations that pass the more distorted that the information gets.

              1.2.1.7.1.3.1.2. Pro:
              The amount and dates of extant accounts of Jesus's ministry and resurrection are outstanding in ancient history, where writing by eyewitnesses is not common, expected, or required when questioning ancient historical events.

                1.2.1.7.1.3.1.2.1. Pro:
                The first surviving account of Alexander the Great's life came ~200 years after his death, with the next not appearing until ~200 years after that.

              1.2.1.7.1.3.1.3. Pro:
              Oral tradition was much more reliable at the time, when reading and writing were uncommon, and especially among Jewish communities. This tradition is even continued into this day, with sections of the Torah being memorized and recited for bar mitzvahs.

                1.2.1.7.1.3.1.3.1. Con:
                Oral tradition has never been reliable, especially when maintained and spread by uneducated and illiterate people with very little understanding of anything.

            1.2.1.7.1.3.2. Pro:
            Mark and Luke were companions of Peter and Paul, respectively, but are not themselves relevant to the Gospel narrative. If the Church was just assigning authors to the Gospels, they would have chosen more important authors (such as Jesus's original disciples), like the Gnostic Gospels did, rather than unknown names like these.

          1.2.1.7.1.4. Con:
          There is good evidence that the gospels are anonymous accounts [428]

        1.2.1.7.2. Pro:
        Many people believed that they had some sort of experience of Jesus after his death.

          1.2.1.7.2.1. Pro:
          This is attested to by multiple independent sources, including Josephus, Paul, and all four Gospels and Acts. This is also preserved in earlier creeds, such as 1 Corinthians 15:3-7.

          1.2.1.7.2.2. Con:
          It is possible that belief in the resurrection was due to hallucinations.

            1.2.1.7.2.2.1. Con:
            Most postmortem appearances were to multiple witnesses at once, with at least one report of a large group appearance (to 500 people at once). Group hallucinations are incredibly rare, with no known formally documented case, so this is unlikely to have happened multiple times.

            1.2.1.7.2.2.2. Con:
            Witnesses reported seeing, hearing, and sometimes touching the resurrected body. Hallucinations typically only influence one sense, with simultaneous hallucinations in many senses (such as these) being incredibly rare, so this is unlikely to have happened multiple times.

            1.2.1.7.2.2.3. Con:
            If all the witnesses of the resurrected body of Jesus were hallucinating, it's unlikely that their accounts of what he said and did would agree to the extent that they do, since his words and actions would be created independently in each of their minds.

            1.2.1.7.2.2.4. Con:
            If the witnesses of the resurrected body were hallucinating, they likely would have concluded that it was only a vision rather than a bodily resurrection, since we know that both their culture and the specific individuals involved had a concept of spiritual visions.

              1.2.1.7.2.2.4.1. Pro:
              In Acts 12:14-15, a servant tells members of the early Church that Peter had escaped from prison, and she is dismissed with the claim that it was only his angel. This shows that the early Church wasn't quick to assume that the appearance of someone is necessarily their physical presence.

          1.2.1.7.2.3. Con:
          It's possible that early belief in the resurrection was fabricated by later generations.

        1.2.1.7.3. Con:
        -> See discussion #43783: The Bible contains contradictions.

        1.2.1.7.4. Con:
        The disciples never wrote their own accounts of their experience of Jesus. The earliest copies of the books we now call the Gospels date to decades after the occurance of the proported events, and were not authored by the men whose names they are title after.

        1.2.1.7.5. Con:
        There is no contemporary or direct testimony on Jesus. While there are records from Rome during the time he lived, there are zero contemporary references. The gospels appear to have been written at least 48-300 years after the fact [429]. It's implausible that nobody noticed him [430] at the time.

        1.2.1.7.6. Pro:
        The disciples bore testimony to having witnessed the resurrected Jesus along with many others. They bore this testimony even at the cost of their lives.

          1.2.1.7.6.1. Pro:
          The Apostles preached the resurrection immediately in Jerusalem, where the Jewish and Roman authorities were already prepared to combat them, rather than waiting or beginning their ministry elsewhere.

            1.2.1.7.6.1.1. Pro:
            The Roman historian Tacitus mentions that after Jesus's death, Christianity first began to rise in Judea.

            1.2.1.7.6.1.2. Pro:
            Cults are typically formed far away from or long after the events they preach, to avoid being persecuted or disproven.

          1.2.1.7.6.2. Pro:
          The deciples had no reasonable motivation to lie about having witnessed the resurrected Jesus.

            1.2.1.7.6.2.1. Con:
            They may very well have witnessed Jesus, who had nearly died, and returned having narrowly escaped death.

              1.2.1.7.6.2.1.1. Con:
              The Roman soldiers would have broken Jesus' legs to finish the crucifixion process [431] if He were not already dead. Yet even Jesus' enemies knew He died, and one soldier even put a spear in Jesus' side to check if Jesus was dead.

          1.2.1.7.6.3. Con:
          The resurrection account is very weak evidence since the claim is a matter of individual revelation (even though it was several of Jesus' disciples who may have made the claim).
          
          Individual revelation [432] (from 12:00 to 12:40) has an extremely low value (near 0%) as scientific evidence. The evidence relies on whether we believe the disciples of Jesus not.

            1.2.1.7.6.3.1. Con:
            The resurrection account is also based on several group reports (i.e., involving 10+ people)

          1.2.1.7.6.4. Con:
          The disciples may not have even existed and where merely figments of literature.

        1.2.1.7.7. Con:
        Rather then reports of witnesses, what is provided is hearsay in the form of posthumously written texts.

        1.2.1.7.8. Pro:
        Jesus' disciples, who were witness to his life and miracles, identified him as being the Son of God. (See Matthew 16 verses 13-17)

          1.2.1.7.8.1. Con:
          There is documented evidence of the existence of Jim Jones, whose 1000 followers claimed was God, yet the existence of Jim Jones is not clear evidence of the existence of God.

            1.2.1.7.8.1.1. Con:
            Because one person's claim is a lie does not have any bearing on another's claim. This statement neither supports nor undermines the parent claim.

          1.2.1.7.8.2. Con:
          This testimony is only of a man performing some kind of miracles. There are many examples [433] of miracles and other forms of magic being faked throughout history.

            1.2.1.7.8.2.1. Pro:
            People today fake divine power and deeds on YouTube and Christian television shows.

              1.2.1.7.8.2.1.1. Pro:
              Some videos [434] have shown how fake healing shows generate illusory miracles.

            1.2.1.7.8.2.2. Pro:
            Even in the information age, people are still deceived by rather obvious and well known gimmicks (for instance the leg extention [435] "miracles", which are not miraculous [436] at all).

            1.2.1.7.8.2.3. Pro:
            Even in cases where faking miracles is not intentional, a person's actions can be exaggerated and distorted by those who tell and retell the episode.

      1.2.1.8. Con:
      There are questions as to the consistency and accuracy of such scriptures over time between their supposed events, time of recording and potential alteration over time.

        1.2.1.8.1. Pro:
        -> See 1.1.7.1.

        1.2.1.8.2. Pro:
        The Exodus is alleged to has occurred in the 13th century BCE [437] however, tales of the Exodus do not appear until the 8th century BCE [438] and potentially mixed with additions or revisions in the 5th century BCE [439] and even then, do not appear to be uniformly communicated across Israel as a whole through that entire period of time.

        1.2.1.8.3. Pro:
        -> See 1.2.1.2.4.

        1.2.1.8.4. Pro:
        -> See 1.2.1.7.5.

        1.2.1.8.5. Pro:
        Prior to Emperor Constantine there were only 21 books that Christians could agree on [440]. 10 others were disputed and some labelled heresy. The Council of Nicea [441] wasn't called until 325CE and the canon's completion took until 397CE [442]. Many of the books that went into the finished product were fabricated, the alleged authors centuries dead. Only the original 21 books stand any chance of being at all authentic.

      1.2.1.9. Pro:
      God spoke to Moses "as a man speaketh to another." Exodus 33:11. [443]

        1.2.1.9.1. Con:
        Auditory and visual psychosis, also known as Distorted Reality [444], has been proven and directly observed. Sufferers of Distorted Reality commonly 'know' that a person, who does not exist from independent observers, there is no evidence that Moses actually spoke to God, only that Moses reported he spoke to God.

        1.2.1.9.2. Con:
        -> See 1.1.7.4.5.

        1.2.1.9.3. Con:
        You cannot use the book stating that God exist as a proof that God exist.

        1.2.1.9.4. Con:
        There is no archaeological or paeleological evidence [445] to suggest Moses existed. In fact, most biblical historians do not believe he existed.

      1.2.1.10. Con:
      There is no agreement between and within major world religions about the existence and nature of God. This implies that either all of them are wrong or only of them is right.

        1.2.1.10.1. Con:
        There is no contradiction between the existence of an absolute almighty power that created the universe and there being multiple contradictory conceptions of this entity.

          1.2.1.10.1.1. Pro:
          If all religions shared an identical understanding of God, this would then necessitate that humans could not freely make up their own minds about him.  Without multiple possible choices, freedom does not exist.  Therefore disagreements about the nature of God are only able to be evidence for humanity not understanding him.

            1.2.1.10.1.1.1. Pro:
            The morality of major religions has changed drastically over time, in line with the Zeitgeist and cultural mores of the time. For example slavery was deemed perfectly moral by most religions 600 years ago, eg. by Judaism, Christianity, Islam, Hinduism as well and non-theist  ideologies like Buddhism.

              1.2.1.10.1.1.1.1. Con:
              This does not show a change in morality, but a change in man's perception of morality. 
              
              If it was actually morality itself that changed, then slavery was actually moral in the Southern United States in 1840. In fact, many persons recognized that slavery was immoral during the founding of the nation, and fought to limit its practice in the new country.

            1.2.1.10.1.1.2. Con:
            When something actually exists before us, we are not free to choose its characteristics.

              1.2.1.10.1.1.2.1. Con:
              One may take his painting and change the nature of his painting in any way he chooses therefore this argument is invalid

          1.2.1.10.1.2. Pro:
          Disagreements over the qualities something has doesn't tend to disprove that such a thing exists. In fact, multiple differing perspectives about the same object suggests the thing does exist, but is simply not well understood.

            1.2.1.10.1.2.1. Pro:
            This argument has nothing to do with God - it is a comment on man's autonomy and man's intellect. It is a non-argument in the context of the debate. God's role in this confusion is explained as being of His divine design in response to man's idiocy in Genesis Ch. 11 v.1 to v.9. [446]

              1.2.1.10.1.2.1.1. Con:
              There is no need to hypothesise about God's role in this confusion if God does not exist, so if nothing else the argument makes the God hypothesis less Occamian, which is enough to be more than a non-argument.

            1.2.1.10.1.2.2. Pro:
            This is not an argument that supports the non-existence of God, but an argument that says "Humans cannot yet see beyond contradictions."

              1.2.1.10.1.2.2.1. Con:
              To consider that "Humans cannot yet see beyond contradictions" is to entertain the idea that we hold the logical absolute of the excluded middle in too high esteem. One would have to presuppose the logical absolutes to prove them wrong, and so there is no way around the presupposition that they are true.

              1.2.1.10.1.2.2.2. Con:
              Unless God's existence is assumed, there is no reason to believe there is any need to "see beyond contradictions", so this is begging the question.

                1.2.1.10.1.2.2.2.1. Con:
                It's not necessarily begging the question because what it does is show how the contradictions can be present both in the case of God existing and of God not existing. It thus neutralizes the grandparent claim by establishing the fact that contradictions can be expected to exist in a world in which God exists.

        1.2.1.10.2. Pro:
        The evidence of any religion would confirm the truth of only that religion. And since religions, and their sects, preach mutually incompatible theologies, they may not all be correct.

          1.2.1.10.2.1. Pro:
          If God does exist there would not be this many diverse religions. God would have made it clear that there should exist only one religion.

          1.2.1.10.2.2. Con:
          The evidence for other religions, while potentially detrimental for the exclusive claims of those religions, might still be mutually reinforcing as evidence for the existence of God.

          1.2.1.10.2.3. Pro:
          The existence of a mythology does not inherently support the existence of the gods in said mythology. If that were the case, you would have to acknowledge the existence of Zeus, Ra, Shiva, Odin, etc. alongside the Christian God. The existence of those gods would then render the claim of the Christian God being the only god or "one true god" a falsehood.

            1.2.1.10.2.3.1. Pro:
            The concept of God/s has changed through time reflecting cultures and cultural understandings.  A particular concept of God/s that arose in a definite place and time (within history) cannot be separated from its circumstances.  We might as well argue whether Odin exists or Artemis or Ahura Mazda.  God (YHWH) is merely one more cultural artifact.

              1.2.1.10.2.3.1.1. Pro:
              There are as many concepts of God as cultures, but are "models" (approximations) of God adapted to the reality of each culture / depend on each culture. The reality of God must be beyond / beneath the cultures.

            1.2.1.10.2.3.2. Con:
            A continued strong and active belief in God in spite of extreme persecution over centuries is not the same as a continued knowledge that a people group once believed something, such as in the case of Greek or other mythology. Those belief systems essentially died out and simply became part of history when they went out of political/social favor.

        1.2.1.10.3. Con:
        If the teachings of at least one monotheistic religion are true, it is sufficient to conclude that God exists.

          1.2.1.10.3.1. Pro:
          Islam maintains its classical definition, followed by virtually all Muslims. Allah is defined by his 99 names and attributes mentioned in the Quran and Hadith. Islam maintains that its definition of Allah coheres completely.

            1.2.1.10.3.1.1. Con:
            Islam is not the only religion. The god of Islam is different than and irreconcilable with the God of Christianity (which is a trinity and for which no prophet followed Jesus).

            1.2.1.10.3.1.2. Pro:
            There needs to be only one definition to cohere thoroughly for the problem highlighted by this con to go away.

          1.2.1.10.3.2. Con:
          As many monotheistic faiths claim that the Word of God/Allah is inerrant there is only one logical response. For each, inerrant, word of a God, there is one God. This, in turn, disproves the concept of 'A God of Classical Theism' as there would be many. If however, the Word of God is not inerrant. There is no way to show the teachings of any monotheistic religion as being true.

        1.2.1.10.4. Pro:
        Christianity is far too ideologically diverse to yield one coherent system of belief that all Christians accept.

        1.2.1.10.5. Pro:
        There is no consistency between religious texts, different sects and anecdotal experiences.

          1.2.1.10.5.1. Pro:
          There exist many different religions and beliefs for which God has different goals, attributes and properties, etc. For example see a list of Chistian denominations [447] and their different conceptions of God.

          1.2.1.10.5.2. Con:
          Religious texts, sects and anecdotal experiences could be likened to the different paths climbing a single mountain. People who start from the north would describe a very different geography while travelling - inconsistencies in narrative - from those starting from the south. The ultimate destination, however, remains the same.

          1.2.1.10.5.3. Con:
          There is little consistency between modern recounting of even the most recent events. It does not follow that this lack of consistency is evidence against an event occurring.

        1.2.1.10.6. Pro:
        The non-existence of God is consistent with widely accepted views on the deities of dead, or fringe religions. If all other gods are mythological and created by man, then likely all gods are.

          1.2.1.10.6.1. Pro:
          As a believer of any religion, you have to believe that you are right while all the contradicting religions are wrong. There is no evidence to support such statements, as the evidence for most religions is of similar value, individual experiences and (old) man-written texts.

            1.2.1.10.6.1.1. Pro:
            Most people believe that all faiths, besides theirs, are wrong. Thus, on average, all religions are being rejected by all people, besides the individual believers of a particular faith.

              1.2.1.10.6.1.1.1. Con:
              Christian (JC-LDS) adherents have no doctrine supporting rejecting anyone holding to other belief systems.

              1.2.1.10.6.1.1.2. Pro:
              There is no way of evaluating which one of two religions is truer.

                1.2.1.10.6.1.1.2.1. Con:
                There are many ways of evaluating the relative correctness of any given religion.

                  1.2.1.10.6.1.1.2.1.1. Pro:
                  Historicity, authority, logical coherence, practical results, and anecdotal experience are just some of the ways to evaluate which one is more correct.

                  1.2.1.10.6.1.1.2.1.2. Pro:
                  If a religion contradicts itself, then that is a mark against the correctness of that religion. Therefore, logical coherence is a factor that can be used to evaluate relative correctness.

                    1.2.1.10.6.1.1.2.1.2.1. Con:
                    Most religions contradict themselves in their own texts, so that is a poor measure of comparison.

              1.2.1.10.6.1.1.3. Pro:
              A polytheist must believe that all monotheistic religions are wrong, and a monotheist must believe that all polytheistic religions are wrong.

                1.2.1.10.6.1.1.3.1. Con:
                This is not true. Vaishnava's (worshipers of Lord Vishnu) hold that there is one supreme God and many demo-gods. They focus on the worship of the one supreme God and consider the demi-gods to be servants of God who have some mystical powers. They see people who have polytheistic beliefs (even within the same canon) as missing the point if they don't hold their focus on the one supreme God. In other words polytheists are seen to have a partial truth.

              1.2.1.10.6.1.1.4. Con:
              Some believers hold that those of other faiths are unknowingly worshiping the God of their own faith.

                1.2.1.10.6.1.1.4.1. Con:
                Believers of certain faiths disagree with the nature of gods of other faiths because of what they accept as positive proofs for the nature of their god, rather than simply making a positive statement about the non-existence of the others' god.

                1.2.1.10.6.1.1.4.2. Con:
                The testimony of men can disagree on the full nature of a god which they believe they have witnessed holds more weight on the argument for that god's existence than any number of men who simply say that they saw nothing yet.

              1.2.1.10.6.1.1.5. Con:
              Atheism is rejected by people of almost all other faiths and beliefs. Thus, Atheism is rejected by more people, throughout history, than any other faith.

                1.2.1.10.6.1.1.5.1. Con:
                This is true but does not contradict the parent.

              1.2.1.10.6.1.1.6. Con:
              The strong, nearly universal debate about the nature of a thing, tends to more confirm the existence of that thing in some form than to confirm that it does not exist. It implies at the very least that there are many people who suppose they have a reason for that belief.

              1.2.1.10.6.1.1.7. Con:
              The fact that many people believe something says nothing about the truth value of that thing. To presuppose so is the fallacy of argumentum ad populum [448].

                1.2.1.10.6.1.1.7.1. Pro:
                The Meme hypothesis [449] is a more plausible explanation for the propagation of the God concept than his truthful existence.

            1.2.1.10.6.1.2. Pro:
            Believers of one faith, just like atheists, have no way of proving a negative, unless blatant contradictions are made in specific faiths (e.g. the world will end and it doesn't..)

              1.2.1.10.6.1.2.1. Pro:
              It is impossible to prove that there is no deist God who created the world and then turned their back on it.

                1.2.1.10.6.1.2.1.1. Con:
                A deist God could be proven wrong by proving a theist God to exist. If a God who has loving relationships with us livings entities exists then this can be proven by the experience of such a relationship.

              1.2.1.10.6.1.2.2. Pro:
              It is impossible to prove that there are no fairies.

              1.2.1.10.6.1.2.3. Con:
              A specific failed claim does not invalidate the entire basis of a faith. It is possible that those making the claim (i.e. that the world would end in 1912) were mistaken. The rest of the claims of the faith must be evaluated on their own terms.

            1.2.1.10.6.1.3. Con:
            The evidence in favor of different religions differs wildly. Thus, there is reason to believe one religion is correct and the others are wrong.

              1.2.1.10.6.1.3.1. Pro:
              Religions that have been present for a longer period of time have tradition on their side, which newer religions lack.

                1.2.1.10.6.1.3.1.1. Con:
                Tradition carries no weight in arguments, as something being assumed in the past is not sufficient reason to believe it is true. Neither is the fact that something was done in the past evidence that it is the best option. In fact, history is littered with examples of erroneous traditions.

                  1.2.1.10.6.1.3.1.1.1. Con:
                  It can be true that "something being assumed in the past is not sufficient reason to believe it is true," while at the same time being also true that "tradition carries weight in arguments."
                  
                  This is because though tradition is not a sufficient reason for belief, it can be an important part of a set of reasons, and often is a necessary condition for belief.

                    1.2.1.10.6.1.3.1.1.1.1. Con:
                    If there are a set of reasons something was believed in the past, the reasons themselves should be presented, instead of the fact that they were believed in the past.

                1.2.1.10.6.1.3.1.2. Con:
                Human societies had the tradition of not granting people equal rights, but the West abandoned that practice as being wrong. Thus, something being a tradition is thus not of importance.

              1.2.1.10.6.1.3.2. Con:
              Difference in the strength of the evidence is reason to believe that some are more likely than others to be true, if at least one religion is true. It is not reason enough to go the further steps to believe one specific religion is in fact true.

              1.2.1.10.6.1.3.3. Con:
              This is merely to punt to something beyond religious experience, making the argument from religious experience itself moot.

              1.2.1.10.6.1.3.4. Pro:
              If a true God would not allow his religion to die, then it is necessarily the case that all the myriad pagan religions which have died off are false, and one of the currently existent religions must be true.

                1.2.1.10.6.1.3.4.1. Con:
                There is no reason for us to belive that a true God would not allow his religion to die and, as it stands, it is a mere speculation.

                  1.2.1.10.6.1.3.4.1.1. Pro:
                  Most religions have a "free will" notion which would not be possible if God "would not allow" his religion to die as that would be violation of the free will.

            1.2.1.10.6.1.4. Con:
            Even if the evidence or source of authority of most religions were questionable, that doesn't affect classical theism [450], since it isn't a religion but a philosophical view.

            1.2.1.10.6.1.5. Con:
            As a believer of Atheism, one must believe that his belief or religion is right while all the other contradicting religions and beliefs are wrong. And yet, it lacks any evidence, not even having individual experiences nor ancient texts which some of the other religions can suggest as proof of validity.

              1.2.1.10.6.1.5.1. Pro:
              A belief in Atheism, unlike weak agnosticism, is a positive belief in the non-existence of a thing. As such, it is a belief about a god similar to how Theists have beliefs about a god.

              1.2.1.10.6.1.5.2. Pro:
              A belief in a god is a part of a larger world view, rather than an idea that stands on its own. It answers questions about the meaning of existence, the purpose of our actions, the cause of physical phenomenon, and other questions necessary to functioning well as a human. Removing that single belief does not remove the need for the larger system. Such a system would be a religion, whether Atheist or Theist, formalized or unspoken.

            1.2.1.10.6.1.6. Con:
            Saying that the evidence for different religions is similar because they all use individual experiences and/or texts as evidence is a critique of the type of evidence, not the content. It is similar to saying that because witnesses are called to testify in trial, both the plaintiff and defendant have the same evidence. What remains is to assess an individual religion's evidential claims.

            1.2.1.10.6.1.7. Con:
            Truth is, by definition, exclusive. If one claim is true, an infinite number of contradictory claims are necessarily false.

            1.2.1.10.6.1.8. Con:
            It is important to differentiate the existence of something (a God in this case) and the beliefs of a given doctrine (in this case, religion). Religion is in fact only a theoretical approach to the idea of God, but that people may have a wrong idea of something does not mean it does not exist.

          1.2.1.10.6.2. Con:
          The burden of proof to disprove the existence of lesser deities has not been met.

          1.2.1.10.6.3. Con:
          It could be that along with God existing many less powerful entities also exist. These entities could function as God's engineers overseeing the operation of the natural forces much the same as how engineers in a factory over see operation of machinery and plant equipment.

          1.2.1.10.6.4. Con:
          Even if these lesser deities do not exist, all it shows is that some people are capable of being deluded, something which is already well known fact. It does not prove that all people are deluded all the time about if they believe in anything supernatural.

          1.2.1.10.6.5. Con:
          "If all other gods are mythological and created by man, then likely all gods are," is an unwarranted conclusion. There are many plausible explanations. For example, there may only be one God.

        1.2.1.10.7. Con:
        Popular consensus is not a necessary nor sufficient means of arriving at truth (e.g. whether a claim conforms with reality).

          1.2.1.10.7.1. Pro:
          Disagreements about the definition of something is hardly evidence that something doesn't exist.

            1.2.1.10.7.1.1. Pro:
            People disagree on definitions all the time. It does not follow that the object being defined does not exist.

              1.2.1.10.7.1.1.1. Pro:
              People disagree on what defines marriage, and yet no-one would deny that it exists.

            1.2.1.10.7.1.2. Con:
            Some of the definitions are mutually exclusive, so it is not possible for God to exist according to all definitions.

              1.2.1.10.7.1.2.1. Con:
              It isn't necessary for God to exist according to all definitions.

                1.2.1.10.7.1.2.1.1. Con:
                If there are multiple definitions and only a small part of them are true (and we don't know which part), then the term is ill-defined. This means we cannot use the term 'God' in well-formed sentences, since we don't know what 'God' is.

                  1.2.1.10.7.1.2.1.1.1. Con:
                  Neither an entity nor it's definition is affected by our knowledge (or lack thereof) of either. Our incomplete knowledge invalidates neither the entity, or the proper definition of the entity.

                1.2.1.10.7.1.2.1.2. Pro:
                Some "definitions" may simply be wrong or inaccurate.  In such cases, it is the definition, not the object, that should be discarded.

              1.2.1.10.7.1.2.2. Con:
              This poses no real argument against God existing because it is true of everything. Incoherent definitions could be concocted for anything.

            1.2.1.10.7.1.3. Pro:
            There are many competing interpretations of quantum mechanics [451], but the fact that only one of them can be entirely right doesn't mean that we are forced to reject all of them, or to reject quantum theory itself.

              1.2.1.10.7.1.3.1. Con:
              The different interpretations of QM make the same predictions about what we'd observe in the world. The same cannot be said of religions.

            1.2.1.10.7.1.4. Con:
            We cannot establish a test for or against a claim without first accepting the definitions.

              1.2.1.10.7.1.4.1. Con:
              If God exists, then it would only seem logical that God could never be defined for he is Alpha and is Omega therefore he is, but also isn't from a philosophical point of view. It takes a whole lifespan in order for a certain individual to define himself, therefore God is self explanatory because it takes the entirety of creation to explain Himself.

            1.2.1.10.7.1.5. Pro:
            The versions of God are human approximations of a being more vast than the people writing the stories. Since they're human perspectives, contradictions are almost inevitable. For someone reading about it... Put it this way. Imagine a lit candle in the centre of a room, surrounded by a huge number of curved and bent and flat and twisted mirrors. If you had to draw a picture of the candle using only the reflections, it would almost have to be a composite, and dependent on where you were standing.

            1.2.1.10.7.1.6. Pro:
            The object may seem different to all observers but is fundamentally the same object. This is the nature of truth and perhaps the reason we have so many religions.

              1.2.1.10.7.1.6.1. Pro:
              Religions may all contain elements of truth and non-truth. It does not follow from this that there is no collection of religious propositions that correspond with reality.

          1.2.1.10.7.2. Pro:
          There are conflicting views and definitions on Quantum Theory, but still all of them believe the equations to be true.

        1.2.1.10.8. Pro:
        If there were one true God, they would pass down the definitive knowledge of who they were to the exclusion of all others.

          1.2.1.10.8.1. Con:
          It is not reasonable to claim to know what an all powerful, all knowing entity would do.

            1.2.1.10.8.1.1. Con:
            If, it is unreasonable to claim to know what an all powerful, all knowing entity would do. Then it logically follows that it is not reasonable to claim that the Bible, or any other text, conveys the intention of such a being.

          1.2.1.10.8.2. Pro:
          The Biblical God passed down the definitive definition of who He was in the Bible, and asked for exclusive worship. Exodus 20:1-4 [452]

            1.2.1.10.8.2.1. Pro:
            If there were only one true God, and it was the God of the bible, he has passed down that knowledge of who he is multiple times throughout the scripture.  Jesus Christ was a perfect example of what God was like since it is written that the son and the Father are one.

        1.2.1.10.9. Con:
        There is more agreement than disagreement among world religions.

          1.2.1.10.9.1. Pro:
          Even if not all religions align, god-like figures are shown throughout most religions

            1.2.1.10.9.1.1. Con:
            Recurrence of a concept across civilizations does not prove the veracity of this concept.

          1.2.1.10.9.2. Pro:
          The fact that people who disagree strongly about the nature of God, particularly across different cultures and histories which agree on very little, can still agree on the existence of God tends to provide more evidence for the existence of God than against it.

            1.2.1.10.9.2.1. Pro:
            In science, a lot of knowledge is based on data that repeats over and over. The repetition of similar ideas and images from religions that have no connection to each other is common. Therefore, there must be some sort of being that is omnipresent into our reality.

              1.2.1.10.9.2.1.1. Con:
              Just like languages borrow words from each other, many religions borrow concepts from older or concurrent religions and mythology. This can explain why ideas are sometimes repeated without necessarily being true.

                1.2.1.10.9.2.1.1.1. Pro:
                The story of Noah's Ark borrows heavily from the Epic of Gilgamesh [453].

              1.2.1.10.9.2.1.2. Con:
              This data could just as easily support a claim about humanity itself and a psychological impulse to explain the unknown.

                1.2.1.10.9.2.1.2.1. Pro:
                That psychological impulse is, in other words, "innate cognitive bias to commit false-positive errors rather than false-negative errors" (Satoshi [454]).

            1.2.1.10.9.2.2. Con:
            Agreeing on the existence of gods of strongly distinct natures only provide an evidence that God is not a unified concept.

              1.2.1.10.9.2.2.1. Con:
              It is perfectly coherent to believe that most religions simply have an incomplete or different experience or understanding of the same God, so it's not necessary to reject every doctrine of their belief or declare that they're entirely wrong. This belief is called inclusivism [455].

                1.2.1.10.9.2.2.1.1. Pro:
                Prior to modern archeology, many cultures discovered dinosaur bones and called them dragons. The fact that they were wrong about many of the attributes of dinosaurs doesn't mean we have to completely reject their experience of fossils.

                1.2.1.10.9.2.2.1.2. Con:
                That is effectively stating that either polytheists or monotheists are wrong, as to why, is irrelevant. Both cannot be right at the same time.

                1.2.1.10.9.2.2.1.3. Pro:
                -> See 1.2.1.10.7.1.3.

                1.2.1.10.9.2.2.1.4. Pro:
                God may have revealed Himself in different ways to suit different cultures, and to help them with their specific spiritual journey.

                  1.2.1.10.9.2.2.1.4.1. Con:
                  A god that reveals himself in such ways as to support contradictory conceptions of god would be making himself seem incompatibly different to different people. This constitutes deception and thus wouldn't be something a good and loving God would do.

                  1.2.1.10.9.2.2.1.4.2. Pro:
                  That would in fact be what a God who was good as defined by the classical definition would do. We know that problems are very different and require different approaches and solutions.

                1.2.1.10.9.2.2.1.5. Con:
                By saying many/all religions have a flawed/incomplete/different understanding of the same god it brings humanity no closer to understanding God.

              1.2.1.10.9.2.2.2. Con:
              "Agreeing on the existence of gods" is only proof of individual choices. In other words, this is only proof of humanity's free will and the fact that people may choose what to believe in. This is not "evidence". This is "belief" and people choose their own beliefs without proving anything else then their own possibility of choosing what to believe.

            1.2.1.10.9.2.3. Pro:
            Perhaps each of these religions is partially correct, some of them more correct than others but none of them absolutely correct. Those accounts which are common among all religions can be thought to be very likely true.

              1.2.1.10.9.2.3.1. Con:
              If God could communicate truth there would be no reason to have much difference at all between religious claims. Even supposing that at some point that truth got distorted, God could still correct us. Therefore, people having to compare religions and decide what comes (or does not come) from God is evidence that there is no God that communicates.

                1.2.1.10.9.2.3.1.1. Con:
                Communication is dependent on both the sender and the receiver. The existence of a perfect sender does not imply perfect communication when sending to an imperfect receiver.

                  1.2.1.10.9.2.3.1.1.1. Con:
                  The communicating is not just sending information, it includes the reception of information. The existence of such a perfect sender would certainly imply better transmission and reception than wildly inconsistent revelations.

                    1.2.1.10.9.2.3.1.1.1.1. Pro:
                    If done today would imply a perfectly received message, which would be recorded and passed on without relevant change, as is the case with any minimally competent youtuber.

              1.2.1.10.9.2.3.2. Con:
              If God could communicate He could also correct people. People having to compare religions and decide that comes from God makes it unlikely that there is any God communicating.

                1.2.1.10.9.2.3.2.1. Con:
                The conclusion doesn't follow from the premise; parents are often ignored by children who simply do not want to be corrected.

                1.2.1.10.9.2.3.2.2. Con:
                Those holding this position must show that God would want to "correct people" in the precise way that they have in mind. Perhaps God does correct persons, but in a different manner. 
                
                For instance, Christianity holds that God has given and preserved His word in the Bible, and that scripture is available to correct us. Hindus adherents would hold that Karma serves as a corrective or retributive force.

            1.2.1.10.9.2.4. Con:
            For this to be true, you need first to define God. The term is highly subjective and interpreted differently by everyone. If I say God is a teapot and you say God is a prime number divisible by 6, then obviously my God exists and your doesn't, which says nothing about some universal definition of God.

            1.2.1.10.9.2.5. Con:
            Belief in a thing is not evidence of the thing's existence.

            1.2.1.10.9.2.6. Pro:
            Many religions that describe God with different natures may be looking at different beings.  For example most Monotheistic religions see a creator God, while the polytheistic religions describe spirits of powers, much like the Angels and Demons are described in Monotheistic religions.  Individual reported experiences could be true but mis-understood.

          1.2.1.10.9.3. Pro:
          By simply following the common [456] moral code, or the law of reciprocity [457], one can attain salvation (or in religions that believe in reincarnation, be closer to attaining it.)

          1.2.1.10.9.4. Con:
          This is patently false, hinduism, buddism, islam, christianity, paganism, fundamentally have different 'truths' including, who is god, how many gods there are, if they have competing messages and intents, if they are tangible. Go back further and you even have gods which did not create the world or universe. If the fundamentals disagree it cannot be claimed that there is more agreement than disagreement.

            1.2.1.10.9.4.1. Con:
            All monotheist traditions have the same God. They have the same essential philosophical description of God. The differences are largely in names and details only.

              1.2.1.10.9.4.1.1. Pro:
              The seeming irreconcilable disagreements among Christianity, bhakti yoga and Islam to name a few is a result of immature, fanatical and unphilosophical members of each camp.

              1.2.1.10.9.4.1.2. Con:
              There are fundamental theological differences between the main abrahamic religions, which makes them difficult to reconcile.

                1.2.1.10.9.4.1.2.1. Con:
                The perception of fundamental differences is the result of poor hermeneutics and fanatical ideas.

              1.2.1.10.9.4.1.3. Pro:
              God is defined by all these traditions as the goal of life and the object for our love.

              1.2.1.10.9.4.1.4. Pro:
              God is defined by all these traditions as the creator. There can be only one creator and this common description narrows down who they are referring to to the same guy.

                1.2.1.10.9.4.1.4.1. Pro:
                It's like saying the artist who did this painting or the chef cooked this meal. People may have various ideas about the artist, some of them more accurate than others, but they are all referring to the same person..

              1.2.1.10.9.4.1.5. Pro:
              Historical elements of the various religions can be expected to differ. The agreement has to be found in the philosophy. For example Christians teach that Jesus appeared and performed various acts. Bhagavad GIta teaches that Krishna appeared and spoke to Arjuna. If we look at the historical elements we will find disagreement but if we look at the philosophy we will find a lot in common. Both are monotheistic and teach that we can have a personal loving relationship with God.

          1.2.1.10.9.5. Con:
          "More agreement than disagreement" is vague and poorly-defined, and a study could be made to return the desired result either way by tailoring how the amount of agreement/disagreement is to be quantified.

          1.2.1.10.9.6. Pro:
          Monotheism is central to all the major world religions with the exception of Buddhism.

            1.2.1.10.9.6.1. Con:
            Religions become "major world religions" due to historically contingent facts - e.g. European colonialism making Christianity dominant over large swaths of the globe. It is not clear that these make religions more worthy of note than minor or dead religions, such as Aztec paganism.

              1.2.1.10.9.6.1.1. Con:
              A study of the history of religions reveals that philosophical elements of a religion effects its success.

            1.2.1.10.9.6.2. Pro:
            The highest teachings in the Hindu tradition [458] are monotheistic and center around love for God

              1.2.1.10.9.6.2.1. Pro:
              Followers of the Vaishnava tradition have great respect for profits from other traditions such as Jesus and Mohamed, and see commonality in the core teaching that the goal of human life is to develop love for God.

              1.2.1.10.9.6.2.2. Pro:
              The modern impersonalist form of Hinduism which is in vogue is an off shoot of the Vaishnava tradition as explained in the above linked lectures series

            1.2.1.10.9.6.3. Pro:
            Judaism, Christianity, Islam, Vaishnavism and Sikhism all teach that the goal of life to to Develop our love for God.

      1.2.1.11. Pro:
      Testimonial evidence is the chief tool of the apostles.

        1.2.1.11.1. Pro:
        The Apostles were eye witnesses to miracles, which are evidence that God exists.

          1.2.1.11.1.1. Pro:
          There are many accounts [459] of the Apostles witnessing miracles.

            1.2.1.11.1.1.1. Con:
            Accounts claiming that the apostles witnessed miracles is not evidence for the existence of a god.

          1.2.1.11.1.2. Con:
          If "miracle" is defined as "an observed phenomenon which is not explained by current knowledge and seems to violate the laws of nature", then miracles are not evidence that a God exists.

            1.2.1.11.1.2.1. Pro:
            Shows made by magicians seem to violate the laws of nature, however, they are known to use "tricks" to give the appearance of violating the laws of nature, and nothing supernatural actually happens. Spectators only "believe" the shows by willing suspension of disbelief [460], for entertainment purposes.

              1.2.1.11.1.2.1.1. Pro:
              While spectators usually cannot explain what they have seen, they should not come to the conclusion that the magician has supernatural or divine powers.

          1.2.1.11.1.3. Con:
          Even when assuming that what they described matches and what they witnessed and that they indeed witnessed real miracles, this wouldn't be evidence God exists. Mere miracles do not point to this explanation in specific – it would rather point towards extraterrestrials or ET-origin beings deploying advanced technology that to humans appears indistinguishable from magic [461].

          1.2.1.11.1.4. Con:
          We cannot asses how reliable the Apostles themselves are. People tell lies, or just are wrong sometimes, and that might be the case for them too.

            1.2.1.11.1.4.1. Pro:
            Examples include that they had hallucinations, were pressured into lying, unknowingly were under the influence of psychoactive chemicals, had illusions, were misunderstood over time, or misinterpreted events.

          1.2.1.11.1.5. Con:
          Attempting to prove existence by means of “evidence” found within the pages of questionable texts is a circular argument that requires a leap of faith.

        1.2.1.11.2. Con:
        Witness testimony is the lowest form of evidence, both in the court room and in scientific inquiry. In fact, the reliance on testimony is one of the major weaknesses of the notion that God exists.

        1.2.1.11.3. Con:
        -> See 1.2.1.8.5.

      1.2.1.12. Pro:
      Hinduism supports the existence of God.

        1.2.1.12.1. Pro:
        The vedic scriptures teach monotheism, that Krishna [462] is the Supreme personality of Godhead [463].

          1.2.1.12.1.1. Pro:
          The other personalities mentioned are demi-gods, empowered personalities who work like engineers in God's creation, something like the idea of angels in Christianity.

          1.2.1.12.1.2. Pro:
          Some of the other personalities are Krishna Himself exhibitting different features of His personality much like a court judge has one personality he shows at work and another he shows at home with his kids climbing on him.

          1.2.1.12.1.3. Pro:
          The Tattva Sandardba of Jiva Goswami establishes the Srimad Bhagavatam as the highest authority in the Vedas and that the Srimad Bhagavatam teaches pure devotion to Sri Krishna.

          1.2.1.12.1.4. Pro:
          The word Krishna means all-attractive one. This name is therefore no sectarian name but is a very fitting name for the supereme personality of Godhead.

          1.2.1.12.1.5. Con:
          That scriptures describe what a deity is like does not itself prove that such a deity exists.

        1.2.1.12.2. Con:
        Because Hinduism is a pantheistic or panentheistic belief system, this is not possible.

          1.2.1.12.2.1. Pro:
          God in this discussion is defined to be the God of classical theism, which is a monotheistic God. If Hinduism "proves" the existence of this God, then it would also prove itself to be false... in which case, it could prove nothing.

          1.2.1.12.2.2. Pro:
          If Hinduism proves the existence of it's own concept of God, this would defeat the premise of a monotheistic God, thereby defeating the initial premise of this discussion.

            1.2.1.12.2.2.1. Con:
            The kind of God that Hinduism proves the existence of is a monotheistic God, so the Hindu proof would not defeat the premise in any way.

          1.2.1.12.2.3. Con:
          The Hindu concept of God is not [464] necessarily [465] incompatible with monotheism.

            1.2.1.12.2.3.1. Con:
            Hindu followers disagree.
            
            "To call Hinduism monotheistic or to try to scale it down into the perimeters of western monotheistic religions is misleading and erroneous." -- American Institute of Vedic Studies [466]

              1.2.1.12.2.3.1.1. Con:
              The American Institute of Vedic Studies is not remotely representative of what Hindus believe.

            1.2.1.12.2.3.2. Con:
            While some Hindu followers would agree with the above ("But in reality Hinduism is monotheistic. [467]" --- Swami Radhanandaji), even those followers would affirm the existence of other "gods" emanating out of the Brahaman. This is incompatible with monotheism.

            1.2.1.12.2.3.3. Pro:
            "Worship practices in Hinduism are as diverse as its traditions, and a Hindu can choose [468] to be polytheistic, pantheistic, monotheistic, monistic, agnostic, atheistic, or humanist."

          1.2.1.12.2.4. Pro:
          From the link on the root claim [469]: "Classical theism is a form of theism in which God is characterized as the absolutely metaphysically ultimate being, in contrast to other conceptions such as pantheism, panentheism, polytheism and process theism."

          1.2.1.12.2.5. Con:
          Hinduism is the belief of a single God of many faces

    1.2.2. Con:
    Belief based evidence is only considered by worshippers as valid where it lends support to their own religion; this is an example of relativist fallacy [470].

      1.2.2.1. Pro:
      Any belief based claim of evidence can be used to support any belief based proposition such as polytheism.

        1.2.2.1.1. Con:
        Many examples of Polytheistic religions show the Gods often being in conflict with one another. This may infer that creation by a committee of gods would be problematic and less likely to succeed than creation by a single entity.

          1.2.2.1.1.1. Pro:
          Multiple people with equal power sometimes reach an impasse. A disagreement among gods would result in chaos. This is not consistent with the world we observe which is ordered and predictable.

            1.2.2.1.1.1.1. Con:
            A disagreement between gods need not result in chaos, as beings of equal power could instead reach an impasse. Indeed, everywhere in nature, we see predators optimised to catch prey that are in turn optimised to not be caught. If the order in nature came from a divine source, this would seem a clear example of such an impasse; the god of rabbits and the god of foxes disagree about whether foxes should be able to catch rabbits, and their designs express this conflict.

        1.2.2.1.2. Pro:
        The revelation experienced by worshippers of polytheistic religions would support the existince of multiple Gods, which is a direct contradiction of the claim that the same experience supports the existence of one God only.

      1.2.2.2. Pro:
      If belief based evidence were considered infallible, then there would not be differences in the teachings of the Abrahamic religions.

        1.2.2.2.1. Pro:
        Those of the Jewish faith do not recognise the "faithful practices, religious experiences and testimony" of the Christian and Islamic prophet Jesus and the followers of his founded religion, Christianity.

        1.2.2.2.2. Pro:
        Islamic prophet Muhammad's religious experiences are not afforded the same consideration by Christians as that of their religion's founder.

          1.2.2.2.2.1. Pro:
          Christians do not recognise the revelation received by the Islamic prophet Muhammad as he disbelieved the claim that Jesus was the son of God/Allah.

      1.2.2.3. Con:
      The question, "Does God exist?," is different from the question, "Which God exists?" All theists share the same answer to the first question, so their use of belief-based evidence does not commit the relativist fallacy, which pertains more to the second question.

        1.2.2.3.1. Con:
        If the question of "Which God exists?" can be answered by refusing to accept belief based evidence (to avoid committing the relativist fallacy) all Gods are eliminated, answering the question of "Does God exist?" with no.

    1.2.3. Con:
    Subjects that experience such divine revelations could actually be experiencing a form of the placebo effect because of their pre-existing belief in God.

      1.2.3.1. Con:
      It is possible divine revelations are truthful, but are rejected out of hand by non-believers due to their lack of belief.

    1.2.4. Con:
    Testimonial evidence is not accurate most of the time.

    1.2.5. Con:
    Testimonial evidence is inherently anecdotal [471] and thus is unreliable and of limited value due to it's clear weaknesses.

      1.2.5.1. Pro:
      The same kinds of experiences involving the divine happen with deities other than the Christian God, which undermines the credibility of religious experiences as evidence for any particular deity or religion.

        1.2.5.1.1. Con:
        -> See 1.2.1.10.6.1.3.

        1.2.5.1.2. Pro:
        If all of these experiences are real, then the God of classical theism cannot exist.

        1.2.5.1.3. Con:
        The fact that the belief in God is prevalent, even among groups who refuse to believe or accept any of the other claims from competing groups, tends to add further weight to the belief in God.

          1.2.5.1.3.1. Con:
          Many of the religions referred to are polytheistic rather than monotheistic, and therefore a belief in gods, rather than a belief in God.

          1.2.5.1.3.2. Con:
          The "further weight to believe" is not evidence of existence.  All religions cannot be right at the same time, because their gods and beliefs contradict each other.

        1.2.5.1.4. Con:
        The differences between deities experienced in revelations are surface-level differences. The same ultimate being is at the root of some, many, or all of them.

        1.2.5.1.5. Con:
        Christianity, which endorses classical Theism, does not claim that God is the only spiritual entity in the universe. Encounters with a spiritual entity do not necessarily mean that such encounters are with God Himself. Therefore, a spiritual encounter with a being who is not God in no way implies that God does not exist.

      1.2.5.2. Pro:
      Anecdotal evidence is prone to cognitive bias [472].

      1.2.5.3. Pro:
      Anecdotal evidence is prone to selective cherry picking [473], potentially for self-serving interests.

      1.2.5.4. Pro:
      Anecdotal evidence is highly prone to confirmation bias. [474]

    1.2.6. Con:
    When such testimonies are more or less exclusively from only millennia or at least hundreds of years ago and are largely ambiguous, that substantially weakens the case for regarding such as credible supportive data or even evidence.

      1.2.6.1. Pro:
      One would expect a God to communicate more unambiguously. Theist religious texts such as the Bible were written long ago and basically not extended since then [475] and religious events such as prophets and notable divine revelations also do not seem to occur anymore.

    1.2.7. Pro:
    The teachings of prophets provide credence to the idea that God exists.

      1.2.7.1. Pro:
      There are prophets who can reform people even if such reform seems unachievable. This wouldn't be possible without divine help.

        1.2.7.1.1. Con:
        If free will exists, then there should be no-one for whom reform is impossible without divine intervention.

        1.2.7.1.2. Con:
        Something that "seems unachievable", but actually happens, shows that it was not as unachievable as that.

        1.2.7.1.3. Con:
        This is an argument from ignorance.

      1.2.7.2. Pro:
      God the Father and Jesus Christ visited Joseph Smith. First Vision [476]

        1.2.7.2.1. Con:
        -> See 1.2.1.1.4.

      1.2.7.3. Con:
      The claim that the aforementioned prophets are endorsed by God lacks confirmation.

      1.2.7.4. Con:
      Prophets often endorse contradictory claims (example: Matthew 5:44 [477] and Koran 2:191 [478]).

        1.2.7.4.1. Pro:
        -> See 1.2.1.7.3.

        1.2.7.4.2. Pro:
        The Quran contains contradictions and inconsistencies. [479]

        1.2.7.4.3. Con:
        It is not surprising to find prophets from two different religions making contradictory claims.

          1.2.7.4.3.1. Con:
          Often the contradictions [480] are [481] within [482] the same religion

          1.2.7.4.3.2. Pro:
          Two prophets from two dissimilar religions worshiping different concepts of God (one or both of which may be false Gods) provide no basis for an expectation of consistency.

            1.2.7.4.3.2.1. Con:
            The fact that different prophets have different views while claiming to have all used the same method indicates that they cannot be trusted.

      1.2.7.5. Con:
      This point is circular within the broader argument, because it essentially makes the claim: “We know how to know God, because God told the prophets and they told us.” If god doesn’t exist, he didn’t tell the prophets anything.

      1.2.7.6. Con:
      No prophet has ever been shown to have specific, precise, and accurate knowledge of the future that cannot be explained by chance or their personal influence.

        1.2.7.6.1. Con:
        Biblically speaking a prophet’s principal role is to be a forthteller [483], not a foreteller.

        1.2.7.6.2. Pro:
        There have been many supposed prophets since the scientific revolution, and so have been many opportunities for these people to prove themselves to be genuine, if they wished.

    1.2.8. Pro:
    Testimonies of experiences involving God are a reliable form of evidence.

      1.2.8.1. Con:
      A religious person claiming that they had a religious experience is not proof of anything other than that person's religiousness.

        1.2.8.1.1. Con:
        This does not provide an explanation for atheists and non-believers who have religious experiences.

          1.2.8.1.1.1. Pro:
          An atheist who has a compelling religious experience would no longer be an atheist, so by this standard no account of personal revelation would be valid no matter how trustworthy or reliable. It's like a flat-earther denying all orbital photographs of the Earth's curve that weren't taken by a fellow flat-earther.

          1.2.8.1.1.2. Pro:
          Some converts to Christianity attribute their conversion to an experience of God that they had (while still a non-believer).

        1.2.8.1.2. Con:
        The above claim is predicated on a bias world view (materialism) which filters evidence (anything indicating a metaphysical reality) according to its bias and then claims there is no evidence to contradict the bias. It's therefore guilty of begging the question.

        1.2.8.1.3. Con:
        If we apply the same principle (personal experience is not evidence) to materialism we run into the same problem as when applied to theism. If personal experience is not evidence then there is no way to prove that the material world exists. Extreme skeptism about human experience has unintended consequences as it devastates any conceivable epistemological foundation.

      1.2.8.2. Con:
      Anecdotal accounts of religious experiences cannot be regarded as credible evidence for the existence of God.

        1.2.8.2.1. Pro:
        -> See 1.2.5.1.

        1.2.8.2.2. Pro:
        Religious people may deliberately lie or be unintentionally mistaken.

        1.2.8.2.3. Pro:
        -> See 1.2.5.2.

        1.2.8.2.4. Pro:
        -> See 1.2.5.3.

        1.2.8.2.5. Pro:
        A testimony is not worth much without supporting tangible or testable evidence.

        1.2.8.2.6. Pro:
        Even if the revelation is truly from God and is properly interpreted by the one receiving revelation, such an experience is personal and only justifies belief for that individual. However, it cannot be used as part of an argument to persuade others who have not had that experience.

          1.2.8.2.6.1. Con:
          If religious experience is the primary mode of an individual's epistemic contact with the divine, then it should be regarded as important subjective evidence in an individual's consideration of God's existence, even if it is not convincing as objective evidence in a debate.

          1.2.8.2.6.2. Con:
          While it's true that the proof is only evident to those who have the eyes to see it or who have had such experiences, what makes it scientific is that anyone can develop their consciousness to be able to see the proof also. Religions offer a process for pleasing God to the point where God reciprocates with the subject and and his existence becomes evident. The same process also elevates the subjects consciousness to be able to recognize such self-evident truths.

            1.2.8.2.6.2.1. Con:
            This is Pascal's wager. People also leave religion for atheism, cults or in many cases, to ensure they do not miss the Superbowl. This suffers from the replication and reproducibility problems as the effects do not hold for all who take Pascal's wager.

        1.2.8.2.7. Pro:
        Anecdotal evidence is the least reliable kind of evidence. This means experiences (in this context with god) have little to no value when it comes to proving whether a proposition (the existance of god) is true or false.

          1.2.8.2.7.1. Pro:
          The fact that memories are faulty [484] means that recollection of experiences can not be trusted at face value, hence the unreliability of anecdotal evidence.

        1.2.8.2.8. Pro:
        Humans report religious experiences and develop religious narratives, including the existence of God, as a result of their painful experiences and hardships. If you alleviate the hardships, there would be no need to attribute success to God.

          1.2.8.2.8.1. Pro:
          It seems God is held responsible for both good & bad tidings when He may not even exist. E.g., a regular church-going friend lost his spouse to death & stopped attending church, blaming God for her death.

          1.2.8.2.8.2. Con:
          Some have reported about God without (or at least before) having any major hardship. King Solomon is one example.

          1.2.8.2.8.3. Pro:
          Losing young children may be the hardest thing to cope with. It is often rationalized by the idea that "God needed another angel" It doesn't make any sense, but the belief could help someone cope with a terrible loss.

          1.2.8.2.8.4. Con:
          Hardships may account for some persons generating belief in God. However, it is improbable that this is the only reason.

        1.2.8.2.9. Pro:
        Anecdotal evidence doesn't provide a quantifiable amount of verifiable information to use as a determination for whether a given claim is true or not true.

          1.2.8.2.9.1. Con:
          Religious experience as such needs an explanation, even if anecdotal. Inference to the best explanation shows that the Christian worldview outstrips any other explanations for all instances of religious belief and unbelief.

            1.2.8.2.9.1.1. Pro:
            The Christian doctrine of the Holy Spirit gives a plausible explanation for the perseverance of Christian belief, even in times of persecution.

            1.2.8.2.9.1.2. Pro:
            The Christian teachings about freedom of the will and divine election give a plausible explanation of Christian belief and unbelievers / heresies.

            1.2.8.2.9.1.3. Pro:
            The Christian doctrines on the existence of a deceiver (Satan) add credibility to explanations on religious diversity, unbelief and heresies.

            1.2.8.2.9.1.4. Pro:
            Christian worldview doctrines are internally logically consistent.

            1.2.8.2.9.1.5. Pro:
            Naturalism, Darwinism and materialism fail to plausibly explain the perseverance of belief in times of persecution.

              1.2.8.2.9.1.5.1. Con:
              The number of people who die before reproducing due to being overly committed to a particular belief is not high enough that evolution should have eliminated any propensity to adopt such beliefs to the point of dying for them, so the persistence of belief in times of persecution is perfectly consistent with evolution.

              1.2.8.2.9.1.5.2. Con:
              That a single specific belief system is actually true fails to explain how those of other belief systems, whether religions or political ideologies, have persisted with such belief in times of persecution.

          1.2.8.2.9.2. Pro:
          If anecdotal evidence were legally enough evidence to convict a person of a crime then it would only take a small group of people making a claim that a crime took place to convict the accused, which would make it easy to unjustly convict people. Thus, anecdotal evidence in favor of the existence of God should be ignored.

            1.2.8.2.9.2.1. Con:
            Saying that the anecdotal evidence for Jesus could not convince in a court of law is simply false since many great legal minds, including Simon Greenleaf [485], suggest otherwise.

          1.2.8.2.9.3. Pro:
          There's no evidence to show that divine experiences are anything other than wishful-thinking, fallacious reasoning, or fraudulent reporting.

            1.2.8.2.9.3.1. Con:
            1. The view that the 5 senses are the only ways people can perceive evidence is unnecessarily limited. 
            2. Well over half of all people in the world believe in God, many of whom claim that they can perceive God in a way that they don't trace to the 5 senses. 
            3. Therefore, it is not irrational to consider the possibility of God's existence and that people may be perceiving God in a way other than through the 5 senses.

              1.2.8.2.9.3.1.1. Con:
              There are more then 5 senses, but there is no reason to believe any of them can sense something divine. The "divine sense" just moves the problem of not having a reason to believe in God to one of not having a reason to believe in the sense of God.

            1.2.8.2.9.3.2. Con:
            The currently accepted standards for science and epistemology may not be adequate to account for true religious experience, even if it were assumed there was such a thing.

          1.2.8.2.9.4. Pro:
          Arguments from personal experience are highly subjective and not verifiable or observable.

            1.2.8.2.9.4.1. Con:
            Personal experience is often held in high regard.

            1.2.8.2.9.4.2. Con:
            To say all personal experience cannot be trusted because of its private nature is a sweeping generalization. Individual reports must be taken into account on a case by case basis.

          1.2.8.2.9.5. Con:
          The possibility of revelation and direct experience gives reason to give some credence to claims, and while not being enough for an external determination is very compelling to the one who experienced it first hand.

            1.2.8.2.9.5.1. Pro:
            Even if the only way for a hypothetical external observer to test this is to cease being an external observer, this is a relevant point to the enquiry (as none of us are truly external).

            1.2.8.2.9.5.2. Con:
            Since we want to know whether God exists in our shared world (reality), rather than in some person's halucinations and mistakes, personal experience is unfit and unreliable for a task that is a prori implausible.

              1.2.8.2.9.5.2.1. Con:
              Because Merriam-Webster includes a definition of "a priori" as "presupposed by experience [486]", it seems that personal experience is precisely the tool required to evaluate a priori assumptions.

          1.2.8.2.9.6. Con:
          Once you analyse all the known points of view of a certain Historical Data from all the dimensions you may now interpret that historical data(i.e.cultural context of the time,human habits of that period, the roots and all known coincidences (i.e. The existence of dinosaurs discovered through bones in different parts of the world) one develops distinct capacities in relation to that certain data. This simply means:You become a master of it and your logic may filter the bias. You become a scholar.

          1.2.8.2.9.7. Con:
          Anecdotal evidence is often used in courts of law, often as a cumulative case, to determine guilt. The case for God has many many numerous anecdotes that can culminate with philosophical thought (which is often also based on physical reality and/or intuitive reasoning about the human experience) and even some scientific findings/proposals (on prayer, the soul).

        1.2.8.2.10. Pro:
        The object of one's faith and the subsequent practices, as well as the context of ones experiences can all be based on provable falsities. Importantly, revelation is necessarily a first person experience and merely hearsay to everyone else.

          1.2.8.2.10.1. Con:
          God is a necessary condition for divine revelation to occur. The statement here seems analogous to saying that reading the newspaper is only proof of the existence of newspapers, and not of the existence of journalists. That would be absurd.

      1.2.8.3. Pro:
      Rejecting this would be like assuming a missing soldier is dead, while ignoring individual reports that he is alive.

        1.2.8.3.1. Con:
        One must have good evidence said soldier existed in the first place, in addition to a lack of evidence for his supposed death and good reason to believe said reports are credible. None of which are present nor sufficient in a testimonial claim of the existence of an all-powerful supernatural being.

        1.2.8.3.2. Con:
        Individual reports that a dead person is alive do occur in the case of famous people like Elvis Presley [487]; seeing as the Christian God is almost as popular as the Beatles [488], one may surmise the same would apply to Him.

        1.2.8.3.3. Con:
        Rejecting this would be like accepting you don't know whether or not a missing soldier is dead, regardless of reports he is alive, because he has not yet been found in either state.

        1.2.8.3.4. Pro:
        Believing a missing soldier is dead means giving up hope. Similarly, discarding hope in the presence of uncertainty is unhelpful, so it is important for people to practice faith and hope in the existence of God.

          1.2.8.3.4.1. Con:
          Giving up hope allows to abandon a false belief and replace it with a true one, which can better allow to improve the world.

          1.2.8.3.4.2. Con:
          This does not substantiate why it would be important for people to practice faith and hope in the existence of God. A globally large population of irreligious people [489] and various [490] surveys and studies [491] show [492] that religion is not necessary and that one can be hopeful, successful and happy without such beliefs.
