People can also substitute religious doctrine with beliefs that are less false or have much higher likelihoods, while for many doing so is not needed either.

      1.2.8.4. Pro:
      Testimonial evidence is used in courts of law to establish facts beyond reasonable doubt.

        1.2.8.4.1. Con:
        A supernatural claim will not be accepted in court.

        1.2.8.4.2. Con:
        Tangible evidence, or a lack of expected tangible evidence, tends to override testimonial evidence and lends far more to reasonable doubt or a conviction.

        1.2.8.4.3. Con:
        In a court of law testimonial evidence is rarely accepted by itself.

          1.2.8.4.3.1. Pro:
          Testimonial evidence will be rejected when the claim is extraordinary or outside of our normal understanding of physics.

            1.2.8.4.3.1.1. Pro:
            No eyewitness is going to be accepted in a court of law for something like resurrection that would require something like specialist medical expertise.

              1.2.8.4.3.1.1.1. Con:
              Specialists medical experts are not needed to know that crucified men don't come alive.

                1.2.8.4.3.1.1.1.1. Con:
                They might be needed to ascertain whether or not a person was actually dead.

          1.2.8.4.3.2. Con:
          Testimonial evidence is acceptable on its own if there are many independent reliable witnesses, and if the testimony includes facts that can be established objectively.

            1.2.8.4.3.2.1. Con:
            If it is accepted with many other statements, then it isn't on its own.

        1.2.8.4.4. Con:
        This claim is highly country dependent, some court of law will give more value to witness testimony than the other.

      1.2.8.5. Con:
      Testimonial evidence has been already proved to be unreliable. Not only people can lie but also hallucinate, have memory distortion etc. Testimonials are far from being scientific evidence.

        1.2.8.5.1. Pro:
        Moses is said to have spoken to God through a burning bush, yet there are many plants that have hallucinogenic [493] effects when burned.

        1.2.8.5.2. Pro:
        Eyewitness accounts are incredibly unreliable. 1 [494] 2 [495] 3 [496]

        1.2.8.5.3. Pro:
        Using written stories that were originally oral tradition [497] as evidence is the equivalent of 'hearsay' in a court setting.

          1.2.8.5.3.1. Pro:
          The Old Testament was written around 1000 -- 750 BC, and theologians have determined Genesis to be around 10,000 years ago for the time to work. That means 7,000 years of oral tradition.

          1.2.8.5.3.2. Pro:
          There were flood stories predating the writing of Noah that had a number of similarities. Noah was likely borrowed and re-adapted.

          1.2.8.5.3.3. Pro:
          The authors of the Bible collected oral tales and pieced them together. Thus, it is essentially a second-hand account, which is not admissible as evidence [498].

          1.2.8.5.3.4. Con:
          Historical evidence is not held to the same standard as legal evidence.

            1.2.8.5.3.4.1. Con:
            Either way, hundreds and hundreds of generations passing a story forward diminishes its accuracy.

        1.2.8.5.4. Pro:
        The game / experiment Chinese Whispers [499] easily show how unreliable a testimony can be.

        1.2.8.5.5. Pro:
        A 1999 U.S. Department of Justice [500] panel claimed that, "Like trace evidence, eyewitness evidence can be contaminated, lost, destroyed or otherwise made to produce results that can lead to an incorrect reconstruction [of events]."

        1.2.8.5.6. Con:
        In this specific case, which I assume is testimonies about the life of Jesus, hundreds of people supposedly saw him. Therefore there should be hundreds of contradictory testimonies, which is not the case.

          1.2.8.5.6.1. Con:
          They could have gotten their information at the same source.

          1.2.8.5.6.2. Con:
          Testimonials of various miracles performed by Jesus would certainly generate contemporary accounts which simply do not exist.

            1.2.8.5.6.2.1. Pro:
            Matthew 27:50 [501] details that upon the death of Jesus the earth shaking, tombing breaking open and the bodies of holy people returned to life. That this extraordinary series of events does not appear to have been recorded by any contemporary record, or even the other gospels defies credulity.

      1.2.8.6. Con:
      Testimonial evidence supports the existence of mythical creatures such as Santa Claus and Big Foot.

        1.2.8.6.1. Pro:
        Accepting this would be like assuming fairies exist because people claim they've had a lost tooth replaced with money by the tooth fairy at night.

        1.2.8.6.2. Con:
        Testimonies about Jesus talk about him living a very public life and interacting with many thousands of people, so it would have been easy for people to disregard the testimonies at the time because they would have obviously been false.

    1.2.9. Con:
    To call any testimonial evidence "divine revelations" assumes the existence of a divine agent, which begs the question.

      1.2.9.1. Con:
      Just as with the use of assumptions in logic, the existence of the divine can be assumed in order to build on the argument with additional evidence and reasoning.

        1.2.9.1.1. Con:
        A logical assumption is used to explore ideas, not to prove them. In any kind of reasoning, assuming something to be true in order to prove itself lacks validity and persuasiveness.

    1.2.10. Con:
    Contradictory experiences are commonly reported by adherents of different belief systems.

      1.2.10.1. Pro:
      People have had similar experiences regarding e.g. pagan gods and nature spirits, so unless we accept that all of these exist we cannot use this as proof of God's existence.

        1.2.10.1.1. Con:
        -> See 1.2.1.10.6.1.3.

        1.2.10.1.2. Pro:
        Such experiences do not necessarily validate God specifically. They could validate any of numerous other things. The individual might only interpret the experience as religious as a result of confirmation bias.

          1.2.10.1.2.1. Con:
          Lack of validation is no indication of the opposite. Even the scientific method is not interested in validation, only falsification and partial support, which is offered by personal religious experiences.

            1.2.10.1.2.1.1. Con:
            The scientific method definitely cares about validation precisely because so many scientific observations are of effects with only statistical results.   The more independent trials validating the result the more unlikely the negative is. What you are observing is a publication bias caused by journals so aren't interested in publishing results like "Yes, relativistic effects have been demonstrated yet again", etc.

      1.2.10.2. Pro:
      Some people have religious experiences of the absence of God. If the same standard applies in these cases, these would serve as evidence for atheism.

        1.2.10.2.1. Con:
        It is likely that these people's preconceived biases and lack of faith inform their experiences of the absence of God.

      1.2.10.3. Con:
      There are experiential arguments for the existence of God as a universal power. Those reports come from some practitioners of Yoga, Meditation, and other spiritual practices. Even though these reports are not yet scientifically verified, the accounts are nearly identical.

        1.2.10.3.1. Con:
        The account of a report that is not scientifically verified cannot afford a scientific impact.

          1.2.10.3.1.1. Con:
          The basis of empirical science rests on the assumption that only things which can be empirically verified can be taken with any certainty to be true. This assumption is circular in nature because there is no empirical way to back up this assumption.

        1.2.10.3.2. Con:
        The practices of yoga and meditation do not require a belief in a god to be effective. Since a god is not common in all of these practices, it is not required to get the effects.

          1.2.10.3.2.1. Con:
          The claim is that many people all over the world have an experience of God. If some practice yoga and are atheists this is not relevant. Someone could use a microwave and not believe in radiation. Their food will still cook.

          1.2.10.3.2.2. Con:
          The practice of yoga was originally for the purpose of developing the ability to sit for long periods to meditate on God. The practices have many benefits for the body and mind which are useful for things other than meditating.

          1.2.10.3.2.3. Con:
          Atheistic practitioners of Yoga are only getting a shadow of the benefits to be had from Yoga. There are higher benefits to be had, namely developing Love for God, directly perceiving God, and loosing attraction for the dualistic pleasures of this world.

          1.2.10.3.2.4. Con:
          Bhakti Yoga provides a method to developing love of God with a process to do so and results that can be measured.

            1.2.10.3.2.4.1. Pro:
            By chanting Hare Krishna maha mantra repeatedly while avoiding sinful activity ones heart becomes pure and one gradually realizes pure love for God.

        1.2.10.3.3. Pro:
        The advanced and mature practitioners of religious/spiritual faiths from all over the world while disagreeing on details all see the same principles.

          1.2.10.3.3.1. Con:
          The experience is reproducible. The source of the experience is pure speculation. While the subjective experience is commonly felt across the world, the speculation of what causes it varies widely and tends to match preconceived notions taught by local tradition. Those interpretations contradict each other to such a great degree that one can conclude that there must not be universal source for the experience. If it was divine, then all experiencers would have the same explanation.

        1.2.10.3.4. Con:
        The experience of Yoga, Meditation, etc can be easily explained by modern neurology [502] as not being from God. The experiencer must interpret the subjective experience. Some interpret it as being from God, but that does not make it so. A person's interpretation will depend on their beliefs. So the argument is circular: a person who believes that Yoga experiences come from God, will interpret it as being from God. But it is not evidence of anything.

      1.2.10.4. Pro:
      People of all religions claim to have had religious experiences that pertain to their belief systems. This suggests that religious experiences are psychological or social phenomena, rather than genuine experiences of a particular god.

        1.2.10.4.1. Pro:
        Evangelical Christians claim to have experienced the presence of Jesus. Catholics claim to have have experienced the Virgin Mary or a saint. Muslims claim to have had a religious experience with Allah. Buddhists with Buddha and Hindus with Krishna or another deity and so on and so on.

        1.2.10.4.2. Con:
        People have also reported [503] having religious experiences associated with other belief systems that are not their own, and later converted to the religion in question.

    1.2.11. Con:
    Psychological and sociological explanations question the authenticity of religious beliefs and experiences reported by followers.

      1.2.11.1. Con:
      The psychology of a subject or their social surroundings do not affect the ontology of God.

      1.2.11.2. Con:
      Certain human behaviors and experiences can be explained only by the existence of God.

        1.2.11.2.1. Con:
        Believers automatically attribute things to God because they experience their life through that perspective. Subjective experience isn't reliable, trustworthy evidence.

          1.2.11.2.1.1. Con:
          Believers in Naturalism also filter their experiences through their world view. They automatically attribute experiences to matter. The accusation of bias can just as readily be leveled at skeptics rendering it a meaningless claim.

          1.2.11.2.1.2. Con:
          All experiences are an aggregate composed of subjective experiences. If subjective experiences have no epistemological value then a collection of subjective experiences which we call objective experiences also has no value.

        1.2.11.2.2. Pro:
        Christian martyrs wouldn't have gone as far as sacrificing their lives if they didn't genuinely believe in the existence of God. Thus, Christian martyrdom [504] is proof that God exists.

          1.2.11.2.2.1. Pro:
          Many of the supposed eyewitnesses to the resurrected body went on to be martyred for their faith.

            1.2.11.2.2.1.1. Pro:
            If the witnesses were lying or doubtful about the resurrection, they could have saved their lives by simply denying it happened, yet multiple people were willing to die for their beliefs.

            1.2.11.2.2.1.2. Con:
            There are no confirmed [505] "eye-witnesses" to the resurrection of Jesus.

            1.2.11.2.2.1.3. Pro:
            There are in fact confirmed [506] witnesses [507] of the resurrection [508] of Jesus [509] in modern [510] day times and who died [511] with that testimony.

              1.2.11.2.2.1.3.1. Pro:
              The death [512] for a testimony indicates that one is willing [513] to give their life for what they learned [514] which shows the importance [515] of the knowledge [516] even over their own lives and their eternal [517] perspective [518] than deny what they know. [519]

              1.2.11.2.2.1.3.2. Pro:
              Joseph Smith and Oliver Cowdery recorded seeing the resurrected Jesus Christ [520] .

                1.2.11.2.2.1.3.2.1. Pro:
                Joseph Smith was martyred [521] for the restorative work Christ asked him to complete, thus sealing his testimony of the resurrected Christ like those in ancient times.

            1.2.11.2.2.1.4. Pro:
            Some of the scrolls preserve letters of Peter, who claimed to be an eyewhitness of the resurrection and died for his faith.

              1.2.11.2.2.1.4.1. Con:
              People often present themselves as witnesses to convince people. It is a form of rhetoric that is viewable throughout. Some even believe Socrates was a shared character to give value to morals and ideas of a group. We have many of these today, and no shortage of new ones appearing.

          1.2.11.2.2.2. Con:
          If martyrdom is to be considered evidence of truth, then all faiths (monotheistic, polytheistic or cult based) who have martyrs would be on equal footing.

            1.2.11.2.2.2.1. Pro:
            Buddhist monks lit themselves on fire, while Ättestupa took place by Vikings to avoid death by old age. Very contradictory versions of gods have people sacrificing themselves for their beliefs.

            1.2.11.2.2.2.2. Pro:
            Many Jews have gone through martyrdom as a form of kiddush hashem [522], sometimes to avoid conversion to Christianity.

          1.2.11.2.2.3. Con:
          There are several competing explanations for religious martyrdom.

            1.2.11.2.2.3.1. Pro:
            Those who believe in God don't realize that their love for god blinds them, preventing them from logically assessing the decision to be a martyr.

            1.2.11.2.2.3.2. Pro:
            People sometimes voluntarily make sacrifices in the name of strongly held community beliefs. This is why young men go to war, government officials stuck to their posts during a Japanese Tsunami [523], and parents insist on female circumcision for their daughters.

              1.2.11.2.2.3.2.1. Con:
              In the case of the 11 faithful apostles, this is obviously not what happened. In their case, they were in a place to know what happened. They were eyewitnesses. Yet to a man, they never recanted their story, even in the faith of persecution and death. To believe 11 men maintained a lie for decades, even to their own disadvantage, is illogical and unreasonable.

            1.2.11.2.2.3.3. Pro:
            How unlikely it is that someone would be willing to be killed for a false belief depends on the perceived risk of being killed, the possible rewards and the tolerance of the individual to risk.

            1.2.11.2.2.3.4. Pro:
            Martyrdom can be achieved through steady desensitisation and role modelling by authority figures.

              1.2.11.2.2.3.4.1. Pro:
              A far simpler explanation for martyrdom would be that it was a culmination of self-sacrificial behavour (e.g. chastity, submission during marriage etc.) stemming from indoctrination beginning from birth and reinforced by all authority figures and peers.

          1.2.11.2.2.4. Pro:
          Millions of Christians [524] have been martyred over the centuries for their religious beliefs, indicating a genuine belief in the existence of God.

            1.2.11.2.2.4.1. Con:
            New Testament scholar Candida Moss argues that "Christians were never the victims of sustained, targeted persecution." [525]

              1.2.11.2.2.4.1.1. Con:
              During the 1500's Christians were systemically targeted, persecuted and murdered by majority religion powers. One of the most horrific periods was in 1572 [526].

                1.2.11.2.2.4.1.1.1. Con:
                Violence and persecution among different denominations of Christians, such as Catholics and Protestants, is not an example of persecution of Christians as such.

              1.2.11.2.2.4.1.2. Con:
              Moss claims that Christians weren't persecuted for their beliefs, but does state that they were executed for "publicly denying the divine status of the emperor" - which is a fundamental part of Christian belief (that there is only one God).

              1.2.11.2.2.4.1.3. Con:
              In the Annals 15.44, written by Roman historian Tacitus, Jesus and the Christians are mentioned. It recounts [527] how Emperor Nero went after Christians in order to draw attention away from himself after Rome's fire of 64 AD.

              1.2.11.2.2.4.1.4. Pro:
              According to Harari [528], the Roman persecutions of Christians over five centuries amounted to the deaths of no more than a few thousand people.

          1.2.11.2.2.5. Con:
          Oscar Wilde [529] famously quipped, "A thing is not necessarily true because someone dies for it." Accordingly, throughout history, many have chosen to die for causes that have proven to be unworthy [530].

            1.2.11.2.2.5.1. Con:
            A majority of martyrs did not have first-hand proof that their religion was true or if their cause was worthy. In contrast, the Christian apostles who were martyred knew through first-hand experience [531] that what they believed was true.

              1.2.11.2.2.5.1.1. Con:
              If Jesus were resurrected, it may be true that the apostles were in a position to know firsthand about it. But if Jesus wasn't actually resurrected, the apostles being in the same position wouldn't matter.

            1.2.11.2.2.5.2. Pro:
            Many people have died and continue to die on both sides of religious wars and for religious causes. They cannot all be right, so dying for a cause is not proof that one is right.

            1.2.11.2.2.5.3. Pro:
            Breatharians [532], like Jasmuheen [533], put their life in jeopardy for their indisputably false beliefs, namely, that human beings can live solely on air.

            1.2.11.2.2.5.4. Pro:
            The Aztecs used human sacrifices to keep the sun aloft.

              1.2.11.2.2.5.4.1. Con:
              Those sacrificed by the Aztecs were often captives taken in battle [534] and sacrificed against their will; these are not examples of martyrs.

              1.2.11.2.2.5.4.2. Pro:
              Aztec human sacrifices are comparable to martyrdom [535] in other major religions like Islam, as the afterlife is portrayed as a paradise that one can attain by, among other things, laying down one's life.

          1.2.11.2.2.6. Con:
          History is written by the victors. The stories of early martyrs might have been "improved" in order to paint the whole religion in a better light.

            1.2.11.2.2.6.1. Pro:
            Stories often embellish the truth and exaggerate, mainly if their main purpose is to spread a message or persuade someone.

              1.2.11.2.2.6.1.1. Pro:
              Luke 1 [536] does not claim to be an accurate account but one designed to persuade.

            1.2.11.2.2.6.2. Pro:
            People have a psychological inclination to root for the underdogs [537]. Stories of extreme martyrdom and great deeds inspire devotion and sympathy.

            1.2.11.2.2.6.3. Con:
            Christianity has plenty of examples of early Christian cowards (Peter), people slow to understand (the disciples) and other embarrassing testimony. It is not a movement that seems to recast it's characters as heroes.

              1.2.11.2.2.6.3.1. Pro:
              Peter was called "Satan" by Jesus. The disciples fled in terror while the women were found at the cross and were the first to the tomb in a society that did not value women or their testimony! The only "hero" in the story is Jesus himself.

              1.2.11.2.2.6.3.2. Con:
              The narrative of "bad" turning into "good" with Christianity as a catalyst of that change is one of the central parts of Christian lore and teaching. The existence of said examples does not contest the possibility of existence of embellished martyrdom stories.

        1.2.11.2.3. Con:
        The shared understanding of God is disassociated from the record of how religious leaders act in accordance to their shared core beliefs, on such a large scale that would suggest there was no God at all.

          1.2.11.2.3.1. Pro:
          The name of "God" has been used for malicious things such as a point of bigotry, racism, fascism, and a lot more.

          1.2.11.2.3.2. Pro:
          God, as the Abrahamic religions [538] understand him, formed a covenant with Abraham. This suggests they share the same God, yet these religions' historical relationships and questionable leadership tactics are proof enough that God does not exist.

            1.2.11.2.3.2.1. Con:
            -> See discussion #8539: Religion has been a good thing for humankind.

            1.2.11.2.3.2.2. Con:
            It is commonly assumed that the concept of God is consistent across Judaism, Christianity, and Islam. This is not the case in many of the most important aspects of any understanding of God, including salvation, his relationship with men, and many other key considerations.

              1.2.11.2.3.2.2.1. Pro:
              In Christianity, God is Trinitarian... one God consisting of three persons. Judaism and Islam are strictly Unitarian... one God, one person.

              1.2.11.2.3.2.2.2. Pro:
              In Islam, salvation is found in obedience to Allah. In Christianity, salvation is found by faith in Christ's obedience to God through his salvific death and resurrection.

              1.2.11.2.3.2.2.3. Pro:
              In Christianity, God is knowable on intimate terms... we can call him "Abba Father". This is less true in Judaism, and blasphemous in Islam, in which God is considered to be so "other" to man that he is practically incomprehensible.

              1.2.11.2.3.2.2.4. Con:
              The idea of God may differ religion to religion, just as their cultures and traditions do, but the account of God's covenant with Abraham is indicated in each religion's foundational narrative. From self-reported accounts of genealogy, these religions follow the same God.

                1.2.11.2.3.2.2.4.1. Con:
                A few shared stories are hardly sufficient to claim identity. If God is immutable, and these religions differ about key aspects of God, it's obvious that at least two (and perhaps all three) of them are wrong in their belief.

              1.2.11.2.3.2.2.5. Con:
              They share genealogy [539], Abraham is a founder of all three, that do in fact worship the same God.

            1.2.11.2.3.2.3. Pro:
            The holy wars and conflicts [540] between each other are in direct opposition to their core beliefs [541], and are especially troubling in light of the fact they follow the same God.

              1.2.11.2.3.2.3.1. Pro:

              1.2.11.2.3.2.3.2. Pro:

              1.2.11.2.3.2.3.3. Con:

                1.2.11.2.3.2.3.3.1. Pro:

          1.2.11.2.3.3. Con:

            1.2.11.2.3.3.1. Pro:

            1.2.11.2.3.3.2. Con:

              1.2.11.2.3.3.2.1. Con:

            1.2.11.2.3.3.3. Con:

              1.2.11.2.3.3.3.1. Con:

              1.2.11.2.3.3.3.2. Con:

          1.2.11.2.3.4. Con:

God is Immutable and also God is able to change any aspect of Creation.Creation is an aspect of God's will or in other words:creation is one of God's artworks painted with the crayons of life and death.

            1.2.11.2.3.4.1. Con:

          1.2.11.2.3.6. Con:

            1.2.11.2.3.6.1. Con:

          1.2.11.2.3.8. Pro:
          Religious leaders don't necessarily have anything to do with God. There are those who try to act in God's name and those who do not. We are called to a relationship with His Son Jesus Christ which must NEVER go through other men.

          1.2.11.2.3.9. Con:
          The two ideas in this statement are not linked. Solely because there is wide debate on a topic does not mean that the entire topic is invalid. Many aspects of politics are hotly debated, but this does not mean that the freedom for anyone to murder without punishment is any less wrong.

        1.2.11.2.4. Con:
        The symptoms of some mental disorders like schizophrenia [546] cause people to hear voices in their head. Without a modern understanding of this condition, an Iron Age man may have perceived it as divine revelation, or at the very least, supernatural.

          1.2.11.2.4.1. Con:
          Altered states of consciousness including dreams, hallucinations, astral projections and other out-of-body experiences, often are of a spiritual nature or are instructive, hinting at a greater intelligence or at least the existence of something beyond the physical.

            1.2.11.2.4.1.1. Con:
            -> See 1.1.7.3.1.

            1.2.11.2.4.1.2. Con:
            Dreams, hallucinations, astral projections and OBEs find physical expressions and can be interpreted as body phenomenons. Psychological experiences can explain and reproduce such phenomenons without involving "God".

            1.2.11.2.4.1.3. Con:
            Dreams and hallucinations can be interpreted in any way to confirm your beliefs. If you believe in 'anything', you can then twist dreams to fit within the narrative of your beliefs. Who's to say dreams don't 'actually' show that we are living in a virtual universe created by omnipotent chickens?

            1.2.11.2.4.1.4. Con:
            All mental states can be proven to be a function of the material operation of the brain.  There is no science suggesting there is anything supernatural going on. When a brain dies, it ceases any mental activity.

          1.2.11.2.4.2. Pro:
          -> See 1.1.7.3.1.

          1.2.11.2.4.3. Pro:
          Hallucinations are extremely common [547] even within the normal population. Yet, these experiences are not evidence of the existence of anything observed or experienced in the hallucination.

            1.2.11.2.4.3.1. Pro:
            Studies [548] have shown the religious hallucinations are common symptoms of a variety of mental health disorders.

            1.2.11.2.4.3.2. Pro:
            There is no objective standard for whether a given religious experience is a hallucination or a revelation. It is judged differently by observers [549], in accordance with their bias, such as their upbringing.

            1.2.11.2.4.3.3. Con:
            Unlike religious experiences, hallucinations are not shared. They take place in one mind independent and irrespective of another.

              1.2.11.2.4.3.3.1. Pro:
              Eye witness accounts by a large number of people cannot rationally be explained as a shared hallucination.

                1.2.11.2.4.3.3.1.1. Con:
                The earliest account of mass hallucination in western society was pre 1500AD, where a nun started biting people and it became 'infectious'. More recent accounts including the Ketereh, Malaysia episode where a group of students claimed to see a face of pure evil [550].

                1.2.11.2.4.3.3.1.2. Con:
                Theories suggest that there are scientific explanations for the historically common belief in witches

                  1.2.11.2.4.3.3.1.2.1. Pro:
                  It has been theorised that hallucinogenic properties in contaminated rye [551] caused consumers to believe in witches, and is largely responsible for witch trials and executions.

                  1.2.11.2.4.3.3.1.2.2. Pro:
                  It has been suggested that the witch related hysteria in Salem in 1692 was related to the symptoms of PTSD which has been theorised as afflicting many civilians following the Native American Wars [552].

              1.2.11.2.4.3.3.2. Con:
              There have been shared hallucinations recorded as well. The phenomenon is called "folie à deux [553]."

            1.2.11.2.4.3.4. Con:
            This claim equates, without defense of such equivocation, an individual's religious experiences and hallucination. Without evidence to show that these are the same, this claim becomes a strawman argument.

            1.2.11.2.4.3.5. Con:
            There is no way to prove that everything beyond human understanding is a hallucination. Using this as an argument against the existence of God is both a false premise and circular argument.

            1.2.11.2.4.3.6. Con:
            There is no empirical way to establish that this life and the world we perceive is not just one long and convincing hallucination. The quality of the experience proves itself.

              1.2.11.2.4.3.6.1. Con:
              Being less certain that there exists an objective external reality at all doesn't in any way make us more certain that that external reality includes a God.

              1.2.11.2.4.3.6.2. Con:
              A hallucination is defined as "an experience involving the apparent perception of something not present". It is not possible to have a hallucination within a hallucination, as the person within the hallucination would not be present to experience their own hallucinations.

          1.2.11.2.4.4. Con:
          In ancient civilizations, people had some understanding of mental illness, as evidened by mentions of "madness," and believed in demon possession. Yet, these groups selectively accepted certain individuals' claims of divine revelation, choosing not to believe that it was madness or possession.

            1.2.11.2.4.4.1. Con:
            It is common for relatives and friends to deny the reality of a loved ones mental illness, it can be more comforting to believe that they are in contact with divine beings than have an illness which could impact the family's social standing, marriage prospects etc.

            1.2.11.2.4.4.2. Con:
            Those in a position of power such as a priest may make a conscious decision to take advantage of a confused mentally ill person and use the symptoms of their mental illness to further the respect and faith he has in a community.

            1.2.11.2.4.4.3. Con:
            A person who has only known their religion and has no other worldly knowledge to offer an alternate view may decide that the most logical conclusion is that either they or someone they love is in communication with a divine being.

            1.2.11.2.4.4.4. Con:
            In Mesopotamia diseases and mental disorders [554] were believed to be caused by specific deities. This aspect shows that Mesopotamians believed all disease, mental illness and any kind of revelation was all because of Gods.

      1.2.11.3. Con:
      Religion confers several positive psychosocial benefits on humanity. Thus, it makes pragmatic sense to believe in God.

        1.2.11.3.1. Pro:
        Choosing a religious lifestyle has a net positive effect on people; therefore, one should believe in God regardless of how correct the view.

          1.2.11.3.1.1. Pro:
          A Pew 2012 study [555] found that actively religious participants were 5 % more likely to say that they are 'Very happy' than unaffiliated participants.

            1.2.11.3.1.1.1. Con:
            This 5% increase in happiness could be the result of the close-knit community that goes along with religion. People could experience a similar boost in happiness through joining a club, volunteering, or being more engaged in their communities without having to join a religion.

          1.2.11.3.1.2. Con:
          Even if one 'should' believe of a god's existence, this is not reason to be convinced that a god exists.

          1.2.11.3.1.3. Pro:
          The unity and generosity of religious communities supports the idea that God brings people together.

            1.2.11.3.1.3.1. Con:
            The unity and generosity of religious communities only supports the idea that the concept of a God brings people together, it is not an indication that God exists.

              1.2.11.3.1.3.1.1. Pro:
              Social gatherings around fictional universes (sci-fi conventions for example) can bring people together in unity and generosity.

            1.2.11.3.1.3.2. Con:
            Any shared value system (e.g., veganism), cause (e.g., activism), or event (natural calamity) can bring people together. This effect is not unique to the existence of God.

            1.2.11.3.1.3.3. Con:
            Bringing people together is not always a positive outcome. Religious fundamentalists, for example, can bring people together to perpetuate extremism [556] (p. 23).

          1.2.11.3.1.4. Con:
          An increase in happiness when joining a religion is contingent on a lot of factors, including if the religion accepts you. Several studies have shown that same-sex attracted people [557] have reduced happiness when joining a church that does not accept them.

          1.2.11.3.1.5. Pro:
          Adopting a similar philosophy of people with whom you can benefit by association is a time-honored practice.

          1.2.11.3.1.6. Pro:
          There is peer-reviewed research which supports the effects of prayer [558].

            1.2.11.3.1.6.1. Con:
            The cited article [559] is limited to visual and auditory improvements. It is an overgeneralization to assume that beneficial effects from a very limited scope of research could provide sufficient evidence for the existence of god.

            1.2.11.3.1.6.2. Con:
            Proof of the effects of prayer does not correlate to proof of god(s).

            1.2.11.3.1.6.3. Con:
            The placebo effect is well documented with sugar pills as well.

        1.2.11.3.2. Pro:
        The role of divine being such as God is typically intrinsically linked to stories of the afterlife in a vast amount of cultures and religions. It is a convienient and encouraged explanation to compensate for the loss felt by others following a death. Such beliefs alleviate grief and promote a feeling of hope of reunion with and peace for the deceased.

        1.2.11.3.3. Pro:
        God exists for those who need him, similar to the idea that "beauty is in the eye of the beholder."

        1.2.11.3.4. Con:
        There are pragmatic reasons not to believe in God as well.

          1.2.11.3.4.1. Pro:
          The idea of worshiping a God for metaphysical reasons and the power Christianity gives other men over other people in this world poses a conflict of interests.

          1.2.11.3.4.2. Pro:
          Such reasons include not having to follow set-in-stone rituals that use up one's time and energy (e.g. prayer five times a day for islam, shabbath for judaism, church on sunday for christianism, and many others )

            1.2.11.3.4.2.1. Con:
            Many people enjoy [560] rituals such as prayer.

          1.2.11.3.4.3. Pro:
          People don't have to feel guilty for normal behaviours such as (but not restricted to) masturbation or out-of-wedlock-sex; indeed religions tend to shame such practices whereas they are actually normal and healthy [561]. The guilt that such activities bring can prevents people from doing them, or cause mental distress when people do.

          1.2.11.3.4.4. Pro:
          Different meats are forbidden [562] in different religions. An atheist can eat whatever foods one wants, whenever one wants (Ramadan in Islam, Lent in Christianity).

        1.2.11.3.5. Con:
        Pragmatism is irrelevant to philosophy and truth.

          1.2.11.3.5.1. Con:
          The meaning of hypotheses can be found by tracing their ‘practical consequences’ – their implications for experience in specific situations. Thus, the hypothesis that God exists can be judged based on its practical consequences.

            1.2.11.3.5.1.1. Pro:
            Belief in the existence of God contributes to social progress. Thus, 'God exists' is true because the concept operates successfully within a conceptual scheme that advances the well-being of humanity.

              1.2.11.3.5.1.1.1. Con:
              The same could be said of non-belief in the existence of God based on the advancement of human well-being in secular countries like Japan and Sweden. Thus 'God does not exist' is true because the concept operates successfully as well.

              1.2.11.3.5.1.1.2. Con:
              Social progress based on faith tends to only advance well being if everyone believes in the same faith. Events such as the St Bartholomew's Day massacre [563], cannot be accurately described as advancing the well-being of humanity.

          1.2.11.3.5.2. Pro:
          Shared religious beliefs confer several psychological benefits, regardless of the actual existence of God. Thus, the benefits of belief in God do not support the existence of God.

            1.2.11.3.5.2.1. Pro:
            God does not physically exist but serves as a moral metaphor for us to distinguish between right and wrong.

            1.2.11.3.5.2.2. Pro:
            There is good evolutionary benefit from assumption of a false positive. Belief in God can be attributed to the evolutionary mechanism, Agenticity, [564] that leads to people assuming there is a god who may punish them if they don't behave. This explains why a belief in a god would evolve as a defence mechanism regardless of whether or not there is actually a god.

            1.2.11.3.5.2.3. Pro:
            Many religious groups fiercely dispute that belief has no benefit. There's a popular notion of "Placebo God" in which God is seen as a beneficial idea even if untrue.

            1.2.11.3.5.2.4. Pro:
            Earliest god figures were that of the sun, moon, stars, and animals known as animism [565]. As a way to bring order to society and people wanting to gain power over others, feigning communion with the gods or spirits would achieve this. This would provide an evolutionary societal benefit. Insisting laws be divinely mandated could keep people from killing each other and provide for a better primitive society, creating early stability, and benefiting those who believed.

            1.2.11.3.5.2.5. Pro:
            A commonly cited one is that shared beliefs allow communities of >150 people to trust strangers thus promoting greater exchange of goods and ideas and thereby promoting survival odds of those sharing [566] the belief [567].

          1.2.11.3.5.3. Pro:
          Just because a belief has psychosocial benefits does not make the belief legitimate. For example, it may be beneficial for the psychosocial wellbeing of his congregation if a priest is believed to be celibate, but the reality may be he is in fact abusing young parishioners.

          1.2.11.3.5.4. Pro:
          There are many inconvenient truths.

          1.2.11.3.5.5. Pro:
          Illusions can have pragmatic benefits [568], but this does not mean they reflect reality.

      1.2.11.4. Pro:
      God is just an excuse to avoid our fear of death as a whole.

      1.2.11.5. Pro:
      -> See 1.1.7.3.

      1.2.11.6. Pro:
      -> See 1.1.7.6.

      1.2.11.7. Pro:
      God existence drifts from an instinct collocated near the epilepsy-related part in our brains [569] and not from logical thinking.

      1.2.11.8. Con:
      Belief in God is intrinsic to human psychology [570] and fundamentally instinctual.

        1.2.11.8.1. Pro:
        Human desires, like hunger and thirst, come from the biological drive to seek something real in the world (e.g. water and food). As human beings have a desire to believe in God, it is likely that this desire exists because God is a real entity.

          1.2.11.8.1.1. Con:
          These desires can be explained in neurological terms without the existence of an unseen realm.

            1.2.11.8.1.1.1. Con:
            Human beings experience hunger because we need food. This is a neurological function, yet we do not disregard the reality that food must exist. Reducing something to a neurological function does not invalidate the reality of the need or desire nor the object that will satisfy it.

              1.2.11.8.1.1.1.1. Pro:
              The fact that we hunger for something does not suggest we will be satisfied, just that the object exists; a person stuck on an island will be hungry, but may starve to death. Likewise, some people may never see God, even though they yearn for what is beyond our world.

                1.2.11.8.1.1.1.1.1. Con:
                Comparing universal psychological states such as hunger to non-universal states such as desiring God is a false analogy [571].

          1.2.11.8.1.2. Pro:
          The inherent requirement and craving for God in virtually every culture suggests an evolutionary benefit for acceptance of the existence of God. Thus, the a priori existence of God can be inferred.

            1.2.11.8.1.2.1. Con:
            Evolutionary benefits do not have to correlate to facts. It is evolutionarily beneficial for humans to be omnivorous. This does not mean that humans are obligate omnivores.

            1.2.11.8.1.2.2. Con:
            The selection pressure to conform to religious belief and practice was man-made through punishment, isolation, and other disadvantages. This had nothing to do with God's existence.

              1.2.11.8.1.2.2.1. Pro:
              With each generation indoctrinating the next well before critical thinking develops, it becomes ingrained, and is as much cultural as religious. It is a "requirement," especially in religiously homogeneous regions. The existence of a tradition alone, does not prove its worth, or that it is logical.

            1.2.11.8.1.2.3. Con:
            If anything, this evolutionary benefit would imply that only those who have a tendency to be gullible or compliant are more likely to survive; this can, in no way, be considered to be acceptable evidence for the existence of God.

              1.2.11.8.1.2.3.1. Pro:
              Humans evolved to be superstitious, because this conferred a survival benefit. This does not mean superstitions—be they related or unrelated to God—are real.

            1.2.11.8.1.2.4. Con:
            The "requirement and craving for God" is more likely a craving for understanding of the unknown, which is then filled with a created "God" because it is convenient to give the answer: "God made it that way," and be satisfied.

              1.2.11.8.1.2.4.1. Pro:
              These can be defined as a mere "hole" in the scientific theory that needs to be scientifically filled.

                1.2.11.8.1.2.4.1.1. Pro:
                This can be easily seen in the evolution of politheistic religion, in which every deity had a "power" (Zeus -> lightning, Apollo -> Sun) that were easily eradicated with the comprehension of the real world through science.

                1.2.11.8.1.2.4.1.2. Con:
                The idea that science has proven that there are not personalities controlling the forces of nature is philosophically erroneous because demonstrating the mechanism of an action or force does not show why it happens.

                  1.2.11.8.1.2.4.1.2.1. Pro:
                  For example, it's always possible the sky is blue because it's an artistic choice and that the scientific explanation of how it is made blue is the mechanism used by the artist to achieve the desired effect.

                    1.2.11.8.1.2.4.1.2.1.1. Con:
                    The sky is not actually blue [572].

          1.2.11.8.1.3. Con:
          This claim is logically faulty.

            1.2.11.8.1.3.1. Pro:
            The analogy between hunger or thirst and spiritual desires is weak, which invalidates the argument.

              1.2.11.8.1.3.1.1. Pro:
              The cravings for something beyond the physical are not universal in the way hunger and thirst are universal.

                1.2.11.8.1.3.1.1.1. Pro:
                Desire for food is seen throughout the animal kingdom while the desire to believe in God is seen only in humans.

              1.2.11.8.1.3.1.2. Pro:
              Food is needed for subsistence. In contrast, belief in God only serves as a convenient explanation for how the world works and for what happens after death.

              1.2.11.8.1.3.1.3. Pro:
              The absence of a belief in God has no verifiable ill effects on those lacking them, in the same way starvation and thirst do.

                1.2.11.8.1.3.1.3.1. Pro:
                Atheists and agnostics feel no particular spiritual drive; nonetheless, they lead perfectly fulfilling, happy lives.

            1.2.11.8.1.3.2. Pro:
            This claim commits the post hoc ergo propter hoc fallacy [573]. Deducing that God must exist because some people search for Him is like deducing that thirst creates water without ever having seen any water.

            1.2.11.8.1.3.3. Pro:
            This argument is a perfect example of confirmation bias [574].

          1.2.11.8.1.4. Pro:
          C.S. Lewis has pointed out, in "Mere Christianity [575]," that we thirst, and there is water; we hunger, and there is food. For every "craving" we have, there exists an object of that craving.

            1.2.11.8.1.4.1. Con:
            C.S. Lewis was demonstrably wrong. Sexual fetishes, such as furaphilia [576], a craving for anthropomorphic animal sexual partners exist. This does not mean furries, anthropomorphic animals, exist.

            1.2.11.8.1.4.2. Con:
            C.S. Lewis may have been a theologist, and a well loved author, but he was not a scientist. His comments on the nature of desire can only be taken as his personal opinion and not conclusions of peer reviewed research. Anyone could make the same assertion as him and it is only his well known name that lends it any semblance of credibility. This is clearly an example of the appeal to authority fallacy.

          1.2.11.8.1.5. Con:
          Evolved characteristics can be superfluous or counterproductive.

            1.2.11.8.1.5.1. Pro:
            Human beings have evolved to seek and determine patterns, to the point that humans falsely determine patterns. This innate trait is as natural to humans as any other biological drive, but does not derive fact.

              1.2.11.8.1.5.1.1. Pro:
              If one sees a connection between two unrelated data points, it does not mean there is in fact a connection. This is called apophenia [577].

            1.2.11.8.1.5.2. Pro:
            Human beings may desire the existence of magic, dragons, or extraterrestrial life, but this is not proof that any of those things actually exist.

            1.2.11.8.1.5.3. Pro:
            Biological drives evolve because the drive itself provides a survival benefit. That benefit need not be tied to the physical existence of something. Otherwise, all superstitions would be real.

              1.2.11.8.1.5.3.1. Pro:
              Paul Kurtz argued that because nature is full of chaos and impermanence, it can cause great unease. Some seek to counter that with something stable and eternal. This is what "God" gives them; he's a safe harbor in a storm.

            1.2.11.8.1.5.4. Pro:
            Dogs lick their wounds even though it is counterproductive [578] for them to do so.

            1.2.11.8.1.5.5. Pro:
            Cancer is a case of misuse of evolutionary traits (such as cell growth, replication, irrigation).

        1.2.11.8.2. Pro:
        For some people, psychological methods reliably bring out a sense of presence of God and similar figures, depending on the beliefs of the subject.

          1.2.11.8.2.1. Con:
          The ability to reliably stimulate a sense of the presence of God only proves that people's "sense of the presence of God" is suspect and not indicative of the actual existence of anything outside of the brain.

          1.2.11.8.2.2. Con:
          This reasoning is not applicable to similar claims. For example, the claim, "For some people, psychological methods reliably bring out a feeling of floating. Therefore, they must actually be floating," is false.

          1.2.11.8.2.3. Pro:
          An example is the effects of the God helmet [579] and similar experimental devices and protocols.

        1.2.11.8.3. Con:
        Humans are social animals, and naturally predisposed to understand the world in those terms, which produces superstition; this is a plausible explanation for why children would be inclined to teleological beliefs.

        1.2.11.8.4. Con:
        The postulated atheist argument (implied by the linked source) that children cannot distinguish between imagination and reality is a strawman.

        1.2.11.8.5. Pro:
        Belief in deific beings spans cultures and various cultural developmental milestones.

          1.2.11.8.5.1. Con:
          Things that are intrinsic to human nature or psychology do not necessarily correlate to being true.

        1.2.11.8.6. Con:
        -> See 1.2.11.8.5.1.

        1.2.11.8.7. Con:
        Intrinsic is defined as "belonging naturally, essential". If this were true then humans lacking belief in God would be in constant conflict with their instincts. Atheists for example, can live fulfilling lives without ever feeling they are missing religion.

        1.2.11.8.8. Con:
        The admission of religion as an intrinsic social behavior, damages the claim of a god actually existing, more then it bolsters it.

          1.2.11.8.8.1. Pro:
          -> See 1.2.11.8.3.

          1.2.11.8.8.2. Pro:
          -> See 1.1.7.2.

          1.2.11.8.8.3. Pro:
          It makes no sense for a god to create beings to be intrinsically disposed towards religion, but not intrinsically disposed towards a particular religion.

          1.2.11.8.8.4. Pro:
          Intrinsic social behavior [580] can be observed in animals, lending greater credence to naturalistic explanations over divine ones.

      1.2.11.9. Pro:
      Increasingly, human beings are looking for meaning and purpose from within than from an external divine being. This shows that God is becoming more and more irrelevant to determine human meaning and purpose.

        1.2.11.9.1. Pro:
        Global belief patterns [581] suggest that people are becoming less religious [582] but more spiritual [583]. This implies that benefits previously associated with organized religion (e.g., meaning and purpose) can now be derived through individual spirituality.

        1.2.11.9.2. Con:
        Where human beings derive their meaning and purpose from has no relevance to the question of God's existence; whether they fail to consider God has no impact on his existence.

      1.2.11.10. Pro:
      There are human experiences ('divine moments', 'spiritual moments', moments of ultimate joy, serenity or peace) that can easily be attributed to a god but can also be explained in other, more rational ways. It is not necessary to attribute these experiences, which might just be biological processes and "epiphanies" in our brains, to a superior being.

      1.2.11.11. Pro:
      In most instances, followers are born into their religion. Their parents expose them to faithful practices and religious experiences from a young age. This is a form of indoctrination, which cultivates poor tolerance for divergent thought.

        1.2.11.11.1. Con:
        This is a genetic fallacy [584]. Hence, how someone was brought up has no bearing on the veracity of their beliefs. Each belief must be evaluated in terms of the evidence for it.

          1.2.11.11.1.1. Con:
          Beliefs only have to be evaluated in terms of the evidence for them to be justified true beliefs. People can believe unjustified things as well.

            1.2.11.11.1.1.1. Pro:
            One can believe that their partner is faithful and the evidence may even support this. However, that belief can still be incorrect.

        1.2.11.11.2. Pro:
        Stating that the means of obtaining information is tainted, therefore the information gained from it is too, is nothing more that an example of Fruit of the poisonous tree. [585] This is an argument that is good enough for the U.S. judicial system.

          1.2.11.11.2.1. Con:
          The U.S. judicial system is not considered to be very good in global comparisons [586]. Using a poor quality benchmark does not convey good evidence.

        1.2.11.11.3. Con:
        The same explanation also applies to non-religious families. In other words, atheists may have been raised in atheism by their parents.

          1.2.11.11.3.1. Pro:
          Darwin's grandfather Erasmus Darwin [587] likely influenced his beliefs and theories.

          1.2.11.11.3.2. Con:
          The scientific method is an antidote to indoctrination: any inherited knowledge would be verifiable, and one does not assume that a theory is correct without adequate proof. This supports atheistic upbringing but opposes theistic upbringing.

      1.2.11.12. Pro:
      God is an association of agency where there is none (Type I error [588]).

        1.2.11.12.1. Pro:
        From an evolutionary perspective, Type 1 errors have more survival value than Type 2 errors because assigning agency to the unknown may have saved humans from potential danger. As a result, many people now see God where there might be none.

          1.2.11.12.1.1. Pro:
          Religion formed naturally from hyperactive agency detection [589] - a tendency to attribute natural or unexplained events to a sentient agent (in this case, God).

            1.2.11.12.1.1.1. Con:
            Studies have shown that, while believers in superstition and the paranormal do show hyperactive agency detection [590], believers in classical religion don't. In fact, some studies have found that atheists and skeptics often have underactive agency detection [591], meaning they deny the existence of agency even when it is actually there.

            1.2.11.12.1.1.2. Pro:
            Psychological studies indicate people have tendency to shift responsibility to somebody else when many people are present. The God could be a target for such shift when no other target is available. Groupthink in wikipedia [592], Diffusion of responsibility [593]

            1.2.11.12.1.1.3. Con:
            Modern theological arguments (teleological, moral, ontological, cosmological) arrive at the existence of a God deductively, by revealing a situation that could only be properly explained by something we'd call a God, not by seeing unanswered questions and proposing a God to explain them.

        1.2.11.12.2. Con:
        By this logic, it can be argued that atheism is a cognitive error because it fails to ascribe agency when there is an agent at play.

          1.2.11.12.2.1. Con:
          Knowledge when soundly based upon current available evidence can be found to be wrong in the future, but it is not a cognitive error. Assumptions are cognitive errors.

          1.2.11.12.2.2. Con:
          The rational person begins with the null hypothesis: a thing is not true or does not exist, until it is proven to be true or to exist. Theism requires belief without sufficient evidence.

        1.2.11.12.3. Con:
        This claim is based on circular reasoning: this argument would be true only if God does not indeed exist.

  1.3. Pro:
  Philosophical arguments support the existence of God.

    1.3.1. Con:
    -> See 1.1.6.1.

    1.3.2. Con:
    It is not possible to strictly prove the existence of God on a rational level, but only on a spiritual level of being. Spiritual being is in many ways contrary to the rational approach. The more we try to rationally prove God, the more we move away from spirituality and from God itself.

      1.3.2.1. Con:
      If belief in the existence of god can't be proven to be rational, that by definition states said belief is irrational.

    1.3.3. Con:
    Philosophical arguments are not homogeneous. Therefore, there are strands of philosophy that disagree with the existence of God.

      1.3.3.1. Pro:
      Philosophical arguments which support the existence of one God would also support the existence of many Gods, hence undermining the classical depiction of the monotheistic God.

        1.3.3.1.1. Con:
        This is not true of Aristotle's "prime mover [594]" argument, also known as the argument from contingency.

        1.3.3.1.2. Con:
        The claim "At least one God exists" is separate from the claim "If at least one God exists, only one God exists" and is supported using separate evidence. An argument for the former claim is not rendered unsound just because it does not prove the latter claim.

        1.3.3.1.3. Pro:
        Philosophical arguments support the existence of the Roman [595] and Norse [596] Pantheons, which are widely believed [597] to not exist.

          1.3.3.1.3.1. Con:
          While the existence of Roman and Norse Pantheons is not consistent with some theologies, there are others which it is consistent with.

            1.3.3.1.3.1.1. Pro:
            For example, the Srimad Bhagavatam talks of one supreme God who us functioning under Him an assortment of demi-gods who over see the running of the material world, sort of like engineers.

          1.3.3.1.3.2. Con:
          There are people who believe in demi-gods and that they co-exist with a supereme God. An inclusivist or syncretistic approach would allow these demi-gods to be known by different names and stories in different cultures.

            1.3.3.1.3.2.1. Pro:
            For example, the bhakti tradition of India holds that God exists and is known by many names in different cultures and that He empowers demigods to over see the material affairs of the material world while He remains aloof in His spiritual abode. These various pantheons such as the Norse and Greek gods might be these same servants of God understood by different names and stories.

          1.3.3.1.3.3. Con:
          The classical arguments (the cosmological, contingency, ontological, and moral arguments) for God's existence would only allow for the existence of a single deity that is common to the general monotheisms.

            1.3.3.1.3.3.1. Con:
            Classical arguments don't hold more weight than others.

      1.3.3.2. Con:
      Apologetics is a branch of Christian philosophy [598] that provides systematic argumentation supporting the existence of God.

        1.3.3.2.1. Con:
        Apologetics attempts to rescue a God whose existence one has accepted a priori [599].

          1.3.3.2.1.1. Con:
          A priori beliefs can be justified [600] even without evidence.

            1.3.3.2.1.1.1. Con:
            Empiricists argue that no a priori [601] judgments are justified and that all justified beliefs are formed through experience.

          1.3.3.2.1.2. Con:
          The preaching done by skeptics can likewise be described as the endless attempt to justify beliefs which they accept a priori.

            1.3.3.2.1.2.1. Pro:
            The encounter Rupert Sheldrake had with Richard Dawkins illustrates this clearly youtu.be [602]

          1.3.3.2.1.3. Con:
          A truth accepted through a different kind of epistomology called self-evidence can be bolstered by other epistemologies.

            1.3.3.2.1.3.1. Pro:
            For exampe, we all accept the existence of the material world on a self-evident basis. The benefits that come from carrying this belief are consistent with it being true and therefore reinforce the belief.

          1.3.3.2.1.4. Con:
          The suggestion that apologetics can be dismissed on the grounds that its proponents accept the conclusion a priori is a clear example of the genetic fallacy [603].

          1.3.3.2.1.5. Con:
          The aim of apologetics is not to defend God, but to defend the apologist's belief in God --not to convince nonbelievers, but to demonstrate why the believer is convinced.

            1.3.3.2.1.5.1. Pro:
            The Christian definition of apologetics comes from 1 Peter 3:15, advising believers to be ready to give "a reason (apologia) for the hope that is in you". It is couched in a greater admonishment toward an outward manner that reflects the integrity of the individual's belief, as described in 1 Peter 3:14-16, with apologetics being only one facet of this manifestation.

            1.3.3.2.1.5.2. Pro:
            People should accept God based on testimonies and changed lives of His followers, which they can themselves experience if they allow themselves to have faith in God.

              1.3.3.2.1.5.2.1. Con:
              Personal experiences can be claimed for every religion and other extraordinary ideas. They are therefore unconvincing.

                1.3.3.2.1.5.2.1.1. Con:
                A loving God would reciprocate with sincere attempts to come closer to him regardless of theological details. The above argument is predicated on an exclusivist view of different religions or the idea that they are mutually exclusive. The exclusivist view which considers one religion true and all others false is its self logically incoherent with respect to the claim that God is all loving and compassionate. An all loving God would not be cold to sincere aspirants over theological details.

              1.3.3.2.1.5.2.2. Con:
              Changed lives can show that lives can be changed by ideas - but that does not show that those ideas are indeed true.

      1.3.3.3. Pro:
      Materialism arguments [604] do not support the existence of God.

        1.3.3.3.1. Con:
        As materialism is defined largely in terms of what it excludes, this is asking for evidence of absence.

          1.3.3.3.1.1. Con:
          It is appropriate to request evidence of absence from those who claim that a thing does not exist.

          1.3.3.3.1.2. Con:
          Materialism must also account for what it chooses to include (for instance: a belief in mind, combined with a rejection of objective moral principles, needs to be established as a non-arbitrary position).

            1.3.3.3.1.2.1. Con:
            There are people who believe that objective moral principles consistent with materialism exist, such as Sam Harris, so materialism should not be said to include a rejection of objective moral principles.

              1.3.3.3.1.2.1.1. Con:
              That some people believe a thing does not make it true. One must establish that Sam Harris (or whomever) is being fully consistent with materialism.

                1.3.3.3.1.2.1.1.1. Pro:
                Regarding this example, Harris writes [605]: "Some intuitions are truly basic to our thinking. I claim that the conviction that the worst possible misery for everyone is bad and should be avoided is among them." This is a siding with an axiomatic adherence to moral truth over materialism. It is, therefore, questionable whether Harris is actually a materialist.

              1.3.3.3.1.2.1.2. Con:
              Regardless of the particular example, the choice of accepting some claims about reality, while rejecting others, must be justified. If materialism is going to maintain that its reasons are non-arbitrary, it needs to provide a consistent standard by which it makes these judgments.

            1.3.3.3.1.2.2. Pro:
            Materialism makes a number of ontological and epistemological claims, and is as obligated to support those claims as any view.

            1.3.3.3.1.2.3. Pro:
            Materialism tends to justify itself by some variation of positivism [606]. This puts it at odds with axiology [607], but there is no reason to favor positivism (or materialism) over axiology.

            1.3.3.3.1.2.4. Pro:
            Materialists have notoriously been unable to solve the mind-body [608] problem, leading some materialists to deny the existence of our common experience of mental states (via eliminative materialism [609]).

        1.3.3.3.2. Con:
        Theistic claims on moral truth and the human mind are better supported than materialism

          1.3.3.3.2.1. Pro:
          As objective moral truth can't be cached out in materialist terms, materialism is implicitly committed to moral relativism and/or nihilism.

            1.3.3.3.2.1.1. Pro:
            The materialists are essentially running afoul of the is-ought problem [610].

          1.3.3.3.2.2. Pro:
          There is as much reason to accept one's moral sense as pointing to objective reality as there is to accept one's physical senses as pointing to (a different layer of) objective reality. Materialism, however, denies this.

            1.3.3.3.2.2.1. Con:
            One's "moral sense" is a term for an emotional reaction to things perceived with real senses - eyes, ears, etc. - and materialism does not and need not deny the objective reality of the actual things perceived with those senses.

              1.3.3.3.2.2.1.1. Con:
              To claim that the moral sense is purely emotional reaction is to presume that objective moral principles do not exist. It is begging the question to presume this before materialism is established.

              1.3.3.3.2.2.1.2. Con:
              Without an additional argument, it would be special pleading to assume that the moral sense is simply the product of emotion, while the physical senses are not simply the product of delusion.

            1.3.3.3.2.2.2. Con:
            This assumes that people's moral feelings are experienced as a sense that things are "objectively" wrong, which has no reason to be any more universal than belief in objective morality itself.

              1.3.3.3.2.2.2.1. Con:
              The typical human claim that something "is wrong" is a claim about objective fact. This is not only the understood meaning of "is", but is the only way to make sense of most conversation about moral claims: the demands that people make clearly reveal claims that are seen as binding on others—rather than a mere opinion, which others are free to take or leave without giving a reason.

              1.3.3.3.2.2.2.2. Pro:
              This would be assuming that people who don't believe in objective morality are denying the experience of their senses, or are otherwise in constant contradiction, with the only evidence being the subjective experience of those who do believe in objective morality.

            1.3.3.3.2.2.3. Pro:
            For any argument against the validity of the claims of the moral sense, a parallel argument can be made against the physical senses.

              1.3.3.3.2.2.3.1. Con:
              Moral claims describe qualitative experiences, more analogous to the "redness" of read than the observable frequency of photons. As such, the corresponding arguments against physical senses would only deny that "redness" is a real property of photons - perfectly consistent with physicalism.

                1.3.3.3.2.2.3.1.1. Con:
                All sensory experience has some degree of "qualitativenss" to it. If one utterly rejects qualia (immediate first-person experiences), one has no evidence for trust in science or its discoveries (such as photons).

            1.3.3.3.2.2.4. Pro:
            Even those who object to talk of non-physical realities can frequently be found implicitly supporting claims of moral truth, such as presenting alternative lists of the Ten Commandments [611], claiming that they are better (in an objective sense?) then Biblical morality.

            1.3.3.3.2.2.5. Con:
            Even our physical senses often don't point to an objective reality. This includes hallucinations, hearing someone's voice who isn't nearby, seeing a person in your peripheral vision that isn't there, feeling like something wet is touching you when really it's just cold, seeing faces in everyday objects/scenes, various optical/auditory illusions, etc.

          1.3.3.3.2.3. Pro:
          As Nagel [612] and Jackson [613] have famously argued, the actual experience of everyday sensations (colors, sounds, and other qualia [614]) can never be fully reduced to third-person, scientific claims about the objects / events being experienced.

          1.3.3.3.2.4. Pro:
          Materialists who behave morally are implicitly admitting that they have large areas of life in which their decisions are based on unfounded assumptions.

            1.3.3.3.2.4.1. Con:
            Moral relativism doesn't conflict with moral behaviour any more than believing taste in films is subjective conflicts with watching good films.

              1.3.3.3.2.4.1.1. Con:
              No one who claims that taste in films is subjective can coherently claim that there is such a thing as a "good" film. Similarly, materialists who behave morally cannot coherently claim that there is any such thing as genuinely moral behavior.

                1.3.3.3.2.4.1.1.1. Con:
                People who acknowledge taste is subjective call films "good" all the time, without contradiction - that this does not mean the film is "objectively" good is unspoken and assumed.

              1.3.3.3.2.4.1.2. Pro:
              People can and do watch films they consider good, and avoid behaviour they consider bad, without contradicting themselves or otherwise being stumped by the fact that their preferences are not "objectively" correct.

              1.3.3.3.2.4.1.3. Con:
              One may well conclude that such filmgoers were irrational if they demanded the forcible removal of certain films or seriously recommended prison for those who made or watched certain films—while declaring others mandatory. This, however, is common behavior in moral discussion.

            1.3.3.3.2.4.2. Pro:
            -> See 1.3.3.3.2.1.1.

          1.3.3.3.2.5. Con:
          This is a Sherlock Holmes fallacy [615]. No amount of evidence against something can support the truth of something else. If you have evidence that X did not commit a murder, it cannot be inferred that Y did commit the murder. In the same way, proving materialism false does not prove theism true.

        1.3.3.3.3. Pro:
        A majority of philosophers accept or lean towards physicalism [616].

          1.3.3.3.3.1. Con:
          The majority of philsophers are not experts in philosophy of religion. They should not be considered experts.

            1.3.3.3.3.1.1. Con:
            Materialism and physicalism do not belong to philosophy of religion, and philosophers of religion have no special claim to expertise on questions like the nature of consciousness.

          1.3.3.3.3.2. Pro:
          Notwithstanding the object-level question of which position is true, which position is "more well-supported" is about the state of the field, which expert consensus should be an even stronger indicator for.

            1.3.3.3.3.2.1. Con:
            It is often the minority position which is more well-supported, in that those with minority views are more apt to respond to the majority view, whereas the majority often ignores maverick views.

        1.3.3.3.4. Con:
        Materialistic religious arguments support the existence of God.

          1.3.3.3.4.1. Pro:
          Materialistic religion treats conceptual, metaphysical and abstract ideas such as the afterlife, divine beings and the narration of creation as physical facts, rather than seeing them as allegory.bahaiteachings.org [617]

            1.3.3.3.4.1.1. Con:
            "Religious materialism" as defined at that source is exactly contradictory to materialism [618]; supernatural beings which are not reducible to mundane atoms, such as a physically-existing angel, are the thing that materialism asserts do not exist.

          1.3.3.3.4.2. Con:
          These arguments insist that scripture can only be interpreted literally, in order for materialism and theism to be in concurrence.

          1.3.3.3.4.3. Con:
          Materialistic relgious arguments directly contradict widely accepted metaphors found in scripture.

            1.3.3.3.4.3.1. Con:
            Both positions support the existence of God.

      1.3.3.4. Pro:
      According to the nihilist viewpoint, God does not exist.

        1.3.3.4.1. Con:
        God can be subjective and still exist.

          1.3.3.4.1.1. Pro:
          Many argue that numbers and even "random" ideas have real existence (platonism).

            1.3.3.4.1.1.1. Con:
            Platonism [619] is not subjective. Platonists hold that abstract objects exist in a "third realm", not that they exist as subjective ideas. Subjectivism [620] as a philosophy holds that no external truths exist.

          1.3.3.4.1.2. Con:
          If God is subjective, then being "God" is not a property of the object, but intrinsically an ideal ascribed within the view of someone looking at the object from a certain prism.

          1.3.3.4.1.3. Con:
          The God of classical Theism is the uncreated creator of all created things. A god that is subjective is, by definition, a created thing. Thus the two definitions are completely incompatible.

            1.3.3.4.1.3.1. Con:
            That "creator God" does not exist, as creating requires time and there was no time before the big bang.

            1.3.3.4.1.3.2. Con:
            That "creator God" does not exist if the B theory of time [621] is true.

              1.3.3.4.1.3.2.1. Con:
              The B theory of time does not eliminate the need for a creator, who could have created and determined the entire course of history.

                1.3.3.4.1.3.2.1.1. Con:
                If the universe always existed (as per the b-theory of time) then it was not created.

                  1.3.3.4.1.3.2.1.1.1. Pro:
                  -> See 1.1.1.4.2.2.1.3.15.2.1.1.1.1.2.2.2.1.1.

              1.3.3.4.1.3.2.2. Pro:
              -> See 1.1.1.4.2.2.1.3.15.2.1.1.1.1.2.2.2.1.1.

            1.3.3.4.1.3.3. Con:
            It is compatible with the definition of God being "ultimate metaphysical being".

        1.3.3.4.2. Pro:
        If all things are metaphysically equal, there is no metaphysical ultimate or supreme.

          1.3.3.4.2.1. Con:
          Assuming that all things are metaphysically equal assumes naturalism, so this claim begs the question.

        1.3.3.4.3. Pro:
        The idea of a ultimate metaphysical being (and therefore it's mapping onto any thing) is subjective. God's existence is therefore purely subjective.

          1.3.3.4.3.1. Pro:
          Conceptions of ultimate beings can, and often do, differ between people and cultures.

            1.3.3.4.3.1.1. Con:
            Inconsistent concepts of God may show that some (or all) aspects of a given concept is incorrect. It does not show that no concrete object exists behind the concepts.

          1.3.3.4.3.2. Con:
          That persons subjectively perceive a thing, even in different forms, does not entail that the thing is *merely* subjective.

          1.3.3.4.3.3. Con:
          Just because an analysis is not empirical, doesn't mean it is subjective. Mathematical proofs are not empirical but are certainly not subjective.

            1.3.3.4.3.3.1. Con:
            This does not disprove that philosophical arguments aren't subjective.

            1.3.3.4.3.3.2. Pro:
            Many philosophical arguments use empirical evidence, for example, the Kalam cosmological argument.

        1.3.3.4.4. Con:
        From a nihilistic viewpoint, every argument is ultimately meaningless including arguments for nonexistence.

          1.3.3.4.4.1. Pro:
          "No philosophical claims are objectively true" is a philosophical claim. So it is either false, or not objectively true.

        1.3.3.4.5. Con:
        Nihilism, just as religion, is a viewpoint and does not provide any proof to the existence, or non-existence of God.

          1.3.3.4.5.1. Con:
          For something to be considered divine the attribution of meaning is necessary, which disproves the fundamental existence of divinity.

            1.3.3.4.5.1.1. Con:
            If God exists necessarily (which implies immutability) and eternally, then meaning has also existed necessarily and eternally in the mind of God, which provide divinity with its fundamental existence.

      1.3.3.5. Con:
      Philosophical arguments for the existence of God were made by people like Aristotle, Plato, Leibniz, and Einstein. The great intellect of these people and their contributions are without question. They were definitely capable of providing sound arguments for their beliefs.

        1.3.3.5.1. Con:
        Those largely were not referring to the God(s) of religion(s) but rather metaphysical philosophical thought which are would be misinterpreted to refer to something related to 'classical theism' or even classical polytheism.

          1.3.3.5.1.1. Pro:
          Pantheism [622] may be a partly fitting term for what they referred to, not classical theism.

          1.3.3.5.1.2. Pro:
          Einstein was not a Christian or theist [623], criticized the Bible [624], and described conventional religious belief as naïve.

    1.3.4. Pro:
    Cosmological arguments [625] support the existence of God.

      1.3.4.1. Pro:
      -> See 1.1.1.4.2.2.1.

      1.3.4.2. Pro:
      -> See 1.1.1.4.2.2.1.3.

      1.3.4.3. Con:
      Just because cosmology says that there was a beginning doesn't entail the existence of an almighty creator. Cosmology is ever changing, and it's possible that, in the near or far future, humanity will discover the real cause of the Big Bang.

        1.3.4.3.1. Con:
        The "cosmological argument" refers to philosophical arguments about the existence of the cosmos, and is not directly related to the science of cosmology, so this is confused.

        1.3.4.3.2. Con:
        "Speculation" of what the future holds is not a coherent argument with regards to scientific method, nor does it dismiss The Creator as probable First Cause.

          1.3.4.3.2.1. Con:
          The argument is not about speculation as to what will be shown in the future but that, in cosmology, nothing or not much points toward an almighty creator – it does dismiss such as a "probable" first cause even though it may not dismiss such as a possible or potential first cause of our Universe.
