      1.3.4.4. Con:
      Cosmological arguments do not support the existence of God as the existence of God has no explanatory power as it does not explain how God came into being or always existed and therefore has the exact same unsolved problem that question of the first causation of anything represents.

      1.3.4.5. Pro:
      The argument from composition [626] supports the existence of God.

        1.3.4.5.1. Pro:
        -> See 1.1.1.4.2.2.1.3.1.

        1.3.4.5.2. Con:
        The argument presupposes that something non-composite and immaterial can cause something material to come into existence.

          1.3.4.5.2.1. Con:
          Given that the human mind is at least partially immaterial, non-material entities can cause material effects.

            1.3.4.5.2.1.1. Con:
            That the mind is even partially immaterial requires that physicalism is false. This is far from being established; a majority of philosophers accept or lean towards physicalism [627].

          1.3.4.5.2.2. Con:
          The argument does not "presuppose" that something non-composite and immaterial can cause something material to come into existence, but rather, argues that something non-composite and immaterial can cause something material. The difference is in assuming as a premise or in accepting as a conclusion.

        1.3.4.5.3. Con:
        Composition can be perfectly explained without the existence of God. Anything that can happen will happen, so it is possible for particles to randomly form atoms which can randomly form molecules which can randomly form chemical structures and so on.

        1.3.4.5.4. Con:
        Composition is a mental abstraction rather than caused by another entity.

          1.3.4.5.4.1. Con:
          Assuming the existence of a mental shift (and, therefore, a mind) is to assume the existence of a composite object (a human being who possesses a mind).

            1.3.4.5.4.1.1. Con:
            Obviously our understanding of our own mental abstractions will involve mental abstractions, that doesn't make them anything more than that.

              1.3.4.5.4.1.1.1. Con:
              If there is an objective "our" (or "me"), then there is at least one psychologically complex object. Almost certainly, this object is both physically and metaphysically composite.

            1.3.4.5.4.1.2. Con:
            Describing mental events requires only "cogito, ergo sum" - that such things as human beings exist is not required, so this is wrong.

            1.3.4.5.4.1.3. Pro:
            If there is any external world (presumably consisting of elementary particles), then the mind experiencing this mental shift is either an immaterial substance, or a composite object.

            1.3.4.5.4.1.4. Pro:
            The human mind is fundamentally complex. It contains various thoughts, emotions, and other traits. It is a clear example of a composite object, meaning that the idea of composite objects cannot simply be dismissed as illusory.

          1.3.4.5.4.2. Con:
          This would lead to the conclusion that human beings are not composite objects. That is, one's mind is divorced from any particular physical object (such as the brain).

            1.3.4.5.4.2.1. Con:
            The mind being divorced from any physical object does not follow from composition being a mental abstraction. This is just conflating separate meanings of what "not composite objects" could mean.

          1.3.4.5.4.3. Pro:
          For a composite object such as a clock, seeing the clock and not merely the component parts it is made of is a conceptual abstraction in the human mind, and does not reflect anything physically primitive

        1.3.4.5.5. Con:
        Changes can accumulate within a complex environment of interacting atoms and molecules. This explains how composites form naturally, not supernaturally.

          1.3.4.5.5.1. Con:
          Despite the numerous experiments [628], and even assuming the conditions in which they were preformed matched the conditions of the early earth (rather than favoring positive results), they all failed to produce life out of lifeless matter.

          1.3.4.5.5.2. Pro:
          If chemicals accumulating are inorganic, then this is an example of inorganic evolution. If they are organic, then this is an example of organic evolution. If they are molecules carrying mutable information inherited by a biological organism, this accumulation is an example of biological evolution.

          1.3.4.5.5.3. Con:
          These "natural forces" or "laws of physics" would then be the (intermediate) cause which keeps composite entities together. If the laws themselves are in some way composite, then they too require a cause. If they are not composite, then they are absolutely simple, which has been argued elsewhere to equate to God.

            1.3.4.5.5.3.1. Con:
            Natural causes are still natural causes regardless of whether they are composite natural causes or non-composite natural causes.

            1.3.4.5.5.3.2. Con:
            Simply because it has been claimed elsewhere that God is absolutely simple and implied that God is the only such being, does not mean that anything absolutely simple has been shown elsewhere to equate to God. Claims are neither proof nor evidence. They are claims.

        1.3.4.5.6. Con:
        Composite things are not "caused by" simpler things, but "composed of" simpler things.

        1.3.4.5.7. Pro:
        God is this absolutely simple or non-composite [629] cause of all composite things.

          1.3.4.5.7.1. Con:
          No justification is given as to why the argument determines that the non-composite entity is God.

          1.3.4.5.7.2. Con:
          There are zero scientifically validated examples of a thinking entity which is more simple than a hydrogen atom, which is a complex composite of subatomic particles. Therefore it would be ludicrous to assume from that claim that one could logically conclude such a hypothetical first cause to be capable of thought. Therefore, it could not be a God, at least in the classical theology sense.

            1.3.4.5.7.2.1. Con:
            God is immaterial. Science confines its study and its methods to only what portions of the material world are measurable. Therefore the lack of "scientifically validated examples" is irrelevant. It is like expecting a yardstick to be able to gauge what something sounds like. Science is the wrong tool for the job of analyzing God. Science is the right tool for analyzing His creation.

              1.3.4.5.7.2.1.1. Con:
              If there is no way to measure a god then there is no reason to believe a god exists.

          1.3.4.5.7.3. Con:
          To define a God of classical theology to be basically the simplest thing imaginable is to define away everything that defines it as a God according to classical theology. There are no known mechanisms by which an unchanging nondescript simple thing with no moving parts can accomplish anything at all. That's not a God. It's more like an infinitesimal neutrino field without even a neutrino. Basically as close to nothing as anything can be. You might as well say God is nothing.

            1.3.4.5.7.3.1. Con:
            Divine Simplicity [630] implies many of the other features of God:
            There can be only one absolutely simple thing. The absolutely simple thing must be unchanging (no potentiality). Something devoid of potentiality must inherently be immaterial, for material implies potential. Thus already we have Oneness, Unchanging, Immateriality, all common features of the classical definition of God.

            1.3.4.5.7.3.2. Con:
            Divine Simplicity [631] does not imply smallness. It actually implies immateriality which makes the concept of physical size inapplicable.

            1.3.4.5.7.3.3. Con:
            To be absolutely simple [632] is a very different thing from nothing. Existence is absolutely contrary to non-existence. There are no "gradations" of existence. Something either exists or it does not.

          1.3.4.5.7.4. Con:
          The argument does not claim that composite objects are caused by simpler things, but that they are caused by simple parts being held together by some thing or things outside of the composite object.

            1.3.4.5.7.4.1. Con:
            Then that claim would disagree with physics, since the forces which hold such composites together are carried by the parts.

        1.3.4.5.8. Pro:
        This [633] is another formulation of the proof of God's existence from the composition of things.

    1.3.5. Pro:
    The "argument from degrees [634]" supports the existence of God.

      1.3.5.1. Con:
      Traits such as beauty, nobility and goodness are subjective properties that different people rank differently, so there is no basis for expecting an objectively 'greatest' example of any such trait.

      1.3.5.2. Con:
      Truth, out in objective reality, is a binary thing; things are either true or false without degree. Degrees of truth appear in subjective probability, but therein the shared 'most true' facts are facts about the self, not some external entity which is somehow 'most true'.

    1.3.6. Con:
    The existence of arguments does not prove anything. It asserts something because someone supported it. It is hearsay. You have to show which argument you propose. This entire branch needs to be eliminated.

    1.3.7. Pro:
    Axiological arguments [635] support the existence of God.

      1.3.7.1. Con:
      In ancient societies that do not share the moralities of us today, even just being from the wrong branch of a particular religion could have gotten oneself killed.

        1.3.7.1.1. Pro:
        Most people would likely concede their viewpoint rather than give up their life.

        1.3.7.1.2. Pro:
        Religious wars happen only because religions exist.

          1.3.7.1.2.1. Con:
          Because it can be demonstrated that religion can exist without religious war, religion alone cannot be considered to cause religious wars.

            1.3.7.1.2.1.1. Pro:
            Instead we must analyse further and find a cause for religious wars in specific ideas or approaches to religion.

              1.3.7.1.2.1.1.1. Pro:
              The idea that God is not all loving and instead will torture for eternity anyone who doesn't take to the one true religion creates an internal logic which makes killing members of those religions reasonable because they are taking people to hell and preventing them from taking to one true religion, their only chance at salvation. youtu.be [636]

            1.3.7.1.2.1.2. Pro:
            India has a long history [637] of religious tolerance free from religious wars. It wasn't until the Muslim invasion that religious intolerance entered. Because religion can exist separate from violence it can't be claimed to cause violence. Specific fanatical ideas within religion however can be attributed as causes [638] of violence.

        1.3.7.1.3. Con:
        There always was an option to believe in God or not to believe in God. Atheism is not a new concept.

          1.3.7.1.3.1. Con:
          Discrimination against atheists has historically discouraged those questioning the faith of their peers from expressing their opinions.

      1.3.7.2. Con:
      The existence of God is not contingent on the value of that existence. This is an example of a moralistic fallacy [639].

        1.3.7.2.1. Con:
        This statement, while true, is not a con for the claim that axiological arguments support the existence of God.

          1.3.7.2.1.1. Pro:
          Axiological arguments are value-based arguments, but the vast majority of value-based arguments are predicated on the value of things, material and otherwise, which are not God. Therefore, the truth of the statement has no bearing, one way or the other, on the claim that axiological arguments support the existence of God.

        1.3.7.2.2. Pro:
        Morality does not factor into the existence, or non-existence of an entity.

          1.3.7.2.2.1. Con:
          If one accepts moral truth as in any way objective, then one is committed either to accepting it as a brute fact (an unparsimonious option) or to accepting that an entity is a causal factor in instantiating moral truth (hence, moral truth is evidence of a entity).

          1.3.7.2.2.2. Con:
          There would be no reason to be moral without the existence of a higher entity.

            1.3.7.2.2.2.1. Con:
            It may be that there is no reason to be moral; arguing for God's existence on the basis of how bad that would be is the fallacy of appeal to consequences [640].

            1.3.7.2.2.2.2. Pro:
            The word ‘reason’ is used intentionally here. Without a higher being, we can ‘explain’ moral behaviour through evolution or the social contract theory etc. But without a higher being keeping us accountable - providing we could get away with something and avoid punishment - there is no ‘reason’ to be moral.

            1.3.7.2.2.2.3. Con:
            Rational actors behaving 'morally' often achieve maximal combined utility outcomes.

            1.3.7.2.2.2.4. Con:
            Moral behavior is the product of instinctual psychological processes hardwired into humans from birth, so God is not needed for one to behave morally.

              1.3.7.2.2.2.4.1. Con:
              This is an explanation of our moral intuitions, but not a justification that our moral intuitions are correct.

                1.3.7.2.2.2.4.1.1. Con:
                To assume that our morals stem from God is stacking assumptions. In any case, many people disagree with God, and with other people. The God proposition doesn't answer the question of the origins of morality.

              1.3.7.2.2.2.4.2. Pro:
              The involvement of mirror neuron system [641] (MNS) is implicated in neurocognitive functions (social cognition, language, empathy, theory of mind) and neuropsychiatric disorders.

              1.3.7.2.2.2.4.3. Pro:
              Humans have physiological empathy-based [642] reactions when doing things that harm or help other people.

          1.3.7.2.2.3. Pro:
          Morality itself doesn't support or refute anything as a fact but rather provides an argument why one action should be taken above another.

            1.3.7.2.2.3.1. Con:
            This is an example of normative ethics, the discussion of what is right and what is wrong. The moral argument is an exercise in metaethics, the question of what moral statements mean and how they come to exist, which is very different.

      1.3.7.3. Pro:
      The existence of objective morality is evidence for the existence of God.

        1.3.7.3.1. Pro:
        Without a transcendent creator and arbiter which gives objective value to truth, there is no objective value to the pursuit of truth.

          1.3.7.3.1.1. Pro:
          Truth has no inherent value. Truth has value if and only if there is an arbiter of mankind which can give value to truth.

            1.3.7.3.1.1.1. Con:
            If we grant that truth has no inherent value, there's no reason why a God should be necessary or sufficient to change that.

            1.3.7.3.1.1.2. Con:
            Objective or subjective, my value of truth does not depend on anyone else's approval or validation to be legitimate and rational.

            1.3.7.3.1.1.3. Con:
            Truth has objective value due to it's nature of being able to predict future events.

          1.3.7.3.1.2. Pro:
          The discussion of whether God exists is not a question of the effectiveness practicality of the belief, but of the truth of the belief. Therefore, the discussion itself only has value if truth has value.

            1.3.7.3.1.2.1. Con:
            This discussion will be taking place whether it has value or not, so whether there is value to this discussion is not relevant to the discussion.

          1.3.7.3.1.3. Con:
          The objective value of truth can come from the utilitarian point of view (truth is valuable because it helps us in achieving our everyday goals).

            1.3.7.3.1.3.1. Pro:
            Science is inherently valuable because it works, and science needs to stick to truth in order to work.

            1.3.7.3.1.3.2. Con:
            Utility is a subjective property; a truth's utility to me is not the same as its utility to you. As such, the value of truth this provides is a subjective one.

          1.3.7.3.1.4. Con:
          If the value attributed to something were only subjective rather than objective, that's not an argument that the value is zero.

          1.3.7.3.1.5. Con:
          An alternative to divine truth could be consensus truth which would prevent this discussion from being rendered moot.

        1.3.7.3.2. Con:
        Being created for a purpose is not warrant for the goodness of that purpose. Building a gun to kill does not make it a moral good for that gun to fire and kill even though it does what it was designed to do.

          1.3.7.3.2.1. Con:
          A gun being built for murder and then being successfully used for murder does make it a correctly functioning, and therefore "good", gun, even if it doesn't justify the moral actions taken by the human using it.

          1.3.7.3.2.2. Con:
          Under this theory, manmade purpose isn't identical to morality, only Godmade purpose is.

        1.3.7.3.3. Con:
        Objective values do not exist. Human beings are the likely creators of values, including so-called "objective" values.

          1.3.7.3.3.1. Pro:
          In Plato's Socratic Dialogue "Euthyphro," it is proved that morality cannot feasibly come from a god, and thus must either exist or not independently.

            1.3.7.3.3.1.1. Pro:
            If God commands actions because they are right, then morality isn't grounded in God but instead supersedes him. But if actions are right because God commands them, then morality is arbitrary, as it is subject to the whims of God.

              1.3.7.3.3.1.1.1. Con:
              A thing is good because it's found in God's nature, and God wills things that are in his nature.

              1.3.7.3.3.1.1.2. Pro:
              According to the Euthyphro dilemma, something must be a part of God's nature because that thing is good, or that something must be good because it's part of God's nature. However, in the first case, the goodness derives from something outside of God's nature, and in the second case, God's nature becomes arbitrary.

              1.3.7.3.3.1.1.3. Con:
              God is unchanging, so morality is not subject to his whims.

                1.3.7.3.3.1.1.3.1. Pro:
                God never changed his nature to be what it is now. Instead, God has always had his current nature and has simply never changed it. Thus, his nature has not been shaped by his whims and is not subject to his whims.

                  1.3.7.3.3.1.1.3.1.1. Con:
                  It doesn't follow from the fact that he didn't change his nature that his nature is not subject to his whims.

              1.3.7.3.3.1.1.4. Pro:
              To determine that God is perfectly moral, an objective yardstick is necessary. However, the existence of such a yardstick would mean that God is not the measure of morality.

            1.3.7.3.3.1.2. Con:
            Euthyphro's dilemma could be applied to any objective moral standard: one could question why a moral standard is considered one in an infinite regress.

              1.3.7.3.3.1.2.1. Con:
              Euthyphro's dilemma [643] applies solely to divine morality, because it is premised on his supposed holy nature and perfect morality.

                1.3.7.3.3.1.2.1.1. Pro:
                The argument would not work with socially constructed morality: "Is an action wrong because society forbids it or does society forbit it because it is wrong?"

              1.3.7.3.3.1.2.2. Con:
              God is not the only source of objective morality. Instead, it can be defined based on outcomes, namely, the harm or benefits created for oneself or others.

          1.3.7.3.3.2. Pro:
          The mere existence of theories about morality does not imply their correctness and applicability to the real world. Although these views might govern the morality of a possible world, they do not account for morality in the real one.

            1.3.7.3.3.2.1. Pro:
            Moral theories do not make a claim to the authority of their ideas of morality. They merely suggest possible explanations to behavior that is already observable and operative in the world.

          1.3.7.3.3.3. Pro:
          The basis of most religions is morality, yet many different sects have completely different points of view (e.g. on abortion, LGBT people) and different systems (e.g. in Catholicism the current pope decides the church's views). The "geography" is an integral part of the journey. Some Catholics believe even non-Catholic Christians are destined for hell.

          1.3.7.3.3.4. Con:
          “The human mind has no more power of inventing a new value than of imagining a new primary colour, or, indeed, of creating a new sun and a new sky for it to move in.” C.S. Lewis - The Abolition of Man [644]

        1.3.7.3.4. Pro:
        C.S. Lewis [645] reasoned from universal experience that universal law exists and thus, a universal law-giver exists.

          1.3.7.3.4.1. Pro:
          The epistemology employed to perceive and prove the existence of moral truths (self-evidence [646]) can, with the same validity and epistemic strength, be applied to God's existence.

            1.3.7.3.4.1.1. Con:
            Absolute moral truths do not exist; therefore, their proof cannot be applied to God's existence.

              1.3.7.3.4.1.1.1. Pro:
              The morality of humans has changed over time and is dependent on their location, thus suggesting that morality is subjective.

            1.3.7.3.4.1.2. Con:
            Even if a proof of moral truths exists, that doesn't mean that that proof can be applied to the existence of God (with the same validity and epistemic strength).

              1.3.7.3.4.1.2.1. Pro:
              If they exist, moral truths and the existence of God would be in different categories of things. Moral truths are qualities of actions, whereas God is an entity.

          1.3.7.3.4.2. Pro:
          If moral judgments have objective truth, there must exist an objective standard or anchor point to justify them. Such a morally perfect, objective, rational, and transcendent anchor would be considered God.

            1.3.7.3.4.2.1. Pro:
            In order for morality to be objective, its source must also be objective and unchanging. The universe and all of its contents are contingent and therefore not objective. So it follows that the moral law-giver must be transcendent and necessary.

              1.3.7.3.4.2.1.1. Con:
              If morality is objective, it may exist without a source; there is no need to postulate another separate thing which is in turn sourceless, and call that separate thing God.

            1.3.7.3.4.2.2. Pro:
            The discipline of ethics shows that morality is considered a rational pursuit, that is that it requires reason to reach certain ethical conclusions. As such, any embodiment of the moral standard must also be rational.

              1.3.7.3.4.2.2.1. Con:
              Morality does not require embodiment, it just requires minds capable of reason to idealize those moral principles - thus it requires humans, not God.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.2.1.1. Pro:
                Humans often idealize behaviors, situations and objects and those idealizations don't need to (and perhaps never actually do) correspond to reality.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.2.1.2. Pro:
                The idea of a line does not correspond to anything real, it is an idealization based of real characteristics which are not a [perfect] embodiment of the concept of line.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.2.1.2.1. Con:
                  An abstract line doesn't measure anything. It has no dimension of width or height. It's length is infinite, so it is rather useless as a standard of measurement.
                  
                  A meter stick on the other hand is useful precisely because it idealizes a standard for measurement.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.2.1.3. Con:
                Morality is not a list of rules, but a standard of behavior. Any standard is something by which another thing is measured, and must idealize the quality which it measures. Therefore, the moral standard must idealize the quality of behavior, which implies a person.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.2.1.3.1. Pro:
                  You cannot measure the length of a line using color; rather, the standard used must have length. "24 blue" is meaningless with regard to the length of a line. "24 inches" is meaningful because the standard idealizes the quality of length.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.2.1.3.2. Pro:
                  A standard is not subject to being measured in terms of the quality that it idealizes. In other words, it is illogical to say a meter is not a meter. It must be a meter by definiton.

            1.3.7.3.4.2.3. Con:
            If God is morality, then God can not choose what they do, as they must always do what is moral, and thus lacks libertarian free will, and is merely a force of the universe. As such, God would not be omnipotent, as they could not choose.

              1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1. Con:
              According to compatibilism [647], an individual will freely choose according to his nature. Therefore, given the same situation and the same individual, the same free choice will be made in all cases.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.1. Con:
                Compatibility would argue that a rock has free will, as it is not put under distress to make a choice, and that clocks choose what time they tell. Rocks do not have free will, and clocks do not make choices.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.1.1. Con:
                  Free will is a quality only found in minds.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.1.1.1. Con:
                    By either definition of free will, it is conceivable that it would cover true artificial intelligence if such a thing would be developed, as an AI is not a mind in the traditional sense, a mind is not a necessary condition of free will.

                      1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.1.1.1.1. Con:
                      If such AI should be developed with all of a mind's faculties and fully independent it could be argued that it has a mind in the "traditional" sense.

                        1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con:
                        It could be argued that an AI is just an over-complicated clock. Where the mind necessary for soft deterministic  free will begins is still a valid question.

                      1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.1.1.1.2. Con:
                      No such AI has been developed yet. Until then, it is not safe to affirm that AI's can have a mind.

                        1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.1.1.1.2.1. Con:
                        We can conceptualize such an AI, which is sufficient to judge whether or not they would be able to have free will.

                          1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.1.1.1.2.1.1. Con:
                          If it is conceivable, you should be able to conceive of it.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.1.1.2. Pro:
                    In order to be able to make a choice, one must have certain qualities (such as consciousness, perception, thinking, judgement) essential to a mind.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.2. Con:
                Compatibilism exists as a response to incompatibility, and answers the dilemma posed to them by changing the definitions of choice and free will.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.3. Pro:
                Free will [648] is the ability to choose between different possible courses of action unimpeded.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.3.1. Con:
                  Harry Frankfurt has shown that alternate possible choices are not required for free will [649].

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.3.1.1. Con:
                    The Harry Frankfurt argument does not move to show that alternate possible choices are not required for free will, but instead argues that responsibility is not dependent upon choice. "The best definition, by his reckoning, is this: "[A] person is not morally responsible for what he has done if he did it only because he could not have done otherwise."" [650]

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.3.2. Pro:
                  The definition of free will doesn't include the need for different outcomes for the same individual in the same situation.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.3.3. Con:
                  This view of  soft determinists, as coined by William James is nothing but an effort to create a quagmire of evasion by stealing the name freedom to mask it's underlying determinism. As Immanuel Kant pointed out, it is nothing more then wretched subterfuge, and jugglery. It is nothing more then attempting to treat the disease by masking, not treating, masking, the symptom.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.3.3.1. Con:
                    Determinism isn't "underlying" in compatibilism, it is both admited and thought to be compatible with free will the way it has been defined.

                      1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.3.3.1.1. Con:
                      The way it has been defined by compatablists, which is the way nobody else defines it. Pop-Culture is littered with examples of people exploring the concept of libertarian free will, or as they call it, free will, and how it conflicts with determinism, but nobody but compatablists uses the definition compatablitisest use.`

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.3.4. Pro:
                  Even under circumstances where people feel they do not have a choice, they do. A person with a metaphorical gun to their head, for example, can still choose to give in to the demand, fight their opponent, or do nothing.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.4. Con:
                As Ted Honderich explained, the mistake of the compatibilistic definition of free will is to assert that nothing changes as a consequence of determinism, when clearly we have lost the life-hope of origination.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5. Pro:
                Compatibilism holds that for every individual there exists a hypothetical exhaustive list of preferences and tendencies called their nature, which is not freely determined by the individual (and thus determined by something external to them). That individual then wills (makes decisions) in a way that predictably follows from their nature and other data, and acts in a way that predictably follows from their will. It's unclear how any change to this formula would make an individual more free.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.1. Pro:
                  The ability to contradict or determine one's own nature is what Plantinga calls maximal autonomy [651] - something that is both distinct from free will and, as he argues, logically impossible, since it would require an infinite regress.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.1.1. Pro:
                    No mainstream philosopher - compatibilist or incompatibilist, determinist or indeterminist - argues that humans have maximal autonomy.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.2. Pro:
                  The existence of a "nature" as a hypothetical exhaustive list of what an individual will choose is tautological - since any free individual makes choices, there must be a source for those choices. That source, whatever its properties, is what is called their nature.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.2.1. Pro:
                    Importantly, an individual's nature doesn't need to be predictable, unchanging, known by the individual themself or anyone else, or even knowable in order for compatibilism to be valid. It only needs to exist in each moment and be the source of their preferences and tendencies.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.2.2. Con:
                    If every source that could influence choice making is covered under the umbrella of nature, the parts that change one's nature must also fall under this umbrella, this doesn't resolve the problem of free will, it just moves it down from a discussion about the mind to one of nature.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.3. Pro:
                  It's trivial that a hindrance to an individual's ability to act based on their will constitutes a reduction in freedom. If someone decides to do something, but is then restricted from doing so, then they are clearly not free. So being able to act predictably following one's choices is required for free will.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.3.1. Con:
                    The entire argument of composition, besides semantics they invented to support their point, seems to be that if someone doesn't make a choice they were per-programed to make, they are not free. The ultimate, and certain example of soft-deterministic free will is not man, but the computer it so perfectly manipulate to serve it's needs, yet it is quite obvious that a human is freer then a computer.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.3.2. Con:
                    An unpredictable being is uncontrollable, slight hindrances to the easiest route to a goal are nothing compared to avoiding being turned into a mindless gear in a machine.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4. Pro:
                  It's unclear how an individual would be freer if their choices didn't predictably follow from their preferences and tendencies (nature), combined with their thoughts, judgments, observations, physiological needs and desires, mood, etc.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.1. Con:
                    Consider the instance when a entity without libertarian free will has weighed two options as equally good, and there is urgency, meaning there is no time for further evaluation, without the ability to make decisions outside of their evaluation, they would freeze, and take the usually worse default action, as we can make these decisions, we know libertarian free will exists.

                      1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.1.1. Pro:
                      The lollipop shown here [652] are all of 4 distinct, types, but otherwise entirely identical, and it's set up so that to get a lollipop, one must choose which lollipop they want, but the display doesn't cause confusion, people don't scan the lollipops for defects until they have ruled out all but one, they just make a decision, and go.

                        1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.1.1.1. Con:
                        Even when choosing to pick blindly, the individual is choosing according to his nature given the external conditions.

                      1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.1.2. Pro:
                      We can see the ability to make decisions quickly as opposed to rationally that we developed to avoid dangerous indecision in the form of bad decisions, like impulse buys.

                        1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.1.2.1. Con:
                        An individual being limited to choose either quickly or rationally is a false dichotomy. Many factors influence a decision (availability of time, emotions, personal preferences, affordability) even if the individual isn't concious of them at the moment.

                          1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.1.2.1.1. Con:
                          An individual being limited to choose either quickly or rationally is only a false dichotomy when there is more then a split second to make a decision. Many, many events exist in which this is not the case, and our mind must be able to cope with them.

                            1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.1.2.1.1.1. Pro:
                            In these situations, there is not enough time to even subconsciously evaluate how much time there is to make a decision, but there might be time to review it if it is not a pressing matter.

                      1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.1.3. Con:
                      The only thing that changes in the decision making process by adding urgency is the given conditions. The individual still chooses according to his nature given the external conditions (either by taking any of the options or doing nothing).

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.2. Pro:
                    Any obstruction in the predictability of their choices (given all the relevant data) would have to result from some external source (randomness, demonic possession, etc), not determined by the individual's will. The interjection of such an external source, though making the individual less predictable, would arguably make them even less free, since it would restrict their ability to choose according to their nature.

                      1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.2.1. Con:
                      Saying that if a person makes a choice, it must be do to an external source, therefore they can't make a choice, therefore making a choice is impossible, is circular reasoning.

                        1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.2.1.1. Con:
                        Librarian free will isn't librarian free will, in fact, libertarian free will directly contradicts the idea of libertarian free will, because the only possible way it could exist is if it somehow impeded part of the decision making process.

                      1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.2.2. Con:
                      Under compatablism, the individual is nothing but an equation with the outside world as variable, every action is determined solely by external forces.

                        1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.2.2.1. Con:
                        In compatibilism the individual's actions are determined by his own (internal) nature deciding upon the external conditions given.

                          1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.2.2.1.1. Con:
                          Under compatablisum, what that nature is has been predecided. At best, it ignores the past, and calls the present free will.

                            1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.2.2.1.1.1. Pro:
                            this is a violation of the definition of free will, which requires the decision making process to be made at least partly without the influence of past experiences.

                      1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.2.3. Con:
                      Under the branching model of the universe, every time there is an opportunity for a declension, the individual makes all decisions available to them, each choice making a new universe where the events are carried out, no external force needed.

                        1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.2.3.1. Con:
                        In each hypothetic universe the same individual would only make one decision according to his nature in that universe. In other alternative universes that same individual's nature would be different and he would act acordingly in each one.

                          1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.2.3.1.1. Con:
                          Until the decision is made, the other universes do not exist. There is only one universe going in, only one 'nature' going in, but many going out.

                      1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.2.4. Con:
                      The person is the 'external force', they 'posses' themselves.

                        1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.2.4.1. Con:
                        A person can not act externally over oneself, the closest to that would be a crazy person (who by its own influence loses control over its own actions).

                        1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.2.4.2. Pro:
                        One example of how it might be that one is external to themselves is mind-body dualism [653].

                          1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.2.4.2.1. Con:
                          Mind-body dualism doesn't claim that one's will acts externally over one's decision.

                        1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.2.4.3. Pro:
                        The 'external force' is not an external force, people are capable of libertarian free will.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.4.3. Con:
                    People's choices do very often follow from their preferences and tendencies, combined with their thought, judgments, observations, physiological needs, desires, mood. etc. but many times that only narrows it down to a set of options from which a choice must be made.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.5. Con:
                  Compatibilism ignores the "make" part of "makes decisions", or 'makes choices', if these things were indeed made, then they did not exist before.

              1.3.7.3.4.2.3.2. Pro:
              Since God under this definition would be just a force, we could manipulate, and control them, as we do other forces, which would ultimately make us more powerful then God. If we can be more powerful then God, God can not be Omnipotent.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.3.2.1. Con:
                We don't manipulate or control other forces. For instance, we can only predict and work around gravity and electromagnetism, not change them - this kind of utilization certainly doesn't imply that we are more powerful than these forces.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.2.1.1. Con:
                  We can defy gravity, or harness it for our own ends, gravity in turn can only gravitate. We are thus the stronger.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.3.2.2. Con:
                Not all things that lack libertarian free will are "just a force".

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.2.2.1. Pro:
                  Even if He lacked libertarian free will, God could still think and act (even if those thoughts and actions are determined), making Him a conscious agent and thus greater than a force.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.2.2.1.1. Con:
                    If those thoughts and actions are all predetermined, then God would still be nothing more then an animal, or a machine.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.2.2.2. Con:
                  Not all things that lack libertarian free will are forces, some are objects, animals, or machines, but as morality is an ever-present entity that acts upon us, it, and thus, under this definition, God, must be a force.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.3.2.3. Con:
                In order to utilize God in a way similar to electromagnetism He would have to be completely predictable in His actions. Lacking libertarian free will doesn't mean that something is completely predictable.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.2.3.1. Pro:
                  Basic quantum events are fundamentally unpredictable, but don't have "free will" in any meaningful sense.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.2.3.2. Pro:

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.2.3.3. Con:

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.2.3.3.1. Pro:

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.2.3.4. Con:
                  A force doesn't have to be perfectly understood to be utilized, we were using compasses many years before we discovered electromagnetism.

              1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3. Con:
              If God's nature is identical to morality, then God is not compelled by anything, since morality is simply defined as "what God would do." Under this definition, the statement is "God must always do what God would do," which isn't a compulsion, only a tautology.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1. Pro:
                An inability to contradict His own nature does not count against God's omnipotence.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.1. Pro:
                  Free will only implies the freedom for one to act based on one's nature, not the freedom to defy one's nature. If perfect morality is a part of God's nature, it isn't necessary or expected that He be able to defy it.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.1.1. Pro:

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.1.2. Con:

                      1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.1.2.1. Con:
                      The legal interpretation of free will is irrelevant.

                        1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.1.2.1.1. Con:
                        the legal definition is tied to the actual definition, what one says tells us a lot about what the other says.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.1.3. Con:
                    People have the ability to do things they were not instructed to do, or even instructed not to do, and that are against their best interest, not due to a mistake, not even only for a greater good, but simply because they can, an action that would require them to have libertarian free will. When we read the story of Pandora's box, or Eve, and the Apple, we don't blink an eye, we empathise with the characters, because it's not outlandish, it is the natural result of a creature with free will.

                      1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.1.3.1. Con:

                        1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.1.3.1.1. Con:

                      1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.1.3.2. Con:

                        1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.1.3.2.1. Con:
                        Everyone makes mistakes, so even with only one example, the evidence is in favor of the necessity.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.1.4. Con:
                    Following commands is not free will, one's nature is a form of command, therefore, if one is only capable of following their nature, they do not have free will.

                      1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.1.4.1. Con:
                      Free will is only contradicted when one is constrained by commands external to oneself. One's nature is internal to oneself, and so following it doesn't contradict free will.

                        1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.1.4.1.1. Pro:
                        My brain commands my fingers to move to type this sentence. I wouldn't be more free if my fingers didn't follow that command - in fact, I'd be less free, since I wouldn't be able to act upon my will.

                          1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.1.4.1.1.1. Con:
                          If one's finger acted independent of oneself, they would be less free, but their finger would be more free, the part is not the whole, and the whole is not the part.

                        1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.1.4.1.2. Con:
                        A computer holds many commands within itself, and acts upon the majority of them without external stimulation of any kind, but a computer does not have free will. As such, even internal commands are not sufficient to define free will.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.2. Pro:
                  Freedom to defy one's nature is logically impossible, because an agent's decisions are necessarily the result of its nature. An agent couldn't decide to defy their nature, because that decision would itself be following its nature.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.2.1. Con:

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.1. Con:

                      1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.1.1. Con:

                          1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.1.1.1.1. Con:

It has previously been assumed that a nature could not change itself, and that to do so it would have to defy itself. Since the nature would, under these assumptions, defy itself, free will must exist.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2. Con:

Since the universe is not God, something we've assumed must be wrong, omnipotence without agency, or God being morality seem like the most likely candidates.

                              1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.2.2. Con:

                              1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.2.3. Con:
                              Job 33:4, it states: “The spirit of God has made me, and the breath of the Almighty gives me life.”, so the soul is not imparted until the first breath.

                              1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.2.4. Con:
                              As the giver of life, God has the prerogative to take life at any time in whatever manner he chooses.

                            1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.3. Pro:
                            An omni-benevolent, omniscient and omnipotent God would never have created a world where more than six million Jews had to die in the Holocaust.

                              1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.3.1. Con:
                              It is equally likely that God created a world where people had free will, even if they used it foolishly or for evil. The alternative would be God forcing all people to do good.

                              1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.3.2. Con:
                              Even were that true, the fact that God is not omni-benevolent does not imply that He does not exist. Many of the Gods worshipped throughout history have not been omni-benevolent.

                              1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.3.3. Con:
                              He would if he felt that mankind should be allowed to have free will.

                              1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.3.4. Con:

                            1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.4. Pro:
                            An omni-benevolent, omniscient and omnipotent God would never have created a world where terrible things like famine and starvation exist.

                              1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.4.1. Con:
                              This not a problem if reincarnation exists because God could be giving lessons and karma to souls who have done bad things in previous lives. In other words, they are not only little babies if they are souls starting off again in a new body.

                                1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.4.1.1. Pro:
                                Many religious traditions believe in reincarnation, and if they don't they often used to at some point in their development, or had off shoots that did.

                                1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.4.1.2. Pro:
                                That suffering baby could be little baby Hitler.

                                1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.4.1.3. Con:
                                This explanation abrogates free will; if a child is born into a family situation where he will be abused as punishment for sins in a past life, then his abuser cannot have free will in choosing whether to abuse him.

                                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.4.1.3.1. Con:
                                  The above premise is false. It's possible for God to arrange for someone who chooses to do something bad to someone to do it to someone who has that coming as part of their karma.

                                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.4.1.3.1.1. Pro:
                                    It's also possible for someone who wants to do something bad to someone to be unable to do it. This is consistent with the theological view that God only allows those to be abused who have some lesson to learn from the experience.

                                1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.4.1.4. Con:
                                This explanation implies God uses cruel and unusual punishment that would be regarded as an unconscionable atrocity if it were carried out by a human justice system, and so is itself an argument against a God with the three "omnis".

                                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.4.1.4.1. Con:
                                  On a theoretical level the above claim can be defeated because it's theoretically possible that a greater evil would be done by not allowing evil. On a practical level what this translates to is the fact that sometimes pain and suffering are necessary in order to prevent a greater evil.

                                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.4.1.4.1.1. Pro:
                                    An example of this is going to the dentist. The dentist causes some pain in order to prevent a worse pain.

                                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.4.1.4.1.2. Pro:
                                    Unfortunately many people don't take good instruction, don't learn from the mistakes of others, and often, even when faced with negative consequences, don't learn the folly of their ways. For such stubborn and slow learners pain and suffering can be the only thing that will teach them.

                                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.4.1.4.1.3. Pro:
                                    If a person causes suffering to others what they need to learn is compassion. Compassion is best learned through first hand suffering. Think of suffering in a future birth for actions committed now like cosmic sensitivity training.

                                1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.4.1.5. Pro:
                                -> See 1.1.10.4.6.8.

                              1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.4.2. Con:
                              In a sense, none of the Abrahamic religions say that that world is the one God created: human evil was a later edit on God's creative work.

                              1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.4.3. Con:
                              -> See 1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.1.6.

                            1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.5. Pro:
                            An omni-benevolent, omniscient and omnipotent God would never have created a world where billions of people never had the chance to follow his religion and ascend to heaven, because they lived in places that had never heard of the Bible.

                              1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.5.1. Con:
                              It is entirely possible that he has some other plan that will allow them to learn of it and accept it or not.

                            1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.6. Con:
                            We are not in a position to know whether particular sufferings are unnecessary from God's position because our understanding of the vast web of causes and effects of particular sufferings could not reasonably be expected to measure up to an all-knowing God's.

                            1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.7. Con:
                            The idea that the greatest good is the reduction or complete avoidance of suffering is not true. There are greater goods that result from suffering. For example: perseverance and sacrifice.

                              1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.7.1. Con:
                              Neither perseverance, nor sacrifice are valuable on their own, they are only valuable as a means to an end. For example, we don't go around killing ourselves to promote the good of sacrifice, nor do we compel other too.

                          1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.4. Con:
                          Power and ability are related, and therefore an unlimited amount of power means an unlimited ability.

                          1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.5. Con:
                          Quoting from the cited link, "The term omnipotent has been used to connote a number of different positions. These positions include, but are not limited to, the following:
                          
                          1. A deity is able to do anything that it chooses to do."

                          1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.6. Pro:
                          One might consider logically incompatible actions such as creating a round square, or a married bachelor, as not within the grasp of omnipotence, as they by definition cannot exist.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.3. Con:
                    -> See 1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.1.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.4. Pro:
                  -> See 1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.5. Pro:
                  -> See 1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.1.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.6. Con:
                  To contradict one's nature is an ability. The lack of such an ability is a limit to God's power and therefore 'counts against' His omnipotence.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.2. Con:
                If whatever God does is arbitrary defined as good, then morality is arbitrary, and no more valid then what any random individual does as the bases of what should be done.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.2.1. Con:
                  Good is not defined as what God does, it's defined as what's in his nature. This makes the concept of good an objective and unchanging fact about the universe and therefore no more arbitrary than phenomena like gravity and magnetism.

            1.3.7.3.4.2.4. Con:
            Rationality itself is a sufficient source.

              1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1. Con:
              Without values, rationality only deals with what is, whereas morality is concerned with what ought to be/what we ought to do. It isn't possible to derive "ought"s solely from "is"s [660].

                1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.1. Con:
                Economics, as an example, derives what ought to be from what is valuable, and possible.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.1.1. Con:
                  Economics relies on a presupposition of value (i.e. that property is valuable). The field itself is concerned only with how best to obtain what is already considered to be valuable, but doesn't compel anyone to give personal value to property.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.1.1.1. Pro:
                    Some people - such as hermits and communists - don't consider property valuable nearly as much as others.

                      1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.1.1.1.1. Con:
                      All people consider property valuable, even hermits, and communists. Those groups just treat possession differently.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.1.1.2. Con:
                    Economics only requires that each individual actor values something, not necessarily that they want the same things, or value them the same.

                      1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.1.1.2.1. Con:
                      This is still a presupposition of value, so economics fails to produce "ought"s with no presupposed "ought"s.

                        1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.1.1.2.1.1. Con:
                        Value to the individual is objective, they have a need, and the thing they want can sait it, so this is an is, not an ought.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.2. Con:
                Natural law [661], is a body of philosophical thought that derives ought (at least partially) from is.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.2.1. Con:
                  Secular conceptions of natural law still rely on axiomatic "ought"s - for instance, that one ought not infringe upon another's rights.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.2.2. Con:
                  All ethics is concerned partially with what is, using "is"s to supplement "ought"s and create more specific or complex "ought"s. However, no ethical system can successfully reach "ought"s without at least one axiomatic "ought" as its basis.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.2.3. Con:
                  Natural law is a system that has done great harm to the world, by hindering the rights of women, trans individuals, and homosexuals, repeatedly come up with false conclusions, like that women should have no autonomy, and homosexuals are evil, and that is based on a false interpretation of reality, that things were created with a purpose instead of evolving to adapt to the world. It is by every measure a failed moral system. As such, it has not derived real 'oughts'.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.3. Con:
                Morality was observed before it was studied, those observations are the 'is's upon which we built our systems.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.3.1. Con:
                  In order for morality to be observed it must first exist. If morality exists, then the moral arguments hold true.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.3.1.1. Con:
                    The existence of morality is only one of the assumptions needed for the moral arguments to prove the existence of God.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.3.2. Con:
                  Morality being built upon prior observations of morality is circular - it attempts to ground morality in itself.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.3.2.1. Con:
                    It was seen, then it was studied, once we saw it, we moved to figure out why it was, and how it worked, the observations are the starting point of moral studies, but not the bases of it.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.3.3. Con:
                  Observations of what humans think is moral do not indicate that those humans are correct. The fact that I think it is good to exercise does not prove that it is good to exercise.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.3.3.1. Con:
                    If morality was not observable, we would not know that it exists.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.3.3.2. Con:
                    That morality exists, and that morality should be followed are two different statements.

                      1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.3.3.2.1. Con:
                      Definitionally, morality is the means to determine what one should do. The statement "one should follow morality" is equivalent to "one should do what one should do," which is tautologically true.

                        1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.3.3.2.1.1. Con:
                        morality: a doctrine or system of moral conduct [662]
                        moral: sanctioned by or operative on one's conscience or ethical judgment [663]
                        neither of these definitions disallow someone from acting upon another measure of what they should do, like their
                        self interest [664], or says that doing one is preferable to the other.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.3.4. Con:
                  If morality is based solely upon an observation of what humans think is moral, then moral progress would be impossible, because it would never be possible to be more moral than what is currently thought to be moral.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.3.4.1. Con:
                    We started with what we saw of morality, and built upon it, just as we did with science, learning it's in's and outs, and applying them to the world around us.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.4. Con:
                we have to use rationality to determine what values deserve to be valued.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.5. Con:
                Selection (via survival) favors a kind of morality that indicates as prescriptive certain norms and values: a moral framework which exists independently of any deities and independently of whether survival is right or not outside of said framework.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.5.1. Con:
                  Selection via survival cannot ground several of our strongly held moral principles.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.5.1.1. Pro:
                    Selection via survival cannot explain why punching someone in the face for fun, even if the damage isn't severe enough to impact someone's survival, is wrong.

                    1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.5.1.2. Pro:
                    Selection via survival cannot explain why celibacy is more permissible than murdering an infant. Both result in an individual not contributing offspring to the population, which has a negative effect on the reproductive fitness of the population.

            1.3.7.3.4.2.5. Con:
            God cannot ground objective moral values.

              1.3.7.3.4.2.5.1. Pro:
              There is no logical reason why being created in the image of God means that humans are objectively valuable (infidels.org [665]).

                1.3.7.3.4.2.5.1.1. Con:
                If God is the basis of objective morality, then it follows that God himself is objectively valuable. This means that any attempt to harm anything of God's, including his image, is wrong.

              1.3.7.3.4.2.5.2. Pro:
              There is no logical reason why God's commands/nature, not the commands/nature of other beings, ground objective moral values. (infidels.org [666])

                1.3.7.3.4.2.5.2.1. Con:
                God alone has the omniscience necessary to truly know what is best for his creation, unlike other beings.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.5.2.2. Con:
                Unlike other beings, God created the universe and its inhabitants with a purpose in mind, and this purpose was to do things in accordance with his nature.

            1.3.7.3.4.2.6. Con:
            Morality can't be transcendent, because different moral standards apply to different historical contexts.

              1.3.7.3.4.2.6.1. Con:
              Different moral standards did not apply to different historical contexts. Even in the historical context of the 1850s where slavery was legal and widely accepted in the U.S., we are just as right in condemning slaveholders then as we are in condemning modern traffickers.

              1.3.7.3.4.2.6.2. Pro:
              Cultural relativism states that the morality of an action is determined solely by its cultural contexts.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.6.2.1. Pro:
                Cultural relativism is supported by moral disagreements between cultures.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.6.2.2. Con:
                The divide between cultures isn't clear or well-defined - as such, the decision of which cultural moral framework to follow would depend on overarching moral rules. Those rules separating cultures cannot themselves be decided by any culture, and as such, at least some objective moral standards would have to exist.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.6.2.3. Con:
                Under cultural relativism, the moral framework decided on by a culture is true, and anyone who defies it is morally wrong. However, we agree that certain figures such as Martin Luther King acted morally, even though they defied the moral framework of the culture they lived in. This is known as the reformer's dilemma.

            1.3.7.3.4.2.7. Pro:
            All attempts to generalize morality into uncontroversial and exceptionless broad rules have failed to match perfectly with our moral judgments. As such it seems likely that morality isn't simplifiable or reducible to general axia, and requires situational judgments. Therefore, if morality is objective, it must be contained in an external mind able to make such judgments - and such a mind would be considered God.

              1.3.7.3.4.2.7.1. Con:
              "It is wrong to torture small children for fun" is an uncontroversial and exceptionless broad rule.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.7.1.1. Con:
                A utility monster [667] could torture small children for fun and be morally justified.

              1.3.7.3.4.2.7.2. Con:
              If morality had to match human moral judgements perfectly, then where humans disagree on ethical dilemmas, it would have to match both sides of the disagreement at once and thus be self-contradictory. Thus, if there is an objective morality, it does not conform to human moral judgements, and thus could be composed of exceptionless broad rules with no need to be contained in a mind.

              1.3.7.3.4.2.7.3. Con:
              -> See 1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.5.

              1.3.7.3.4.2.7.4. Con:
              Situational judgments are made by the minds of people, thus do not require God.

            1.3.7.3.4.2.8. Con:
            Morality may not have "objective truth." This is demonstrated by the fact that people differ in their moral values and decision-making.

              1.3.7.3.4.2.8.1. Con:
              Disagreement on an issue does not indicate that the reality of the subject is not objective.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.8.1.1. Pro:
                The claim that objective moral truths exist and that we can perceive these truths does not require or imply that our perceptions are perfect. Disagreements surrounding moral issues can be attributed to failings in perceptions of objective moral truths.

                  1.3.7.3.4.2.8.1.1.1. Pro:
                  People in different roles in society have different moral obligations. A doctor is expected to treat all patients equally, where as a police officer is expected to discriminate. A mother is commended for favoring her child while a school teacher would be condemned for showing favoritism upon the same child.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.8.1.2. Pro:
                Sometimes there may be more than one correct way to navigate moral dilemmas. For example, there are multiple ways to engineer a bridge which is stable and a similar thing can be true of moral issues.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.8.1.3. Pro:
                Moral issues are often complex being comprised of multiple differing principles all in tension with one another, therefore disagreement is to be expected as we struggle to come to mature understandings of how objective moral truths apply in specific cases.

            1.3.7.3.4.2.9. Con:
            If moral judgements have objective truth, that truth may stand independently, without need for "justification".

              1.3.7.3.4.2.9.1. Con:
              There are ontological implications for the existence of objective moral truth and they are fatal for naturalism because moral truths indicate a metaphysical layer of reality.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.9.1.1. Pro:
                The existence of a metaphysical reality and the subsequent death of makes the existence of God plausible.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.9.1.2. Pro:
                -> See 1.3.7.3.4.1.

                1.3.7.3.4.2.9.1.3. Con:
                Naturalism and monotheism are not the only metaphysical views, so if naturalism were false that would not necessarily mean God exists.

          1.3.7.3.4.3. Con:
          The moral argument for God's existence is not sound as it presupposes that objective moral laws exist, which has not been demonstrated.

            1.3.7.3.4.3.1. Pro:
            In a theistic universe, laws are conceived entirely in the mind of God, and hence subjective. The only difference between God's subjective morals and human's subjective morals is that God has enough power to enforce his morals.

              1.3.7.3.4.3.1.1. Con:
              The unchanging and universal moral law still suffices as an explanation for our experience, even if it is "subjective" from the perspective of God.

              1.3.7.3.4.3.1.2. Pro:
              At least some of our morals are rooted in some goal, or valuable concept of value.

                1.3.7.3.4.3.1.2.1. Con:
                Opposition to a subjective goal is not an example of objective evil. The existence of activity which opposes some person's subjective goals would in no way be considered evidence for the non-existence of a classical theistic god.

                  1.3.7.3.4.3.1.2.1.1. Con:
                  There is no evidence to support the assumption that all forms of morality that don't really on God are subjective.

              1.3.7.3.4.3.1.3. Con:
              Lewis does not argue that God creates the law by His divine subjectivity or His will to impose them. That view is associated with nominalism and theological voluntarism, which Lewis rejected in favor of something more like Christian Platonism.

              1.3.7.3.4.3.1.4. Con:
              The argument that God's morals are subjective does not apply if God is of a different kind and mind than us. If we are created rather than begotten (that is being of different, more limited kind merely resembling the creator) then our perception of available morality is not going to be equal to that of God.

                1.3.7.3.4.3.1.4.1. Con:
                If God's morality is beyond our comprehension, even given our imperfect being, we must act on the information that we have, because it is the only information we have, and thus what God wanted us to see. If we see God's morality as subjective, it is God's will that we interpret it as such.

                  1.3.7.3.4.3.1.4.1.1. Con:
                  It is circular reasoning to assume our limited view and reasoning is God's will for our understanding of eternal truth... but our limited view and reasoning is precisely what prevents us from fully understanding eternal truths.

                    1.3.7.3.4.3.1.4.1.1.1. Con:
                    It is tautological to assume we know God's will without basis. This can be remedied either by denying God's existence, or assuming he desires us to know what we do. A third option does not readily present itself, so we should assume what we can deduce.

                1.3.7.3.4.3.1.4.2. Con:
                Non sequitur; there is no more logical connection in "God is of a different kind and mind than us, therefore morality is objective" than in "the sky is blue, therefore the moon is made of stilton".

            1.3.7.3.4.3.2. Con:
            The existence of objective moral laws has been persuasively defended by many philosophers, including many philosophers who are not theists, such as Russ Shafer-Landau [668], David Enoch [669], and Michael Huemer. [670]

              1.3.7.3.4.3.2.1. Con:
              Just because some philosophers defend it doesn't mean it's true. This is an example of an appeal to authority [671].

            1.3.7.3.4.3.3. Con:
            In support of premise (1), Lewis [672] argues that we all have within us the sense of right behavior and character. There is a sense of “oughtness” that presses upon us. He says, “human beings, all over the earth, have this curious idea that they ought to behave in a certain way, and cannot really get rid of it.” [Mere Christianity p 8]

              1.3.7.3.4.3.3.1. Con:
              The moral laws that apply to human beings should not be confused with a "Universal" moral law, which applies to everything.

                1.3.7.3.4.3.3.1.1. Con:
                Applicability to everything is not necessary for a law to be objective. The statement "water is a liquid at room temperature and sea level pressure" is objectively true, despite only applying to water. This type of objective truth is all that is necessary for the moral argument.

              1.3.7.3.4.3.3.2. Con:
              -> See 1.3.7.3.4.1.1.1.

              1.3.7.3.4.3.3.3. Con:
              Human beings all over the earth have conflicting ideas of how they ought to behave.

          1.3.7.3.4.4. Pro:
          For a universal Moral Law to exist, there must be a sort of 'order' in the universe. This order in the universe is what dictates humans acting/reacting according to the universal Moral Law.

            1.3.7.3.4.4.1. Con:
            Widespread disagreement in morality undermines the idea of "universal morality".

              1.3.7.3.4.4.1.1. Con:
              Scientific disagreement is also everywhere, but it doesn't follow from this that the outside world doesn't follow reasonable rules, only that some people are mistaken about scientific truths.

                1.3.7.3.4.4.1.1.1. Con:
                Scientific claims rely on observable, verifiable evidence. Moralistic claims rely on relativistic interpretation based on cultural references. Science is objective, whereas morality is subjective. 
                
                The outside world, and its rules, are neither all reasonable nor all unreasonable. It's a mixture of both. Filled with both objective scientific truths and a mix of subjective and often conflicting moralities, it follows that people would have trouble reconciling scientific truths and their faith.

              1.3.7.3.4.4.1.2. Con:
              Universal morality isn't required for the moral argument to hold, only that moral statements have truth value and things are objectively right or wrong.

                1.3.7.3.4.4.1.2.1. Pro:
                Morality can have objective truth even if no one knows of it.

              1.3.7.3.4.4.1.3. Con:
              Disagreement in morality often derives from factual, not moral, error. For instance, witch hunts were once conducted on the erroneous assumption that witches could have negative effects on others and the world.

                1.3.7.3.4.4.1.3.1. Con:
                While this is true, ethical disagreements can be elicited with thought experiments in which the facts of the matter are given and not in dispute, so factual disagreement can only explain some specific cases, and not moral disagreement on the whole.

                1.3.7.3.4.4.1.3.2. Con:
                The contentious issue of abortion demonstrates disagreement in morality.

                  1.3.7.3.4.4.1.3.2.1. Con:
                  The debate of the morality of abortion largely comes down to two points, one of which is clearly a factual disagreement, rather than a moral disagreement;
                  
                  Is the fetus meaningfully alive?
                  
                  The other question, which is debatable less factual in nature, is if a pregnant individuals right to choose outweighs the right to life the fetus may, or may not have, but many people consider rights to be based in facts.

              1.3.7.3.4.4.1.4. Con:
              Most general statements on morality are universally agreed upon by healthy societies. For instance, unjust murder is always considered to be wrong, as is thievery. Even if morality on the whole isn't universal, these general statements still require explanation.

              1.3.7.3.4.4.1.5. Con:
              The fact that people might disagree on a further point does not mean that originally they felt the same inclination towards agreeing with (or disagreeing with) that moral point. It could be (and probably often is) that a person convinces himself/herself of a particular moral point, thereby then finding evidential arguments for their holding of that moral view.

              1.3.7.3.4.4.1.6. Con:
              Disagreement about a concept "A" is warranted if and only if A actually exists. Therefore moral disagreement supports, rather than undermines, the idea that objective morality exists.

                1.3.7.3.4.4.1.6.1. Con:
                That disagreement exists does not mean the disagreement is actually warranted; two people arguing over "A" need only believe that A exists, and they may be wrong.

                1.3.7.3.4.4.1.6.2. Con:
                If each individual has a subjective morality, but that morality has rules for what other people should do, then disagreement when those perspectives clash is entirely warranted regardless of whether any such perspective has "objective" value.

              1.3.7.3.4.4.1.7. Con:
              Morality must be something more than shared human opinion. In 18th-19th century America, slavery was widely considered to be moral. If morality is merely "shared relative morals", then in their time and place, slavery was in fact, moral. Yet we rightly consider those persons today to have been morally flawed, even in their own time.

                1.3.7.3.4.4.1.7.1. Con:
                That we consider people in the past who endorsed slavery to have been wrong is itself nothing more than shared human opinion. That our opinions are not overruled by the opinions of long-dead others who we see as moral monsters, is no evidence that our opinions are more than opinions.

            1.3.7.3.4.4.2. Con:
            All morality is necessarily situational. Take a moral pronouncement and you can derive both a situation where it's justified and an alternate situation where it is not.

              1.3.7.3.4.4.2.1. Con:
              If there isn't a standard morality, then there is no need to qualify any action by its context, because all things would be acceptable across all situations.

              1.3.7.3.4.4.2.2. Con:
