              Most theists reject moral absolutism (that certain actions are always either right or wrong, regardless of situation). Moral realism (that the rightness or wrongness of any given, situational action is objective) still holds, and is all that is needed for the moral argument to work.

              1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3. Pro:
              Moral relativism [673] holds that actions can be considered right or wrong, but that such a judgment depends on the context.

                1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.1. Pro:
                Agent relativism states that the morality of an action can only be determined by the person taking the action.

                  1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.1.1. Con:
                  Agent relativism isn't intuitive or held by many people - for instance, we still consider a murderer to be wrong, even if the murderer thinks that murder is morally permissible or even obligatory.

                    1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.1.1.1. Pro:
                    Under agent relativism, since moral framework is decided entirely by the individual, individuals should be able to change their moral framework whenever it's convenient to them or they have a whim to. In reality, people don't treat morality as if it's subject to their own whims in such a way, and will deem it right to adhere to it even when it's inconvenient.

                  1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.1.2. Con:
                  Agent relativism isn't supported by the human instinct to justify one's own actions based on moral standards. For instance, if someone steals, they will attempt to justify the theft by saying that they need the stolen thing more than the original owner does, rather than simply saying that stealing is morally right to them. This implies that morality is based on something outside of the agent.

                  1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.1.3. Con:
                  Under agent relativism, morality is only motivated by internal beliefs, not by any external motivators. However, there are many examples of people's views of morality being changed by external motivators. For instance, imagine a man who believes that poor people deserve to suffer for the choices they make, but changes his mind after spending time with a poor family and coming to care about them. This scenario would not be possible under agent relativism.

                1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.2. Pro:
                Speaker relativism states that moral statements are true or false only according to the one who makes the statement.

                  1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.2.1. Con:
                  According to speaker relativism, when a claim is made, that claim is referring to the moral framework of the speaker. As such, when two people disagree about morality, they are speaking about two separate frameworks - and, as such, do not have shared meaning. Without shared meaning, real disagreement is impossible. Intuitively, this is not what moral disagreement is.

                    1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.2.1.1. Pro:
                    If one person says that planes fly through the air, and another person states that they don't, believing the word "plane" to refer to a woodworking tool [674], then the two people don't truly disagree because they aren't referring to the same thing.

                  1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.2.2. Con:
                  Speaker relativism isn't intuitive or held by many people. For instance, if someone says that murder is permissible, we intuitively believe that they are wrong, rather than just stating a truth about their own moral framework.

                1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.3. Pro:
                -> See 1.3.7.3.4.2.6.2.

                1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.4. Con:
                All forms of moral relativism fail to account for the general agreement amongst all of humanity of basic moral principles.

                  1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.4.1. Pro:
                  -> See 1.3.7.3.4.4.1.4.

                1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.5. Con:
                The concept of relative morality is incoherent.

                  1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.5.1. Pro:
                  "Goodness" and "badness/evil" are irrelevant if they are only subjective constructions. One may consider something "bad", but be mistaken. For the argument to succeed, the action must be objectively bad. Objective moral values and duties cannot exist unless God exists.

                    1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.5.1.1. Pro:
                    Theists have long defended the concept of objective moral principles, distinguished from moral absolutism in that principles will require different behaviors in varied roles and situations. As such, a flat claim that a particular act must be "good" or "bad", regardless of context, is too simplistic a view of theistic moral claims.

                    1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.5.1.2. Con:
                    Using the assertion that objective morality requires God to exist, to refute an argument that God is morally bad, doesn't in and of itself refute any argument that God is morally bad, so it just produces an internally-contradictory view of a God who is both evil and the foundation of good.

                  1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.5.2. Con:
                  If you claim that the only "good" that matters is the "good" of humans (for example), absolute good could exist in that sense.

                    1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.5.2.1. Pro:
                    There can be an objective reason to say that the "good" of humanity is the ultimate good if we consider that humans are the only known creatures capable of reason. Since reason is the only way for the universe to know itself (as discussed by Carl Sagan), the fact that only humans possess it can justify a special status among living beings.

                  1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.5.3. Pro:
                  If morality is relative, then each person gets to decide what is moral. However, this renders each of us incapable of saying what is moral or immoral for someone else. Such a morality is incapable of informing us of how we should treat others--yet, how to correctly interpersonal relationships is a huge part of our moral framework.

                    1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.5.3.1. Con:
                    Subjective morality does not prevent us from saying what is moral or immoral for someone else, it just makes that a subjective judgement rather than a judgement of what we subjectively believe to be objectively morally correct.

                  1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.5.4. Pro:
                  Relative morality cannot explain why any person would ever consider something that they did to be immoral or wrong.

                    1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.5.4.1. Pro:
                    If one's subjective moral opinion is the ultimate arbiter of morality, then when a person believes they have done something "wrong" or "immoral", they have only violated their own internal standard.

                    1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.5.4.2. Pro:
                    Given subjective, relative morality, one's moral standard cannot be applied to anyone other than oneself. To do so would be to assert that said morality is objective and universal.

                      1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.5.4.2.1. Con:
                      Humans make subjective judgements about things and people outside of themselves all the time, everywhere. Morality being subjective does not mean one cannot make judgments applying one's morality to other people.

                        1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.5.4.2.1.1. Pro:
                        Aesthetics are commonly considered to be a matter of subjective opinion, and judging things, people or landscapes to be beautiful or ugly is entirely commonplace.

                    1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.5.4.3. Pro:
                    When a consistent moral relativist believes they have done something immoral, they can only mean that they have violated their own internal code. This raises the question of why they would ever believe any action (especially their own action) to be immoral to begin with. The value of a moral code that can only apply to oneself is questionable in the extreme. It is of little value to believe that oneself should behave in a given manner, while not holding others to any standard at all.

                      1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.5.4.3.1. Con:
                      One needs no more reason to find one's own actions immoral than to dislike the taste of something one eats. In both cases, one might hypothetically be better off if one could simply choose to have a different preference, and not feel guilty/disgusted, but neither morality nor taste work that way.

                  1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.5.5. Pro:
                  Relative morality cannot explain why, in the absence of an objective morality, one would even have a moral sense to begin with.

                    1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.5.5.1. Con:
                    It is generally understood that taste is subjective, and yet "relative taste" cannot explain why humans have a sense of taste to begin with; this does not make "relative taste" incoherent.

                    1.3.7.3.4.4.2.3.5.5.2. Con:
                    Empathy for others can explain having a moral sense - even in the absence of an objective morality.

              1.3.7.3.4.4.2.4. Pro:
              Ends can justify means, so long as they are sufficient to do so.

                1.3.7.3.4.4.2.4.1. Pro:
                In the Bible, Rahab told a lie to the Jericho soldiers to protect Israelite spies. She is later spared by God during the attack because of her help. Joshua 2

                1.3.7.3.4.4.2.4.2. Pro:
                If Nazi's are banging on your door, lying to protect the Jews that are hiding in your basement is justified.

                1.3.7.3.4.4.2.4.3. Con:
                No end can justify the things God does, like the ordering of genocides, commuting genocides, and eternal torture.

                1.3.7.3.4.4.2.4.4. Con:
                Examples involving lying seem to only suggest that lying is a justifiable means, so long as the end is sufficient, or perhaps that lying on it's own is not immoral, but they do not show that in general, ends can justify means, so long as they are sufficient to do so.

          1.3.7.3.4.5. Con:
          Morality is relative, so there cannot exist a person or character that would seem good to every sentient being.

            1.3.7.3.4.5.1. Pro:
            There are many cases where God is defined as good but claimed by believers to have done things which those same believers would consider bad if done by anyone other than God (or someone claiming to be doing God's will).

              1.3.7.3.4.5.1.1. Con:
              The morality of a "being" that is so much more than what the sum of all humanity could ever be could never be as simple as humanity's morality. This sentence is debating humanity's morality not God's morality and it does so from a humane point of view, for we are not God if God exists and we do not have His moral compass.

              1.3.7.3.4.5.1.2. Con:
              As the author and rightful owner of all creation it is God's prerogative to do things that us, mere members of that creation, may not.

                1.3.7.3.4.5.1.2.1. Con:
                A sentient being cannot be owned.

                  1.3.7.3.4.5.1.2.1.1. Con:
                  A sentient being can be owned if the entity owning the sentient being is as supreme as God is understood to be.

                    1.3.7.3.4.5.1.2.1.1.1. Pro:
                    There is no proper analogy between God's right as an "owner" of his creation, and our rights as "owner" of our creations.

                    1.3.7.3.4.5.1.2.1.1.2. Con:
                    It is special pleading and a cop out to make an exception for God. In effect and in the absence of the contrary, no degree of "supremacy" can prevent a sentient being from being its own individual.

                1.3.7.3.4.5.1.2.2. Pro:
                As Isaiah 45:9 [675] says, "Does the clay say to the potter, ‘What are you making?’"

                1.3.7.3.4.5.1.2.3. Pro:
                The only person who can rightfully play God, is God.

                  1.3.7.3.4.5.1.2.3.1. Con:
                  The term "play God" is defined [676] in terms of that which is reserved for God, so this is a mere tautology: "that which is reserved for God is reserved for God"

                  1.3.7.3.4.5.1.2.3.2. Pro:
                  The popular colloquialism "don't try to play God" is evidence that there is an idea of a being called God that holds proper authority to do actions that other beings do not hold.

                    1.3.7.3.4.5.1.2.3.2.1. Con:
                    What people say, when not backed by adequate reason, is not a sufficient reason to do things, as people say things like that a mirror being broken containing one's reflection is bad luck, or will damage one's soul.

                1.3.7.3.4.5.1.2.4. Pro:
                When we create something we create it from "borrowed" ingredients. Everything down to the smallest particle was made by God and for God. We can not make anything the same way He made everything.

                  1.3.7.3.4.5.1.2.4.1. Con:
                  The argument that 'when we create something we create it from "borrowed" ingredients. Everything down to the smallest particle was made by God and for God. We can not make anything the same way He made everything" is somewhat undercut by the fact that it is a thing created without the use of any of these particles purely by a human.

                    1.3.7.3.4.5.1.2.4.1.1. Con:
                    If the Christian God exists, then the power of reason and logic are part of his nature and can then be said to belong to him. When we use these capabilities we are borrowing from God.

                      1.3.7.3.4.5.1.2.4.1.1.1. Con:
                      If the Christian God does not exist, we can still use our faculties of reason and logic. It's just that we would not describe it as "borrowing from God".

                      1.3.7.3.4.5.1.2.4.1.1.2. Con:
                      Believing in a God as a presupposition to justify using reason and logic is rationally worse that presupposing reason and logic directly. Occam's razor [677].

                      1.3.7.3.4.5.1.2.4.1.1.3. Pro:
                      Colossians 1:16 explains that the Christian understanding of God is that "by him [God] all things were created, in heaven and on earth, visible and invisible, whether thrones or dominions or rulers or authorities—all things were created through him and for him."

                    1.3.7.3.4.5.1.2.4.1.2. Con:
                    If the Christian God exists, then our mental substance (whether material or immaterial) was created by God and when we exercise this mental substance to formulate arguments and thoughts we are borrowing from God.

                      1.3.7.3.4.5.1.2.4.1.2.1. Pro:
                      -> See 1.3.7.3.4.5.1.2.4.1.1.3.

                1.3.7.3.4.5.1.2.5. Pro:
                Whether an action is moral depends on who performs the deed. Soldiers can kill, police officers can arrest and lock people up, and doctors can cut open a person. If these actions were committed by others, they would be murder, kidnapping, and assault, respectively.

              1.3.7.3.4.5.1.3. Con:
              It is possible for an omniscient and benevolent being to do things which would be immoral for finite creatures because of His ability to see and guide all ends.

                1.3.7.3.4.5.1.3.1. Pro:
                Given the complexity of events, it is likely that the "bad" action is not actually the actually being taken by God, but is merely a logical consequence of a benevolent action taken by a being with much more far-reaching goals.

            1.3.7.3.4.5.2. Con:
            For a sentient being to recognize that some things are "good" and some are "evil" implies a recognition of a moral standard by which to distinguish between the two. Only God's nature is sufficient to be that standard.

              1.3.7.3.4.5.2.1. Con:
              Morals are, to at least some extent, evolutionary. As such, no standard is required.

                1.3.7.3.4.5.2.1.1. Con:
                God would be complete, therefore not evolutionary. The whole spectrum of possible evolutions of morals would be contained in God.

            1.3.7.3.4.5.3. Pro:
            Any interpretation of a God's actions would have to be made by humans, who are fallible. No person would be truly privy to the mind of God.

              1.3.7.3.4.5.3.1. Con:
              Human interpretation is, however, the filter through which we perceive everything. To dismiss such human ability is to dismiss - among everything else - the very possibility of knowing whether God exists and also whether God does not exist.

            1.3.7.3.4.5.4. Con:
            The good suggested by classical theism is the creator of the universe, and as such, would be an objective arbiter of morality unlike entities within the created universe which would only have a subjective view.

              1.3.7.3.4.5.4.1. Con:
              There is no reason why creating the universe would make one an "objective" arbiter of morality rather than just another subjective moral viewpoint, albeit one with tremendous power.

            1.3.7.3.4.5.5. Con:
            Human morality is subjective. An all-knowing god would be privy to all these subjective perceptions, so He can be objective.

              1.3.7.3.4.5.5.1. Con:
              An all-knowing God who oversees all things has the subjective perception of a being who sees all things. This is just another different perception of things, quantitatively more in what is seen but not any more objective in quality.

          1.3.7.3.4.6. Pro:
          Various basic human instincts such as responsibility (and other similar instincts such as gratitude and awe) are implicitly theistic.

          1.3.7.3.4.7. Con:
          CS Lewis's premise, "If there is a universal Moral Law, there is a Moral Law-giver," is unsubstantiated. Laws do not require lawgivers.

          1.3.7.3.4.8. Con:
          CS Lewis's premise, "If there is a moral law-giver, it must be something beyond the universe," is unsubstantiated. Laws can be from internal sources (e.g., laws passed by the parliament).

        1.3.7.3.5. Con:
        Humans would have morals regardless of God.

          1.3.7.3.5.1. Pro:
          Those that follow God-free, logic-based moralities are much better behaved, and consistent than theists do in no small part to fundamental foundational problems with theism.

            1.3.7.3.5.1.1. Con:
            Since the ability to determine whether a person who claims to follow some moral code actually does is limited to him being honest, there is no reliable method to evaluate what every person does in relation to the moral code he claims to follow.

            1.3.7.3.5.1.2. Pro:
            The horrible practice of the gospel of wealth [678] is an example of theistic indiscretion.

            1.3.7.3.5.1.3. Pro:
            Theistic sources are contradictory, allowing one to choose morals convent for them, rather then to be consistent, or good. For example, the bible argues both for [679] and against [680] slavery. It is impossible to be good when what is good isn't even consistently defined.

            1.3.7.3.5.1.4. Pro:
            Greater societal religiosity is associated with higher levels of "homicide, juvenile and early adult mortality, STD infection rates, teen pregnancy, and abortion" (p. 7 [681]).

          1.3.7.3.5.2. Con:
          To say that humans need God in order to know/understand morality is mischaracterizing the moral argument - that is a statement about moral epistemology (why we know about morality), whereas the moral argument is about moral ontology (why morality exists in the first place).

            1.3.7.3.5.2.1. Con:
            If a God is not necessary from the moral argument then the moral argument does not imply God's existence.

          1.3.7.3.5.3. Pro:
          Things like murder are wrong because it hurts the survival of our species.

            1.3.7.3.5.3.1. Con:
            If the only thing that makes murder wrong is because it "hurts the survival of our species," then failing to have a child is just as wrong as murder. This is because both actions result in one less person being in the population than would have happened without the action.

            1.3.7.3.5.3.2. Con:
            Without objective moral axia, there is no way to derive that we ought not hurt the survival of our species (i.e. that hurting the survival of our species is wrong).

              1.3.7.3.5.3.2.1. Pro:
              -> See 1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.

              1.3.7.3.5.3.2.2. Con:
              -> See 1.3.7.3.4.2.4.1.5.

            1.3.7.3.5.3.3. Con:
            The survival of our species is not a moral priority.

            1.3.7.3.5.3.4. Con:
            Eugenics and social darwinism help the survival of humanity by removing unfit genes. Forced conversion of homosexuals also helps human survival by increasing reproduction rate. Yet most agree that these actions are still morally abhorrent. Therefore, the survival of humanity is not identical to morality.

              1.3.7.3.5.3.4.1. Con:
              Social darwinism is not a moral framework that came out of evolution - rather it is a moral framework intentionally designed by people drawing on a parallelism with evolutionary principles.
              Conversely the moral framework that came out of evolution does take into account the well being of individuals.

            1.3.7.3.5.3.5. Con:
            It is not self evident why the survival of our species would be morally good without God.

              1.3.7.3.5.3.5.1. Con:
              Immoral behavior harms the ability of humanity to survive.

                1.3.7.3.5.3.5.1.1. Pro:
                Murder decreases the population.

                1.3.7.3.5.3.5.1.2. Pro:
                Theft removes resources from those who can put them to good use.

              1.3.7.3.5.3.5.2. Con:
              Moral behavior increases survival odds.

                1.3.7.3.5.3.5.2.1. Pro:
                Saving lives keeps the genetic pool diverse.

                1.3.7.3.5.3.5.2.2. Pro:
                Moral behavior promotes group cohesion.

                1.3.7.3.5.3.5.2.3. Pro:
                Taking care of the needy discourages unrest.

              1.3.7.3.5.3.5.3. Pro:
              The is-ought problem [682] in philosophy states that you cannot derive what ought to be true solely from what is true.

      1.3.7.4. Pro:
      There are aesthetic arguments [683] for the existence of God.

        1.3.7.4.1. Pro:
        There isn't any reason a priori to expect the world to be fundamentally beautiful - however, if it was created by a perfect God, it would almost certainly be. As such, the fact that we exist in a fundamentally beautiful universe counts as evidence for a perfect Creator.

          1.3.7.4.1.1. Pro:
          In accordance with The Likelihood Principle, the existence of a fundamentally beautiful universe is likely assuming a perfect creator, and less likely assuming the absence of such a creator.

            1.3.7.4.1.1.1. Con:
            There exists in the claim a fallacy of Affirming the Consequent [684]. Allowing that God would probably create an ordered (a.k.a. "elegant") universe, it cannot be concluded that an ordered universe was necessarily caused by said God.

            1.3.7.4.1.1.2. Con:
            This demonstrates an argument from rarity [685]. It conflates the probability of causation with the plausibility of explanation. By analogy, it is like arguing that the winner of the lottery must have cheated, due to the low probability of any given person winning.

          1.3.7.4.1.2. Con:
          Much of the world isn't beautiful.

            1.3.7.4.1.2.1. Pro:
            Human beings and animals are capable of destroying life. This damages natural beauty.

              1.3.7.4.1.2.1.1. Con:
              The works that God is solely responsible for are fundamentally beautiful. However, his works are sullied by human beings and animals, to whom he gave free will, including the freedom to defile natural beauty.

              1.3.7.4.1.2.1.2. Con:
              Excluding these acts, the vast majority of the universe being fundamentally beautiful is very unlikely without assuming intentional design.

            1.3.7.4.1.2.2. Pro:
            The universe is not fundamentally beautiful - for example, life is impossible in 99.99% of the universe.

              1.3.7.4.1.2.2.1. Con:
              From a distance, the cosmos are extremely beautiful, though they are not capable of sustaining life. This does not induce fear, but awe.

            1.3.7.4.1.2.3. Pro:
            Natural disasters or infections are not works of free creatures, but they are not beautiful. Thus, if God exists, did not create a fundamentally beautiful universe.

              1.3.7.4.1.2.3.1. Con:
              Natural disasters may wipe out much moral evil, and so may have the beauty and awe of divine justice.

              1.3.7.4.1.2.3.2. Con:
              During natural disasters, many heroic acts are done, which are beautiful in their own right, and only possible in such dire situations; thus, natural disasters can be made beautiful by acts of heroism.

          1.3.7.4.1.3. Con:
          It's possible that the human conception of "beauty" adapted from experience of the outside world, and so it would be natural for humans to consider nature "beautiful."

            1.3.7.4.1.3.1. Con:
            Many things not visible to early humans - such as planets, nebulae, and galaxies, or the microscopic structures of many materials - are still considered beautiful, despite humans having no time to adapt to them and come to see them that way.

              1.3.7.4.1.3.1.1. Con:
              Our value of these other structures may be evolutionary spandrels [686].

        1.3.7.4.2. Pro:
        There is no particular reason a priori to expect natural laws to be elegant or beautiful, or even so accurately described by mathematics [687]. Yet, they are elegant and beautiful.

          1.3.7.4.2.1. Pro:
          The fundamental laws of our universe are extremely elegant, whereas the vast majority of possible sets of natural laws are incredibly arbitrary and complex. This suggests maximization by some agent.

            1.3.7.4.2.1.1. Con:
            Elegance in scientific theories is an illusion.
            We, as pattern finding masters, project the elegance into these equations and modify them to fit our aesthetic values.

              1.3.7.4.2.1.1.1. Con:
              We can't arbitrarily "modify the equations to fit our aesthetic values" because they still have to describe objective reality. If Newton's second law [688] were mathematically different (e.g F = ma + 0.001), there would be no way to legitimately simplify the equation into the much more elegant (and real) F = ma.

            1.3.7.4.2.1.2. Con:
            Proving the universe has a purpose or was "maximized by some agent" does not prove the existence of a classical theistic God. It merely proves the existence of "some agent", which does not need to be God, or that the universe has a purpose.

              1.3.7.4.2.1.2.1. Con:
              An agent that can maximize some quality about the universe would have to be transcendent and enormously powerful, if not omnipotent.

                1.3.7.4.2.1.2.1.1. Pro:
                Since some of the properties of a classical God are met, this argument can be considered to constitute evidence for classical theism.

          1.3.7.4.2.2. Pro:
          Intentional design by a perfect God could account for such elegance. As such, the fact that physical laws are mathematically elegant counts as evidence for a perfect Creator.

            1.3.7.4.2.2.1. Con:
            There is not sufficient evidence for intelligent design.

              1.3.7.4.2.2.1.1. Con:
              Elegance in the parent argument isn't being used in the aesthetic sense. It's not just pleasing to the eye, it's mathematically balanced and coherent.

              1.3.7.4.2.2.1.2. Pro:
              Aesthetics are subjectively pleasing, and something being found aesthetically pleasing doesn't necessitate it being made for personal appreciation.

          1.3.7.4.2.3. Con:
          A priori, by the Occamian principle, we should have every reason to expect simple (and therefore elegant, beautiful) sets of natural laws to be more likely than complex ones.

          1.3.7.4.2.4. Con:
          A complex and baroque set of natural laws, or even an ineffable and indescribable nature not governed by such laws, could just as well be seen as beautiful, so this beauty does not need explanation.

        1.3.7.4.3. Pro:
        If aesthetic judgments have objective truth, there must exist [689] an objective standard or anchor point to justify them. Such an aesthetically perfect, objective, and transcendent anchor would be considered God.

          1.3.7.4.3.1. Con:
          Aesthetic judgements do not have objective truth, and indeed the subjectivity of taste [690] is widely attested.

          1.3.7.4.3.2. Con:
          Many atheists have an aesthetic distaste for the idea of God; the description of God as "aesthetically perfect" is thus in dispute.

        1.3.7.4.4. Con:
        There is no thing necessitating objectivity in aesthetics. Indeed art, literature or any other thing that exhibits or conjures beauty is subjective in appreciation. One may fail to find beauty even where a billion others succeed, no known physical laws deny the possibility.

          1.3.7.4.4.1. Con:
          A difference in appreciation of art need not be grounded in mere difference of opinion. It may just as well be a consequence of some sort of defect in the one person over the other.

    1.3.8. Pro:
    Teleological arguments [691] support the existence of God.

      1.3.8.1. Pro:
      Something cannot posses categories of qualities which its source lacks. People exist; therefore, a personal God must exist.

        1.3.8.1.1. Con:
        Things can have qualities their origin doesn't.

          1.3.8.1.1.1. Pro:
          The concept of a chessboard has its origin in the human mind. Humans, and the human mind, do not possess the qualities of an 8x8 monochrome pattern of squares. Hence, chessboards are an example of something possessing qualities that its origin does not possess.

            1.3.8.1.1.1.1. Con:
            Proposing an intelligent mind as an example of something that can produce products possessing qualities which it does not have does not lend itself to atheism and naturalism. The parent poses it as an exception. If we grant that premise we haven't made progress towards atheism.

            1.3.8.1.1.1.2. Con:
            The ability of intelligent minds to create is dependent on them having been exposed to ideas and concepts which they later combine together in novel ways.

              1.3.8.1.1.1.2.1. Pro:
              One example of this is how it can shown that in music, movies and technology everything is a remix [692].

          1.3.8.1.1.2. Pro:
          Chemical reactions make compounds.

            1.3.8.1.1.2.1. Pro:
            Sodium Hydroxide and Hydrochloric acid together make water and salt, which have different qualities.

            1.3.8.1.1.2.2. Con:
            In chemical reactions, the type of elements and the amount of each don't change. They just join with new elements to make new compounds.

            1.3.8.1.1.2.3. Pro:
            Hydrogen and oxygen are each individually gases and do not possess any property of "wetness" or "potability". Yet, when appropriately combined, they produce water, which has the properties of being liquid, being wet, and being potable.

          1.3.8.1.1.3. Pro:
          Every electrical device have qualities that no individual part could do without the rest.

            1.3.8.1.1.3.1. Pro:
            The precursor to modern technology couldn't do the things technology can do now, such as use a touch screen.

            1.3.8.1.1.3.2. Con:
            The materials which something is made out of is not what is meant by source. The source of something means its creator. In the case of technology the creator is intelligent minds.

              1.3.8.1.1.3.2.1. Con:
              The creator of electronic devices cannot be used as an electronic device.

          1.3.8.1.1.4. Pro:
          Orange paint, when created by mixing yellow and red paint, has the quality of being orange, which its origin - yellow and red paint - does not possess.

            1.3.8.1.1.4.1. Con:
            Orange posseses a combination of the qualities of its origins. Orange is between red and yellow on the colour spectrum. If less red and more yellow is added the result will be closer to yellow and vice versa.

          1.3.8.1.1.5. Pro:
          Sand and water aren't hard or tall, but can make sandcastles with those properties [693].

            1.3.8.1.1.5.1. Con:
            Sand and water are both capable of being hard and tall, even without being assembled in the form of a sandcastle.

              1.3.8.1.1.5.1.1. Pro:
              Water, when cold enough, turns to ice which is a solid object.

              1.3.8.1.1.5.1.2. Pro:
              Individual grains of sand are hard.

            1.3.8.1.1.5.2. Pro:
            Water on its own will be flat with other parts of water, and a grain of sand doesn't stick well enough to other bits of sand, so the piles can't get too tall. But together, the water and sand stick together to bond the sand stronger and allow it to be taller before it collapses on its own weight.

          1.3.8.1.1.6. Pro:
          Intelligent life evolved from unintelligent matter.

          1.3.8.1.1.7. Pro:
          Thermal induction [694] produces heat. However, thermal induction does not contain the property of heat.

        1.3.8.1.2. Pro:
        Sunlight and heat come from the sun which has the qualities of heat and light.

          1.3.8.1.2.1. Con:
          The atoms in the Sun's photosphere which produce [695] the photons we receive as heat and light from the Sun do not have the quality of light themselves.

        1.3.8.1.3. Pro:
        A flower produces fragrance which has less qualities than the flower. The flower has form and smell, but the fragrance when carried by the air has only smell and no form.

          1.3.8.1.3.1. Con:
          If we are to regard a flower and its fragrance as separate things, then the flower as a separate entity does not have a smell, and hence the fragrance has a quality its source does not possess.

          1.3.8.1.3.2. Con:
          Odor originates from chemical substances, which are molecular structures. Therefore, odor does have a form.

        1.3.8.1.4. Con:
        This commits the fallacy of division, which is when one assumes that a property of a whole must apply to some or all parts of that whole.

        1.3.8.1.5. Con:
        Mud comes from water and dirt. Neither water nor dirt are squishy. Things can possess qualities that their sources lack.

        1.3.8.1.6. Pro:
        Our experience and evidence indicate that meaning cannot arise from meaninglessness, order cannot arise from chaos, nor can design arise from purposelessness. In an atheistic reality where there is no ultimate meaning, order, or purpose, this must happen for meaning, order, and design to exist.

          1.3.8.1.6.1. Con:
          There are many examples of meaning arising from random processes.

          1.3.8.1.6.2. Con:
          "Meaning" is a subjective concept within human minds, it does not "exist" in external reality so, insofar as it does exist, it is constantly arising from meaninglessness (when humans read into the meaningless universe) and that's all that ever happens.

          1.3.8.1.6.3. Con:
          Order can increase locally in cases where energy is input from elsewhere (and thus entropy increases elsewhere); for example, biological evolution on Earth while the Sun is shining.

          1.3.8.1.6.4. Con:
          Design happens when a human has a purpose in mind and designs something. We have no evidence of anything truly designed that is not human-made, which is consistent with an atheistic reality.

            1.3.8.1.6.4.1. Con:
            It is extremely improbable for our universe to support life without God. Yet our universe has life, so it is designed.

      1.3.8.2. Pro:
      The complexity and precision found in nature and in living organisms indicate God's existence as the creator of all things.

        1.3.8.2.1. Pro:
        Life in our universe would be impossible [696] if any of a number of laws, constants, and initial conditions were changed even unimaginably slightly. This implies the strong probability that our universe [697] was created for the purpose of harboring life (fine tuning).

          1.3.8.2.1.1. Con:
          Life adapted to fit in the environment, rather than the environment being made for life.

            1.3.8.2.1.1.1. Pro:
            Fine-tuning arguments can be explained via multiverse theory [698], without resorting to God as the source of order.

              1.3.8.2.1.1.1.1. Con:
              Multiverse theory and anthropic reasoning can successfully explain why the laws of nature permit living beings to exist, but cannot explain why the laws of nature are so simple and elegant, since the vast majority of possible worlds with intelligent beings have extremely convoluted and arbitrary natural laws.

                1.3.8.2.1.1.1.1.1. Con:
                We only know about our own universe and therefore have no frame of reference for how simple and elegant our universe is.

                  1.3.8.2.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con:
                  Other sets of natural laws are conceivable, and those are often very complex. This is all that's required for them to be considered possible worlds under modal logic, which serves as our frame of reference to judge this universe.

                    1.3.8.2.1.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con:
                    if they are conceivable they are presentable, and they have not been presented.

                1.3.8.2.1.1.1.1.2. Con:
                Intelligent life living in a universe with less elegant and more arbitrary laws could just as well ask why they lived in a world with that specific arbitrary set of laws rather than another.

              1.3.8.2.1.1.1.2. Con:
              Multiverse theory attempts to resolve the origins of the universe by allowing random chance to compensate for the lack of a creator, but it fails to explain the source of the multiple universes. The question of a creator still remains.

              1.3.8.2.1.1.1.3. Con:
              No system has been proposed that would result in randomized laws, constants, and quantities in a multiverse.

                1.3.8.2.1.1.1.3.1. Pro:
                Although pseudo-random events seem to be common within our universe, due to unpredictable events and chaos theory, this would not apply outside of our universe without a similar system in place. We can't assume that randomness is the default state without reason.

                1.3.8.2.1.1.1.3.2. Pro:
                There is no theory in physics that explains how physical laws themselves would change or come into being, and so we have no basis on which to assume that this would happen at random in a multiverse.

                  1.3.8.2.1.1.1.3.2.1. Pro:
                  It could be argued that this would be outside of the jurisdiction of physics, since physics deals with the results of physical laws, not the laws themselves. No amount of understanding of physics can conclude *why* physics exists.

                  1.3.8.2.1.1.1.3.2.2. Pro:
                  By contrast to the multiverse, the god of classical theism would have the means to create and change physical laws due to its omnipotence.

                1.3.8.2.1.1.1.3.3. Pro:
                Perfect randomness may be a concept that could only exist within a mind.

                1.3.8.2.1.1.1.3.4. Con:
                This is false, as such theories have been proposed, for example, Steinhardt et al.'s cyclic models [699]. However, they don't need to be randomized, just changing in a way that allows adequate constants to arise.

              1.3.8.2.1.1.1.4. Con:
              If an infinite multiverse existed, then not only would implausible beings like Santa Claus and the Easter bunny exist, but so would every pagan/animist god and goddess. Therefore not only is a multiverse lower probability than virtually every religion, but belief in it entails beliefs in gods.

                1.3.8.2.1.1.1.4.1. Pro:
                There may be a world where God does not exist. Since God does not exist in every possible world, He does not exist.

                  1.3.8.2.1.1.1.4.1.1. Con:
                  Ontological arguments for God's existence and those for his non-existence demonstrate that God's existence is either necessary, or that his non-existence is necessary. Of these two options, God's necessary existence would be more plausible unless the definition of God can be shown to be incoherent.

                1.3.8.2.1.1.1.4.2. Con:
                Santa Claus and the Easter bunny are only implausible within our universe. Their existence in other universes, where they may be perfectly plausible and probable, doesn't count as evidence against the multiverse theory.

                1.3.8.2.1.1.1.4.3. Con:
                Pagan gods existing in causally disconnected universes are irrelevant to our lives, and as such their existence can be safely ignored.

                  1.3.8.2.1.1.1.4.3.1. Con:
                  Some of these gods may be very powerful and able to travel to different universes such as ours.

                  1.3.8.2.1.1.1.4.3.2. Con:
                  Depending on how disconnected they actually are, if they have followers in this world, they are influenced by these Gods, and warrant attention.

              1.3.8.2.1.1.1.5. Con:
              -> See 1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.

            1.3.8.2.1.1.2. Pro:
            The universe is so unimaginably immense, has existed and will exist for such a huge amount of time that it's not statistically impossible for the conditions for life to exist at least at some point of the universe, at some period of time.

              1.3.8.2.1.1.2.1. Con:
              The argument from fine-tuning isn't concerned with the likelihood of life existing within the universe, but with the likelihood that the universe would exist in such a way that it permits life at all.

              1.3.8.2.1.1.2.2. Con:
              An unlikely event still requires a mechanism. Infinite time is only useful in having monkeys compose Shakespeare if the type writers have ink or if the moneys are bashing keys. There has never been a demonstration of how proteins could form before any protein existed.

                1.3.8.2.1.1.2.2.1. Con:
                It's not neccessary for life to be based on proteins, esspecially in world which would be different from our. Therefore for any universe's laws / constants etc. there could be some matter, which could create life.

                  1.3.8.2.1.1.2.2.1.1. Con:
                  This is shear speculation as there is no observational data to suggest it to be true.

                1.3.8.2.1.1.2.2.2. Con:
                Our organism is functioning on a basis of really probabilistic mechanism since cells are moving quite randomly and when they randomly appear in good place certain procces happens. It is somehow controlled for avoiding critical conditions and aiming good ones, but it's still higlhy probabilistic. Therefore even highly complicated mechanisms could work on probabilistic basis.

                  1.3.8.2.1.1.2.2.2.1. Con:
                  This con doesn't address the parents assertion that random chance can only bear fruit where a mechanism exists to throw the dice so to speak and that there has been no demonstration of how a protein could form before any protein existed.

                  1.3.8.2.1.1.2.2.2.2. Con:
                  The probabilities that govern biological systems sit within the range of likely events given the odds involved and the frequency and volume of opportunities for them to occur. This is converse to protein forming before protein existed which is neither statistically likely to occur, nor is there any explanation of how it could have a mechanism to arrange the proteins.

                    1.3.8.2.1.1.2.2.2.2.1. Con:
                    The process of the first formation of proteins was first explained over 50 years ago. [700]

            1.3.8.2.1.1.3. Con:
            The fine-tuning argument refers to constants and quantities that, if changed, would not allow for the formation of stars, matter, or complex chemistry, or else would result in near-instant heat death or collapse of the universe. It's clear that no universe such as these could support any form of life.

              1.3.8.2.1.1.3.1. Con:
              Our current understanding of physics partially supports this claim, however our current understanding is incomplete and physical behavior if the constants were outside the goldilocks zone can only be extrapolated based on our incomplete understanding.

              1.3.8.2.1.1.3.2. Con:
              The origin of these physical constants and the origin of the physical forces they apply to is unknown, there could be a common as yet undiscovered constant or variable controlling the values of these constants which can explain fine tuning without resorting to God.

                1.3.8.2.1.1.3.2.1. Con:
                Arguments hinging on evidence that may be discovered in the future are exceedingly weak. The claim could be made that it's possible we'll discover a faraway planet with the entire New Testament carved into its side, so God almost certainly exists. We can't investigate arguments like these because we don't have the hypothetical evidence in front of us.

                1.3.8.2.1.1.3.2.2. Con:
                Although a shared constant may lessen the amount of fine-tuning required, it would not eliminate it, since such a constant would itself be arbitrary and thus need to be finely tuned.

                1.3.8.2.1.1.3.2.3. Pro:
                The fine tuning could stem from the theory of multiple/infinite daughter universes posed by quantum physics: there could be a universe with any configuration of forces, and the chances are one of these would have the correct values for our observable universe to exist as it does, as unlikely as it is

                  1.3.8.2.1.1.3.2.3.1. Con:
                  This assumes that the values of the fundamental forces are assigned probabalistically, of which there is no evidence

                  1.3.8.2.1.1.3.2.3.2. Con:
                  This assumes that the daughter universes theory is true, which has no evidence

            1.3.8.2.1.1.4. Pro:
            DNA, RNA, and proteins [701] made up of the top four [702] most common elements in the universe, excluding noble gases, meaning life used what it had available to be made.

            1.3.8.2.1.1.5. Pro:
            Life could still have adapted to differences in the universe.

              1.3.8.2.1.1.5.1. Pro:
              There are other known sets of laws of physics congenial to life [703], that have significant difference to our own, including ones with an entire fundamental force missing and these lie in the tiny region of laws of physics we're able to conceive of and simulate.

            1.3.8.2.1.1.6. Pro:
            The low amount of life found in the universe so far suggests that it is not particularly hospitable to life.

              1.3.8.2.1.1.6.1. Pro:
              The universe does not appear to be fine tuned for life. It appears to be better tuned for making black holes.

              1.3.8.2.1.1.6.2. Con:
              In the context of this argument a hospitable universe is one that supports life, not necessarily produces life.

            1.3.8.2.1.1.7. Con:
            The purpose of the universe is the conclusion of the fine-tuning argument, not any of its premises.

            1.3.8.2.1.1.8. Pro:
            People are bad at understanding probabilities, and this is formalised as the Lottery Fallacy. The chance of me winning the lottery is astronomically small, but every week or so someone has a big win.

              1.3.8.2.1.1.8.1. Con:
              The problem of fine-tuning isn't based on popular understanding of probability, but on formal probability such as the Likelihood Principle and Bayes' Theorem. See here [704] for an in-depth explanation of the logic behind it.

            1.3.8.2.1.1.9. Pro:
            With an infinite amount of time (including whatever existed before time started), and an infinite number of universes, even low probability events become quite likely.

              1.3.8.2.1.1.9.1. Con:
              Claims that derive conclusions from infinite time and space are indefensible and overly dependent upon unverifiable assumptions.

              1.3.8.2.1.1.9.2. Con:
              -> See 1.3.8.2.1.1.2.2.

            1.3.8.2.1.1.10. Con:
            There is no contradiction between believing that the universe was fine-tuned to permit life to exist and that life arose naturally within the universe and adapted to its environment.

              1.3.8.2.1.1.10.1. Con:
              The fine-tuning argument's conclusion that the universe was created to support life is what is contradicted by the idea that life exist by the sheer size of the universe, and ability of natural adaption to occur.

          1.3.8.2.1.2. Pro:
          The numerous physical constants discovered by modern physics have made the fine tuning [705] argument more compelling.

            1.3.8.2.1.2.1. Pro:
            There are at least six finely tuned laws and constants [706] that make life possible in our universe: gravitational constant, cosmological constant, initial entropy, expansion rate and mass density of the universe, and the relative strength of electromagnetic force and gravity.

            1.3.8.2.1.2.2. Con:
            Claiming that something is 'finely turned' assumes someone tuned it, this is begging the question.

            1.3.8.2.1.2.3. Con:
            The number of physical constants and their seemingly highly unlikely arrangement for habitability of the Universe do not make the argument more compelling because, with a sufficiently large number of Universes – parallel, superimposed, or cyclical – and variations in these constants, the likelihood can be infinitesimally small and still be actual at one point.

          1.3.8.2.1.3. Con:
          We cannot calculate the individual probabilities of each combination of these parameters, so we cannot compare the probability of our universe to the other possibilities.

            1.3.8.2.1.3.1. Pro:
            For example, it could be possible that the elementary charge of the electron had another value that the one we observe in our universe. But we have no way to know which other values it could have had, neither the individual probability of each of these values.

            1.3.8.2.1.3.2. Pro:
            As there is only one universe, we will ever know about or examine, no notion of probability can be applied.

              1.3.8.2.1.3.2.1. Pro:
              Probability requires comparing against a space of alternative possibilities, yet we have no way to know that there are or could be alternatives since there is but one universe.

                1.3.8.2.1.3.2.1.1. Con:
                Alternative possibilities do not have to have actual existence or potential for probability to be relevant.

                  1.3.8.2.1.3.2.1.1.1. Pro:
                  In a deterministic universe, if a coin comes up heads the possibility that it could have come up tails does not actually exist, and yet it is still meaningful to apply probability to coin flips.

              1.3.8.2.1.3.2.2. Pro:
              Probability requires some notion of the distribution of the relative probabilities of the possibilities. Yet with only one data point (the universe as we know it) no justified estimate of the probability distribution can be educed and thus no justified estimate of probability can be made.

              1.3.8.2.1.3.2.3. Con:
              We can create simulated universes. One can argue that we have already simulated very simple simulated universes through cellular automata.

            1.3.8.2.1.3.3. Con:
            Knowing the exact probabilities is not necessary to intuit that they are very low.

              1.3.8.2.1.3.3.1. Con:
              Human intuition is not reliable for knowing about something that we do not have access to and have no idea about.

          1.3.8.2.1.4. Pro:
          Some interpret biological structuralism [707] to support a creator, as they argue it requires physical constants to be fine tuned to enable the life forms we see.

            1.3.8.2.1.4.1. Pro:
            According to process structuralism, biological systems would be a bit like crystals, in that they're likely to form and propagate wherever conditions are right (instead of only in individual, random circumstances).

            1.3.8.2.1.4.2. Con:
            Given what is known of the conditions for life on Earth, it is not possible to definitively claim that life would not emerge if conditions were different.

          1.3.8.2.1.5. Pro:
          The anthropic principle [708] favors theories that portray the universe as conducive to conscious life.

            1.3.8.2.1.5.1. Pro:
            The multiverse may be real and, by the anthropic principle, would explain why we observe a universe like ours.

              1.3.8.2.1.5.1.1. Con:
              Even in a multiverse scenario, it still needs to be explained why values for these constants that are life-permitting are actually possible [709].

                1.3.8.2.1.5.1.1.1. Pro:
                Leslie pointed out [710] that some of the finely tuned constants must each satisfy multiple constraints to have a life-permitting value, and it is highly coincidental that for each constant, these constraints all "line up," being completely satisfiable by a single value.

                1.3.8.2.1.5.1.1.2. Con:
                There already are some draft attempts to do so in cosmology and doing so is easier, more plausible and more in line with evidence than explaining the existence of a "God" whose cause of existence would also need to be explained.

            1.3.8.2.1.5.2. Pro:
            The many-worlds interpretation may be real, and, by the anthropic principle, would explain why we observe a universe like ours.

              1.3.8.2.1.5.2.1. Con:
              The many-worlds interpretation relates to quantum interactions within our own physics, and contains no mechanism for different physical laws or constants to arise.

            1.3.8.2.1.5.3. Pro:
            The universe may be cyclical or branching, and that, by the anthropic principle, would explain why we observe a universe like ours.

            1.3.8.2.1.5.4. Con:
            The anthropic principle (at least in a single-universe model) does not mean that fine tuning requires no explanation.

              1.3.8.2.1.5.4.1. Pro:
              In the analogy of Leslie's Firing Squad [711], one finding out that all the bullets missed and one is still alive does not make it irrational to wonder whether the marksmen missed on purpose.

                1.3.8.2.1.5.4.1.1. Con:
                In the case of us and the universe, "still alive" isn't an apt analogy. The analogy is more akin to our mother being put in front of the firing squad before we were born. Then, our existence requires that she survived.

            1.3.8.2.1.5.5. Con:
            The anthropic principle doesn't explain why we observe not just a universe that can be observed, but specifically one where scientific study of physics is possible and fine tuning can be observed. A universe with observers would still be unlikely to have this latter trait.

              1.3.8.2.1.5.5.1. Pro:
              If the laws of physics were not stable over time [712], physics would be much more difficult.

              1.3.8.2.1.5.5.2. Pro:
              The laws of physics are simple enough to apply in practice [713].

              1.3.8.2.1.5.5.3. Con:
              If the universe was chaotic enough that the scientific study of physics was impossible, theists would attribute humanity's continued existence despite that chaos to the grace of God, rather than inventing "fine-tuning"; thus an argument for God's existence that ignores the anthropic principle would exist either way.

            1.3.8.2.1.5.6. Con:
            The anthropic principle doesn't have any explanatory power.

              1.3.8.2.1.5.6.1. Con:
              The anthropic principle explains why we observe a universe capable of supporting our existence. If the universe had not been capable of supporting our existence, we wouldn't observe it. Thus, it has some explanatory power.

              1.3.8.2.1.5.6.2. Pro:
              The anthropic principle, when used correctly, only applies to scenarios where there are many possible spaces observers could occupy, but only a few wherein observers could actually come to exist/survive. For instance, it's pointless to wonder why we find ourselves on a planet with liquid water, rather than another planet - if there were no liquid water, we couldn't exist. The same doesn't apply here, where no probability spaces other than our universe are known to exist.

                1.3.8.2.1.5.6.2.1. Con:
                If the universe is a one-off event, it is not coherent to apply probabilities to it, as it is incoherent to apply probabilities to one-off events.

                1.3.8.2.1.5.6.2.2. Con:
                There are good reasons to think there may be other universes.

              1.3.8.2.1.5.6.3. Pro:
              The ability to ask a question doesn't factor into whether the question is meaningful. I can ask why my parents got together, even though if they hadn't I wouldn't be able to ask such a question - there is still a meaningful explanation for their getting together.

                1.3.8.2.1.5.6.3.1. Con:
                The anthropic principle wouldn't explain why any particular parents got together to produce a particular child, but it would explain why the necessary preconditions were met for someone to be present to wonder about their origination.

              1.3.8.2.1.5.6.4. Pro:
              The fact that we can know a certain event happened only gives us certainty that it happened, not why it happened. The latter is what the fine-tuning argument is concerned with.

                1.3.8.2.1.5.6.4.1. Con:
                "Why do I observe the sort of universe I do?" is answered by the anthropic principle. Namely, that if the universe were significantly different, there wouldn't be anyone to ask the question.

          1.3.8.2.1.6. Pro:
          The constants referred to are dimensionless [714], meaning their value doesn't depend on any other value or measurement. It's unclear how these values couldn't be different.

          1.3.8.2.1.7. Con:
          Analogically, one could argue, "My existence in our universe would be impossible if any number of laws, constants, and initial conditions were changed even unimaginably slightly," implying that the universe was created for the purpose of harboring that specific person.

            1.3.8.2.1.7.1. Con:
            As a member of the set of "life", the argument is expected to apply just as well to you as to any other creature or to life as a whole.

            1.3.8.2.1.7.2. Con:
            A theist would typically agree that the universe was made for the purpose of hosting you, in a sense, since God has an individual purpose for everyone.

            1.3.8.2.1.7.3. Con:
            The reason the above statement suggests the universe is created is that there was only one "shot" (an assumption) at the universe and we got one that supported life - ie that we were exceedingly lucky. Your version is clearly different because there were many opportunities to have you or someone like you. Once we assume the human race, unlikely individuals existing is very likely (it is unlikely anyone will win the lottery but people often do). The two are different cases.

          1.3.8.2.1.8. Con:
          Conway's game of life [715] shows that life-like phenomenon can emerge from radical different "laws of physics." Most laws may pass the level of congeniality to life, and we just exist in an island surrounded by hostility.

            1.3.8.2.1.8.1. Con:
            Even if more than one life-permitting set of constants exists, they are still vastly outnumbered by non life-permitting sets. Thus, this does not help much to explain fine tuning.

          1.3.8.2.1.9. Con:
          Humans occupy 0.03% of the planet, and the remaining 99.97% is uninhabitable [716]. If the universe was created for the purpose of harboring life, it would be designed to accommodate more people.

            1.3.8.2.1.9.1. Con:
            This misunderstands the parent claim. It doesn't just say that the conditions appear "fine tuned" specifically for Earth, but that a small change in the constants would render 100% of the universe uninhabitable. The fact that we can live anywhere at all in the universe requires explanation.

            1.3.8.2.1.9.2. Con:
            It doesn't matter that most of the universe is uninhabitable; what matters is that the habitable portion is large enough to support humanity.

            1.3.8.2.1.9.3. Pro:
            Our galaxy is a very small portion of our universe, meaning that the majority of the universe is uninhabitable for humans. This suggests that the majority of the universe was not designed for life.

        1.3.8.2.2. Pro:
        If we apply inference to the best hypothesis to an attempt to explain the origin of the complex specified digital information found in DNA, we find an intelligent mind [717] is the only cause for which we have observational experience as minds are known to create information.

          1.3.8.2.2.1. Con:
          Just because DNA and other types of digital codes share one thing in common, information, this doesn't mean they share something else in common, an intelligent creator. This is commonly know as the false analogy fallacy [718].

            1.3.8.2.2.1.1. Con:
            The accusation of a false analogy is unjustified. For it to be a false analogy the differences would have to be more significant than the similarties. This is not the case with the coded information in DNA and that produced by human minds.

              1.3.8.2.2.1.1.1. Con:
              A false analogy [719] takes place when one asserts that since A and B have X in common, they also have Y in common, where A is DNA, B is a digital code, X is information and Y is a designer.

              1.3.8.2.2.1.1.2. Con:
              This is as invalid as stating that since DNA and other digital codes both have information and DNA looks like a ladder, therefore other digital codes look like ladders.

          1.3.8.2.2.2. Con:
          This is the God-of-the-gaps argument [720], as it says " we don't know how DNA came to be, therefore God made it come to be." Also known as an argument from ignorance [721].

            1.3.8.2.2.2.1. Con:
            It's not an argument from ignorance [722] in this formulation. It's an argument based on what we do know. It's an inference to the best hypothesis based on what we know about the cause and effect relationships capable of producing coded information.

              1.3.8.2.2.2.1.1. Pro:
              A God of the gaps argument would be one in which the claim is merely that a natural process could not have done it therefore God must have done it without any additional evidence or premises.

                1.3.8.2.2.2.1.1.1. Con:
                A god of the gaps argument states that since we do not know of an alternative explanation presently, God must be the explanation. God of the gaps does not involve the much stronger antecedent of rejecting all possible natural processes.

              1.3.8.2.2.2.1.2. Pro:
              In this form of the arguement an extra premesis has been added which states that we know intelligent minds are capable of producing information and we know of no other cause that has this capability.

                1.3.8.2.2.2.1.2.1. Con:

          1.3.8.2.2.3. Con:
          The argument commits the black-and-white fallacy, also known as a false dilemma [745] by claiming that either God or dumb luck created DNA (see the video), without justifying why these are the only possibilities.

            1.3.8.2.2.3.1. Pro:
            A black and white fallacy occurs when a proponent of an argument states that there are an X number of possibilities without justifying why there aren't any other possibilities.

            1.3.8.2.2.3.2. Pro:
            -> See 1.3.8.2.2.2.1.2.1.

            1.3.8.2.2.3.3. Con:

          1.3.8.2.2.4. Con:
          The following claim of the Stephen Meyer is provably wrong: "If you're a computer programmer, you know that if you start randomly the sections of functional code, you're gonna degrade that long before you come up with a new program or operating system."

            1.3.8.2.2.4.1. Pro:
            Genetic programming [746] offers a domain representation of programs in which every random change yields a new functional program.

            1.3.8.2.2.4.2. Pro:
            Genetic algorithms [747] involve random changes to programs which produce increasingly good programs as generations progress.

            1.3.8.2.2.4.3. Pro:
            Error-correcting codes [748] permit random changes to decode to functional programs, even if the number of such programs are relatively small.

          1.3.8.2.2.5. Con:
          Estimates [749] place the information contained in the human genome to only 4 MB. Algorithms produce novel digital information of greater complexity.

            1.3.8.2.2.5.1. Con:
            These algorithms don't mimic conditions which genetic mutations face.

              1.3.8.2.2.5.1.1. Pro:
              The mutation selection mechanism can only select for functional advantage [750]. Algorithms however, can select for changes which are closer to the desired result despite not offering any advantage yet.

                1.3.8.2.2.5.1.1.1. Con:
                Evolutionary algorithms almost universally select by fitness, i.e. functionality, not for closeness to a predefined code.

                  1.3.8.2.2.5.1.1.1.1. Pro:
                  The textbook Introduction to Evolutionary Computing [751] defines a general schema for evolutionary algorithms with selection only on fitness, and provides two examples where fitness is defined from function.

                  1.3.8.2.2.5.1.1.1.2. Pro:
                  This video [752] explains a simple evolutionary algorithm where selection is only on functional advantage.

                  1.3.8.2.2.5.1.1.1.3. Con:
                  The algorithm devised by Dawkins (1986) worked nothing like real evolution as it selected letter by letter, essentially having the entire sentence pre-specified. (P. 9, 2005 [753])

              1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6. Pro:

                1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.1. Pro:

                1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.2. Con:
                There are good reasons to think a mind could not simulate a universe.

                  1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.2.1. Pro:
                  The observer effect [766] implies that an uncollapsed wave-function could not exist in a mind.

                  1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.2.2. Pro:
                  All known minds are based in physical substrates, and there is good reason to believe all minds would be. Thus, suggesting a mind is simulating the universe only pushes the question back to an unknown universe serving as substratum to that mind.

                    1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.2.2.1. Pro:
                    Minds process information. But information requires some way to store it. A pure mind couldn't store information, as any stored information would entail something other than the mind.

                  1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.2.3. Pro:
                  A simulation is a system that replicates certain desired features of another system without recreating it. A mind cannot replicate a system, but only imagine or consider one. Thus, a mind couldn't give rise to a simulation.

                1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3. Pro:
                The entity simulating our universe is God (theological idealism [767]).

                  1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.1. Con:

                    1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.1.1. Pro:

                    1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.1.2. Pro:

                    1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.1.3. Pro:

                      1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.1.3.1. Con:
