                    1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.1.4. Pro:
                    The simulator might have finite computational resources, which would be incompatible with an omnipotent God.

                  1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.2. Pro:
                  Consciousnesses such as ours cannot be simulated in a computer, but only within another consciousness - a mind.

                    1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.2.1. Pro:
                    -> See 1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.3.1.1.1.

                    1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.2.2. Pro:
                    -> See 1.1.1.4.4.12.8.4.3.1.1.2.

                  1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.3. Pro:
                  There are three broad types of things that may be simulating our universe: a quantum computer in a universe such as ours (see Bostrom's ancestor simulation hypothesis), a classical computer in a classical universe, or a mind. We do not ultimately exist in either a quantum or classical computer, so we must exist in a mind.

                    1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.3.1. Pro:
                    An ancestor simulation or other simulation within a quantum computer would have to exist in a quantum world very similar or identical to ours. According to the law of indiscernibility of identicals [768], if our universe is likely simulated, then any identical universe is also likely simulated. So any quantum simulation must itself ultimately exist within a mind.

                      1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.3.1.1. Con:
                      Rather, what would follow is that the world is equivalent to its simulation, and thus positing anything beyond the world to explain it would be unnecessary. Thus, it undermines the simulation hypothesis itself.

                    1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.3.2. Pro:
                    In order for a classical computer to simulate a universe it must contain as many states as the universe it is simulating, and thus be larger than it. A computer larger than our universe would be incredibly inefficient and impractical to build for any entity or civilization that isn't omnipotent. Therefore, we are not simulated in a classical computer.

                      1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.3.2.1. Con:
                      A simulated universe/reality could be much smaller than the entire universe that we believe exists, such as a simulation of only our Solar System.

                      1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.3.2.2. Con:
                      A simulated universe could drastically simplify many aspects of the universe if the simulators were not interested in them; most stars could be modelled as perfect spheres with the properties expected of those stars without us being any the wiser.

                    1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.3.3. Con:
                    Even if we exist in some kind of a mind, there is no reason to call that mind "God".

                  1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.4. Con:
                  Between man and God, there could be any number of beings capable of simulation.

                    1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.4.1. Pro:
                    The simulation could be created by a species not that much more advanced than humans.

                      1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.4.1.1. Pro:
                      Such a being wouldn't necessarily meet the classical definition of God (omnibenevolence, timelessness, simplicity, transcendence, immutability, etc).

                  1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.5. Con:
                  Form fitting the term "God" to a system proven to exist does not make attached religious concepts of God true.

                    1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.3.5.1. Pro:
                    Any objectively provable "God" would disprove many if not most religious assertions about the nature of god.

                1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.4. Con:
                The simulation hypothesis is an independent hypothesis from the god hypothesis, and it does not support or detract from it.

                  1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.4.1. Pro:
                  The possibility of the universe being simulated has little to do with whether or not the simulator is 'God'.

                  1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.4.2. Con:
                  Both hypotheses have far-reaching implications over an entire worldview if one believes in them, and for the hypotheses to be truly independent, those implications would have to (improbably) never interact.

                    1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.4.2.1. Con:
                    This is conflating mutually exclusive hypothesis with independent hypothesis. Independent means one being true does not require the other to be true in order to be true.

                1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.5. Con:
                The simulation hypothesis involves rationalising things outside the universe as if they were within the universe, there is no evidence to suggest this is valid.

                  1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.5.1. Con:
                  Equally, there is no evidence to suggest that this is invalid, rendering this point moot.

                  1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.5.2. Pro:
                  The simulation hypothesis is untestable.

                    1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.5.2.1. Con:
                    Any 'higher being' hypothesis is likely to be fundamentally untestable. If you take an ant after giving it all the training in the world, it still wouldn't be able to solve algebraic equations. For us to be able to test the existence of God we would need to be able to comprehend God's existence, which, for our brains, may well be physiologically impossible. Being untestable for this reason is not a reason to reject the hypothesis.

                      1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.5.2.1.1. Con:
                      The only higher beings a human can meaningfully hypothesise are those they can comprehend well enough to describe the hypothesis, which suggests they should not be so incomprehensible as to be untestable for that reason. Hence, we can reject such hypotheses for not being well put.

                1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.6. Pro:
                Within a simulation, origin notwithstanding, a non-human designer may be omnipotent, omniscient, for all intents and purposes omnipresent, and perfectly benevolent.

                  1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.6.1. Con:
                  They would not be omnipotent (even restricting it to the simulation), by example they would not be able to bring an arbitrarily large number of beings inside the simulation, because of the limited computational resources.

                    1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.6.1.1. Con:
                    As the laws of physics in the parent universe may be different from our own, and we do not know them, it is possible that our simulator's computational resources are not limited.

                  1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.6.2. Con:
                  Humans already don’t know what precisely go inside their own complex simulations. There is no good reason to think it would be different for the designer who created our world.

                    1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.6.2.1. Con:
                    Instead of thinking about the computer simulations, consider fictional worlds, which may be regarded as a sort of conceptual simulation. With respect to a story, the author is easily considered to be omniscient, omnipotent, omnipresent and timeless (outside of the time flow of the story).

                  1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.6.3. Con:
                  If humans can act as omnipotent etc., and humans are not gods, it would follow that gods would not be needed to explain various facts within such a simulation.

                  1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.6.4. Con:
                  This is begging the question. There is no reason provided to explain why a designer would be inherently benevolent.

                    1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.6.4.1. Pro:
                    God is God to me as Will Wright is God to the Sims; yet, even in that context, we'd be hard-pressed to call Mr. Wright omnipotent, omniscient, and omni-benevolent, even relative to the Sims themselves these attributes are vacuous.

                  1.3.8.2.3.2.2.6.6.5. Con:
                  They *may* be these things, but our universe does not appear as if it were designed or managed by one who is omnipotent and perfectly benevolent.

            1.3.8.2.3.2.3. Pro:
            Such an overwhelming consensus among educated experts is not decisive, but it does serve as strong indirect evidence.

          1.3.8.2.3.3. Con:
          This claim commits the logical fallacy called an appeal to authority.

            1.3.8.2.3.3.1. Pro:
            There was once a time when medical professionals rejected the need for hygiene. The fact that a majority of professionals held this opinion did not make it true.

              1.3.8.2.3.3.1.1. Pro:
              There was a time when the heliocentric model was rejected by scientists in a similar way. The popular opinion of authorities did not make it false.

                1.3.8.2.3.3.1.1.1. Con:
                This is not analogous to modern scientists' rejection of creationism, because no scientific community as understood in the modern sense existed at the time.

                  1.3.8.2.3.3.1.1.1.1. Pro:
                  The first record of an editorial pre-publication peer review is from 1665 [769], and the first peer-reviewed publication was published from 1731. Insofar as the scientific method requires peer review, it can be said that there were no scientists around in Copernicus' lifetime.

            1.3.8.2.3.3.2. Con:
            Reference to expert opinion and the scientific consensus is not logically fallacious.

              1.3.8.2.3.3.2.1. Con:
              It can be justified if the subject matter, arguments and evidence is beyond the ability of non-experts to comprehend. For subjects with great consequence and which are comprehensible to lay people then it is prudent to ensure the scientists are representing good science well with the opinions in question.

                1.3.8.2.3.3.2.1.1. Pro:
                If the subject matter can be understood by the general public then the evidence and arguments should be presented and not simply and appeal to authority.

              1.3.8.2.3.3.2.2. Con:
              Critiques of Neo-Darwinian are not permitted in most universities. This is evidence that science has become dogmatic about the issue and has abandoned institutional disconfirmation in favour of an echo chamber.

            1.3.8.2.3.3.3. Pro:
            -> See 1.3.8.2.2.2.1.2.1.3.3.1.1.

            1.3.8.2.3.3.4. Pro:
            -> See 1.3.8.2.3.3.2.1.

            1.3.8.2.3.3.5. Pro:
            Scientists holding a certain opinion does not carry epistemic weight because scientists can be wrong, can be bribed, and can be swayed by peer pressure and institutional pressure. The epistemic weight rests in the evidence and reasoning which informs those opinions and we must look there in our quest for truth.

            1.3.8.2.3.3.6. Con:
            The claim is only offered as an indirect justification. Scientists are competent authorities in their fields and their judgment is of some value in assessing matters touching on their fields of competence.

          1.3.8.2.3.4. Con:
          The reason scientists reject intelligent design as a valid hypothesis is invariably motivated by prior philosophical commitments (a commitment to scientific materialism).

            1.3.8.2.3.4.1. Pro:
            Scientists also don't admit and often aren't even aware that they are making philosophical assertions making it an action done in cheating (because they call it science) at best and out of ignorance at worst.

            1.3.8.2.3.4.2. Con:
            Intelligent design as a hypothesis does not specify that the "designer" is God, or is even supernatural. As such, intelligent design is consistent with materialism, and so a commitment to scientific materialism cannot be the reason scientists reject intelligent design.

              1.3.8.2.3.4.2.1. Con:
              Intelligent Design has heavy theological implications which everyone in the industry is aware of, therefore commitment to philosophical materialism can motivate one against Intelligent Design.

          1.3.8.2.3.5. Con:
          Modern science was founded with the metaphysical commitment to a divine intelligent creator.

            1.3.8.2.3.5.1. Pro:
            The founders of modern science, such as Newton, accepted intelligent design.

              1.3.8.2.3.5.1.1. Con:
              Newton believed in intelligent design for religious reasons, not scientific reasons.

                1.3.8.2.3.5.1.1.1. Pro:
                Newton was born just ten years after the trial of Galileo, the church might have punished Newton if he didn't agree with intelligent design.

                1.3.8.2.3.5.1.1.2. Con:
                For Newton, it's not clear that there is a strict distinction between scientific and religious reasoning.

                  1.3.8.2.3.5.1.1.2.1. Pro:
                  “Though these bodies may,indeed, persevere in their orbits by the mere laws of gravity, yet they could by no means have, at first, derived the regular position of the orbits themselves from those laws….[Thus] this most beautiful system of the sun, planets, and comets could only proceed from the counsel and dominion of an intelligent and powerful being.” - Newton in the General Scholium (introduction) to the Principia.

              1.3.8.2.3.5.1.2. Pro:
              -> See 1.3.8.2.3.5.1.1.2.1.

              1.3.8.2.3.5.1.3. Con:
              Isaac Newton preceded many of the major advances in evolutionary biology, such as Charles Darwin's On the Origin of Species, the discovery of most fossils, and the discovery of DNA.

            1.3.8.2.3.5.2. Pro:
            "The British philosopher Alfred North Whitehead explained, “There can be no living science unless there is a widespread instinctive conviction in the existence of an Order of Things. And, in particular, of an Order of Nature.” Whitehead argued that confidence in this proposition was especially inspired by the “medieval insistence upon the rationality of God" - Signature in the cell page 120

            1.3.8.2.3.5.3. Pro:
            "Sociologist of science Steve Fuller notes, Western science is grounded in “the belief that the natural order is the product of a single intelligence from which our own intelligence descends.”" - Signature in the Cell p121

            1.3.8.2.3.5.5. Con:

            1.3.8.2.3.5.6. Con:
            Contemporary science has no such commitment, showing that the commitment was not necessary and thus not foundational to science.

          1.3.8.2.3.6. Pro:
          If the universe were to sustain life, it should be doing a better job at it, such as life in any solar system with the right sized star [771]. But life is far rarer. This suggests life has to struggle to come into the universe, not the universe being made for life.

          1.3.8.2.3.7. Con:
          Convergent evolution [772] has been observed and is more likely under intelligent design than unguided evolution.

            1.3.8.2.3.7.1. Pro:
            It is very unlikely that unguided evolution would happen to create identical, complex features among unrelated species.

              1.3.8.2.3.7.1.1. Con:
              If the features are highly adaptive and as simple as possible, unguided evolution would predict that unrelated species could independently evolve it.

                1.3.8.2.3.7.1.1.1. Con:
                Some examples of convergent evolution show a level of similarity that goes far beyond what would be necessary for the animals to adapt to the situation supposedly inducing the convergent evolution.

                  1.3.8.2.3.7.1.1.1.1. Pro:
                  Human and koala fingerprints are [773] so similar that it is difficult to tell them apart even under an electron microscope. It seems this level of similarity goes far beyond what would be needed for each animal to effectively adapt to grasping objects.

            1.3.8.2.3.7.2. Pro:
            Examples of convergent evolution, where genetically unrelated animals have essentially identical features, exist.

              1.3.8.2.3.7.2.1. Pro:
              Dolphins and bats are genetically unrelated but both [774] evolved echolocation.

              1.3.8.2.3.7.2.2. Pro:
              Vertebrates and octopi independently evolved [775] very similar eyes.

              1.3.8.2.3.7.2.3. Pro:
              Koalas and humans evolved [776] almost identical fingerprints.

            1.3.8.2.3.7.3. Pro:
            It is less unlikely that an intelligent designer would decide to reuse traits across genetically unrelated species, resulting in these identical traits.

              1.3.8.2.3.7.3.1. Pro:
              Human engineers commonly [777] use standard parts in multiple different engineering projects, so it is plausible a designer might similarly use standard parts across his or her "projects."

            1.3.8.2.3.7.4. Con:
            Under intelligent design, all phenomena are designed and therefore equally likely, including our own comparison of intelligent design and randomness, so it then becomes impossible to decide what the likeliness of design is.

            1.3.8.2.3.7.5. Con:
            In the absence of empirical evidence, the 'likelihood [778]' of an event cannot be determined.

            1.3.8.2.3.7.6. Con:
            Calculating the 'likelihood [779]' of an event requires someone to divide the favorable number of outcomes by the total number of possible outcomes. If God is all powerful, then the total number of possible outcomes is infinite. No matter how large the number of favorable outcomes is, once it is divided by infinity [780], the likelihood comes to 0.

            1.3.8.2.3.7.7. Con:
            Convergent evolution is not as unlikely as it might first intuitively appear because of extremely helpful traits and properties which emerge from certain kinds of organs or other evolutionary adaptions.

              1.3.8.2.3.7.7.1. Pro:
              As a sensory adaptation, echolocation [781] has evolved separately in cetaceans [782] (dolphins and whales) and bats, but from the same genetic mutations. (Excerpt from the Wikipedia page on convergent evolution [783])

              1.3.8.2.3.7.7.2. Pro:
              Flight is a good example of an extremely useful emergent trait which would make convergent evolution much more likely. [784]

          1.3.8.2.3.8. Con:
          Intelligence is suggested by the incredibly precise conditions found in the universe that allow life to exist.

          1.3.8.2.3.9. Pro:
          Arguments for design or the watchmaker analogy [785] are fallacies. Human design, which is the only design we know of, is inspired by nature and uses natural phenomenons. It's a sophistry to then conclude that nature is designed.

            1.3.8.2.3.9.1. Con:
            The watchmaker analogy was a particular design argument, not a general category under which all design arguments fall. Even if it is fallacious, it doesn't follow that all other design arguments are fallacious too.

        1.3.8.2.4. Pro:
        For all known models, it is almost impossible for life through natural processes to have originated and evolved in only four billion years.

          1.3.8.2.4.1. Pro:
          -> See 1.3.8.2.2.2.1.2.1.3.2.

          1.3.8.2.4.2. Pro:
          The prevalence of DNA sequences which code for functional proteins is estimated [786] to be 1 in 10^64. This creates a search space too large for mutations to navigate in amount of time earth has existed.

            1.3.8.2.4.2.1. Con:
            This implies that random mutations occur with a uniform distribution across the available search space. It is reasonable to expect that environmental conditions accentuate which changes are likely to be occur, thereby reducing the breadth of mutations that will be observed to take place.

            1.3.8.2.4.2.2. Con:
            Multiple mutations may take place simultaneously [787] in different parts of the DNA, largely independent of each other, thereby reducing the number of generations required to observe the complexity or perceived sophistication of modern DNA.

            1.3.8.2.4.2.3. Con:
            This is an argument from personal incredulity.

          1.3.8.2.4.3. Con:
          Though almost impossible to occur on a lone planet, it was inevitable to happen eventually.

            1.3.8.2.4.3.1. Pro:
            There is around [788] 10^24 planets in our observable Universe and possibly more in our unobserved universe, up to infinite, increasing the overall chance of life eventually occurring.

            1.3.8.2.4.3.2. Pro:
            The universe, as known now, is at least 11 billion [789] years old and estimated to be almost 14 billion. This gives life a lot of time to appear in the universe.

          1.3.8.2.4.4. Con:
          Genetic evidence and numerous fossils have been found and dated which corroborate the current theories of the origin and evolution of life starting around 4.5 BYA.

          1.3.8.2.4.5. Con:
          No known models indicate the existence of a god.

        1.3.8.2.5. Con:
        This is a non-sequitur fallacy [790] as there is nothing about complexity or precision that implies a God to exist.

        1.3.8.2.6. Con:
        Precision and Complexity cannot be established without first understanding the actual precision or complexity. We do not have enough data and understanding to specify either.

        1.3.8.2.7. Con:
        -> See 1.1.10.4.2.1.

        1.3.8.2.8. Pro:
        The DNA structure within a living organism reads as a highly complex computer code of four chemicals ordered and structured for specific instructions to the cell. If source code requires a coder and DNA is a source code, then DNA had a creator.

          1.3.8.2.8.1. Con:
          The similarity of DNA to computer code is a simile, not a metaphor [791]. Hydrogen and oxygen combine chemically in ways that cannot be realized using computer code, and thus chemical reactions cannot be used to conclude that God exists as a programmer or creator of DNA code.

        1.3.8.2.9. Con:
        Scientific evidence has proven that mankind wasn't created, but evolved from earlier life forms.

          1.3.8.2.9.1. Con:
          Evolution does not explain the appearance of life - rather only how it changes and adapts.

            1.3.8.2.9.1.1. Con:
            Given what we know of the world before, and after that point, this argument seems equivalent to the famous 'missing link' argument, while this is something we need to work on, it seems that in order for simple facts to be accepted by some, we must map out the location of every proton from the start of the universe to present.

            1.3.8.2.9.1.2. Pro:
            The appearance of life is referred to as abiogenesis [792]. At present there is no single generally accepted model for abiogenesis.

          1.3.8.2.9.2. Pro:
          Natural selection [793] explains the diversity of life.

            1.3.8.2.9.2.1. Con:
            Natural selection explains the survival but not the arrival of the fittest. Mutation and natural selection does not have the ability to generate novel genetic information and body plans. youtu.be [794]

              1.3.8.2.9.2.1.1. Con:
              The video claims new proteins can't form via natural selection. This is false, as bacteria have to sometimes change proteins to earn antibiotic resistance.

                1.3.8.2.9.2.1.1.1. Con:
                The mutations required for antibiotic resistance to develop require a change of only 3 nucleotide bases. Statically this is to be expected. Adaptions requiring 6 or 7 changes of nucleotide bases are statistically implausible and a study [795] has shown they are unable to be produced in a lab.

                  1.3.8.2.9.2.1.1.1.1. Con:
                  Two separate mutations, each changing 3 nucleotide bases at a time would [796] add up to 6 changed nucleotide bases.

                    1.3.8.2.9.2.1.1.1.1.1. Con:
                    This only shows that it is possible for 2 separate mutations requiring only 3 nucleotide base changes to occur. It would not demonstrate changes requiring 6 or more changes.

                  1.3.8.2.9.2.1.1.1.2. Con:
                  The study provided has been shown [797] to misunderstand how evolution works.

              1.3.8.2.9.2.1.2. Con:
              Claiming that "macroevolution" can't occur but "microevolution" can is like saying a 30 km trip can't be finished on foot because a foot is too small.

                1.3.8.2.9.2.1.2.1. Con:
                This analogy is not fitting because macro evolution doesn't have a clear pathway like is suggested by the analogy. To make the analogy appropriate we need to extend it by adding pits, caverns and oceans. Michael Behe [798] shows in his book 'The Edge of Evolution [799]' how the limits of evolution are like gorges and oceans which cannot be traversed on foot.

                  1.3.8.2.9.2.1.2.1.1. Con:
                  Dr. Kenneth Miller [800] offers a full refutation of Behe's claims, including his claims of mutation probabilities and pathways.

              1.3.8.2.9.2.1.3. Pro:
              -> See 1.3.8.2.9.2.1.1.1.

              1.3.8.2.9.2.1.4. Con:
              Novel genetic information can form through mutations. Mutations aren't just subtraction or substitution, it is also addition as well.

          1.3.8.2.9.3. Con:
          Paleontogical evidence suggests that phyla appeared in large numbers [801] out of nowhere and decrease in numbers as time goes by. While there are a small number of historical periods when phyla emerged, there are no fossils that support the alternative evolutionary hypothesis of gradual change.

            1.3.8.2.9.3.1. Con:
            The evolution of Phyla can support theories that are incompatible with God's existence, then it does not support the existence of God.

            1.3.8.2.9.3.2. Con:
            The absence of fossils does not prove the absence of ancient living beings gradually evolving. Their fossils may have been destroyed by natural catastrophes.

            1.3.8.2.9.3.3. Con:
            The Cambrian period lasted 53 million years, so is hardly 'out of nowhere'. 
            To give  an intuitive sense of how long this is, if the Cambrian explosion was as long as  a 100m athletics track, then for comparison Homo sapiens first emerged as a species 40-50cm away, and Jesus was born 4mm away (less than the thickness of Usain Bolt's running shoe sole).

          1.3.8.2.9.4. Con:
          Macro evolution is not proven, only micro evolution. To consider micro evolution proof for macro evolution is an unjustified extrapolation.

            1.3.8.2.9.4.1. Con:
            There are multiple [802] sources [803] which contradict the idea that macro-evolution is an unverifiable theory.

              1.3.8.2.9.4.1.1. Con:
              Both sources are biased towards a particular outcome, and are thus unreliable.
              
              The BBC piece itself admits that current research methods into evolutionary science are yet incomplete to fully determine how distinct species can somehow transition into an entirely distinct species.

            1.3.8.2.9.4.2. Con:
            If you believe in microevolution then macroevolution is logical, as it's just microevolution over a much longer period of time.

              1.3.8.2.9.4.2.1. Pro:
              Macro and micro-evolution describe [804] fundamentally identical processes on different scales.

            1.3.8.2.9.4.3. Pro:
            It's generally thought that natural selection acts on isolated traits however natural selection acts on organisms not on isolated traits. This limits the ability of natural selection to select for traits because organisms have many traits and the combined impact of the other traits likely has more impact on the fitness of an organism then any single trait.

              1.3.8.2.9.4.3.1. Con:
              The selection process for applications to a mathematics course must act on students, not traits like mathematical ability, but we would nevertheless find that students at a highly competitive maths course are much better at maths than the average person on the street, suggesting that this kind of limitation on selection processes is not very limiting.

                1.3.8.2.9.4.3.1.1. Con:
                Yes math ability may be selected for in that example but traits which don't impact on math ability won't be selected for or against.

                1.3.8.2.9.4.3.1.2. Con:
                This is a false analogy because life is not as simple as a math course acceptance process. A more fit analogy would be financial success because as with evolutionary fitness, financial success is determined by a myriad of factors.

                  1.3.8.2.9.4.3.1.2.1. Pro:
                  Some with great math ability may be financial failures due to other traits held by the individuals, some may be successes due to their math ability and some may be successes due to unrelated traits held by the individuals. Natural selection is muddied in this way too because the net impact of all the traits an organism can outweigh the effect of any 1 trait.

                    1.3.8.2.9.4.3.1.2.1.1. Pro:
                    It's quite likely that financial success or failure is only determined by math ability in a small percentage of cases and even in these cases it is heavily influenced by the presence or absence of other traits.

              1.3.8.2.9.4.3.2. Con:
              This also means if an organism had multiple good traits and its brother had multiple bad traits, the first would be even more likely to have offspring than if it were just one trait. This would also mean when the second dies, more than one bad trait becomes less likely to reproduce.

            1.3.8.2.9.4.4. Pro:
            The overwhelming majority of possible gene mutations would offer no benefit, some of which would be deleterious and the vast majority of which would be nearly neutral. This makes it incredibly hard for random mutations to offer benefit.

              1.3.8.2.9.4.4.1. Con:
              This is one of the reasons why we evolved sexual reproduction [805], which produces more variation between generations and in a less deleterious fashion than mere random mutations.

              1.3.8.2.9.4.4.2. Con:
              That just means evolution would take a long time to offer benefit, which it does.

                1.3.8.2.9.4.4.2.1. Pro:
                Evolution is about the chance that a gene gets passed on. The chance means that if a species had a population of ten, had a 1/4 chance of surviving to adulthood, and had an average of four children, a gene that increased its survival to 1/3 would take an average of three generations before it means only one more offspring would survive to adulthood than usual.

            1.3.8.2.9.4.5. Pro:
            Macro evolution faces [806] major challenges as there are numerous examples of mutations having a limit to how far they can go before they become detrimental.

              1.3.8.2.9.4.5.1. Con:
              Evolution is not for the maximum, it's the bare minimum. Yes, mutations may or may not be detrimental, but once they become beneficial, natural selection then gets to act on it.

          1.3.8.2.9.5. Con:
          The creation story was not meant to be taken literally. It merely delineates the stages in creation.

            1.3.8.2.9.5.1. Con:
            This is just one interpretation. The fact that there are multiple interpretations is confusing, but the Bible says that God is not the author of confusion [807], so the creation story cannot be true.

          1.3.8.2.9.6. Con:
          Genesis 2:21-22 [808] describes how human beings were created, not what they looked like. Therefore, modern humans may have evolved from the first humans, who may have looked very different.

            1.3.8.2.9.6.1. Con:
            Humans are scientifically considered a different species to the primate ancestors we have evolved from. To further this argument, humans can trace their evolution back to fish [809]. Genesis 2:21-22 describes the rib being taken from a man, not a male of any other species which we may have evolved from.

            1.3.8.2.9.6.2. Con:
            Genesis [810] describes man being created from dust. Dust is silicate; humans are carbon. It does not follow that man is made of dust.

              1.3.8.2.9.6.2.1. Con:
              Dust can also refer to mud, which can contain carbon [811].

            1.3.8.2.9.6.3. Con:
            Genesis 1 [812] describes humans being made in God's image. Phylogenetically speaking, the first human that is our direct ancestor is Homo-erectus [813]. By the above logic, God would have a flat face, pronounced brow ridge, dark skin and an upturned nose. None of these features are depicted in Christian representation of God.

          1.3.8.2.9.7. Pro:
          The findings of Charles Darwin on evolution of species are heavily supported [814] and widely accepted by modern scientists.

          1.3.8.2.9.8. Pro:
          Skeletal remains of species from the homo genus eg. Homo Erectus [815] provide evidence of life forms which pre-date the oldest known skeletal remains of a human (Homo Sapien) [816].

          1.3.8.2.9.9. Con:
          The belief in the ability to scientifically describe everything, including miracle of birth or human soul, is again only a belief. Moreover, there are many areas of spirituality and other related topics, which science does not even try to approach. Most humans tend to manufacture arguments to fit their beliefs. It works the same for priests as scientists alike.

        1.3.8.2.10. Pro:
        The existence of Creation (The Planet, Sun, Moon, Stars, Oceans, Trees, Animal and ourselves), point to the existence of a creator.Infinitely complex beings and Systems cannot be the result of random processes, adding a large amount of time is still not plausible as an intelligent force need to be actively creating and sustaining things together (ex:Throw a needle 🪡 and thread 🧵 and wait for a 🧥coat to make itself).

          1.3.8.2.10.1. Con:
          A seed can grow into a plant without the intervention of anything with a brain, so the idea that an intelligent force needs to actively create and sustain things is obviously wrong even before natural selection enters the picture.

        1.3.8.2.11. Con:
        An existence which creates a vast space and some random species, make them fight each other. It is as foolish as it sound.

        1.3.8.2.12. Con:
        The existence of a complex system cannot be justified by referring to the existence of a significantly more complex being. This raises the question of the origin of the creator which is an infinite regress.

          1.3.8.2.12.1. Pro:
          The probability of a complex creator is much lower than the probability of a life-permitting universe with no creator.

            1.3.8.2.12.1.1. Con:
            Complexity does not necessarily reduce the chance of something simply existing without a creator. Informed theists argue that the universe was created not simply because it is complex, but for more specific reasons.

            1.3.8.2.12.1.2. Pro:
            The arguments related to the Ultimate Boeing 747 Gambit [817] provides some insight into why a complex creator is less likely than no creator.

          1.3.8.2.12.2. Pro:
          Many religions consider God to be omniscient,  omnipotent and omnipresent. Each of these qualities requires the God to be at least as much and possibly significantly more complex than the universe which is predicted, controlled or embodied by God.

            1.3.8.2.12.2.1. Con:
            God as classically defined is perfectly simple. Knowledge, power and location do not require complexity if they are absolute and unchanging. God is not relative to the universe.

              1.3.8.2.12.2.1.1. Con:
              A hypothesis about (eg.) "a mind containing X amount of knowledge, which is magically perfectly simple" is necessarily more complex than a hypothesis about "a mind containing X amount of knowledge", so defining God as perfectly simple is only a further complication for all relevant purposes.

          1.3.8.2.12.3. Pro:
          If one suggests that complex things need a creator, they need to explain why the creator doesn't need a creator; otherwise, they have created an infinite loop.

      1.3.8.3. Con:
      All forms of teleological arguments require "teloi" ("purposes"). Until its existence is proven, teleological arguments all fail until purpose is shown to exist.

        1.3.8.3.1. Con:
        Just like you can't prove or disprove the existence of logic, you can't prove nor disprove the existence of purpose. It's an argument that leads nowhere.

        1.3.8.3.2. Con:
        Artificial purposes exist, such as the purpose of an oven is to heat food.

          1.3.8.3.2.1. Con:
          Artificial purposes do prove an agent with intent, but artificial purposes do not assist a teleological argument. Natural purposes are required for teleological arguments.

            1.3.8.3.2.1.1. Con:
            Teleological arguments infer the intent of a particular agent, the hypothetical creator of the universe. It's unclear how anything more is required for the argument to work.

        1.3.8.3.3. Pro:
        Natural purposes do not exist. Assuming purpose to a natural thing, such as a rock, a tree, or a planet, assumes a creator to prove a creator.

          1.3.8.3.3.1. Con:
          Natural purposes do exist. The heart's purpose is to pump blood throughout the organic system. The blood's purpose is to distribute nutrients (and take waste) to/from the organic system. Without these real purposes, the organic system would cease to function. Thus, teloi (natural and unnatural) exist.

          1.3.8.3.3.2. Con:
          Assuming natural things do not have purpose assumes a lack of a creator to prove a lack of a creator.

          1.3.8.3.3.3. Con:
          This only shows that the assertion of natural purposes is fallacious, but it doesn't follow from that that it is untrue.

        1.3.8.3.4. Con:
        Not all teleological arguments assume intrinsic purpose, despite the etymology of the argument's name.

          1.3.8.3.4.1. Pro:
          There are many forms of teleology, including notions like purpose and meaning but also directedness, about-ness, representation, intentionality, function, final causality, and tendency, each of which may be utilized in independent and mutually reinforcing teleological arguments.

        1.3.8.3.5. Con:
        This cycles back to the original thesis of proof for God's existence and does not reach an end in itself.

        1.3.8.3.6. Con:
        Belief in purpose is properly basic, as it is part of human experience. To defeat it, is has to be shown to be faulty or illusory, rather than assumed to be. The burden of proof is on the person trying to disprove the validity of a (nearly) universal intuition.

          1.3.8.3.6.1. Pro:
          If the universe has no meaning, it would be impossible for humans to figure that out. Similarly, if light did not exist in the universe (and therefore creatures with no eyes), nobody would know if it were dark. Darkness would mean nothing. [C.S. Lewis [818]]

            1.3.8.3.6.1.1. Con:
            Physicists in a universe whose physics worked very differently and did not include light could hypothesise about universes that did and confirm that their universe was dark.

              1.3.8.3.6.1.1.1. Con:
              While physicists might be able to make calculations, they wouldn't be "imagining light and dark" or giving those words meaning. Compare the case with the much discussed knowledge argument in relation to qualia in the philosophy of mind (en.wikipedia.org [819]).

                1.3.8.3.6.1.1.1.1. Con:
                Experiencing the qualia of light and dark is not necessary to answer the basic question of whether there is light in the universe. Mary can tell you confidently that cherries are red, even if she doesn't know what that "really means" in some qualitative sense.

          1.3.8.3.6.2. Con:
          The perception of purpose is as common as the perception of taste, but this should not be confused with the intuition that this perception reflects objective purpose/taste, or with the actual belief that objective purposes/tastes exist, which need not be so common.

      1.3.8.4. Con:
      Not everything that exists has a purpose. Thus, the fact that some things have purpose cannot be generalized to all things that exist.

        1.3.8.4.1. Con:
        Just because we don't know if certain things have a purpose does not mean that they don't have one.

        1.3.8.4.2. Con:
        Some teleological arguments neither assume nor require that all objects exhibit teleology. These include arguments from intentionality [820], consciousness [821], and the Fifth Way [822].

        1.3.8.4.3. Pro:
        Vestigial organs [823] are defined as organs that have no known function.

      1.3.8.5. Con:
      Purpose is human-made, in that it is something one wants to achieve, so the definition presupposes a conscious agent, making teleological arguments circular.

      1.3.8.6. Con:
      The teleological argument for the existence of God is purely wishful thinking by people who don't understand what they're observing.

      1.3.8.7. Con:
      Teleological arguments based on the existence of specific things are nullified if natural explanations are discovered for those things.

        1.3.8.7.1. Con:
        The discovery of a natural explanation is irrelevant for the existence of a designer. In other words: The teleological argument is not nullified by such discovery. An explanation of a Ford car in terms of Henry Ford as inventor and designer does not compete with an explanation in terms of mechanism and law, as professor John Lennox [824] points out.

        1.3.8.7.2. Con:
        Here John Lennox's arguments [825] come into play. A natural explanation based on laws is unsatisfactory unless we know how those laws were put in place.

          1.3.8.7.2.1. Con:
          Equating cosmological constants to laws dictated by minds is a false equivalence. Natural "laws" are not dictated rules that must not be broken. They are observations about the natural world.

        1.3.8.7.3. Pro:
        Charles Darwin [826] refutes the Teleological Argument. "The old argument of design in nature, as given by Paley, which formerly seemed to me so conclusive, fails, now that the law of natural selection has been discovered."

          1.3.8.7.3.1. Con:
          A creator God could have planned the use of mechanisms such as natural selection in order to get the intended result. Thus the discovery of natural selection does not fail the argument of design.

          1.3.8.7.3.2. Con:
          -> See 1.1.2.5.4.

          1.3.8.7.3.3. Pro:
          Richard Dawkins refutes [827] the Teleological Argument. "Natural selection is so stunningly powerful and elegant, it not only explains the whole of life, it raises our consciousness and boosts our confidence in science's future ability to explain everything else"

            1.3.8.7.3.3.1. Con:
            The assertion that science will one day explain everything cannot be disproven and is therefore not a scientific claim according to many.

            1.3.8.7.3.3.2. Con:
            The mutation selection mechanism lacks the power to generate novel organs and body plans. This short comings in this theory are widely acknowledged by leading evolutionary scientists rendering the ideas presented above a myth and misinformation. youtu.be [828]

          1.3.8.7.3.4. Con:
          The Theory of Evolution doesn't cover the entire scope of the teleological argument.  Arguments like fine-tuning via the cosmological constant or nuclear force, the strength of gravity etc, cannot be answered by evolution, because evolution has organized matter as a presupposition, but without these features, there is no organized matter.

        1.3.8.7.4. Pro:
        The absence of a presently adequate explanation is not reason to assume there is no adequate natural explanation. Every explanation we currently have was true and adequate before we understood it.

          1.3.8.7.4.1. Con:
          The fact that natural explanations have been found for some phenomenon is not a reason to believe that all phenomenon will one day have natural explanations just as a cancer cure which works in many cases can not be considered effectice for every case . The only possible proof would be to establish natural explanations for everything.

          1.3.8.7.4.2. Con:
          -> See 1.1.10.9.4.4.3.4.

        1.3.8.7.5. Con:
        This argument does not follow because "natural explanations" and "teleology" are not mutually exclusive. Natural explanations can only answer the question, "How?" but teleology addresses, "To what end?"

        1.3.8.7.6. Con:
        So far such natural explanations have not been found (i.e. modern evolutionary theory and the precise assignation of the values of the fundamental forces)

      1.3.8.8. Pro:
      Arguments from consciousness [829] support the existence of God.

        1.3.8.8.1. Con:
        Materialism provides a satisfactory explanation of consciousness which does not require a God.

          1.3.8.8.1.1. Con:
          Materialism can only explain how the brain interprets the information gathered by our senses. It does not explain how the interpreted information could be perceived (consciousness).

          1.3.8.8.1.2. Con:
          As philosophers like Nagel, Levine and Chalmers have shown, conscious states cannot be identified with physical / natural states. Hence, conscious states are incompatible with physicalism/naturalism, though they are compatible with theism. Thus, conscious states give us reason to prefer theism over physicalism/naturalism.

            1.3.8.8.1.2.1. Con:
            As most philosophers are physicalists [830], Nagel, Levine and Chalmers do not seem to have sufficiently shown this.

              1.3.8.8.1.2.1.1. Con:
              Philosophers could be physicalist because they have a preexisting bias against theism, not because the arguments of Nagel, Levine, and Chalmers are unpersuasive.

        1.3.8.8.2. Pro:
        Adams (1992) and Swinburne (1979) argue [831] deductively from the disjunction  that mental events either have a personal or natural scientific explanation. Given that mental events are genuinely nonphysical, a personal explanation demonstrates the mental and physical event type correlations. If the explanation is personal, then it is theistic.

          1.3.8.8.2.1. Con:
          All personal explanations are, at root, natural scientific explanations. Thus, there is no genuine distinction between the two, and the argument collapses.

          1.3.8.8.2.2. Con:
          Mental events are thoughts, which are the product of mind, an emergent property of brain. They are very physical.

          1.3.8.8.2.3. Con:
          Deductive reasoning presumes accurate premises. The non-physical nature of 'mental events' is not a premise with any backing, thus nullifying the deduction.

        1.3.8.8.3. Pro:
        The ability to reason must be a dispensation from a higher being because it can't come from the arational.

          1.3.8.8.3.1. Pro:
          The Catechism of the Catholic Church (#47) [832] declares that "the one true God, our Creator and Lord, can be known with certainty from his works, by the natural light of human reason (cf. Vatican Council I, can. 2 § 1: DS 3026)."

            1.3.8.8.3.1.1. Con:
            This is circular reasoning.  An institution that depends upon the existence of god produces literature that self-verifies its own claims.

          1.3.8.8.3.2. Con:
          Evolution provides a mechanism by which the irrational can produce the ability to reason, without recoursing to an even higher ability to reason magically already existing.

            1.3.8.8.3.2.1. Con:
            The Evolutionary Argument Against Naturalism [833] shows that because naturalistic evolution would involve our reasoning processes being optimized for survival and not truth, there are no reasons to trust the ability to reason given naturalistic evolution.

              1.3.8.8.3.2.1.1. Pro:
              Plantinga's formulation [834] of the evolutionary argument against naturalism is compelling: Belief in naturalism and evolution lead to the conclusion that human cognitive faculties are unreliable. This undermines any belief produced by these cognitive faculties, including those in naturalism and evolution.

                1.3.8.8.3.2.1.1.1. Con:
                If someone has a defeater for the belief that his cognitive faculties are reliable, that would defeat rational belief in theism just as easily as rational belief in naturalism.

                  1.3.8.8.3.2.1.1.1.1. Con:
                  The other premises in Plantinga's argument show that naturalism would entail that one cannot rationally affirm their beliefs. However, they do not show that theism would entail the same thing. Thus, it may be possible to rationally affirm theism if theism is true.

                1.3.8.8.3.2.1.1.2. Pro:
                This claim can be supported using an argument from Bayes' theorem [835], which provides a formula for calculating the likelihood of an outcome based on new or additional evidence.

                  1.3.8.8.3.2.1.1.2.1. Pro:
                  For every true positive belief about a topic, there exist a vast number of false positive beliefs about that same topic. Thus, the vast majority of possible positive beliefs are not true.

                    1.3.8.8.3.2.1.1.2.1.1. Pro:
                    There is one true belief about the Earth's shape (the Earth is [roughly] spherical) and many false beliefs (the Earth is flat, the Earth is cubic, the Earth is a truncated icosahedron [836], etc.)

                    1.3.8.8.3.2.1.1.2.1.2. Con:
                    Some true beliefs only have one corresponding false belief (e.g. "Penguins exist" vs. "Penguins do not exist").

                    1.3.8.8.3.2.1.1.2.1.3. Pro:
                    There is one true belief about the number of states in the United States (50), but many false beliefs (1, 49, 309612, 4, 80, etc.).

                    1.3.8.8.3.2.1.1.2.1.4. Con:
                    The probability that a positive belief is true is not necessarily brought down much by the existence of a comparatively vast number of possible false positive beliefs. Thus P(T) being very low does not follow from the vast majority of possible positive beliefs being false.

                  1.3.8.8.3.2.1.1.2.2. Con:
                  This implicitly assumes that evolution selects for specific beliefs, i.e. that we are born with a hardwired list of things to believe are true. This is very unlike how human brains developed, and very unlike humans, so is not relevant to human rationality.

                1.3.8.8.3.2.1.1.3. Con:
                Someone could have other reasons for believing his or her cognitive faculties are reliable that counteract the negative effect naturalistic evolution has on this probability. (web.ics.purdue.edu [837])

                1.3.8.8.3.2.1.1.4. Con:
                Michael Bergmann [838] (p. 4-5) successfully explains how a person's belief can be justified through nonpropositional evidence, even if all the propositional evidence is against them. Thus, one can justifiably believe in the reliability of his cognitive faculties even if evolution makes this reliability unlikely.

                1.3.8.8.3.2.1.1.5. Pro:
                Evolution has poorly designed a number of bodily systems. Given this, we cannot be confident that it has designed our cognitive faculties well.

                  1.3.8.8.3.2.1.1.5.1. Pro:
                  The pharynx [839] is poorly designed in that it is used for both food and respiration, creating a risk of choking.

                  1.3.8.8.3.2.1.1.5.2. Pro:
                  Allergies [840] are arguably a design flaw in the immune system.

                  1.3.8.8.3.2.1.1.5.3. Pro:
                  Cavities [841] in teeth can often cause far more pain than cancer, despite cancer being far more serious.

                  1.3.8.8.3.2.1.1.5.4. Pro:
                  The laryngeal nerve [842] takes an unnecessarily long path under the aortic arch.

                  1.3.8.8.3.2.1.1.5.5. Con:
                  This seems to be a problem for theism too; if God designed many of our bodily systems poorly, we cannot be confident he designed our cognitive faculties well.

                    1.3.8.8.3.2.1.1.5.5.1. Con:
                    The key difference is that under theism, God plausibly created us so that we can know him and love him. Given this, God would prioritize giving us good cognitive faculties over all other goals, since these are most essential to achieving the goal of humanity knowing God. However, naturalistic evolution has no reason to prioritize good cognitive faculties over other features that help survival.

                  1.3.8.8.3.2.1.1.5.6. Con:
                  Cognitive faculties of a human scale differ from other evolutionary developments in that their accuracy is paramount to their being worth the immense energy cost of maintaining a relatively large brain and its support systems.

              1.3.8.8.3.2.1.2. Con:
              There is a vast difference between a process that is merely less than perfectly reliable, and a process so unreliable that nothing it says can be rationally believed, and the evolutionary argument against naturalism rests entirely on lazily equivocating between the two.

                1.3.8.8.3.2.1.2.1. Con:
                The argument claims that human beliefs have no evolutionary incentive to be even somewhat reliable, as long as the correct behaviors are performed, whether they follow logically from the beliefs or not.

              1.3.8.8.3.2.1.3. Con:
              Examples of poor reasoning or irrationality in humans are abundant, so it is clear that human minds are not optimised for truth and one can't escape from engaging with that fact just by attributing it to naturalism/evolution and then rejecting those.

                1.3.8.8.3.2.1.3.1. Con:
                It is true that humans exhibit poor reasoning sometimes, but the point of the argument is that under naturalism it is highly likely that such a large fraction of human beliefs (almost 50%) would be false that effective reasoning would be impossible. This is far worse than having, say, 5% of one's beliefs being false.

              1.3.8.8.3.2.1.4. Con:
              Given the great value of truth for survival (knowing whether it is true that there are lions in the cave, knowing whether it is true that this mushroom is poisonous, etc.), the truth-aptness of human faculties could reasonably be explained using naturalistic evolution.

            1.3.8.8.3.2.2. Pro:
            Given how advantageous it is to accurately perceive one's environment and make accurate inferences from one's available information, it is predictable that evolution will select for accurate perceptions and reasoning faculties.

          1.3.8.8.3.3. Con:
          "The rational" is not known to be able to produce the ability to reason, so this does not follow.

            1.3.8.8.3.3.1. Con:
            "The rational" does not need to be known to produce the ability to reason. Deductive arguments regarding a scenario with two proposed possibilities need only establish itself as such and prove one impossible to prove the other factual.

              1.3.8.8.3.3.1.1. Con:
              It's not unambiguously such a deductive argument, and in any case where the impossibility of the one scenario is not completely proven, the weakness of the case for the other scenario remains relevant.

                1.3.8.8.3.3.1.1.1. Pro:
                The ability to reason could come from a rational entity which is not a higher being, thus it is not a deductive argument with 2 possibilities.

                1.3.8.8.3.3.1.1.2. Con:
                That deductive arguments with two possibilities must disprove one possibility to prove another is irrelevant here.

                  1.3.8.8.3.3.1.1.2.1. Pro:
                  The con to the deductive argument in question claims that it is invalid because the claim does not follow from the premise it provides. If it is a deductive argument with two possibilities and it claims that one has been disproven, that only leaves one left and it doesn't matter that it is not commonly known to be possible.

                    1.3.8.8.3.3.1.1.2.1.1. Pro:
                    The only way to counter a deductive argument like this is to demonstrate that there might be more than two possibilities or to show that one possibility hasn't been disproven.

                  1.3.8.8.3.3.1.1.2.2. Con:
                  The whole claim is structured around the argument not being unambiguously deductive - that's already an implicit concession that it's irrelevant to a deductive argument, so going on about deductive logic is unnecessary.

          1.3.8.8.3.4. Con:
          Computers can perform calculations far more exactly and quickly and accurately than any human, yet computers are made of unintelligent, unreasoning parts. Thus, such abilities may be emergent.

            1.3.8.8.3.4.1. Con:
            Computers were designed by intelligent humans, and the alleged role of God is designer/creator not raw material, so this is a bad example.

          1.3.8.8.3.5. Con:
          The fundamental physical interactions which underlie the operations of the human brain are not irrational. They are either rational (proceeding according to certain consistent rules and descriptions) or arational (not proceeding from or by reason). What they are not is irrational (contrary to reason).

          1.3.8.8.3.6. Pro:
          As C. S. Lewis identified [843] arguing from reason that God doesn't exists means someone has to  "ask me to accept a conclusion while discrediting the only testimony upon which that conclusion can be based" because irrational processes can't produce the ability to reason.

            1.3.8.8.3.6.1. Con:
            Nothing about evolution is irrational, inasmuch as it is a perfectly understandable and self-sustained process. The ability to reason is just like any ability (to walk, use tools etc.), hence it is also a product of evolution. What is more, the terms 'rational' or 'irrational' more strictly apply to how humans assess things with their reason to be such or the other.

            1.3.8.8.3.6.2. Con:
            Material processes are not irrational (in the sense of being opposed or contrary to reason) but rather a-rational (not exhibiting reason). If it is possible that the rational can emerge from the a-rational, then the parent claim would fail.

            1.3.8.8.3.6.3. Pro:
            Humans, possessing the ability to reason, have the capacity to create other things with the ability to reason, like computers and artificial intelligence.

            1.3.8.8.3.6.4. Con:
            Evolution is the only thing that has been empirically observed that can produce reason, and the evolutionary process lacks any identifiable mind or rationality.

        1.3.8.8.4. Pro:
        Consuming DMT is a pathway to encounters with autonomous entities [844]. Following such experiences, one in two atheists no longer identify as such.

          1.3.8.8.4.1. Con:
          Drug-induced hallucinations are not a reliable form of evidence.

            1.3.8.8.4.1.1. Pro:
            Psychedelic hallucinogens induce varying results in different people.

            1.3.8.8.4.1.2. Pro:
            People under the influence of DMA have yet to create equally valid evidence while sober.

          1.3.8.8.4.2. Pro:
          The entity that one encounters following DMT consumption [845] is often perceived as God.

          1.3.8.8.4.3. Con:
          A person's ability to rationally assess and test whether one is actually interacting with an autonomous being would naturally be hamstrung while tripping off a powerful hallucinogen.

          1.3.8.8.4.4. Con:
          DMT induced hallucinations are not a good source of truth.

        1.3.8.8.5. Con:
        There's no need for any additional mystic or supernatural quality to explain the existence of consciousness.

          1.3.8.8.5.1. Pro:
          In The Emperor's New Mind <en.wikipedia.org [846]>, Penrose proposes a very plausible explanation for the apearence of conscioussness.

            1.3.8.8.5.1.1. Con:
            AI researchers, philosophers, computer scientists and other experts deny the plausibility of the thesis contained in The Emperor's New Mind [847].

          1.3.8.8.5.2. Con:
          Such a naturalistic consciousness would necessarily be deterministic, and any conclusions derived from that consciousness would be the product of naturalistic biochemical reactions and non-directed processes; more akin to a cosmic Plinko game than rational thought. 
          
          If true, then the conclusion reached in the parent claim should not be assumed to be true, as the claimant could not have come to any other conclusion regardless of the evidence.

            1.3.8.8.5.2.1. Con:
            Evidence is linked biochemically to thought, via reactions in photoreceptors in the eyeball and so on, so one would come to the same conclusion "given the same evidence", not "regardless of the evidence".

            1.3.8.8.5.2.2. Con:
            Natural biochemical reactions are not deterministic, they derive from a great deal of chaos and randomness.

              1.3.8.8.5.2.2.1. Con:
              "Chaos" and "randomness" in a naturalistic universe merely imply that too many variables exist to be tracked by humans or our tools. Neither chemistry nor physics are random; they behave according to well-established principles which permit us to understand our universe using the tools of science.

              1.3.8.8.5.2.2.2. Con:
              If the parent claim is true, then consciousness is the product of chaos and randomness, and would produce random thoughts, not rationality. Therefore our reason could not be trusted.

            1.3.8.8.5.2.3. Con:
            The fact that no supernatural explanation is required does not mean that such consciousness is deterministic. Quantum mechanics are not supernatural and are not deterministic

              1.3.8.8.5.2.3.1. Con:
              It has not been shown that quantum events are non-deterministic. Some interpretations, such as the De Broglie–Bohm theory, have deterministic implications.

          1.3.8.8.5.3. Pro:
          AI investigation will eventually be able to explain the rise of conscioussness.

            1.3.8.8.5.3.1. Con:
            This is not only speculation, it is a "science of the gaps" argument. In other words, "we don't understand this, but someday, science will be able to fill in the gaps of our knowledge".

            1.3.8.8.5.3.2. Con:
            In The Emperor's New Mind [848], Penrose proposes that consciousness can never be modeled by a Turing machine. This includes modern AI systems.

    1.3.9. Con:
    Philosophy is not a reliable means to test the existence of God.

      1.3.9.1. Con:
      Philosophical arguments which originated during early human history, especially from career philosophers were in fact cogent and sound [849], and have contributed to the understanding of logic ever since.

      1.3.9.2. Pro:
      Philosophical evidence is down to interpretation depending on whether you believe in the evidence or not. The existence of God is purely down to the individual.

        1.3.9.2.1. Con:
        This is drifting into radical subjectivism. If applied consistently, it would undermine belief in all knowledge. Though problems with interpretation exist for any area of knowledge, some claims are more reasonable than others.

      1.3.9.3. Pro:
      Philosophical arguments are mostly theoretical, and not empirical.

        1.3.9.3.1. Con:
        A field being theoretical does not mean evidence from that field should be ignored.

          1.3.9.3.1.1. Pro:
          Mathematics is a largely theoretical, but still important, field.

        1.3.9.3.2. Con:
        Science and philosophy overlap, but they don't provide a clear answer [850] regarding the existence of God.

        1.3.9.3.3. Con:
        The parent claim assumes that philosophical arguments are "lesser" than empirical arguments, because they are theoretical. This argument is similar to a reverse slippery slope fallacy.

          1.3.9.3.3.1. Pro:
          Advancements in science have proved "verifiable" theories that became empirical false in the face of new evidence; therefore, we should not view a philosophical argument as less valid than an empirical argument based solely on the fact that the philosophical argument is currently only theoretical in nature.

        1.3.9.3.4. Pro:
        Philosophical arguments can support anything that you want to be true by using the right words and rhetoric. For example, God can be defined such that the conclusion is always ''God exists."

          1.3.9.3.4.1. Con:
          The observation that a definition is tailor-made in some way does nothing to show that the definition is a bad one, or that an argument is unsound.

          1.3.9.3.4.2. Con:
          The tacit assumption seems to be here that since God is defined as such, therefore the definition is haphazard. This simply does not follow logically.

          1.3.9.3.4.3. Con:
          An analogous argument would be that since accountants and frauds both use numbers but have different outcomes, therefore mathematics is unreliable. This obviously does not follow.

          1.3.9.3.4.4. Con:
          The fact that dishonest arguments exist does not preclude the duty to deal seriously with honest arguments.

          1.3.9.3.4.5. Con:
          Whether or not one feels that the word "God" should be defined in a particular way, this is not a reason to dismiss these arguments. If a being, so defined, has been shown to exist, the argument is a success and knowledge has been gained.

            1.3.9.3.4.5.1. Con:
            This is only true where the arguments of one known to manipulate can be analysed for logical fallacy.

            1.3.9.3.4.5.2. Con:
            The words of an any argument can be phrased to mean one thing which may be true, but presented in a way which leads the audience to an opposing conclusion.

          1.3.9.3.4.6. Pro:
          Masters in such discussions in ancient Greece were called sophists [851].

          1.3.9.3.4.7. Con:
          Philosophical arguments are often confused with rhetorical arguments. Sophists were masters of rhetoric, they were not philosophers. Rhetoric can be used to support unsubstantiated claims through use of fallacy, philosophy contrarily relies on internal and external consistency, and therefore excludes fallacy.

        1.3.9.3.5. Pro:
        More things are plausible than true. Supporting something with a philosophical argument is not the same as establishing its likelihood of actually being true.

          1.3.9.3.5.1. Con:
          Many philosophical arguments do not merely establish possibility but yield likelihoods or can be amended to yield likelihoods.

          1.3.9.3.5.2. Con:
          Philosophical arguments can establish either that a thing is plausible, or that it is true (depending on the argument). There is no way to dismiss all arguments in a blanket way—each one must be answered individually.

      1.3.9.4. Con:
      All human knowledge is grounded, at some level, on philosophical arguments. To dismiss philosophical argumentation is to reject the search for knowledge and embrace fideism [852].

      1.3.9.5. Con:
      The parent claim is itself an example of a philosophical argument. It cannot be used to dismiss philosophical argumentation without self contradiction.

      1.3.9.6. Con:
      By this logic, we can dismiss any argument for any competing view of reality (such as materialist atheism).

    1.3.10. Pro:
    Ontological arguments [853] support the existence of God.

      1.3.10.1. Pro:
      St. Anselm [854] made an ontological argument for the existence of God.

        1.3.10.1.1. Con:
        The definition [855] of God is not a being which nothing greater can be imagined.

          1.3.10.1.1.1. Con:
          The greatest imaginable being would likely have all the properties included in the classical definition of God, so if the argument shows that the greatest imaginable being exists then it follows that it would be God.

            1.3.10.1.1.1.1. Con:
            That isn't the argument used in the proof, if the argument is changed, the proof is changed.

          1.3.10.1.1.2. Pro:
          God is defined as the ultimate being, this is not necessarily the same as the ultimate imaginable being

        1.3.10.1.2. Con:
        It is not possible to imagine the greatest thing, because we can always imagine something greater than that, in the same way that it is not possible to imagine the highest number, because we can always imagine a higher one.

          1.3.10.1.2.1. Con:
          The claim falsely equates "maximal greatness", a qualitative measure, with "maximal magnitude", a quantitative measure. 
          
          It is true that a maximal magnitude cannot exist, so we use the term "infinity" to represent this imaginary concept. However, maximal qualities can exist. There does exist a maximally great "blue"... a blue that is unadulterated by any other color.

        1.3.10.1.3. Con:
        If God does not exist, and so is nothing, then we can easily imagine a being greater than God.

          1.3.10.1.3.1. Pro:
          This proof would only support the existence of something that nobody could imagine something greater then, that thing need not be God in the traditional sense.

          1.3.10.1.3.2. Con:
          Anselm's exact point is that any being that you can imagine as "greater than God" is, in fact, God.

          1.3.10.1.3.3. Con:
          To follow Anselm's argument, God as a "being than which no greater can be conceived" undermines any ability for God not to exist. The primal characteristic of a being that is greater than which any other can be conceived is that it *is*. It's being is essential to its definition. Therefore, this being must exist.

        1.3.10.1.4. Con:
        The syllogism's third premise--to exist is greater than to not exist--has issue.

          1.3.10.1.4.1. Pro:
          St. Anselm's proof (3rd premise) incorrectly treats existence as a property that can be predicated as part of a thing's definition; existence is better treated as a quantifier, as in first order logic.

            1.3.10.1.4.1.1. Pro:
            One can imagine a being with all God's other properties, this does not make it exist.

              1.3.10.1.4.1.1.1. Con:
              The primal characteristic of an inconceivable being (that is, the being that results by thoughtfully pursuing the greatest conceivable being) is that it exists: that is the definition of a 'being'. Therefore, to Anselm's argument, imagining this being implies that it can, and philosophically does, exist.

          1.3.10.1.4.2. Pro:
          "Greatness" is left ambiguous so as to equivocate [856] its use to correlate the attribute of existence to the definition of God implicitly. Regardless of how greatness is defined, a thing is not intuitively better for existing--disease would be better as only an idea; fantasies no longer exist at all upon being realized, as by definition it loses its critical defining quality--and so the premise that "existing is greater than not existing" remains unsubstantiated.

            1.3.10.1.4.2.1. Con:
            Intuitively, something that is great already as a concept would become even greater by existing.

        1.3.10.1.5. Con:
        Existence is not a property, let alone a better-to-have property. Things that exist are not better than things that don't exist.

        1.3.10.1.6. Con:
        Aquinas rejected [857] Anselm’s ontological argument. He held that God’s existence is “self-evident in itself” but not “self-evident to us”.

        1.3.10.1.7. Pro:
        Anselm's argument defeats the proposition that "just because it can be imagined does not mean it exists", due to the subject of 'being'. The greatest conceivable being not existing faces a blunt contradiction of its primary definition of 'being' vs a state of nonexistence.

        1.3.10.1.8. Con:
        The argument assumes classical Greek notions of causation; which are demonstrably false.  It has never been the case that a single cause has led to a single effect, and it is always the fact that multiple causes lead to multiple effects.

        1.3.10.1.9. Con:
        The idea of 'A being greater than B' is not given a proper definition within the argument. Therefore it's unclear whether definition 1. is logically consistent or not.

      1.3.10.2. Pro:
      The logician Kurt Gödel formulated a modal ontological argument [858] for God's existence.

        1.3.10.2.1. Con:
        Axiom 5 (Necessary existence is positive) is false, as modalities cannot have value, since they are purely descriptive. That is, it violates the fact/value distinction.

      1.3.10.3. Con:
      Ontological arguments go one step further than asserting the existence of something, and assert a relationship between that thing and something else. They are not an argument for any being's existence, rather an additional, equally unsupported claim about what that being might do in their spare time.

        1.3.10.3.1. Con:
        None of the ontological arguments rely on or conclude with any action taken by God, or any relationship God has with a specific thing.

          1.3.10.3.1.1. Con:
          Any ontological argument offered in support of this claim, and this god [859], are being used in support of a number of assertions, not just about the existence of something, but its nature and its habits. This is a major distinction between deism [860] and classical theism.

            1.3.10.3.1.1.1. Con:
            It is more parsimonious to assume that the God argued for in the ontological arguments is the same God concluded by the cosmological and teleological arguments. Therefore, we can reasonably conclude that the God of the ontological arguments did create the universe.

        1.3.10.3.2. Pro:
        It is reasonable that in a finite material universe there are things that have the greatest properties among others. However it is impossible to conclude that "one [861]" sentient entity is the owner of all this greatest properties.

      1.3.10.4. Pro:
      Alvin Plantiga has formulated an ontological argument [862].

        1.3.10.4.1. Con:
        Plantiga's argument takes as a premise that if atheism were to be true, it would be true contingently, rather than necessarily. This is not certain and without this premise the argument simply shows the triviality that if something has necessary existence as part of its definition it either necessarily exists or necessarily doesn't.

          1.3.10.4.1.1. Con:
          A priori does not entail necessary. The parent assumes a priori arguments establish necessary conclusions in virtue of being a priori. This conflates a metaphysical notion with an epistemological notion

        1.3.10.4.2. Con:
        Necessity cannot be a great-making property, as it isn't a property, strictly speaking.

          1.3.10.4.2.1. Pro:
          Given all the properties of some thing X, one can determine whether it is necessary, possible, impossible, or contingent by examining the two propositions "there is a possible world in which X exists" and "there is a possible world in which X does not exist". Based on the truth or falsity of the two propositions, the modal status of X follows.

            1.3.10.4.2.1.1. Pro:
            If true-true, it is possible but contingent. If false-true, it is impossible. If true-false, it is necessary. (False-false cannot occur)

        1.3.10.4.3. Con:
        Hypothetical worlds in which the monotheistic God doesn't exist are trivially possible. Therefore the monotheistic God doesn't have necessary existence.

        1.3.10.4.4. Pro:
        A condensed version of Plantiga's argument:
        (1 - DEFINITION) God is a being that either (a) exists in every possible world or (b) doesn't exist in any possible world
        (2 - ASSUMPTION) Let's assume (b) is false.
        (3 - CONCLUSION) Therefore (a) is true. This implies God exists.

          1.3.10.4.4.1. Con:
          Beings cannot be defined into existence. It is possible that a being defined by (1) does not exist.

          1.3.10.4.4.2. Con:
          It is unclear how a monotheistic God would be compatible with definition (1).

            1.3.10.4.4.2.1. Pro:
            -> See 1.3.10.4.3.

          1.3.10.4.4.3. Con:
          Assumption (2) is baseless. We could just as well assume that (a) is false, and conclude that God doesn't exist.

        1.3.10.4.5. Con:
        It is not known whether or not it is possible for a god to exist.

          1.3.10.4.5.1. Con:
          In the logical sense [863], anything which cannot be proved to be logically contradictory is "possible." It is known that God is not logically contradictory.

            1.3.10.4.5.1.1. Con:
            All claims require evidence. Saying "God is possible" is a claim. Since it has not been demonstrated that God is possible, the claim "God is possible" should not be accepted.

              1.3.10.4.5.1.1.1. Con:
              The claim "x is logically possible" is equivalent to the claim "x contains no logical contradictions." Evidence cannot be given for a lack of logical contradictions, except by disproving any proposed contradictions.

              1.3.10.4.5.1.1.2. Pro:
              The possibility of being true does not make a claim rational to believe. [864]

            1.3.10.4.5.1.2. Con:
            In terms of Platinga's argument it isn't sufficient to establish that God is logically possible. There are more constraints on the universe than logical or internal consistency, even if that internal consistency is that God is contradictory but still possible.

              1.3.10.4.5.1.2.1. Pro:
              Although there is nothing logically impossible about an atom with 4000 protons, it is physically impossible.

            1.3.10.4.5.1.3. Pro:
            The first section of Robert Maydole's ontological argument [865], from the beginning to the 23rd step, proves rigorously that supremity is not self-contradictory, so it must be possible. This is all that's necessary for the rest of the argument to work.

              1.3.10.4.5.1.3.1. Con:
              The third assumption made in Robert Maydole’s Modal Ontological argument begs the question by failing to consider an assumption that would resolve the contradiction, and thus simply assume the conclusion.

                1.3.10.4.5.1.3.1.1. Pro:
