                In the PBC part of the argument, the contradiction arose between P([aS]) and P([a~S]). Since the original assumption was □¬∃x(Sx), we must consider that it is also ◇¬P([aS]). If the contradiction could be resolved with this as a replaced premise (in place of premise 3), then the argument will fail. When you don't consider this at all, you assume that it is possible that a supreme being exists. I.e., you assume the conclusion and beg the question.

      1.3.10.5. Pro:
      Descartes [866] made an ontological argument for the existence of God.

        1.3.10.5.1. Con:
        Descartes was wrong, the fact that we can conceive of a perfect being does not make it real. Also his arguemnt is tautological, it asumes gods has certain charateristics and having such charcteristics proves he exists.

        1.3.10.5.2. Con:
        The structure of the argument can be used to prove that any being exists. I can use the same argument to 'prove' that a universe creating meatball exists.

        1.3.10.5.3. Con:
        Version A of the argument makes the mistake of assuming that a person decides truth.

        1.3.10.5.4. Con:
        Version B of the argument has an fallacious conclusion. It should read is "Therefore, I have an idea that a supremely perfect being exists."

        1.3.10.5.5. Con:
        Existence is not a predicate. It speaks to the quantity of something rather than its qualities. That is, necessary existence is not an essential quality of anything.

          1.3.10.5.5.1. Con:
          This is not Descartes argument. He believed that the idea of the infinite could not be conceptualizable to the finite human mind. Therefore it had to have a source outside it. That source is God.

        1.3.10.5.6. Con:
        The ability to describe a thing, such as "a supremely perfect being," is not the same as being actually able to imagine or comprehend it. For example, I can describe "A perfect sphere with exactly sixty-three corners and eight faces," but I can't imagine or comprehend it. In the latter case, the thing I described is definitionally impossible. That's not necessarily true of the former, but it is the same kind of statement, and being able to make the statement isn't proof it describes a true state

        1.3.10.5.7. Con:
        Properties can be contradictory, meaning that you cannot be maximal in both properties. As such, this argument is invalid.

          1.3.10.5.7.1. Pro:
          "Maximally Just" is contrary to "Maximally Merciful".

      1.3.10.6. Con:
      Ontological arguments require that 'existing' is a quality that must manifest to be 'maximal'. That is to say that it refuses a concept being sufficient; it confuses the conceptual and the actual.

      1.3.10.7. Con:
      A quote from the same article [867] claims that ontological arguments are not persuasive.

      1.3.10.8. Con:
      Robust refutations [868] of ontological arguments for the existence of God have reduced ontological proof for God to little more than the subject of parody.

        1.3.10.8.1. Pro:
        Kant provided a refutation [869] of ontological arguments.

          1.3.10.8.1.1. Con:
          Kant's objection doesn't apply to Plantinga's modal ontological argument  since that version of the argument doesn’t assume that existence is a property or perfection. See Questions on existence and certainty [870] and Kant’s Critique of the Traditional Arguments for the Existence of God [871]

            1.3.10.8.1.1.1. Con:
            A condensed version of Plantinga's argument [872]
            It is implicitly assumed that the being defined by (1) is real.

          1.3.10.8.1.2. Pro:
          Ontological arguments are tautological: if God exists, then God exists.

          1.3.10.8.1.3. Con:
          Kant believed that we needed an intuition from the object to give us knowledge of existence, which the concept alone could never give. His claim that existence is not a property is based on his model of perception and experience, which is problematic in a number of ways and not widely accepted by philosophers in the present day.

      1.3.10.9. Con:
      Ontological arguments for the existence of God misunderstand the emptiness of the universe and ascribe that emptiness the name God. This is basically the Primitive Buddhist position.

      1.3.10.10. Pro:
      Robert Maydole formulated a modal ontological argument [873] for God's existence

        1.3.10.10.1. Con:
        There are counter-examples to the second premise made (∀Y(P(Y) → (Z)(□∀x(Yx → Zx) → P(Z))).
        
        "Power tends to corrupt, and absolute power corrupts absolutely." It would seem that a perfection (i.e. power) implies an imperfection (i.e. corruption). [1 [874]]

          1.3.10.10.1.1. Con:
          Absolute power is the only amount of power that does not corrupt at all, because one in absolute power has nothing to gain from abusing his power, only something to lose.
          
          Example: If God exists, he would be considered to have absolute power, and also be considered to be the only perfectly un-corrupted entity in existence. However, the idea even works in relation to absolute monarchs, whose corruption was in general inversely proportional to the solidity of their reign.

            1.3.10.10.1.1.1. Con:
            Power corrupts not only in that it may be lost, or that one develops a thirst for it, although those are also factors, but in that it allows one to act unchecked. Consider for example how bored players in open world games often decide to play pranks, or outright murder the NPCs just because they can, an action that secures them no power.

          1.3.10.10.1.2. Con:
          The quote, "absolute power corrupts absolutely," is only an aphorism and has not been shown to be logically rigorous.

        1.3.10.10.2. Con:
        -> See 1.3.10.4.5.1.3.1.

        1.3.10.10.3. Con:
        The Barcan formula (a crucial element of the argument) should not be accepted.

        1.3.10.10.4. Pro:
        The S5 axiom of modal logic states that necessary statements must be either true or impossible. For instance, if it can be shown that it's possible that 2x2=4 (such as by bringing 2 sets of 2 pebbles together and counting them), 2x2=4 must be necessary in all possible worlds, and thus true. God's existence is a necessary statement, so if it is possible, it must be true.

          1.3.10.10.4.1. Pro:
          The definition of supremity used in the argument, that it is impossible for anything to be greater than a supreme being, is a necessary statement (since it contains the term "impossible"). If it is can be shown to be possible that it is impossible for anything to be greater than x, then it is impossible for anything to be greater than x, so a supreme being exists. Any supreme being would be considered God.

  1.4. Con:
  The classical [875] definition of God is contradictory or incoherent; thus, a classical God cannot, in principle, exist.

    1.4.1. Con:
    The various paradoxical assertions with the classical attributes of God can't be relied upon to negate the claim of God's existence, since the prime feature of God is that its existence does not rely upon logic.

      1.4.1.1. Pro:
      One cannot beat out an argument which argues that the object it supports to exist transcends beyond all human reasoning.

      1.4.1.2. Con:
      A God that is incoherent but exists anyway in defiance of logic is much less plausible than a God which makes sense, so if we rule out the latter that's still a win for team atheist even if God can't be deductively refuted.

      1.4.1.3. Con:
      Even an omnipotent being could not defy fundamental logic.

        1.4.1.3.1. Pro:
        The three laws of thought [876] must apply even to a god. A god must have the identity of a god, a god is either a god or not a god and a god must exist or not exist.

    1.4.2. Con:
    Inconsistencies and contradictions in how God is defined or described are the products of human limitations; once these are identified and rectified, an internally consistent depiction of God would emerge.

      1.4.2.1. Pro:
      If God exists, then they are incomprehensible to the human mind, so claims to the non-existence of God based on a lack of comprehension or coherence are invalid.

      1.4.2.2. Con:
      This is only valid if there exists a referent independent of the definition or description of God - that is, if God exists, which would be assuming the conclusion.

      1.4.2.3. Con:
      Such inconsistencies in how God is defined are inherent to the apparent evidence for such as God.

      1.4.2.4. Con:
      In classical monotheist definitions of God, God communicated/s with humans and is omnipotent and thus would have preempted such large inconsistencies the religions the large majority if not nearly all theist believers follow. Even if not, such rectifications have not occurred as of 2023 and would require very large changes, including to some of the religions' defining characteristics.

      1.4.2.5. Con:
      If such limitations exist, they are evidence that God is not Omnipotent (as he is unable to create humans without these cognitive limitations), Omniscient (as he it unable to foresee said limitations would cause issues) or Omnibenevolent (as he knowing and creating us with said limitations in mind, has intentionally obfuscated fundamental facts of his existence and creation)

      1.4.2.6. Pro:
      The logical inconsistencies of classical theism could simply be the result of faulty reasoning, in such a case God could still exist.

        1.4.2.6.1. Pro:
        The conclusion "God cannot in principle exist" does not follow from the premise "The 'classical' definition of God is contradictory or incoherent".

          1.4.2.6.1.1. Pro:
          There may be other definitions which are not contradictory or incoherent.

          1.4.2.6.1.2. Con:
          If the definition of 'God' used for the purposes of this debate is contradictory or incoherent, then for the purposes of this debate God does not exist.

        1.4.2.6.2. Pro:
        Not having a consistent definition of an object or phenomenon doesn't mean that it doesn't exist; reality exists independently of out concepts and definitions. Therefore, a better definition may be possible.

          1.4.2.6.2.1. Con:
          Truth claims that cannot be falsified by evidence, or truth claims that are self-contradictory are false. Thus, an internally inconsistent definition of something does mean that thing does not exist.

            1.4.2.6.2.1.1. Con:
            I define God as a force that cannot be defined. That being is  conceivable, in spite of not possessing the attribute that you want to imply upon Him. Also, quantum physics discovered that an electron is both a wave and a particle, which are still two opposite concepts, so a workaround had to be introduced.

          1.4.2.6.2.2. Con:
          Not having a consistent definition of an object or phenomenon prevents claims about its existence from having any impact or meaning.

            1.4.2.6.2.2.1. Con:
            First, there is no consistent definition of a spin in quantum physics, but that does not mean that it does not exist. Next, does it mean that if there is a consistent definition, then a defined object must exist. Then I will define a four-legged pink unicorn, etc...

            1.4.2.6.2.2.2. Con:
            A word has it's meaning before we give it a definition; so neither the absence of a coherent definition nor the difficulty in finding such a definition, strictly entails a term to be meaningless.

          1.4.2.6.2.3. Con:
          Each holy text of the 3 major monotheistic religions gives its own definition of God. If that definition appears to be incoherent or self-contradictory, the problem with suggesting "a better definition may be possible," is that each religion inherently asserts "no better definition than ours is possible."

        1.4.2.6.3. Pro:
        A transcendent God would naturally be difficult to describe with language, so an inconsistent definition is not a failure but a reasonable attempt to describe a transcendent concept.

          1.4.2.6.3.1. Con:
          A truly transcendent thing has no way of making contact with the material world. Any method by which contact is made cannot transcend material interaction.

            1.4.2.6.3.1.1. Pro:
            Therefore it is not only difficult to describe, it is impossible, since language itself is a material interaction.

          1.4.2.6.3.2. Con:
          This logic necessarily concedes that he cannot exist as hitherto defined under classical theism. While allowing some version of "God" to exist, it must necessarily be different from that described by Classical Theism.

          1.4.2.6.3.3. Con:
          An inconsistent definition does not allow for a rational discussion.

          1.4.2.6.3.4. Pro:
          God's infinite existence can be discussed - even proven - without defining that existence with the kind of precision reserved for humanly-knowable, finite objects. Indeed, any being that can be precisely defined by finite human language is not the truly existing infinite God. And this is no theological or philosophical cop-out. Science runs into the same kinds of problems even when it is dealing with finite objects.

        1.4.2.6.4. Con:
        If we cannot reasonably establish a definition of all powerful, all knowing, and all good, we cannot establish that God in the classical sense exists.

          1.4.2.6.4.1. Pro:
          If he does not exist in the classical sense, God could be an antagonist to be avoided or fought against.

          1.4.2.6.4.2. Con:
          The existence of a concept is not dependent on our ability to define it. At best, this would imply that we cannot know whether God exists, which is not the same as implying that God does not exist.

        1.4.2.6.5. Con:
        Suggesting something could exist does not support that it does.

          1.4.2.6.5.1. Pro:
          One could also suggest that fairies and unicorns could exist. Evidence proving they exist is vital for belief.

          1.4.2.6.5.2. Con:
          Suggesting that something could exist weakens the arguments claiming it can not exist.

        1.4.2.6.6. Con:
        An argument for faulty reasoning within Classical Theism actually begins to discredit the resulting theistic claim that a God exists through degrading the reasoning capabilities of those responsible for Classical Theism.

          1.4.2.6.6.1. Con:
          "Faulty reasoning" is proposed as an "alternate pathway" defeater and is not intended to be taken as fact supported by evidence, but only to show that the claim it counters is not necessarily true.

          1.4.2.6.6.2. Con:
          Per wikipedia definition [877], most monotheists agree on three qualities of God: all-knowing, all-capable and completely good. Qualities attributed to god in Classical theism are a superset of these, and considering this superset faulty does by no means discredit (mono)theism as a whole.

        1.4.2.6.7. Pro:
        There is no clear classical definition of God, because the attributes [878] of God are not well defined.

          1.4.2.6.7.1. Con:
          The attributes of God were clearly defined in the parameters of this discussion. No other attributes are relevant to this discussion.

          1.4.2.6.7.2. Con:
          All of the attributes of God have a proper definition.

            1.4.2.6.7.2.1. Con:
            Omnibenevolence cannot be properly defined because goodness cannot be objectively defined.

            1.4.2.6.7.2.2. Pro:
            Omnipotence means that God can actualize any possible world.

            1.4.2.6.7.2.3. Pro:
            Omniscience means that God possesses knowledge of all possible and actual states.

        1.4.2.6.8. Con:
        It means that the word God has no definition, and is therefore meaningless.

      1.4.2.7. Pro:
      Inherent contradictions may merely be apparent contradictions. Therefore, it should be elaborated further, rather than abandoned entirely.

      1.4.2.8. Pro:
      Apparent contradictions in classical theism may appear from translation difficulties.

        1.4.2.8.1. Con:
        If the definition of God used for the purpose of this debate is contradictory due to translation issues, that would still mean that, per the definition of God used in this debate, the definition of God is contradictory and so He does not exist.

        1.4.2.8.2. Pro:
        Possible contradictions in classical theism may appear from transcription difficulties and errors introduced over centuries of existence.

          1.4.2.8.2.1. Pro:
          The source ideas for classical theism - the Bible, the works of Plato, Aristotle, etc - had already existed in documented form for centuries before the classical period. The oldest Bible, for example, is from 330-360 AD [879]. The multiple rounds of transcription and retranscription that preserved those source documents over the thousands of years to today are likely to have introduced errors/ caused loss of subtlety in the original conceptions.

            1.4.2.8.2.1.1. Con:
            The oldest document of the Bible is the Septuagint with fragments dating back to 300BCE. (Of course consisting only of the Old Testament as the events of the New Testament have not yet happened).

              1.4.2.8.2.1.1.1. Con:
              The Septuagint is itself the translation into Greek of a number of earlier texts in Hebrew [880]. While there are fragments of (Hebrew) Biblical text dating back to 300 BC (and before [881]), the oldest complete Bible (compared to the book in its current form) would probably be one containing both Old and New Testament - hence the date of 300 AD. In either case, an older Bible makes an even stronger case for transmission/ transcription errors.

            1.4.2.8.2.1.2. Con:
            The original domcuments of the Bible were transcribed in several different locations, by several different monasteries, each with detailed systems in place to ensure as accurate a transcription as possible. An error made in one transcription did not infect the others due to their isolation. Therefore, we can cross examine these documents to identify and eliminate errors.thebibleproject.com [882]

              1.4.2.8.2.1.2.1. Con:
              Comparing multiple independent transcripts does help with scribal error, but it does not guarantee *perfect* transmission. Another possible problem comes from the choice of certain sources as more authoritative than others. The Masoretic Text [883], for instance, is considered the source book for the Hebrew Tanakh (Bible). Though there may be good reason to choose that source as more correct than others, this also ensures that errors/ interpretations in the Text itself become part of future canon.

          1.4.2.8.2.2. Pro:
          One apparent contradiction in classical theism is that of a theistic God changing its mind. God, per Plato [884], is changeless because a) nothing can change God, and b) God would not change, because it is already perfect. Parts of the Bible, like God repenting the creation of humanity, therefore seem to contradict theism. The problem might exist because today's Bible carries an edited version of the source documents of the Old Testament. 'Repent' might not carry the subtleties of the original text.

          1.4.2.8.2.3. Con:
          An omnipotent God should be able to keep the record of itself clear enough for it to be not contradictory.

          1.4.2.8.2.4. Con:
          Meaning that belief in the "present" definition is wrong, and since the "true" definition has been lost, there's no way to prove or disprove the existence of this definition, but surely proves that the present definition doesn't exist.

        1.4.2.8.3. Pro:
        The ideas within classical theism come from a number of thinkers, including those as far back as Plato and Aristotle. These ideas were not developed in English, and even in their native languages, were reliant on the context of their times for the subtleties of meaning involved. The version of theism we’re debating, off Wiki, is already a compressed version of a complicated concept developed in multiple languages and over several centuries. It would be more extraordinary if there were no errors.

        1.4.2.8.4. Con:
        In theory, a perfect God would be capable of communicating to man in such a way that all possible translations of the original message would still be accurate. In other words, He could make the "word of God" fool-proof; a message so utterly perfect, no man could ever misconstrue or spoil it.

      1.4.2.9. Pro:
      While the terms used to define the God of classical theism may be inconsistent, it does not follow that the referent is inconsistent. It simply means the terms used to describe God should be refined.

        1.4.2.9.1. Pro:
        The Anselmian Perfect Being Theology approach to the concept of God is to understand the various attributes of God in the manner which makes for the maximal *possible* greatness. If the alleged contradictions cannot be otherwise resolved, then the attributes should be re-described to give the maximal possible greatness. Thus the paradoxes are resolved but God remains "that being than which a greater cannot be conceived".

        1.4.2.9.2. Con:
        Refining the classical definition of God would amount to denying that God, as classically defined, exists.

          1.4.2.9.2.1. Con:
          Need for refinement of a definition does not necessarily imply lack of accuracy, but could suggest lack of precision.

            1.4.2.9.2.1.1. Pro:
            Refinement in this case could mean reducing ambiguity, thereby improving coherency.

          1.4.2.9.2.2. Pro:
          The discussion has defined god, as the god of classical theism. To redefining god by different terms, is to in turn to redefines the entire discussion, as objections and evidence supporting and opposing such a being's existence, drastically change.

          1.4.2.9.2.3. Pro:
          The definition(s) is all we have in this case, and some may argue it's all we can get. Therefore it's sensible to argue about what we have.

    1.4.3. Con:
    Only Christian conceptions of God may be contradictory or incoherent. This does not generalize to other religions.

      1.4.3.1. Pro:
      The Latter-day Saint Godhead [885] is biblically more accurate than the classical definition of God because it lacks the biblical misinterpretations of the Trinity found in "classical" Christianity [886].

        1.4.3.1.1. Pro:
        The Father is the ultimate focus of worship, the Son is Lord and Redeemer, and the Holy Spirit is the messenger and revealer of the Father and the Son.

        1.4.3.1.2. Pro:
        Although the Godhead consists of three separate beings, they are perfectly united [887] in purpose, doctrine, and bringing to pass Heavenly Father’s divine plan of salvation.

        1.4.3.1.3. Pro:
        According to Latter-day Saint belief and the Bible, the Father (John 5:17,19-21,25-26 [888]) and the Son (Luke 24:39 [889]) have tangible, glorified bodies of flesh and bone, and the Holy Ghost is a personage of spirit (1 Corinthians 6:19 [890]).

        1.4.3.1.4. Con:
        The teachings of LDS founder Joseph Smith were more trinitarian in his earlier teachings [891], and remnants of trinitarianism persist [892] in LDS teachings today.

      1.4.3.2. Pro:
      The word 'God' refers to a concept that is defined by its valid functional role [893] within a conceptual scheme.

      1.4.3.3. Pro:
      Islam maintains a definitional framework for Allah that encompasses all his attributes without contradiction.

        1.4.3.3.1. Pro:
        The same applies to the Christian God. The Bible maintains a consistent definition of him, and there isn't a single flaw or contradiction found in the Christian God's nature throughout the Bible.

          1.4.3.3.1.1. Con:
          As Richard Dawkins said in The God Delusion: "The God of the Old Testament is arguably the most unpleasant character in all fiction: jealous and proud of it; a petty, unjust, unforgiving control-freak; a vindictive, bloodthirsty ethnic cleanser; a misogynistic, homophobic, racist, infanticidal, genocidal, filicidal, pestilential, megalomaniacal, sado-masochistic, capriciously malevolent bully. Those of us schooled in his ways can become desensitised to their horror. [894]"

        1.4.3.3.2. Con:
        There are contradictions in the Quran. Although murder is forbidden by law [895], there are other instances in the Quran where followers are instructed to kill others [896], seemingly unrighteously.

      1.4.3.4. Con:
      As this debate is about a specific concept of God, an argument does not need to generalize any further in order to be valid.

    1.4.4. Con:
    The classical notion of God is an over-simplified explanation of complex phenomena, intended to avoid time consuming debate of difficult problems.

    1.4.5. Pro:
    The problem of evil [897] implies that God does not exist: a God who is both omnipotent and omnibenevolent would have created a world without sin and excess suffering or be able or willing to eliminate it from the world as it exists.

      1.4.5.1. Con:
      Evil is a privation of good, and does not exist in its own right. Thus, God does not co-exist with evil.

        1.4.5.1.1. Con:
        If evil [898] is a privation [899] of good, then a thing that has no flaw is perfectly good by definition. But if it is perfectly good, then it is "omni-benevolent."

          1.4.5.1.1.1. Con:
          There is no reason to assume that good is the natural state that evil is a privation from.

            1.4.5.1.1.1.1. Pro:
            Evil has not been established to be accurately described as a mere lack of good. For example, if donating a billion dollars to a specific charity were good, failing to make that donation would not mean you are evil.

          1.4.5.1.1.2. Con:
          Flawlessness does not necessitate moral goodness.

            1.4.5.1.1.2.1. Con:
            Flawlessness by definition necessitates moral goodness. For if a thing was not morally good, how could that thing be flawless? Its lack of moral goodness would be a flaw. But if it truly is flawless, then it is necessarily morally good.

              1.4.5.1.1.2.1.1. Con:
              There are no mentions of moral goodness in the definition for flawlessness [900]. Furthermore, morally neutral things can be considered flawless.

          1.4.5.1.1.3. Con:
          "Caring about the wellbeing of humans," as the God of classical theism (particularly Abrahamic religions) is described to, in no way relates to lacking privations: it is something that must be added on to the description. Otherwise, God could be like that described by Aristotle or Spinoza (which care nothing about humans, if they can be said to care at all).

          1.4.5.1.1.4. Con:
          If evil is just a privation of good, then nothing is wrong. It could only be properly called "less good". This would mean that murder, rape, etc, are not wrong, they are simply less good.

            1.4.5.1.1.4.1. Con:
            The words "evil" and "wrong" still have meaning in the context of the privation view of evil. "Wrong" would refer to some action which corrupts the good. And evil could refer to a state of affairs strongly contrary to the good. But thinking of things as "less good" is in fact a more precise characterization, as it contains the privation aspect of evil, where the simple word "evil" can be misunderstood to imply a separately existing standard or entity by which evil is measured.

              1.4.5.1.1.4.1.1. Con:
              If things can only exist on different gradations of goodness, then rape and murder must be inherently good in some way. The fact that they exist does not make them good.

                1.4.5.1.1.4.1.1.1. Pro:
                If a god punishes things that are good, then the god lacks justice and is therefore not omnibenevolent.

                1.4.5.1.1.4.1.1.2. Con:
                Rape and murder are actions which corrupt that which is already good. To the extent they fail to completely destroy the goodness in a thing, they fail to be completely evil. But in order for a rape or murder to take place, there must be a living innocent to start with. There must be some good which is corrupted (or destroyed) by the evil act.

                  1.4.5.1.1.4.1.1.2.1. Con:
                  If it were true that things were evil solely because of the of good they destroyed, murdering an innocent 4 year old would be less wrong then murdering an old rapist, but these acts are equally wrong, therefore, it can not be true that things are evil solely because of the amount of good they destroy.

                  1.4.5.1.1.4.1.1.2.2. Con:
                  This implies that if a person were not innocent or good, that they cannot be raped.

                    1.4.5.1.1.4.1.1.2.2.1. Con:
                    Anyone being raped by definition is innocent. No one deserves rape. That's what rape is: undeserved, forced sex.

                  1.4.5.1.1.4.1.1.2.3. Con:
                  If murder is evil and murder exists, then evil exists. If evil exists, then it is not a mere lack of good.

              1.4.5.1.1.4.1.2. Con:
              If evil is contrary to "the good" then it cannot be considered a privation. It must be its own idea.

                1.4.5.1.1.4.1.2.1. Con:
                Cold is considered contrary to heat, yet cold does not exist in its own right, it is merely a privation of heat. Thus, that two concepts can be contrary says nothing about one being a privation of the other.

            1.4.5.1.1.4.2. Pro:
            Nothing is purely 100% totally "wrong" in this sense, because everything that exists, no matter how corrupted it be, still exists, and existence itself is good.

              1.4.5.1.1.4.2.1. Pro:
              Many areas of science do not rely on 100% certainty. Science is iterative. You hypothesize and then experiment to disprove and refine your hypothesis.

              1.4.5.1.1.4.2.2. Con:
              If things can only exist on different gradations of goodness, then rape and murder must be inherently good in some way. The fact that they exist does not make them good.

            1.4.5.1.1.4.3. Pro:
            -> See 1.4.5.1.1.1.1.

        1.4.5.1.2. Pro:
        If evil were completely annihilating of the good then it would have to annihilate itself because it depends upon the good of existence to manifest itself as lack.

        1.4.5.1.3. Con:
        Pain is not a privation, but rather an added, present sensation. The absence of pain is not pleasure nor joy but just analgesia. All else being equal, pain is an evil.

        1.4.5.1.4. Con:
        By this logic, if God existed, there would be less privation of good. As there is too much privation of good, it follows that God doesn't exist.

        1.4.5.1.5. Con:
        If evil can be defined as privation of good, then good can also be defined as privation of evil. We must either accept that both good and evil co-exist, or that neither exists.

          1.4.5.1.5.1. Pro:
          If good is defined as the level to which one resists and overcomes evil, then evil must be present to be resisted. "Good" cannot exist without "evil" any more than "hot" can exist without "cold". These are comparative concepts in nature, and thus require opposition to necessitate their existence.

            1.4.5.1.5.1.1. Con:
            -> See 1.4.5.1.1.4.1.2.1.

          1.4.5.1.5.2. Con:
          This argument relies on a mis-understanding of the above argument.  It looks as good and evil as a subjective measure whereas this line of reasoning relies on it as an objective truth.

          1.4.5.1.5.3. Con:
          To borrow an analogy from Dr. Frank Turek [901], if you take all of the rust out of the car, you have a better car; if you take all of the car out of the rust, you have nothing!
          
          Goodness can exist without evil, but evil cannot exist without goodness.

        1.4.5.1.6. Con:
        Darkness is a privation. But, according to Genesis, God said "Let there be light [902]," and there was light.

        1.4.5.1.7. Con:
        Neither are good nor evil ontological categories. Neither exist on it's own. Both are consequences of discrimination, which borrows it's ontology from the observer.

          1.4.5.1.7.1. Pro:
          If good and evil are derived from the ontology of the observer, an omnibenevolent god does not have a fundamental existence.

        1.4.5.1.8. Pro:
        -> See 1.4.5.1.1.4.1.2.1.

      1.4.5.2. Con:
      Moral realism is required for the "problem of evil" to exist, but is also incompatible with atheism. Therefore, the problem of evil is self-defeating and can be argued to actually provide evidence of God's existence.

        1.4.5.2.1. Con:
        Atheism and moral realism are compatible therefore the "problem of evil" still persists. Moral realism can be observed in nature through the intuition that some actions are intrinsically and universally wrong, without appealing to God.

        1.4.5.2.2. Con:
        The problem of evil highlights a contradiction in a theistic and moral realist worldview; one need not actually believe evil is objectively real to posit it, only that certain things would be objectively evil if there was such a thing.

        1.4.5.2.3. Con:
        The problem of evil can be phrased as follows: if God exists, moral realism is true. If moral realism is true, genuine evil exists. If genuine evil exists, then God doesn't exist. Thus, if God exists, God doesn't exist. It follows that God doesn't exist.

        1.4.5.2.4. Pro:
        The concept of evil works only if God exists to define right and wrong. Therefore, atheists do not have a moral basis for calling something evil is in a purely physical world.

          1.4.5.2.4.1. Con:
          The existence of evil does not require a moral law beyond man.

            1.4.5.2.4.1.1. Pro:
            Even devoid of some system, someone can believe things right, or wrong, which in turn would mean there are good, and evil.

            1.4.5.2.4.1.2. Con:
            A universal position held by all human beings, (such as "murder is wrong"), suggests a Universal Mind.

          1.4.5.2.4.2. Pro:
          The fact that people presuppose evil exists in the acceptance of using it to refute a particular god suggests that they presuppose an objective moral arbitration, which requires a god.

            1.4.5.2.4.2.1. Con:
            The existence of morality doesn't imply an objective or absolute morality. Subjective morality is still morality.

            1.4.5.2.4.2.2. Con:
            -> See 1.3.7.3.4.3.1.2.

          1.4.5.2.4.3. Con:
          Empathy - which enables us to determine how we would feel about the experience - can be used to evaluate actions as good and bad.

            1.4.5.2.4.3.1. Pro:
            Pain is common, yet people react strongly to people, when they are in pain. This points to empathy being a hardwired moral ability to detect evil.

              1.4.5.2.4.3.1.1. Con:
              People do get desensitized to pain and evil.

              1.4.5.2.4.3.1.2. Pro:
              Understanding other people is evolutionarily advantageous.

            1.4.5.2.4.3.2. Pro:

            1.4.5.2.4.3.3. Con:

          1.4.5.2.4.4. Con:

            1.4.5.2.4.4.1. Pro:

            1.4.5.2.4.4.2. Con:

              1.4.5.2.4.4.2.1. Con:

              1.4.5.2.4.4.2.2. Con:

              1.4.5.2.4.4.2.3. Pro:

      1.4.5.3. Pro:

        1.4.5.4.1. Con:

      1.4.5.5. Con:

          1.4.5.5.2.1. Con:

      1.4.5.6. Con:

        1.4.5.6.1. Pro:

          1.4.5.6.1.1. Pro:

          1.4.5.6.1.2. Con:

        1.4.5.6.2. Con:

        1.4.5.6.3. Con:

        1.4.5.7.1. Con:

      1.4.5.8. Pro:

      1.4.5.10. Pro:
      There is no way an omnipotent all-loving god could co-exist with evil. The two are mutually exclusive by definition. Evil does not merely "imply" that God does not exist, it disproves his existence entirely.

        1.4.5.10.1. Con:
        God may have created the universe but the Bible indicates that people later might have deviated from God's vision through free will.

          1.4.5.10.1.1. Pro:
          Initially, according to the Bible, there was no sin or death in God's creation. When Satan fell [909] (fuller discussion) [910] he tempted Eve to trespass the law of God by eating the fruit from the tree of the knowledge of good and evil. Eve offered this fruit to her husband Adam and he knowingly committed sin. Therefore, sin entered the world and death by sin. God gave man a free will and man brought evil into the world by improperly using that free will.

          1.4.5.10.1.2. Con:
          Nothing in the Bible suggests that the physical nature of humans have been modified from the original design by God. Hence the biological nature of homosexuality, which is backed by a huge scientific consensus, is the will of God.Diversity in human sexuality (2015 meta-study by the Academy of Science of South Africa) [911]

          1.4.5.10.1.3. Con:
          In Matthew 9:11-12 Jesus implies within his cultural context [912] that some people are born homosexual.

            1.4.5.10.1.3.1. Con:
            This could be due to evil coming into world after it's creation.

        1.4.5.10.2. Pro:
        A solution would be the existence of an all-knowing, all-powerful but evil god; this strongly violates classical theism's assumptions [913].

          1.4.5.10.2.1. Con:
          An evil god does not solve the problem of evil, because it is just as strongly disproved by the existence of good in the world as a good god is disproved by the existence of evil.

          1.4.5.10.2.2. Con:
          That is incorrect. It could also be that God has other purposes that we do not understand that are served by giving men free will, even if they use it horribly.

            1.4.5.10.2.2.1. Con:
            Any such purpose would imply that God either can't do away with evil or does not want to, which circles back to the initial claim in this respect [914].

          1.4.5.10.2.3. Con:
          The "evil god" hypothesis assumes the existence of a higher standard of morality, and thereby recognizes that this so-called "god" is a pretender to the throne of the God whose nature and character is the very source of morality.

          1.4.5.10.2.4. Con:
          The "evil god" hypothesis imports our own definition of good and evil into that reality and does not evaluate the reality forged by that god on its own terms.

            1.4.5.10.2.4.1. Pro:
            By recognizing a standard of good and evil that supervenes even on other possible worlds, this claim acknowledges that good and evil are necessary objects. This provides evidence that God necessarily exists in our world and all possible worlds, and the standard of goodness set by his character exists in all possible worlds.

        1.4.5.10.3. Pro:
        Many theists base their belief in God on good in the world, while the stories in their religious texts tend to depict the Gods of classical theism as selfish evil tyrants with bad attitudes, short tempers, lust for death and destruction, and a general disregard for the value of anything other then themselves, even when those same texts defined such Gods as being good no matter what they do.

          1.4.5.10.3.1. Con:
          The Christian God is depicted as slow to anger and rich in mercy.  Jonah actually complains to God that he is not nearly vengeful enough. (Jonah 4)

          1.4.5.10.3.2. Con:
          God's justice and wrath against immorality is not evidence that God is an evil, selfish, tyrant.

          1.4.5.10.3.3. Pro:
          The god of Abraham is described as genocidal. Genocide is immoral. Therefore, moral arguments for the god of Abraham are invalid if they are based on omnibenevolence.

            1.4.5.10.3.3.1. Con:
            The "genocides" depicted in the Old Testament were God's punishment against evil people for their immorality. As such Genocide is not intrinsically immoral.

              1.4.5.10.3.3.1.1. Con:
              Hitler's attempted genocide was punishment for his perceived evil nature of Jewish people, yet it was still immoral.

            1.4.5.10.3.3.2. Pro:
            According to Genesis 6-9 [915], God was the first entity to commit genocide, thereby inventing the practice.

            1.4.5.10.3.3.3. Pro:
            According to Exodus 11-12 [916], God committed genocide against the first born of Egyptian households.

            1.4.5.10.3.3.4. Pro:
            According to Judges 20 [917], God commits genocide against the Benjaminites.

              1.4.5.10.3.3.4.1. Con:
              This is either a deliberate and gross misrepresentation or completely ignorant account of God’s actions as they relate to Israel’s first civil war.

            1.4.5.10.3.3.5. Con:
            Many of the genocides [918] attributed to God are actually commanded by humans (such as Moses), and carried out by humans, not God.

              1.4.5.10.3.3.5.1. Con:
              In many cases where God does not explicitly order the genocide, he encourages it or does nothing to stop it. This still makes God complicit in these genocides.

              1.4.5.10.3.3.5.2. Con:
              In the particular case of Amalekites (1 Samuel 15) God is unambiguously responsible for the genocide.

              1.4.5.10.3.3.5.3. Con:
              In their historical contexts, it is likely that the biblical accounts did in fact intent to attribute these commands to God.

        1.4.5.10.4. Con:
        If God does not exist, there is no basis upon which to affirm objective moral values and duties. Therefore, talking about the morality of God is meaningless.

        1.4.5.10.5. Con:
        There is a way and it is called a free will. Free will is not a valid argument against the classical problem of theodicy, since nobody will wish an evil to himself. But in dharmic religions there is a concept of karma, which means that reactions to our actions are delayed. The purpose is to educate us about what is good and evil, by a personal experience of what is favorable and what is not. Karma explains why is there evil alongside with loving God.

          1.4.5.10.5.1. Con:
          There are many forms of suffering that cannot be attributed to karma. Terrible things happen to even the most innocent and good people.

          1.4.5.10.5.2. Con:
          If the purpose of suffering is to educate us then an omnipotent God could have just created us with that education in the first place, thereby avoiding the need for anyone to suffer.

          1.4.5.10.5.3. Con:
          Dharmic religions also have the concept of "Brahman"... the ultimate reality in the universe. According to Chandogya Upanishad 3.14.1, "This whole universe is Brahman. In tranquility, let one worship It, as Tajjalan (that from which he came forth, as that into which he will be dissolved, as that in which he breathes)."
          
          If all is Brahman, evil and good are essentially identical. Karma cannot explain how to differentiate between two identical things.

          1.4.5.10.5.4. Con:
          According to the doctrine of Karma, all evil that happens to us is brought upon us by our own past evils.
          
          This cannot describe how the first evil was done to us, before we had performed an evil deed. It requires an infinite regress of past lives, or a non-karmic evil to start the chain of karma, which denies the premise that all evil is the result of past evil.

          1.4.5.10.5.5. Con:
          Free will does not justify the ability to execute the will to cause harm, just like free will does not grant the ability to fly (unaided) to those who want to fly.

        1.4.5.10.6. Con:
        There is no reason they are mutually exclusive.

          1.4.5.10.6.1. Pro:
          If a set of claims is consistent, then any subset of that set is also consistent. The following set of claims is consistent: (1) God exists, (2) God has a good reason for allowing evil, (3) Evil exists. Therefore the set comprising (1) and (3) is consistent and there is no incompatibility here.

            1.4.5.10.6.1.1. Pro:
            This "consistency strategy" would fail only if (2) were logically impossible. However, that doesn't seem to be the case. For example, even if the free will defence doesn't provide God's *actual* reasons for allowing evil, it does provide a *possible* reason, and that is all the consistency strategy requires to show there is no logical contradiction between (1) and (3).

          1.4.5.10.6.2. Con:
          A good and able God would both want and be able to stop evil.

            1.4.5.10.6.2.1. Con:
            We don't always do everything that we want to do and are able to do. Sometimes doing those things is incompatible with other things we want.

        1.4.5.10.7. Con:
        The concept of evil could not exist without a moral standard. The existence of God creates a moral standard to define good and evil.

          1.4.5.10.7.1. Con:
          The mere existence of God doesn't create a moral standard of good and evil. It would be God's words and actions that would define good and evil.

            1.4.5.10.7.1.1. Con:
            It's possible that, through His nature, God embodies, rather than commands, what is right. In this view, God and morality are the same thing.

              1.4.5.10.7.1.1.1. Con:
              The Euthyphro dilemma is not avoided by equivocating "God" with "Good" (i.e. morality).

                1.4.5.10.7.1.1.1.1. Pro:
                Supposing "God" and "Good" were synonyms, it would be impossible for a person to be (or do) Good in this sense without that person necessarily also being God. In other words, there would be no function in discussing "the moral capacity of human beings" since Good is reserved by (and indistinguishable from) God.

                1.4.5.10.7.1.1.1.2. Pro:
                Supposing "God" and "Good" are synonyms and also that there remain actions which are morally right or morally wrong for a person to do, the provision of synonyms only tells us about the qualities of God, but has yet to provide any insight whatsoever into distinguishing actions which are morally right from those that are morally wrong. Note the ironic resemblance of this problem to that in the Euthyphro [919] itself (Euthyphro's third definition [920] of "piety").

              1.4.5.10.7.1.1.2. Con:
              Embodiment of a characteristic is distinct from being the cause of it. Regardless of whether or not God embodies what is right, this concept of what is right can and does exist independently of God. Thus, there is no distinct proof for the existence of God based on the existence of morality. Ultimately, then moral arguments for God still fail.

              1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3. Pro:
              The classical definition of God historically includes Divine Simplicity [921], which implies that God is without parts [922] or distinct components or attributes.

                1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3.1. Pro:
                The parts of even the most fundamental material components of the universe, such as elementary particles, are still composed of at least two metaphysical parts: potentiality and actuality.

                  1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3.1.1. Con:
                  The definition of simple that was provided says nothing about not being changeable.

                    1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3.1.1.1. Con:
                    Potentiality and actuality are metaphysical attributes and hence fall under the provided definition.

                  1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3.1.2. Pro:
                  Changeability implies composition of the metaphysical parts: act and potency. [923] Composition is contrary to the definition of simplicity.

                    1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3.1.2.1. Con:
                    If Composition is contrary to simplicity and changeability means composition, then any description of god changing in any way is proof that god is not simple. God becoming his own son, or creating new laws at any point, for instance, point to god being changeable. Therefore, many descriptions of a god are changeable and cannot be considered simple.

                      1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3.1.2.1.1. Con:
                      Whether the descriptions of a particular religion compromise the simplicity of God is another debate entirely, since the God of classical theism could exist even if this or that theistic religion presents an incoherent picture of that God.

                      1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3.1.2.1.2. Con:
                      In orthodox Christian theology, the Incarnation does not represent a change in God's Divine Essence but rather a hypostatic union of Jesus' changeable, human nature with the unchangeable divine nature.

                        1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3.1.2.1.2.1. Pro:
                        “We do not conceive the mode of conjunction to be according to addition (i.e. change)”; just as in the union of man with God, nothing is added to God by the grace of adoption, but what is divine is united to man; hence, not God but man is perfected. [924]

                      1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3.1.2.1.3. Con:
                      God's creating a new (moral) law does not entail a change in God's essence, but rather an addition to the content of revelation granted to humanity.

                  1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3.1.3. Pro:
                  The particles are actual to the extent that any given particle is really present, but they are potential to the extent that any given particle could be in a different location, or energy state, or really could be changed in any way.

                1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3.2. Con:
                God is composed of smaller parts.

                  1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3.2.1. Pro:
                  Brahman in Hinduism is split into smaller gods.

                  1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3.2.2. Pro:
                  In Catholicism, the Holy Trinity is composed of the Father, Son, and Holy Spirit.

                1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3.3. Con:
                The theory of Divine Simplicity is an example of moving the goalposts [925].

                  1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3.3.1. Pro:
                  Divine Simplicity differentiates "good" as it pertains to God from "good" as is (or can be) defined and understood by humans. See Divine Simplicity [926]: "...God's attributes can only be spoken of by analogy."

                1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3.4. Con:
                Divine Simplicity posits that the essence of God is inexpressible [927]. In other words, any definition of God is insufficient. Thus, Divine Simplicity is a technical concession that God cannot exist as defined under Classical Theism.

                1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3.5. Con:
                Divine Simplicity is rejected by some philosophical proponents of theism. For example, Dr. William Lane Craig [928] is a renowned theologian and philosopher on religion who rejects divine simplicity [929].

                1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3.6. Pro:
                Gottlob Frege [930] distinguished between the 'sense' of an expression and its 'reference [931]'.

                  1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3.6.1. Pro:
                  While the statement "God is omnipotent" does not mean the same thing as "God is omniscient", it doesn't follow that they are not statements about the same one reality.

                  1.4.5.10.7.1.1.3.6.2. Pro:
                  "The evening star" does not have the same sense as "the morning star", but both expressions refer to one and the same thing--namely, the planet Venus. Similarly for descriptions of God. (from p 77 of 5 Proofs of the Existence of God [932])

          1.4.5.10.7.2. Con:
          Atheist also have moral standards. Morality is not founded upon religion alone.

        1.4.5.10.8. Con:
        It is impossible to know a priori whether an omnipotent and omnibenevolent God could co-exist with evil.

          1.4.5.10.8.1. Pro:
          To rule out a prior the compatibility of divine omnipotence, divine omnibenevolence, and real evil, one would need to prove that there are in principle no justifiable reasons for God to permit evil to exist. To know this would require knowing all possible outcomes of evil in the world, knowledge which no human has or can have.

            1.4.5.10.8.1.1. Pro:
            It's impossible to say with any acceptable certainty that the state of the world isn't justified from God's perspective.

              1.4.5.10.8.1.1.1. Pro:
              Any given "evil" circumstance has results that can continue to affect other circumstances long after, and far away from, the original circumstance. Chaos theory [933] shows that accurately predicting, and therefore judging the weight of, these consequences is far beyond the scope of human intelligence.

                1.4.5.10.8.1.1.1.1. Con:
                If God is omnipotent then God can do anything. If God can do anything, then good does not have to come from evil unless God decides that it must be the case. If God is omnibenevolent, God would not allow evil. Therefore, God would not require evil for good.

                  1.4.5.10.8.1.1.1.1.1. Con:
                  It may not be possible, even in principle, to have a physical reality with good and without evil.

                1.4.5.10.8.1.1.1.2. Con:
                There is no reason that an all powerful god would have to utilize chaotic systems to realize a certain outcome.

                1.4.5.10.8.1.1.1.3. Con:
                These consequences are not beyond the scope of God's intelligence. And a kind explanation from God is not beyond the scope of human intelligence.

              1.4.5.10.8.1.1.2. Pro:
              We cannot know what God is thinking.

                1.4.5.10.8.1.1.2.1. Con:
                We can if He chooses to share.

              1.4.5.10.8.1.1.3. Con:
              Moral standards should be acceptable, for making a sensible claim. If God bothered to give us moral standards, then He should have also told us why He violates them. Suspicious silence about Himself, while being vocal about human moral issues raise reasonable doubts.

        1.4.5.10.9. Pro:
        An uncaring God is compatible with the existence of evil.

        1.4.5.10.10. Pro:
        If a God is all knowing and good, it should also know the future. If it is the case and it gives free will to people, knowing already that people will do bad, and then punishes them, this is either sadistic or perverted, so not good.

        1.4.5.10.11. Pro:
        A fictional, non-existent God is compatible with the existence of evil.

      1.4.5.11. Con:
      For God to 'do away with all sin' requires limits to be placed on self determination.

        1.4.5.11.1. Con:
        Unlimited self determination may not necessarily be a good thing.

        1.4.5.11.2. Con:
        Self-Determination is a debatable concept as is, even without the potential presence of an omniscient creator.

          1.4.5.11.2.1. Pro:
          An Omniscient creator would know the potential actions of its creation before it was made. Creating them, knowing there will certainly be evil as a consequence of said creation is in itself evil.

        1.4.5.11.3. Con:
        There is a distinction between a perfect agent and a perfect automaton. In heaven, humans may maintain their free will while simultaneously being free of evil; rather than creating humans that can't sin, God could have created humans that won't sin.

          1.4.5.11.3.1. Pro:
          If an inability to sin places limits on free will, then God has no free will. Hence, he should be able to create humans without it too. If, instead, an inability to sin does not limit free will, then God should be able to create humans with free will and without sin.

          1.4.5.11.3.2. Pro:
          An omniscient, omnipotent being must necessarily be the only morally responsible agent in existence. The freedom of other beings to exist or do anything at all is contingent upon the approval (or ignorance) of the one who can make and unmake at will.

        1.4.5.11.4. Pro:
        Free will exists within the universe, and can only be caused by another free will. Thus the thing which caused the universe had free will, and must be an intelligent being.

          1.4.5.11.4.1. Con:
          The assumption that things must come from things like them is false, as the rocks, and sticks that start a fire are not hot.

        1.4.5.11.5. Con:
        This ignores 'natural evil' [934] where natural disasters and diseases cause huge amounts of suffering.

      1.4.5.12. Pro:
      The Epicurean Paradox [935] proves that evil cannot co-exist with an omnipotent benevolent God.

        1.4.5.12.1. Pro:
        The Christian God created homosexuality [936], and yet simultaneously calls for its lethal punishment [937].

          1.4.5.12.1.1. Pro:
          There is no evidence [938] that homosexuality, which some consider a sin, represents an expression of free will.

          1.4.5.12.1.2. Con:
          Jesus never advocated for lethal punishment; in fact, he stopped lethal punishment on more than one occasion.

            1.4.5.12.1.2.1. Pro:
            When the religious priests were ready to stone a woman caught in adultery [939] to death, Jesus said to them, "Let any one of you who is without sin be the first to throw a stone at her."

          1.4.5.12.1.3. Con:
          It is theoretically possible that homosexuality arose via evolution-linked changes to genetic structure, which could be considered a deviation from God.

            1.4.5.12.1.3.1. Con:
            This still presents a contradiction, as only God would be able to create evolution, or the means of its existence. Either God is not in charge of God's plan, or God is still on the hook for creating people in a way they condemn.

          1.4.5.12.1.4. Pro:
          Leviticus 20:13 [940] says, "If a man also lie with mankind, as he lieth with a woman, both of them have committed an abomination: they shall surely be put to death; their blood shall be upon them."

            1.4.5.12.1.4.1. Con:
            At most, scripture calls for punishment of homosexual behavior—which is the action of an individual, rather than a thing created by God.

            1.4.5.12.1.4.2. Con:
            Verses condemning homosexuality are misinterpreted [941] and do not actually call for discrimination.

        1.4.5.12.2. Con:
        Evil is anthropocentrically ill-defined and hugely subjective; things that we think are natural Evils to us (like diseases, natural disasters and death itself) serves themselves as Good to the world, abiding by the scientific laws that govern them.

          1.4.5.12.2.1. Pro:
          God should not change the science of the world specifically for us; if the whole of Creation is good (Genesis 1:31) then God doesn't owe his sustenance to us, but equally to everything in His Creation, all of which are products of His designed Science.

            1.4.5.12.2.1.1. Con:
            We can interrogate the concept of an omnibenevolent God without granting the divine command theory [942] of morality, and hence we can discard the idea that "the whole of creation is good" and recognise that e.g. the smallpox virus [943] was not good and did not deserve to exist.

          1.4.5.12.2.2. Con:
          The Epicurean argument does not assume that some things are objectively evil. A subjective concept of evil is enough to make the argument work.

        1.4.5.12.3. Con:
        God may be able and willing to stop evil, and may in fact be doing so in His own time and in His own way for reasons of His own.

          1.4.5.12.3.1. Pro:
          According to scripture, God is able and willing, but refrains from doing so, because that would mean God would have to eradicate all mankind. God allows evil to exist so we can exist and be saved.

          1.4.5.12.3.2. Con:
          The Epicurean paradox is concerned with the prevention (and not the termination) of evil.

          1.4.5.12.3.3. Con:
          If people who are dying could be saved by prompt and direct intervention by an omnipotent God, then such a God cannot be considered good.

        1.4.5.12.4. Con:
        The epicurean paradox has a hidden assumption [944] that God can create a world where people have free will that is without evil.

        1.4.5.12.5. Con:
        The alleged inconsistency depends on an unproven assumption: that a perfect God could not have any good reason to allow sin for a while.

          1.4.5.12.5.1. Pro:
          Analogically, if life were a video game, sins would be the gameplay obstacles that make the game enjoyable. The use of obstacles in the game does not make the game developer imperfect.

          1.4.5.12.5.2. Pro:
          God allowing sin for a short time is valuable because it means that people in Heaven will be able to appreciate God more, knowing what he freed them from.

      1.4.5.13. Con:
      Impassibility [945] in the classical definition of God takes care of the problem of evil.

        1.4.5.13.1. Con:
        The fact that an all-powerful being can be completely apathetic to human suffering does not make such a deity seem less immoral.

    1.4.6. Pro:
    The God of the Bible is contradictory, as God's character, wishes, directions, laws and outlook all change between the old and new testaments.

      1.4.6.1. Con:
      Between the old and new testament, it's not God that changes, but people and their perception of him.

        1.4.6.1.1. Con:
        This is not scripturally true. The nature of God varies even throughout the old testament. The story of Noah is probably the most well-known example thereof. [Genesis 8:20]

          1.4.6.1.1.1. Con:
          Genesis 8:20 doesn't describe God's nature changing. God smells a burnt offering and says that he will never send another flood. However, there is no evidence that he was planning to do otherwise before this.

      1.4.6.2. Con:
      The New Testament begins with the arrival of Jesus Christ who is the tool used to change God's systems of worship, repentance, and redemption. God's character, wishes, and outlook do not change. The purpose of His law changes, but not the laws themselves. His directions change as necessary according to the fulfillment of Christ's mission (a holy life, death, burial, resurrection, and identification after resurrection.)

    1.4.7. Pro:
    God is said to be all powerful (omnipotent), but also unable to do certain things.

      1.4.7.1. Pro:
      God cannot be both omnipotent and omnibenevolent, because suffering (e.g., natural disasters) exists. This implies that God is either unable or unwilling to stop these events.

        1.4.7.1.1. Con:
        Suffering may be inextricably tied to far greater good things, the loss of which would not be worth the abolition of suffering.

          1.4.7.1.1.1. Con:
          An all powerful God does not need suffering to achieve any other goal than suffering itself, if he does then he is not all powerful.

            1.4.7.1.1.1.1. Con:
            That God might not need to use suffering to achieve his goals is not sufficient to defeat the parent claim.

              1.4.7.1.1.1.1.1. Pro:
              It is possible that God only permits suffering as necessary to achieve the greatest amount of good.

                1.4.7.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con:
                There isn't any reason to believe any negative thing (suffering) is strictly necessary for a good thing, and thus an omnipotent being wouldn't need any negative thing to produce any good thing.

                1.4.7.1.1.1.1.1.2. Con:
                Thinking that God, a benevolent deity, has a plan is simply a coping mechanism, or a narrative to explain an incongruity in the light of seemingly meaningless suffering.

                  1.4.7.1.1.1.1.1.2.1. Con:
                  Without God, all suffering is meaningless.

                    1.4.7.1.1.1.1.1.2.1.1. Pro:
                    -> See 1.3.8.1.6.

                    1.4.7.1.1.1.1.1.2.1.2. Pro:
                    In an atheistic universe, there is no ultimate meaning. Any meaning we manage to create by definition arises from beings with no purpose, and is completely illusory.

                    1.4.7.1.1.1.1.1.2.1.3. Pro:
                    In an atheistic universe, in 200 years, the fate of the one who suffers, and the one who inflicts suffering is exactly the same. Both will be dead and consigned to oblivion forever.

                    1.4.7.1.1.1.1.1.2.1.4. Con:
                    One has the ability to not believe in any god, and still find meaning, or a greater cause [946] in their suffering.

                      1.4.7.1.1.1.1.1.2.1.4.1. Con:
                      The grandparent claim does not address, nor does it depend on one's belief in God, but in the actual existence of God, regardless of one's belief in Him, or lack thereof.

              1.4.7.1.1.1.1.2. Pro:
              God may have chosen suffering in lieu of some other method of achieving the same goal that would actually be worse than the suffering that He permits.

                1.4.7.1.1.1.1.2.1. Con:
                This assumes both that suffering is unavoidable and that the suffering we observe is the least possible to achieve what goods are produced. Both of these are highly doubtful.

            1.4.7.1.1.1.2. Con:
            The conclusion reached in the claim is a non sequitur. Even if God requires suffering to achieve His goals, it does not follow that he is not omnipotent in the classic sense.

              1.4.7.1.1.1.2.1. Con:
              An omnipotent being would be able to attain their goals and eliminate suffering, unless they desired suffering. So either this being ("God") cannot eliminate suffering (i.e. is not omnipotent), or they do not want to eliminate suffering (i.e. not "loving").

              1.4.7.1.1.1.2.2. Con:
              No good thing requires suffering. Thus, either the suffering is unnecessary or God's goals are not good.

          1.4.7.1.1.2. Pro:
          There is meaning in suffering.

            1.4.7.1.1.2.1. Con:
            There are some instances of suffering that have every appearance of seeming needless, having no indirect or compensatory benefit.

            1.4.7.1.1.2.2. Pro:
            Pain and suffering are far more effective as motivators and teaching tools than many alternatives, and so are necessary for salvation.

              1.4.7.1.1.2.2.1. Pro:
              It's very likely that in a world without pain, where all creatures are content at all times, there would be absolutely no motivation to change and grow. As such, creatures need to be motivated to avoid pain and pursue happiness.

                1.4.7.1.1.2.2.1.1. Con:
                A world without pain does not at all imply a world without motivation. Humans are often motivated to learn about things that they find interesting or enjoyable, without any threats or pressure.

            1.4.7.1.1.2.3. Pro:
            Being all powerful, all knowing, with an eternal perspective, God may have determined that finite suffering (however intense) has eternal benefit. God calls us to trust him. (See Romans 8:28 [947]; Psalm 66:10-12 [948])

              1.4.7.1.1.2.3.1. Con:
              The god of the Bible is not perfectly good and therefore does not meet the definition set forth by this debate.

                1.4.7.1.1.2.3.1.1. Con:
                The God of the Bible is attributed the property of goodness (Psalm 34:8, Mark 10:18. Acts 14:17, James 1:17).

                1.4.7.1.1.2.3.1.2. Pro:
                -> See 1.4.5.10.3.3.4.

              1.4.7.1.1.2.3.2. Con:
              An all powerful God ought to be able to create good without suffering.

                1.4.7.1.1.2.3.2.1. Con:
                Omnipotence [949] is sometimes defined as "maximal power," which includes the ability to do anything possible, but does not include the ability to do anything impossible or paradoxical.

                  1.4.7.1.1.2.3.2.1.1. Con:
                  -> See 1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.1.

                  1.4.7.1.1.2.3.2.1.2. Con:
                  -> See 1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.3.

                1.4.7.1.1.2.3.2.2. Con:
                Of course God could have created a good world without suffering. However, to think that a world without suffering is the best possible world that God could have created given His purposes is an assumption. This cannot possibly be known without omniscience. The burden of proof is too high.

              1.4.7.1.1.2.3.3. Con:
              It can easily be proven by geology that there has never been a global flood [950]. This is an observable fact about the earth. Yet, the Bible says clearly that there was a global flood.

            1.4.7.1.1.2.4. Pro:
            The possibility of evil and pain are the necessary consequences to permitting true free human will under an all powerful and all knowing God.

              1.4.7.1.1.2.4.1. Con:
              There are many kinds of pain and suffering that do not facilitate free will. Debilitating genetic diseases that kill children in infancy and are of no fault of the parents or the child yet they still occur.

              1.4.7.1.1.2.4.2. Con:
              If it is so that a world without "free will" cannot be without evil, then it must follow that God created a world wherein this was the case. To assert that God could not possibly have done otherwise is to assert that God is not omnipotent [951]. 
              
              As such, this claim actually proves the problem of evil.

              1.4.7.1.1.2.4.3. Con:
              The possibility of evil cannot be justified by the greater  goodness of free will if free will is itself impossible. Free will depends on the abililty to make a meaningful choice. A choice cannot be both independant of the state of affairs and meaningful.

                1.4.7.1.1.2.4.3.1. Con:
                This argument seems to rest on an equivocation in the meaning of the word "free", shifting it from "not externally compelled" to "not externally motivated". Choice is free so long as it is not compelled, and can meaningfully take into account externalities without being compelled. There is no contradiction

              1.4.7.1.1.2.4.4. Pro:
              Many classical religions offer people a choice between a "heaven" and a "hell", implying that God grants free will to his creations. The Adam and Eve story corroborates this by resulting in the pair learning good and evil. God clearly permits evil to happen, even if He Himself is completely good.

              1.4.7.1.1.2.4.5. Con:
              Intentonally making creatures capable of evil is in itself an act of evil and means that God is not omnibenevolent.

                1.4.7.1.1.2.4.5.1. Con:
                Unfree creatures who always make the "right" decision would not be doing any moral good, any more than an asteroid that hits the Earth and wipes out life would be doing moral evil. So while giving creatures free will does produce some evil, it also allows moral goodness to exist, and thus is justified on the whole.

                1.4.7.1.1.2.4.5.2. Con:
                The argument "making creatures with a capacity for evil is evil" is not actually a counterargument to the point that a just God would do so in order to create creatures with a capacity for free will so much as it simply denies the premise that free will is a supervening good making the capacity for evil itself a good because free will is impossible without the capacity to choose evil.

                  1.4.7.1.1.2.4.5.2.1. Con:
                  There are already various things that we cannot do. There is no justification to not make evil part of the things that we cannot do.

                  1.4.7.1.1.2.4.5.2.2. Con:
                  The claim that free will is a supervening good, directly implies in this context that the capacity for evil (and therefore the existence of evil) is a supervening good, which is self-contradicting.

              1.4.7.1.1.2.4.6. Pro:
              In Genesis, suffering is explained as a failing on Man's part, not on God's.  He created man in the Garden of Eden without sin and asked only that they not partake of the knowledge of good and evil.  They did and as promised, eventually died, the knowledge of which was the first example of human suffering.

        1.4.7.1.2. Pro:
        If God is omnipotent (all-powerful) and omniscient (all-knowing), then God could have created a reality in which pain, suffering and evil were not possible. Therefore, God is responsible for the existence of all pain, suffering and evil.

        1.4.7.1.3. Pro:
        In the Bible, the Christian God is shown as omnipotent, omniscient and omnibenevolent but he is also shown creating natural disasters [952].

        1.4.7.1.4. Pro:
        Biblical writings are contradictory, condone ideas we find repugnant today, or are otherwise problematic.

          1.4.7.1.4.1. Pro:
          Romans 10:17 [953] speaks of faith coming by hearing the word of God. I have read the word of God, and I don't have faith; therefore the scripture did not prove true, therefore it is false.

            1.4.7.1.4.1.1. Pro:
            Deuteronomy 4:2 [954] says Ye shall not add unto the word which I command you, neither shall ye diminish ought from it, that ye may keep the commandments of the LORD your God which I command you.

            1.4.7.1.4.1.2. Con:
            The parent claim is logically flawed. Paul did not claim that hearing the word of God necessarily implies faith, rather that faith implies having heard the word of God.

          1.4.7.1.4.2. Pro:
          Deuteronomy 4:2 [955] says Ye shall not add unto the word which I command you, neither shall ye diminish ought from it, that ye may keep the commandments of the LORD your God which I command you. Time and translation necessarily add unto or diminish from texts as evidenced by a myriad of interpretations. God would not have built in an impossibility.

            1.4.7.1.4.2.1. Con:
            A myriad of interpretations does not prove that time and translation materially change the meaning of the texts.

          1.4.7.1.4.3. Pro:
          Romans 1:20 [956]-21 [957] assumes the claim it is trying to prove twice.

          1.4.7.1.4.4. Pro:
          Romans 1:20 [958] misunderstands the nature of evidence.

          1.4.7.1.4.5. Pro:
          Proverbs 3:5 [959] suggests you can be saved at the low cost of your critical faculties.

            1.4.7.1.4.5.1. Con:
            Rather than giving up one's critical faculties, the proverb is pointing out that humans should trust the knowledge of God, similarly to how small children trust their parents. The Bible often makes assertions that the knowledge and wisdom of God is higher than that of humans and should be preferred. (Proverbs 1:7, 2:1-15; Isaiah 55:8-9)

        1.4.7.1.5. Con:
        That definition of God is not incompatible with the existence of suffering.

          1.4.7.1.5.1. Con:
          Providing that suffering is bad, we could conclude, that allowing for suffering God created world which is not perfect; and while he is, he should have done a perfect one.

      1.4.7.2. Con:
      -> See 1.3.7.3.4.2.3.3.1.3.2.1.1.

      1.4.7.3. Pro:
      The past, present, and future are not changeable. Therefore, God cannot be omnipotent.

      1.4.7.4. Pro:
      Omnipotence and omniscience are contradictory, as it impossible to be able to change your future if you already know what it is; making free-will, even for god, logically impossible.

      1.4.7.5. Con:
      Anything that God can't do isn't problematic as the only things that God cannot do are acts contrary to his nature.

        1.4.7.5.1. Con:
        Omnipotence is a matter of having the power to do anything, whether or not one chooses to use that power. God's desire to do an act is irrelevant when considering his ability to do an act.

        1.4.7.5.2. Con:
        If you claim that omnipotence is the ability to do anything within one's nature, any human could claim to be omnipotent. Can't lift a rock? No problem. It simply wasn't within the nature of the person to be that strong. Of course, one could argue that every being, including the classical gods, are omnipotent, but that rather defeats the purpose.

      1.4.7.6. Pro:
      Omnipotence is inherently contradictory.

        1.4.7.6.1. Pro:
        Black holes [960] are examples of objects that are impossible to lift.

          1.4.7.6.1.1. Con:
          Black holes are not impossible to lift; they have a finite mass and are being accelerated by nearby astronomical objects [961].

            1.4.7.6.1.1.1. Con:
            The effect of gravity does not constitute lifting. Gravity causes objects to fall.

        1.4.7.6.2. Pro:
        God is frequently defined as an "all-powerful" being, which implies the ability to do things which are logically impossible, such as drawing a square circle on a 2D plane.

        1.4.7.6.3. Pro:
        Either an all-powerful being cannot create a stone so heavy that the being cannot lift it, or if such a stone is created it is an object the being isn't powerful enough to lift. In either case the being's power is limited.

          1.4.7.6.3.1. Con:
          "A stone that can't be lifted by an omnipotent being" isn't a thing that God can't create because it's not a thing, only a series of words that have been put in a certain order. This only defeats God's omnipotence in the same way as His inability to create a married bachelor.

          1.4.7.6.3.2. Con:
          Examples of things that God cannot do are logically nonsensical and meaningless (e.g. make a square circle).

            1.4.7.6.3.2.1. Con:
            If a square circle is illogical and meaningless, then so is God. as his very nature (outside space & time) is also logically nonsensical and meaningless.

              1.4.7.6.3.2.1.1. Con:
              This is a non-sequitur [962]. Square circles are impossible because the two terms are mutually exclusive.  The claim that God exists outside of space and time has no inherent contradiction.

                1.4.7.6.3.2.1.1.1. Pro:
                Many things exist outside of space and time - numbers, for example, or pieces of music, stories, abstract properties.

                  1.4.7.6.3.2.1.1.1.1. Con:
                  It is false that constructs that are spaceless or timeless are "logically nonsensical". What is logically inconsistent (nonsensical) are statements that are logically contradictory. Lacking a spatial manifold or timescale doesn't amount to logical contradiction, for example many mathematical systems, so the claim mistakes contradicting known physics for logical contradiction.

            1.4.7.6.3.2.2. Pro:
            The definition of omnipotence doesn't have to include the ability to do things that are logically incoherent in and of themselves.

          1.4.7.6.3.3. Con:
          This argument [963] can be resolved by noting that God has not created this stone he supposedly could not lift.
