            1.4.7.6.3.3.1. Con:
            That God has not created such a stone is also consistent with God not existing, which would be perfectly consistent with the "stone so heavy..." argument against the possibility of an omnipotent being, so this objection does nothing.

              1.4.7.6.3.3.1.1. Con:
              But in this particular branch of the discussion, the atheist is arguing for the non-existence of God from the paradox of omnipotence. This is an attempted resolution of that paradox not, in this branch, an argument for the existence of God.

          1.4.7.6.3.4. Con:
          This argument could be resolved by noting that a rock could be in a position where it can not be lifted, like in orbit.

            1.4.7.6.3.4.1. Con:
            Time and space are relative, so despite the fact that it was in orbit, it could be "lifted" from a relative position.

          1.4.7.6.3.5. Con:
          Logic and order in the universe follow from the logic and order of the Creator. While He might be able to create such a rock by His omnipotence, being consistent, it is unlikely He would.

            1.4.7.6.3.5.1. Pro:
            Not only due to being consistent, logically it may also be due to have an ever-increasing potency. If perfection of God is a dynamic concept, then His potency is not fixed at some point, but is always striving towards infinity. Then it would be possible for Him to create with His maximum, and at the next moment He might as well exceed that maximum.

            1.4.7.6.3.5.2. Con:
            To affirm even the theoretical possibility that God "might" be able to do logical inconsistencies makes the concept of God a contradiction.

              1.4.7.6.3.5.2.1. Con:
              It is reasonable to assume an omnipotent God could refashion the laws of nature and logic to suite such a hypothetical. So to suggest that he "might" is not necessarily a contradiction to the notion of God.

                1.4.7.6.3.5.2.1.1. Con:
                Logic cannot be hacked. 2+2 is always 4, some principles must be followed. As far as we know, there is no example when logic goes bananas. Logic may be a limited tool, which cannot grasp the reality in total, but is an accurate one. We must follow this rule in order to have a meaningful debate, unless we are shown how exactly laws of logic can be refashioned in some possible reality and what are the consequences of that intervention, meaning change in any parameter may  be logically compensated.

        1.4.7.6.4. Con:
        The answer to your conundrum is that there is a way for him to do anything; the contradictions exist because we as finite beings cannot grasp the complexities and magnitude of any reality outside of our space-and-time existence.

        1.4.7.6.5. Con:
        Classical theists do not believe that omnipotence was ever meant to include logical impossibilities. There is no thing which God cannot do, and logical contradictions are not truly things but inconsistent bugs in language.

        1.4.7.6.6. Con:
        The Stone Paradox is ineffective against a Leibniz-Ross formulation of omnipotence [964]. Omnipotence defined as "the ability to do anything" is not accepted by all Christian theologians.

          1.4.7.6.6.1. Con:
          So then God isn't fully omnipotent. If we are talking about a god that is perfect, that implies he is perfectly omnipotent and so, can do anything. If Christian God isn't omnipotent, then he isn't perfect; therefore, he isn't God.

      1.4.7.7. Pro:
      Omniscience, omnipotence and omnibenevolence are mutually exclusive.

        1.4.7.7.1. Con:
        It is not contradictory to be all powerful, all knowing, and perfectly good. One is about ability to act, one is about ability to know, and one is about moral evaluation of actions or character.

          1.4.7.7.1.1. Pro:
          Omniscience means to know the truth value of all (non-devious) propositions. Some propositions like, "This proposition is false" make for interesting puzzles in philosophical logic, but do not impact omniscience. If there is some truth to be known, something omniscient knows it. If not, then it doesn't.

            1.4.7.7.1.1.1. Con:
            An omniscient being would not know how it feels to be ignorant. While it is logically possible to experience one's ignorance, God is incapable of ignorance. This is an essential contradiction.

              1.4.7.7.1.1.1.1. Con:
              Omniscience deals with knowledge, while impassability deals with experience. One can certainly have knowledge of things without having experienced them.

                1.4.7.7.1.1.1.1.1. Con:
                The literal definition of experience [965] is knowledge obtained through observation and/or participation or the act of perceiving and thus observing events or reality. If one cannot experience something, one can't know that experience.

                1.4.7.7.1.1.1.1.2. Con:
                I can have the knowledge of women, yet I fail to experience how does it feel to be one. The parent claim specifically says that this first hand experience is what God lacks. Impassability is about having the experience but not being affected, or "touched" by it, but then that "touch" is just one step away experience that God lacks.

                  1.4.7.7.1.1.1.1.2.1. Con:
                  This does not contest the claim that one can have knowledge of things without having experienced them or that said claim is irrelevant to its parent.

                1.4.7.7.1.1.1.1.3. Con:
                The parent claim introduced ignorance as emotion. I mentioned fear as the experience of an emotion. Your argument should move one level up.

                1.4.7.7.1.1.1.1.4. Pro:
                As human beings, some knowledge is only available to us via experience since it's impossible to accurately describe emotions and we cannot predict accurately how something will feel. This is where God differs as he can predict perfectly what would be the emotions attached to an experience without living it.

              1.4.7.7.1.1.1.2. Con:
              According to his own claim, Jesus is God incarnated as human, and thus would have experienced ignorance.

              1.4.7.7.1.1.1.3. Con:
              The concept of omniscience in Hinduism associated with the God as the Supersoul (Paramatma [966]) incorporates the limits of knowledge through the notion of omnipresence.

              1.4.7.7.1.1.1.4. Con:
              The paradox created by an omniscient God being unable to know ignorance does not make omniscience logically impossible, it merely provides further evidence that paradoxes are common in nature.

              1.4.7.7.1.1.1.5. Con:
              This assumes that "you have to be one to know one", that is, that to know what an experience is like you need to have had that experience. While it may be hard for people to gain knowledge of what others' experiences are truly like, it is still possible.

              1.4.7.7.1.1.1.6. Con:
              This confuses knowledge how and knowledge that. Omniscience only means to know all true propositions, but it says nothing about having particular experiences, such as experiencing ignorance.

            1.4.7.7.1.1.2. Con:
            God cannot know if there is something that he does not know. Thus his knowledge must be incomplete contradicting omniscience.

              1.4.7.7.1.1.2.1. Pro:
              For God to know what He does not know that He does not know, He would have to know what He does not know that He does not know, which is self-contradictory. Therefore, whether such thing exists, its existence is fundamentally unknowable no matter how much God knows.

                1.4.7.7.1.1.2.1.1. Con:
                The initial premise is that God knows absolutely everything.

              1.4.7.7.1.1.2.2. Con:
              This argument begs the question. It assumes that there are certain things that God has no knowledge of under the definition of omniscience in order to prove that there are certain things he has no knowledge of under the definition of omniscience.

          1.4.7.7.1.2. Pro:
          It is coherent and consistent to be perfectly good. It means, at least, that all actions performed by the perfectly good thing are morally permissible. All these concepts are clear and coherent, and being so for every action is not any more incoherent than doing good in some single action.

            1.4.7.7.1.2.1. Con:
            There are conceptions of God in classical theology, such as in the flood story, when God is conveyed as imperfect, or as having the ability to make mistakes.

            1.4.7.7.1.2.2. Con:
            To presume perfect good is to presume a complete picture of morality is known, which it is not, and this is not a problem that can be skirted by the bald assertion of perfection.

            1.4.7.7.1.2.3. Con:
            A question to highlight the competing tensions within omnipotence.
            Is god perfectly just or perfectly merciful?
            Being just means individuals get what they deserve.
            Being merciful means leniency is shown.
            Any increase in justice decreases mercy, and the converse.
            It cannot logically be both.

          1.4.7.7.1.3. Pro:
          Having abilities to act does not restrict knowledge or constitute moral flaws, having abilities to know does not restrict abilities to act or constitute moral flaws, and while moral goodness may lead one to not act some way, it does not make one unable to act in any way or know anything. No contradiction

            1.4.7.7.1.3.1. Con:
            This inadvertently minimises the claim by leaving out the omni aspect.
            'Having abilities to act' is an imperfect claim that falls short of omnipotence.
            Omnipotence restricts knowledge and restricts moral perfection.
            Being able to perform anything means one cannot know what they are incapable of performing (reduction in knowledge), it also means they are less morally perfect than a being incapable of evil (reduction in moral perfection).
            The interaction between the three generates contradiction.

          1.4.7.7.1.4. Con:
          Assuming morality of "good" and "evil" behaviors exists, an entity possessing complete knowledge must be be immoral, not just moral-good as knowledge of experience of only good would be incomplete knowledge.

            1.4.7.7.1.4.1. Con:
            This presents a misunderstanding of omniscience. Omniscience is typically defined as "knowing every true fact". This does not require experiential knowledge of immorality.

            1.4.7.7.1.4.2. Con:
            God is not subject to any external standard of morality. As the ultimate metaphysical reality and the creator of all things created, His character and behavior is the very standard of morality by which all actions by moral agents are judged to be good or evil. 
            
            Therefore, God is not technically "good", as that phrasing implies that He conforms to an external standard of goodness. Rather, He is Goodness Personified.

          1.4.7.7.1.5. Con:
          The contradiction comes from the interaction between two or more omni characteristics. If you have perfect ability to act a realm of knowledge is restricted (knowledge about your limitations to act), also your moral character is reduced (you are less morally perfect than something incapable of evil). This contradiction can be expressed from the perspective of any omni claim.
          Add another omni such as omni-present same contradictions. One omni, logically consistent. Two or more, inconsistent.

            1.4.7.7.1.5.1. Con:
            A being that is both omniscient and omnipotent would not be lacking knowledge about their limits, but rather would have a perfect knowledge that they have no limits.

            1.4.7.7.1.5.2. Con:
            A being with omnipotence would not be limited by logic. It would be capable of things that are logically contradictory.

            1.4.7.7.1.5.3. Con:
            Making moral judgements or evaluations about God based on human understanding would be irrelevant since God would be the only instance that would have full knowledge and control over objective moral code. Also it would be quite likely that moral guidelines that apply to humans would not be intended to apply to God.

          1.4.7.7.1.6. Con:
          Any moral reasoning comes from a compilation of traditions. As the word, from latin <<mores>> means "A set of norms or customs derived from generally accepted practices rather than written laws" An omniscient, eternal entity can have no morality, nor set of customs compiled through practice.

            1.4.7.7.1.6.1. Con:
            This definition of morals does not however apply in the case that God exists. According to Christianity, objective morality is defined by God, not people or "generally accepted practices".

        1.4.7.7.2. Con:
        This does not support the claim that God is said to be omnipotent but is also unable to do certain things.

        1.4.7.7.3. Pro:
        The divine attributes of omnipotence and omniscience are mutually exclusive because perfect knowledge of what will happen in the future precludes having the power to change it.

          1.4.7.7.3.1. Con:
          The concept of "changing the future" is incoherent. Influencing it or bringing about what happens make sense. Changing it does not.

            1.4.7.7.3.1.1. Pro:
            A change requires that things be in one state then later be in a different state. To change the future would mean that at some time t1, things at a future time F were in state S, then later at time t2, things at the time F were not in state S. This is incoherent.

          1.4.7.7.3.2. Con:
          God doesnt have to be in time so that linear "changes" effect him, he is immanent and transcendent

          1.4.7.7.3.3. Pro:
          A being that does not have the power to change the future is not all powerful.

          1.4.7.7.3.4. Pro:
          A being that does not know what will happen in the future is not all knowing.

            1.4.7.7.3.4.1. Con:
            There is no known future, since there is nothing to be known.

          1.4.7.7.3.5. Con:
          Future events, in this context, are logically prior to knowledge of them. That is to say, having knowledge of the future does not entail a lack of ability to change the future, but is merely the additional power to know what one will ultimately "decide".

            1.4.7.7.3.5.1. Pro:
            Molinism [967] holds that God knows in advance what one will freely decide, given any set of circumstances he produces, and (therefore) takes these potential into consideration in ordering events. Thus, both free will and the omniscience of God are preserved.

          1.4.7.7.3.6. Con:
          The idea of omniscience and omnipotence are not mutually exclusive, as the knowledge could simultaneously change through action.

            1.4.7.7.3.6.1. Con:
            If a god creates the future, he automatically negates any chance of free will

              1.4.7.7.3.6.1.1. Con:
              According to compatibilism [968], free will is possible even if the world is deterministic.

                1.4.7.7.3.6.1.1.1. Pro:
                -> See 1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.

              1.4.7.7.3.6.1.2. Con:
              Considering God, who is out of time it is not true - such God possibly is not creating future, he has already already created it. He is out of time, therefore in act of creation all of it was made out of bonds of time, but it's possible that God is not choosing what would be, he created some starting point and taken action at only some points.

            1.4.7.7.3.6.2. Con:
            If the actions of a god change its knowledge, it cannot have been omniscient previously.

              1.4.7.7.3.6.2.1. Con:
              God's knowledge is always up to date.

                1.4.7.7.3.6.2.1.1. Con:
                If knowledge needs updating, then it's by definition not all-encompassing.

                  1.4.7.7.3.6.2.1.1.1. Con:
                  Knowledge base is ever increasing. In order to know everything, God need to be instantly ever updated. This means omniscience is interactive.

                    1.4.7.7.3.6.2.1.1.1.1. Con:
                    God is not discovering things at the same rate we are, just like a parent already knows so much more than his newborn child.

              1.4.7.7.3.6.2.2. Con:
              Classical theism does not claim that God's actions changes His knowledge. Rather, He knows the results of all actions before they are done.

            1.4.7.7.3.6.3. Pro:
            The modern term is "interactive". Everything is changing, so actions are always adapting to the changes in environment. In physiology it is called biofeedback.

          1.4.7.7.3.7. Con:
          This is an attempt to fit an infinite concept into a finite set of restrictions. An omni-being by denotation cannot be limited in any way.

          1.4.7.7.3.8. Con:
          Knowing the future does not preclude being able to change it. That would only be the case if 'the future' was all powerful/ God.

        1.4.7.7.4. Pro:
        -> See 1.4.7.1.1.2.4.5.

        1.4.7.7.5. Con:
        Because we have no full concept and no experience of these terms, perhaps they could actually be contingent on each other. 'Knowing all' may require 'all-power', and 'omnibenevolence' could be decided upon by the entity that fits this description.

        1.4.7.7.6. Con:
        God's omnibenevolence can be taken for granted.

          1.4.7.7.6.1. Pro:
          God decides what is good, therefore God's goodness is uncontestable.

            1.4.7.7.6.1.1. Con:
            His creation subjects great suffering onto other aspects of his creation. Sometimes it's their sole means of survival. [969]

              1.4.7.7.6.1.1.1. Con:
              Fiction writers are not considered evil for creating misery for their fictional characters to deliver experiences to the existent readers. Therefore, God would also not be evil for creating misery for his existent creatures to deliver experiences to his own transcendent perception.

                1.4.7.7.6.1.1.1.1. Con:
                God's (if he existed) creations aren't fictional, they/we actually exist.

            1.4.7.7.6.1.2. Con:
            God does not decide what is good. This is one horn of the Euthyphro dilemma [970], and would make good and evil arbitrary.

              1.4.7.7.6.1.2.1. Con:
              Everything is arbitrary to the ultimate God. Primary principles of omniscience, omnipotence and omnipresence ensure that. However, God doesn't change (immutability), and this results in the definition of an absolute point of moral reference.

                1.4.7.7.6.1.2.1.1. Con:
                Morality being arbitrary to God, even in principle, regardless of whether he chooses to make arbitrary changes, render morality arbitrary. If some "objective morality" truly exists, it must be beyond the possibility of such changes.

          1.4.7.7.6.2. Pro:
          God has unlimited knowledge to determine his own goodness eternally, and he has unlimited power to become absolutely good eternally, therefore God is absolutely good.

            1.4.7.7.6.2.1. Pro:
            The human concept of "good" does not apply to God.

              1.4.7.7.6.2.1.1. Pro:
              -> See 1.4.7.7.1.4.2.

              1.4.7.7.6.2.1.2. Pro:
              The human concept of "good" is conditioned by our limited knowledge of, and limited power over our environment, therefore fallacious when applied to the absolute.

              1.4.7.7.6.2.1.3. Con:
              Yet God passed his word/law onto his people, and by following that law/those words he deems them "good" enough to achieve eternal salvation. God, in the hypothetical of him existing, created us and our understanding of "good".

            1.4.7.7.6.2.2. Con:
            A necessary being cannot "become". God, as a necessary being, is fully actualized, has no potentiality, and cannot change.

          1.4.7.7.6.3. Con:
          God's omnibenevolence can be exaggerated.

            1.4.7.7.6.3.1. Con:
            A maximally-great being would be the greatest possible good; therefore, it is impossible to conceive of any greater good. This allows no possibility of exaggeration.

        1.4.7.7.7. Pro:
        An omnipotent being would be able to create something "unknowable", even if just a 'secret'. This contradicts omniscience.

          1.4.7.7.7.1. Con:
          There is no contradiction here. You are just missing the next logical step. If God can create something unknown, he can also expand him self to know it. It's like the question if God is all powerful can he create something so heavy that he himself cannot lift it. Again the answer is yes he can, but then in the next moment he can expand his strength to be able to lift it.

          1.4.7.7.7.2. Con:
          The above claim is a logical fallacy. Similar fallacies would include an omnipotent being creating a situation where he had no power, or an omniscient being knowing what he does not know.

          1.4.7.7.7.3. Con:
          An omnipotent being would be able to rewrite the way logic works in the universe to eliminate any contradiction with omniscience. But that's not necessary unless God has a reason to create something unknowable.

        1.4.7.7.8. Con:
        There is no direct logical contradiction here. A square circle is an incoherent statement because the words contradict one another. The words, all good, all powerful and all knowing do not contradict one another.

          1.4.7.7.8.1. Con:
          Theodicy [971] makes [972] omniscience, omnipotence and omnibenevolence mutually exclusive

            1.4.7.7.8.1.1. Con:
            Theodicy is actually a different subject. The claim made above was that the three qualities contradict one another. Theodicy or the problem of evil of addresses the claim that the 3 qualities are incompatible with the world we observe. Mutually exclusive and not commensurate with the world we live in are 2 very different claims, therefore it remains that the 3 qualities are not mutually exclusive.

            1.4.7.7.8.1.2. Con:
            Any combination of two of the "omni" words are logically consistent, even according to Theodocy [973] therefore none of them are "mutually exclusive." However, adding the third "omni" to the mix does create a logical paradox.

          1.4.7.7.8.2. Con:
          Any two of these "omni" words will contradict the remaining one.

        1.4.7.7.9. Pro:
        Theodicy [974] problem can only be solved [975] by ignoring one of the three "omni"

          1.4.7.7.9.1. Con:
          This is only even plausibly true if one adds the data that there is real evil in the world. That cannot be demonstrated on the assumption of atheism. Hence, this line of argument undercuts itself.

            1.4.7.7.9.1.1. Con:
            Even if God's existence were necessary and sufficient for "real evil" to be identifiable, one can simply alter the argument to replace "evil" with "that which would be evil, if anything".

          1.4.7.7.9.2. Con:
          This is not true. The assumption of this claim this that the way the world operates now is faulty as it permits evil. It's logically possible that by not allowing evil a greater evil would be done.

          1.4.7.7.9.3. Con:
          The problem of evil can also be compared to children whose loving parents are strict and sometimes enforce discipline. Such children often feel wronged by their parents. When they mature and grow up they can see that their parents were in fact doing what was best for them but they were unable to see it at the time.

            1.4.7.7.9.3.1. Con:
            When a child who is beaten grows up to beat their own children, that's not "growing up and seeing that their parents were doing what was best for them", that's a cycle of violence [976].

          1.4.7.7.9.4. Con:
          Reincarnation solves the problem of evil as people could be suffering reactions to actitivities they perfmormed in previous life times. To solve the problem of evil this reactions would need to be for the purpose of correcting the mentality and conciousness of the person.

            1.4.7.7.9.4.1. Pro:
            For example most bad mentalities some how or other cause others to suffer. Suffering is a good teacher of compassion as we can understand from the common line "I don't want anyone else to suffer the way I did". Therfore suffering can be a kind of cosmic sensitivity training.

      1.4.7.8. Pro:
      God cannot be all powerful because he can't prove his own non-existence.

        1.4.7.8.1. Pro:
        An all knowing, all seeing God would speak (or act) to make his presence known, and most major religions report that God makes his will clearly known by communicating to humans. Yet, no such communication exists today.

          1.4.7.8.1.1. Con:
          Many contemporary people claim to have received communications from God. To assume that they are all wrong in order to conclude that God does not communicate with people today is circular reasoning.

          1.4.7.8.1.2. Con:
          The presumption that an all knowing and all seeing God would speak or act to make its presence known is logically unfounded. An artist, for example, does not maintain eternal vigil alongside the works they have created.

            1.4.7.8.1.2.1. Con:
            If we presume that certain sacred documents are divinely inspired, dictated, or authored, then it seems to follow that God has at least some interest in communicating with humanity, thereby making its presence known.

              1.4.7.8.1.2.1.1. Con:
              God may have had interest in communicating with humanity historically, but may believe that past communication was sufficient enough and is not necessarily tied to an eternity of such communication.

              1.4.7.8.1.2.1.2. Con:
              The interest in communicating with humanity may not stem from a desire to make its presence known - correlation is not causation. The existence of such documents merely indicates a supernatural presence. For an idea of its interests, one has to gather more information, say from the documents themselves.

          1.4.7.8.1.3. Con:
          Mormons believe that God continues to communicate with humans today through living prophets today as he did in ancient times as recorded in the Bible and the Book of Mormon. (see Prophets) [977]

            1.4.7.8.1.3.1. Con:
            Mormon prophets do not demonstrate that they have any more access to divine guidance than other humans.

          1.4.7.8.1.4. Con:
          The Bible is said to be God's communication to men. In the Bible it states why man is left alone without communication for the time.

            1.4.7.8.1.4.1. Con:
            Historical records (including some present within the Bible) suggest it was written and compiled for political purposes. Particularly gaining support for: the attempted annexation of Judah to Israel (~8th century BCE), the Maccabean Revolt (167 BCE - 160 BCE) and the First Jewish-Roman War (66 CE - 70 CE).

            1.4.7.8.1.4.2. Con:
            Nothing within the Bible suggest it was truly inspired by a higher power.

              1.4.7.8.1.4.2.1. Con:
              Being unable to see that something is inspired by a higher power is not evidence that it wasn't inspired by a higher power.

          1.4.7.8.1.5. Con:
          There is no current intellectual consensus on what would constitute proof positive of such communication, which might be a reason why there appears to be no communication at all - if the folder doesn't exist, there can't possibly be any documents in it.

          1.4.7.8.1.6. Con:
          At a personal level, many believe the Vedas to be divinely inspired, if not dictated. Similarly for the Bible and the Quran.

            1.4.7.8.1.6.1. Pro:
            In traditional Hinduism, even the Mahabharata is considered a true tale of direct divine intervention in human affairs. As for whether it truly is an example of divine communication - the story has been studied and commented on by several generations of Indian writers, and has been found to contain deep lessons in philosophy, statecraft, human morality and psychology. In many ways, it is a textbook for human psychological development. (It's also a damn good story, but I may be biased.)

        1.4.7.8.2. Con:
        All powerful God might be able to prove false statements. This is contradictory. By principle of explosion [978], this would allow him to prove any statements.

          1.4.7.8.2.1. Con:
          This does not contest that God cannot prove his own non-existence (the premise), nor does it contest the validity of the parent argument. Therefore, it doesn't oppose the parent claim.

        1.4.7.8.3. Con:
        "His Omnipotence means power to do all that is [possible], not to do the [impossible].... meaningless combinations of words do not suddenly acquire meaning simply because we prefix to them the two other words 'God can.'... It is no more possible for God than for the weakest of his creatures to carry out both of two mutually exclusive alternatives; not because his power meets an obstacle, but because nonsense remains nonsense even when we talk it about God." -- C.S. Lewis, The Problem of Pain

        1.4.7.8.4. Pro:
        If God was all-powerful and therefore could prove his own nonexistence, and his proof would be correct and realistic, he would not exist. This is a contradiction.

          1.4.7.8.4.1. Con:
          The argument begs the question by assuming the conclusion. "If God... could prove his own nonexistence, and his proof [is] correct... he would not exist."
          
          Of course if he correctly proves that he does not exist, he would not exist!

            1.4.7.8.4.1.1. Con:
            No the claim does not assume its conclusion. Just because certain proofs exist doesn't mean the reality agrees with the proof. Nor does it mean the proof is correct. But due to that, each step of the proof must be explicitly stated and justified.

              1.4.7.8.4.1.1.1. Con:
              That the proof is correct is a given in the grandparent claim.

                1.4.7.8.4.1.1.1.1. Con:
                Actually that part had to be explicitly assumed, otherwise the claim would not be sufficiently justified. But this is not in any way circular.

          1.4.7.8.4.2. Con:
          It has not been shown that an all-powerful being can do illogical tasks, such as proving their own non-existence.

            1.4.7.8.4.2.1. Con:
            By definition of all-powerful, a being is not all-powerful, if there exists tasks which the being is unable to perform.

              1.4.7.8.4.2.1.1. Con:
              Because logically incoherent tasks are logically incoherent, they do not exist.

              1.4.7.8.4.2.1.2. Con:
              There is no singular prescriptive definition of "all-powerful" from which to argue "by definition".

                1.4.7.8.4.2.1.2.1. Con:
                There is no requirement for 'singular' for the definition of all-powerful. Many different definitions of all-powerful all satisfy the principle that to be all-powerful, an entity must be able to perform any actions.

          1.4.7.8.4.3. Con:
          It has not been shown that an omnibenevolent being could lie by proving their non-existence when they actually exist.

            1.4.7.8.4.3.1. Con:
            The claim is about whether all-powerful entities can do certain contradictory actions. The claim is not about omnibenevolence, which is unrelated.

              1.4.7.8.4.3.1.1. Con:
              The claim is about the God of classical theism, who is omni-benevolent and cannot lie. If the grandparent claim imagines that God can lie, then it fails because it addresses a straw man god, not the God of classical theism.

              1.4.7.8.4.3.1.2. Pro:
              Only omnipotence of God is needed to prove the contradiction. Omnibenevolence cannot avoid this problem because every notion of God that assumes God is omnipotent would be subject to same proof by contradiction.

          1.4.7.8.4.4. Pro:
          Clearly the abilities of an all-powerful being would be limited, if he cannot do such illogical action. The definition of "all-powerful [979]" would reject such limitation by definition, at least in their most strong form. Therefore all-powerful entities that can perform all, even absurd, kinds of illogical actions cannot exist. Hence, a God that is in such sense all-powerful does not exist.

          1.4.7.8.4.5. Con:
          Even if an all-powerful being can do illogical tasks, it does not follow that He would do illogical tasks.

            1.4.7.8.4.5.1. Con:
            The God of classical theism is not simply an all-powerful being, but He is also the source of morality and cannot lie.

          1.4.7.8.4.6. Pro:
          It is not necessary for the proof that God actually performs any illogical actions. If God is able to perform illogical actions, then these must be consistent. Even if God never actually does such actions. By Compactness theorem [980], If they are consistent with the universe then assuming their occurrence cannot cause contradiction.

          1.4.7.8.4.7. Con:
          Given that God's omnipotence permits him to do logically incoherent things like "correctly prove his own non-existence", existing despite having correctly proven his own non-existence is also within his power.

            1.4.7.8.4.7.1. Con:
            Existing despite having correctly proven his own non-existence would be contradictory according to "principle of explosion [981]" and "Modus ponens [982]".

              1.4.7.8.4.7.1.1. Con:
              God could decide to invalidate the principle of explosion and modus ponens if he chose to (although he would never do this because he wants a reasonable universe).

        1.4.7.8.5. Con:
        An all-good God would not lie, and therefore would never attempt to prove his own nonexistence.

          1.4.7.8.5.1. Con:
          This provides a reason for why God wouldn't prove his own non-existence, but not a counter to the claim that this indicates that God cannot be omnipotent.

      1.4.7.9. Con:
      A better understanding of God's omnipotent nature is that: God can do anything that can be done, and that anything that can be done cannot be limited to my understanding of it.

        1.4.7.9.1. Con:
        But if so, God isn't omnipotent, because he is limited to our comprehension. An omnipotent being can do anything; if he can't do, he isn't omnipotent. If he is limited to what can be done, he cant do what can't be done, so, he cant do a thing and therefore, isn't omnipotent.

      1.4.7.10. Con:
      This is not a contradiction in the definition of God, rather in (some of) the theological systems devised to support certain religions. God is not the same as religion.

    1.4.8. Pro:
    A self-sufficient being would not need praise, yet religious require followers to praise God.

      1.4.8.1. Con:
      God could request praise for the sake of God's devotees, rather than God's own sake.

      1.4.8.2. Con:
      This only produces a contradiction between religious doctrines and the definition of the God of classical theism; there could be a deist God who does not demand worship, and thus no contradiction.

      1.4.8.3. Con:
      No Christian believes He needs it. But we do believe He wants it and we do it out of gratitude, not because He needs it.

      1.4.8.4. Pro:
      A perfect entity needs nothing as it is, by definition, totally developed and unchangeable.

        1.4.8.4.1. Con:
        An unchanging permanence is the definition of a statically perfect (complete) entity. God can be viewed as a dynamically perfect 'entity' - such as the water cycle on Earth. Since the water cycle arises out of basic principles - the transition of a specific compound (water) between solid-liquid-gas states in response to changing energy levels (temperature) - it has existed for billions of years, but still incorporates constant change.

      1.4.8.5. Con:
      'Praise' could also be read as 'worship' - an injunction to practice the preaching.

      1.4.8.6. Con:
      Classical theists would not say that God "needs" praise, but is utterly self-sufficient.

    1.4.9. Pro:
    God is said to be omnipresent but also as having been there when there was nothing yet. This implies that God was simultaneously existent and non-existent.

      1.4.9.1. Pro:
      The apparent definition of God negates God as an existent being. If God is the creator of all things, then God cannot be in the same category of the things created.

        1.4.9.1.1. Con:
        God isn't in the same category of created things (contingent). By definition, God is eternal and necesary.

          1.4.9.1.1.1. Con:
          The logical string that God could not have failed to exist only works if you believe he created the universe. God must exist only if you believe in God. If you don’t believe in God it is wholly possible that he cannot exist.

            1.4.9.1.1.1.1. Con:
            If there is no necessary being to give origin to contingent beings, then there is no way contingent beings could have come to existence. Since contingent beings exist, there must be a necessary being to bring them to existance.

            1.4.9.1.1.1.2. Con:
            Not so. God could exist even if He did not create anything.

        1.4.9.1.2. Con:
        In classical theism, God is not the creator of all things simpliciter, but of all contingent things.

        1.4.9.1.3. Con:
        Not everything that exists is created or crafted.

        1.4.9.1.4. Con:
        God is in his own category. Everything that exists does so in a hierarchy between similarity and dissimilarity with God. A rock is like God only in the fact that it exists. A human is much more like god in the fact that they reason. But God himself is inherent to reality and the only entity that is such.

        1.4.9.1.5. Con:
        If God is assumed to be the universe itself (an oft repeated claim in religious and spiritual documents), then at the instant of the big bang, there was nothing but God - the physical and metaphysical laws that govern everything we know - and an enormous amount of energy. From that initial point, we have evolved to the universe of today, in which everything that we know to exist is a result of what appeared in that first instant. Thus God is both creator of all things and within everything.

        1.4.9.1.6. Con:
        Classical theists do not believe that God is in the same category as things created, and even His existence is qualitatively different from all other kinds of existence. Thomas Aquinas, a famous classical theist, conceived of God not as "a being" but as "Being Itself."

        1.4.9.1.7. Con:
        The claim of God being the creator of all things implies a degree of common sense in its understanding. Namely, God created all things other than himself. The Bible asserts that God is eternal: "from everlasting to everlasting"; without creation; and therefore not in the same category as created things.  (See Psalms 90 verse 2)

          1.4.9.1.7.1. Pro:
          Something created the physical universe that we live in now. There has to be something at the start, and the chances that atoms would become something as complex and meaningful as our world and society are unrealistic without a great and original designer, which would be God. If God does not exist, we would not exist; the amount of fine tuning it takes for humans to exist is reason enough alone to believe in God.

          1.4.9.1.7.2. Con:
          This negates the creation of reality, itself. Either God is the creator of reality, and he was outside of reality during its creation (i.e., is not real), or was within reality, and thus not the creator of all things.

            1.4.9.1.7.2.1. Con:
            Reality isn't a "thing" per se, but only a metaphysical concept - the property of being real. As has been stated, God could not create reality while also being real, so there's no contradiction in saying that God did not create reality.

              1.4.9.1.7.2.1.1. Pro:
              Classical theism doesn't claim that God created every concept and entity, only that He is the necessary cause for all contingent things. This is consistent with metaphysical concepts such as reality not being created by God.

              1.4.9.1.7.2.1.2. Con:
              Concepts are things. If God didn't create them, then he didn't create everything.

                1.4.9.1.7.2.1.2.1. Con:
                -> See 1.4.9.1.7.2.1.1.

            1.4.9.1.7.2.2. Con:
            -> See 1.4.9.1.7.2.1.1.

            1.4.9.1.7.2.3. Con:
            God exists in at least 6 or 7 dimensions this claim only makes sense when bound to 3.

        1.4.9.1.8. Pro:
        The definition of God is self-contradictory in that He is beyond space but also omnipresent.

          1.4.9.1.8.1. Con:
          God may affect space, even if space may not affect God.

          1.4.9.1.8.2. Con:
          Omnipresent does not mean existing "within" space.

            1.4.9.1.8.2.1. Pro:
            Modern understanding of space is within a space-time continuum. They cannot be addressed separately. 'God is beyond space' is strictly what Abrahamic religions claim. Illustration for an alternative opinion can be found in another monotheistic religion, Vaishnavism, where God says that He Himself is time Gita 11:32 [983] . In modern language, God is space/time.

          1.4.9.1.8.3. Con:
          "Beyond space yet omnipresent." These are different expressions conveying the same idea. God is to his creation as an author is to his novel. Nowhere, yet everywhere.

        1.4.9.1.9. Pro:
        The definition of God is self-contradictory in that He is beyond time but also eternal.

          1.4.9.1.9.1. Con:
          God created time. It's like saying a relational human is self-contradictory just because they created unfeeling robots.

          1.4.9.1.9.2. Con:
          Being beyond, or outside of time implies being eternal.

      1.4.9.2. Pro:
      The dilema of an absolute being that contains everything is a variation on the paradox of the irresistible force. It is a false dilema. In this case it is a false dilema that points out that the "existence" cannot possibly be an atribute of an all-containing being.

        1.4.9.2.1. Con:
        Other than a few panentheistic religions, few would support the idea of God being "containing" in any fashion.

      1.4.9.3. Con:
      Our incapable minds are insufficient to comprehend the wonders of God, and just because we don’t understand something does not mean that it is false.

        1.4.9.3.1. Con:
        If we do not understand something, we should approach everything we think we know about that something with a certain amount of doubt. This does not seem to be the prevalent approach to God, however.

        1.4.9.3.2. Con:
        While it is certainly true that our incapability to understand something does not make it false, there exist simpler explanations and theories for God's (non)existence than assuming some mystical ways in which to exist and not exist makes sense, which we can then just label wonders of God and be done with it.

        1.4.9.3.3. Pro:
        There are many things in this Universe that we do not understand yet and there is possibility that God is one (or all) of these things.

        1.4.9.3.4. Con:
        Our incapable minds are insufficient to comprehend many things, but for everything else, there is evidence that we can look for or look at, experiments that we can try to gain more knowledge and so on.

          1.4.9.3.4.1. Pro:
          The only testifiable and shareable piece of evidence for God's existence are words and books made of these words.

            1.4.9.3.4.1.1. Pro:
            A lot of claims in, for example, Bible have been proven as false, such as the existence of global flood at some point in our history, the age of the Earth and the Universe and so on.

              1.4.9.3.4.1.1.1. Con:
              The Bible makes no claims regarding the age of the Earth, nor the universe. Some interpretations argue for an old earth; some for a new earth, but the bible itself does not directly address the issue.

                1.4.9.3.4.1.1.1.1. Con:
                The issue is directly addressed in the Bible. 
                The gospel of Luke contains a genealogy of Jesus [984]. Comparing with the genealogy from Genesis [985], one finds that the Earth and the universe should be approximately 6000 years old.

      1.4.9.4. Pro:
      One of the cornerstones of the classical God is that it is everywhere, though it 's nowhere. It exists yet you cannot point your finger at it. Existence is too human a term to define such a being, regardless it actually may or maynot be.

        1.4.9.4.1. Con:
        Omnipresence excludes being "nowhere". Nothing in the classical definition of God says that God is nowhere.

          1.4.9.4.1.1. Con:
          The fact that God is a creator implies that it was there before there was anything.

        1.4.9.4.2. Con:
        Being present does not necessarily imply being visible. Oxygen is present in the air, yet you cannot see it.

      1.4.9.5. Pro:
      For God to be both the ultimate and encompass everything, they would have to include both 'existing' and 'not-existing' simultaneously.

        1.4.9.5.1. Con:
        It is incoherent to say that something exists and does not exist simultaneously. Nothing in the classical concept of God indicates that even deity is capable of a logical impossibility.

      1.4.9.6. Pro:
      God's higher property beyond existence is probably unfathomable to the human mind. Existence is therefore an unadequate term to use when talking about God.

      1.4.9.7. Con:
      When "there was nothing yet", there was no place in the universe for God to be. Omnipresence is the quality of being at all places at all times. It is not inconsistent with omnipresence to say that God is not in a location that does not exist.

      1.4.9.8. Con:
      -> See 1.4.9.5.1.

      1.4.9.9. Con:
      God both 'existing' and 'not existing' simultaneously is not a mere contradiction that implies his non-existence. It looks more like a quantum superposition. Schrödinger's cat. [986]

        1.4.9.9.1. Con:
        The scale [987] and the interactions with the environment [988] attributed to God make him unlikely to be in a quantum coherent [989] state, therefore can't be described by a superposition of states.

      1.4.9.10. Con:
      This is based on the assumption that God is part of the universe, rather than separate from it. That is a logical fallacy in and of itself, since it would result in causa sui.

    1.4.10. Pro:
    The vast majority of the religious could not comprehend a God that isn't worthy of their faith, yet that is what the classical definition [990] omits: any reference to faith.

      1.4.10.1. Pro:
      The concept of God is generally predicated on belief that following arbitrary tenets can ingratiate you to the being and it would then create and/or improve your afterlife. Without that supernatural promise you have a self-help group instead of a religion and the banal instead of the divine.

      1.4.10.2. Con:
      The classical definition of God defines God as the "absolutely metaphysically ultimate being," and metaphysics [991] deals with the "origins and principles of such things as being, knowing, substance, cause, identity, time, and space"; since these concepts are nonmaterial and "spiritual" is defined as "nonmaterial or opposed to physical or material things," the classical definition of God defines God in a spiritual manner.

    1.4.11. Pro:
    God is defined as having the qualities of both immanence [992] and transcendence [993], which are mutually exclusive concepts.

      1.4.11.1. Con:
      Analogically, a king can influence or visit a prison without becoming bound by the prison. Similarly, God can be a part of the material world while remaining independent of it.

        1.4.11.1.1. Con:
        A jail is indifferent as to who it detains. Absent all but their status, a king is no more able to leave a jail than a subject. In the same way, a god cannot be both manifest in a material universe and also completely independent of it.

      1.4.11.2. Con:
      Ralph Waldo Emerson, father of Transcendentalism [994], often wrote on the transcendent nature of both God and humans.

      1.4.11.3. Con:
      If the fourth dimension is space-time, then a god-like being would only need to exist in a dimension we can not comprehend, like the fifth dimension, to be considered a god to beings in the third dimension.

      1.4.11.4. Pro:
      Transcendence implies that God is beyond humanity's grasp [995], while immanence implies that God is knowable.

      1.4.11.5. Pro:
      If God responds to prayer, then this undermines his transcendence (total independence from all else).

        1.4.11.5.1. Con:
        Prayer does not undermine God's transcendence but illustrates his attributes of mercy, consciousness and free will.

        1.4.11.5.2. Pro:
        Deuteronomy 32:36 [996] says, "Indeed, the LORD will give justice to his people, and he will change his mind about his servants, when he sees their strength is gone and no one is left, slave or free."

      1.4.11.6. Con:
      Transcendence means to “go beyond” or surpass. Therefore, God can transcend creation, yet remain connected to it.

        1.4.11.6.1. Pro:
        In philosophy, transcendence conveys the basic ground concept from the word's literal meaning [997] (from Latin), of climbing or going beyond. Thus, transcendence is not a trait which excludes a being from being in other modes; it actually requires multiple modes of being.

          1.4.11.6.1.1. Con:
          Transcendence in this sense-- being simultaneously capable of subjecting oneself to physical laws (to allow immanence) and also capable of being free of the same--is an explicit contradiction within a single definition. This makes it an unworkable definition by which to define a thing (i.e. God).

            1.4.11.6.1.1.1. Pro:
            The Transcendence of God implies an additional set of laws beyond the Natural Laws which are capable of defining this capacity. This contradicts the definition of Natural Law [998], which is itself universal, precluding a set of laws which is a super set of itself or disjoint from it. God's Transcendence is contradictory by definition, requiring a set of laws greater than the universal set to define it.

        1.4.11.6.2. Pro:
        God manifested in the physical creation of everything at the beginning of the universe and has since maintained a transcendent relationship with creation.

        1.4.11.6.3. Pro:
        According to molinism [999], God considers the free will decisions (including prayers) which will occur in all possible future situations when ordering the world. Thus, God can both "respond" to prayer while remaining immutable.

        1.4.11.6.4. Con:
        "In religion, transcendence refers to the aspect of a god's nature and power which is wholly independent of the material universe, beyond all physical laws." (en.wikipedia.org [1000]) This implies being wholly independent of humans.

          1.4.11.6.4.1. Con:
          If immaterial souls exist that are not subject to physical laws, God could interact with these immaterial souls without violating his transcendence according to this definition.

          1.4.11.6.4.2. Con:
          "Independent of" is not synonymous with "hermetically sealed from". The theist's claim is that God's nature and power exist independently of the material universe. That is, God does not depend on the universe for existence—this is to say neither that God does not effect the universe, nor that his will does not take outside particulars into account.

      1.4.11.7. Con:
      The quality "transcendent" precludes the possibility of an existent referent to provide meaning to the word "God."

    1.4.12. Pro:
    God is said to be all-knowing (omniscient), but there are things that God cannot know.

      1.4.12.1. Pro:
      The definition of God is self-contradictory in that he is omniscient but also impassable [1001]. Either God has experiential knowledge of suffering (which violates impassibility) or does not (which violates omniscience.)

        1.4.12.1.1. Con:
        -> See 1.4.7.7.1.1.1.1.

        1.4.12.1.2. Con:
        -> See 1.4.7.7.1.1.

        1.4.12.1.3. Con:
        Not all classical theists would agree that God is impassable.

      1.4.12.2. Con:
      -> See 1.4.7.7.1.1.

      1.4.12.3. Pro:
      -> See 1.4.7.7.3.

      1.4.12.4. Pro:
      Humans (and perhaps animals) have free will. For this to be true, God could not know what their actions were going to be in advance, otherwise it wouldn't truly be free.

        1.4.12.4.1. Con:
        Norman Swartz contends that arguments using free will to deny omniscience commit the modal fallacy [1002].

        1.4.12.4.2. Pro:
        We seem to have free will because we are conscious.

          1.4.12.4.2.1. Con:
          Our consciousness seems not to require free will as it happens in deterministic settings (ie. cells).

          1.4.12.4.2.2. Con:
          Consciousness does not seem to require free will, as sugested by the ever more complex digital intelligence and understanding which don't rely on free will at all.

            1.4.12.4.2.2.1. Con:
            A simulation of consciousness does not imply consciousness. The "complex" digital intelligences we have today are vastly inferior [1003] to a human mind.

        1.4.12.4.3. Pro:
        Free-will means that humans are free to make choices, which necessarily implies that their choices cannot be known a-priori. If an all-knowing God knows every choice we make before we make it, then it cannot be said that we have free-will.

          1.4.12.4.3.1. Con:
          According to compatibilism [1004] free choices can be made even if God knows them beforehand.

            1.4.12.4.3.1.1. Pro:
            -> See 1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.3.1.

            1.4.12.4.3.1.2. Pro:
            -> See 1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.5.

          1.4.12.4.3.2. Con:
          A-priori here means in advance and refers to something that does not exist. In other words, a non-being. God knows all the being, meaning all there is to be known.

        1.4.12.4.4. Con:
        There is no objective evidence for the existence of free will in the universe, thus free will does not exist.

        1.4.12.4.5. Con:
        Even if God's foreknowledge must result in a fully determined universe, this would not make free will impossible.

          1.4.12.4.5.1. Pro:
          -> See 1.3.7.3.4.2.3.1.

        1.4.12.4.6. Con:
        If God is outside time, as is often understood, then God sees all of history at the same time. We mortals are yet to discover what we will freely decide - but it is all visible to God.

          1.4.12.4.6.1. Con:
          The existence of a being that that can see the future as a fixed certainty (even if only from that being's perspective) contradicts the existence of free will because it would mean we could not do otherwise than what that being knows we will do.

            1.4.12.4.6.1.1. Con:
            Because God is outside of time His "seeing the future" is much the same as his "seeing the present". If the latter wouldn't mean that we aren't free in what we're doing now, then the former doesn't mean that we won't be free then.

              1.4.12.4.6.1.1.1. Con:
              That the perspective of a being outside of time would work that way itself opposes the idea of free will by indicating that the future is fixed.

            1.4.12.4.6.1.2. Con:
            It does not contradict free will, as it doesn't entail that we *could not* do otherwise, only that we *will not* do otherwise.

              1.4.12.4.6.1.2.1. Pro:
              The argument from divine foreknowledge has the form (1) God knows that person P will perform action A. (2) Necessarily, If God knows that person P will perform action A, then person P will perform action A. (3) Therefore, necessarily, person P will perform action A. The "necessarily" in the conclusion here is what apparently makes it the case that we *could not* do otherwise. But the argument is fallacious. Remove the "necessarily" from (3) and it's valid, but then doesn't contradict free will.

        1.4.12.4.7. Con:
        Free will doesn't necessarily exist [1005]. As such, there doesn't seem to be anything escaping omniscience.

      1.4.12.5. Con:
      God (as recognized by the three largest monotheistic religions) is a being of incomprehensible nature. To posit that a human can even fathom what it means that God "knows" is to admit to a fundamentally flawed understanding of omniscience

        1.4.12.5.1. Con:
        -> See 1.3.8.2.4.2.3.

        1.4.12.5.2. Con:
        Saying that X (in this case god) has a property that is incomprehensible is a non-statement. We can't know that something has a property without first defining what that property is. If the property is "all-knowing" or "omniscient" we first have to explain what that means before we can claim that it has that property.

        1.4.12.5.3. Con:
        That omniscience is something we cannot fathom is not a relevant or valid definition.

      1.4.12.6. Pro:
      Many theists hold that God is both all-powerful and all-knowing, yet this entails a contradiction because God would have knowledge of what happens in the future and would be incapable of acting otherwise, and thus lacking free will.

        1.4.12.6.1. Pro:
        An all-powerful being has no limits to what it can do (besides logical impossibilities), and thus must have the ability to make choices and have free will.

          1.4.12.6.1.1. Pro:
          If the universe is deterministic, then free will cannot exist.

        1.4.12.6.2. Con:
        It's possible that God has both made His decisions in advance, and is spontaneously capable of changing His mind and doing something else.

          1.4.12.6.2.1. Con:
          An all-knowing god would already know all the things that he will change his mind about therefore still acting deterministic. He can't both know the future and be able change his mind in a way that he didn't already anticipate beforehand. Therefore god is either all-knowing or can change his mind (mutually exclusive).

        1.4.12.6.3. Con:
        -> See 1.4.9.1.7.2.3.

        1.4.12.6.4. Con:
        Omnipotence only implies determinism for a certain account of freedom, namely that for a being to be free it must be able, all other things being equal, to do otherwise. If the free action of the creature is posited as one of the effects of God's deeds, then the creature is not free in this sense. However this definition of freedom is rejected by many classical theists (cf Thomas Aquinas for whom freedom arises from intellectual universality) and therefore the argument does not follow.

        1.4.12.6.5. Con:
        Even if the universe is deterministic, God, as creator of the universe, is immune from such determinism.

          1.4.12.6.5.1. Pro:
          The creator must be capable of existing without the creation.

            1.4.12.6.5.1.1. Pro:
            If an eternal God requires the existence of His creation, then He could not have existed without it to have created it. This would make the universe either God, or a part of God. This is the god of pantheism or panentheism, not the God of classical theism.

          1.4.12.6.5.2. Pro:
          If the universe is deterministic, then it is the creator who made it so. Because God is immutable, it follows that once God decided (determined) what would happen in the universe, He would not change his mind. In short, it is possible that God freely chose to create a deterministic universe.

        1.4.12.6.6. Con:
        There is no contradiction between all-knowing and all-powerful, unless you mistakenly assume that God is an entity inside of the universe. Classically, he has been conceived of as "outside" of the universe, including time itself. If God is outside of time, then his knowledge of the future does not imply strict determinism.

          1.4.12.6.6.1. Pro:
          God is outside the Universe in the same way a computer programmer is "outside" his program. He can control and affect the program and can see the beginning and end parameters. If the computer program is "artificially intelligent," it can be said to have a measure of "free will," even if the parameters are limited and/or predetermined.

        1.4.12.6.7. Con:
        If the universe unfolds at each step according to the will of God; then the future is knowable by God as it is determined according to God's free will.

      1.4.12.7. Pro:
      -> See 1.4.7.7.1.1.2.

    1.4.13. Con:
    God constrains himself with his own attributes. That is, his love cannot invalidate his justice, his justice invalidate his grace, and his grace invalidate his requirement of righteousness.

      1.4.13.1. Con:
      Justice and Grace and (arguably) love are specifically Christian claims on the nature of God that are not necessarily entailed by the classical theist conception of Pure Act that is omniscient, omnipotent, and benevolent.

      1.4.13.2. Con:
      Self-constraint ("constrains himself") is not counter to the claim it responds to. It is an example of God being omnipotent, not an example of omnipotence being contradictory or incoherent.
